Pia Wallén, CVX/CVE Convex/Concave rug for Asplund, 1996 - produced from 1998  
Matz Borgström, O rug for Asplund, 1995  
Thomas Sandell, Lucia candlesticks for Asplund, 1995 - also produced by Cappellini

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7af01c6bf168fd589b0040d59814ee9296401ad406623c867bd89c6b4d14f2b9.jpg)

Erik Gunnar Asplund, Model no. GA-2 armchair for Nordiska Kompaniet, c. 1931 - later reissued by Källemo

Erik Gunnar Asplund, Karmstol chair for Nordiska Kompaniet, 1931

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/af551bcb48fb7d371ad9d7d2fd8167bcd995fb263b05acd0328710765df15ee0.jpg)

# erik gunnar asplund

(b. 1885 Stockholm - d. 1940 Stockholm, Sweden)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f88b16a78a73cbcf252558e4fb683cb2922f58d752e1d436533f3784809890ee.jpg)

One of the most prominent advocates of functionalism in Scandinavia and generally considered to be Sweden's most important architect, Erik Gunnar Asplund fused common sense with artistic intuition in his life-long search for balanced unity and clarity of form. Having studied at the Kungliga Konsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts), Stockholm, from 1905 to 1909 he initially pursued a career in painting. From 1909, however, he concentrated on architecture and shortly thereafter opened his own architectural practice in Stockholm. He became editor of the journal Teknisk Tidskrift Arkitektur in 1917, and the same year received much praise for his interiors exhibited at the Liljevalchs Art Gallery in Stockholm. From 1911 to 1930, he designed a number of Neo-Classical inspired buildings, most notably the Scandia Cinema, Stockholm (1923), and the Stockholm City Library (1928). During this period he also designed furnishings that were similarly classicist in style, such as his Senna chair of 1925. Asplund's work underwent a dramatic transformation when he was appointed chief architect for the 1930 Stockholm Exhibition organized by the Svenska Sljödföreningen (Swedish Society of Craft and Design). At

this event, which drew significant international recognition, his Modernist glass and metal exhibition building introduced the International Style to Sweden for the first time. By combining Modern functionalism with the grace of Scandinavian Neo-Classicism, Asplund developed a language of design that was less severe than that of contemporary German Modernist architects. Among Asplund's many other achievements was the design of the Skogskykogarden, Stockholm, and an extension to the Town Hall in Gothenburg. From 1931 to 1940, he was also professor of architecture at Kungliga Konsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts), Stockholm.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b52f2be072bddc14e812a80a9fb0d024e3ea0a95db5cab77738d3d070647503a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/957edfabc02c05de4d2ca87dc5e0da589ba7d503c6ecdf080b0345b7c3c68eef.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/926c83a47e5289449cb064540afbb7a392734c6223c5cd2fbab0ab6e86f0b827.jpg)

David Lewis, Beavision 5   
plasma television, 2002

Peter Bang and Svend Olufsen, c. 1925

# bang & olufsen

Known throughout the world for its stylish stateof-the-art consumer electronic equipment, Bang & Olufsen was founded in 1925 by Peter Bang (1900-1957) and Svend Olufsen (18971949) in the remote western Jutland region of Denmark. Peter Bang's father, Camillo, was manager of the progressive Copenhagen department store A.C. Illum (founded 1891), which achieved remarkable success through an innovative sales strategy of offering middle-class customers a wide range of high-quality products by means of a knowledgeable and friendly staff. The store was managed by Camillo Bang in a way that saw the "customer as a person", and this no doubt had a formative influence on the way Peter Bang would later approach the design, manufacture and retail of his electronic products. Indeed, as a child he began experimenting with radio equipment, but found that the many batteries he required needed continual recharging, which involved the use of potentially hazardous acid. Frustrated by the shortcomings of these batteries, Bang eliminated the need for them altogether by building a mains powered radio receiver. Later, Bang trained as an engineer at the Arhus Electrotechnical School,

where he graduated in 1924. One of his fellow students was Svend Olufsen, who subsequently undertook radio experiments at his family's estate in Quistrup. While attempting to build a mains eciver, Olufsen turned to Bang for help. Bang had just returned from America, where he had been researching the latest developments in radio technology as pioneered by the Radio Corporation of America, which under the visionary leadership of David Sarnoff had commenced manufacture of its revolutionary Radio Music Box. In November 1925, the two friends officially founded Bang & Olufsen, which was committed to the idea of "power for the radio directly from the mains." To this end they began working on an aggregate to connect battery receivers to mains power supply, which resulted in the introduction in 1926 of the firm's first marketed product, the B&O Eliminator. A year later, they developed their own mains powered radio known as the Five Lamper, which had a wooden cabinet inlaid with classical motifs and three Bakelite dials, Although B&O equipment was at the cutting-edge of radio technology and renowned for the clarity of its sound, it was wrapped up as period-style furniture. In 1933,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a03be3aa2b08cf11b197e8b35f486f70daf93ee96d1336af31f829a8621536d8.jpg)

the Five Lamper radio and radio gramophone were lambasted for their old-fashioned aesthetic when the prominent architect and designer Ole Wanscher commented ironically, "To make the radio fit in nicely is easily achieved through Caucasian walnut and badger legs or Chinoiserie - like a theatre design conceived by the furniture dealer's youngest apprentice" Taking this criticism to heart, Peter Bang and Svend Olufsen decided to embrace a Modern aesthetic that would be in keeping with the advanced technology they were pioneering. The same year Bang designed the sleek Hyperbo 5 RG Steel radio-gramophone, which was inspired by Marcel Breuer's (1902- 1981) tubular metal cantilevered chairs. This rarkablyrar-lokig den ws old l side other radios and radio-gramophones that continued to be housed in Art Deco inspired wooden cabinets. The real breakthrough for the company came in 1938, however, when it developed a radio that was housed in a casing made entirely of the new "material of a thousand uses" Bakelite. While the elegant Beolit 39 was inspired by the streamlined forms of American industrial design, such as those pioneered by Raymond

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2bf671aaf79a32f6bc6c6c40e54d660a89149b8f765ac5ce832eb6a4c609aa9e.jpg)

4Δ Bang & Olufsen team, radio gramophone Five Lamper 5 RG Funkis for Bang & Olufsen, 1932 - a "Chinoiserie" model that led to criticism from Ole Wanscher

Bang & Olufsen team, Hyperbo 5 RG Steel radio gramophone for Bang & Olufsen, 1934 - inspired by Peter Bang's office chair that had been designed by the Bauhaus designer, Marcel Breuer

Bang & Olufsen team, Beolit 39 radio, 1938 - The idea for Bang & Olufsen's first streamlined design to have a casing made completely from Bakelite was generated by a listeners' competition

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/478037be809c4eb1e7597b19e2bb70480899dace28648364e40db022ec1131f9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fb938e53223d57ebe2f90dae44767060a1aae3a1a532d484bfc20d9d3ead48cb.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/22c42caf9f333c4b1ae7361eca432727c0bba6b790da15c42fbd50089bf0a1b5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/be17abe7ad5e7edb62507d214f30fed1b6738b472c39bde9d4175123c3c68cba.jpg)

Δ Jacob Jensen (assisted by David Lewis), Beolab 5000 hi-fi system for Bang & Olufsen, 1967 - this revolutionary design introduced the new slide-rule dial

Jacob Jensen, Beolit 400-600 radios for Bang & Olufsen, 1970

> David Lewis, BeoCom 2 telephone for Bang & Olufsen, 2000

Loewy (1893-1986) and Harold L. van Doren (1895-1957), it also predicted the sleek rationalism of much post-war industrial design, such as that promoted by Dieter Rams (b. 1932) at Braun. During the Second World War, B&O established an illegal radio service for communications between the British and the Danish Freedom Fighters. Because of the company's links with the resistance, B&O's factory was bombed in 1945. Shortly afterwards, the factory was rebuilt and production of radios resumed, but again the company was criticized, this time by the celebrated designer Poul Henningsen, who said its products had the appearance of furniture-like pastiches. To justify the company's by-line - "The Danish Hallmark of Quality" - B&O began working with some of Denmark's leading architects and designers in the mid-1950s, including Sigvard Bernadotte and Acton Bjørn. These collaborations produced spectacular results - the company's new generation high-quality products were completely integrated, and their refined Modern forms communicated a sophisticated design rhetoric to a new and highly receptive design-conscious audience. In the 1960s, B&O pioneered the design of integrated

stereo record players, "for those who discuss taste and quality before price". Having become a pioneer of audio technology, the company later launched several landmark designs by Jacob Jensen, which married superlative quality with sleek good looks. Jensen's "easy touch" layouts and "flush-designed" systems quite literally redefined the design vocabulary of hi-fi equipment. Throughout the 1960s and 1970s, Jensen's designs came to epitomize both the technical and aesthetic refinement of Danish design. During the 1980s, however, the company suffered badly from Asian competition and eventually formed an alliance with Philips in order to survive. In the early 1990s, B&O changed its approach to manufacturing, moving strategically from mass-production to order-production. There was also an important shift in design terms, which was implemented by David Lewis (b. 1939), who had been Jensen's assistant. Lewis' revolutionary Beosystem 2500 (1990), with its all-in-one layout, set itself apart from the anonymous stacked black boxes produced by B&O's competitors. More recently David Lewis has designed Bang & Olufsen's first plasma screen television, the Beo-Vision 5, which can either be hung on or propped up against a wall - an innovative, flexible and elegant solution that was inspired by paintings in a gallery that were being removed to make room for a new exhibition. Believing that aesthetics are as important to consumers as function and ease of use, Bang & Olufsen skilfully balances external design with internal technological considerations in order to create distinctive products that powerfully communicate the company's values and vision about technology and product concepts. Today, the company continues its long-standing commitment to good design while remaining faithful to its founder's guiding principle: "A never-failing will to want to create the best and find new ways."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/28265bcb09c88c3ba0be13051b8665dd2efaa69e93fe587d0b4b63e5c17d591a.jpg)

David Lewis, Beosystem 2500 upright "all-in-one" system for Bang & Olufsen, 1991

David Lewis, Beosystem 9000 CD-player for Bang & Olufsen, 1996 - this system allows up to six compact disks to be played from any linked room in a house

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0f3f58af4387b74184f80379dcacaf428d72dbe195d73f04dbea63fe47a3bae3.jpg)  
Jacob Bang, Primula glassware for Holmegaard, 1930  
Jacob Bang, Hogla wine glass for Holmegaard, 1928

# jacob bang

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/40a371127c505a7ed72efda57f8887a61ef234b1f61f9988ec01e1a358b46f14.jpg)

Jacob E. Bang initially studied sculpture and later trained as an architect at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) from 1916 to 1921. In 1924 he began working as an industrial designer for the Holmegaard glassworks and four years later was appointed artistic director. Having left Holmegaard in 1942 Bang subsequently established his own studio and began working as a freelance designer. Between 1942 and 1957 he designed ceramics for the Nymølle faïence factory and metalware products for Pan Aluminium and the silversmith F. Hingelberg. In 1957 he was appointed artistic director of the Kastrup glassworks, which subsequently merged with Holmegaard in 1965. While at Kastrup, Bang designed no-nonsense glassware that corresponded with his guiding principle that designed products should be sensible, simple and pleasurable to use. As one of the leading functionalists in Denmark, Bang produced numerous glassware designs, such as his well-known Hogla wine glass and his extensive Primula range (both produced by Holmegaard), which were notable for their simplified rational forms. Although his work was influenced by the Modern Movement, Bang steered clear of the dogmatic functionalism emanating from Germany and produced designs that were softer and more human-focused. By refining his designs thoroughly until every detail had achieved near functional and aesthetic perfection, Bang was able to create some of the most beautiful and timeless pieces of glassware of the 20th century.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0a5a27a1a8177e650ac8e534d95740dc32c4976d88fc3591b347cd2e31e21261.jpg)

Sigvard Bernadotte, Model no. 1015 silver coffee set with plastic bound handles for Georg Jensen, c. 1953

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/db772869017faf9b21b2e16659260eefc264362d98b182e899726e9f13aa3f20.jpg)  
Sigvard Bernadotte, Model Bernadotte silver cutlery for Georg Jensen, 1939

# sigvard bernadotte

(b. 1907 Stockholm - d. 2002 Stockholm, Sweden)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/33ef03cf1294e36b0f579deecfc543abfa615317deaab665ab68e84476b7e4fe.jpg)

The son of King Gustav VI Adolf of Sweden, Count Sigvard Bernadotte was one of the earliest pioneers of industrial design in Scandinavia. Initially, he studied political science as well as several other subjects at the Faculty of Art, Uppsala University, but it was decided that his artistic talent should be cultivated and so he went on to study drawing and painting under Olle Hjortzberg at the Kungliga Konsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts) in Stockholm from 1929 to 1931. Bernadotte later completed his studies at the Staatsschule für Angewandte Kunst (National School of Applied Arts), Munich, in 1931, and following this joined the Georg Jensen workshop. Here he became the first designer in the company to adopt geometric forms as opposed to the naturalistic Arts & Crafts influenced forms that were so characteristic of the silversmith's output. His Bernadotte flatware of 1939 demonstrates the angularity of form that was typical of his work from this period. He also designed a volumetric metal and Bakelite cocktail shaker during the late 1930s for an English manufacturer. During the 1930s he travelled throughout Europe, Africa and, most importantly for his future career, America, where he visited several leading industrial design consultants including Raymond Loewy (1893-1986), Walter Dorwin Teague (1883-1960), Donald Deskey (1894-1989) and Henry Dreyfuss (1904- 1972). Leaving his position as a director of Georg Jensen, he established a Copenhagen-based industrial design consultancy in 1950 with the Danish designer Acton Bjørn (who had previously worked in collaboration with Douglas and Boeing on the design of aircraft for SAs). Known as Sigvard Bernadotte & Acton Bjørn, Industridesign, Merkantil Grafik, this multi-disciplinary practice's first notable success was a compact metal and plastic adding-machine (1952) for Odhner. The practice was later expanded, with offices being opened in Stockholm and New York. Over their careers together, Bernadotte and Bjørn designed a number of widely celebrated industrial products, including the melamine Margrethe stacking bowls for Rosti (1950), which had non-slip rubber bases and highly practical pouring spouts and handgrips, and the compact Privat typewriter (1960) for Facit in "Viking grey", which reflected the influence of Marcello Nizzoli's (1887-1969) earlier Lettera typewriter for Olivetti (1950). Other prod-

##

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5d76749cf67ded6ccb5b0cb00264d6e20df8cd0545cd93b815629c4168d6ce85.jpg)  
Sigvard Bernadotte, Contex 10 adding machine for Contex, c. 1960 - codesigned with Acton Bjorn  
Sigvard Bernadotte, Odhner adding machine for Odhner, 1952 - co-designed with Acton Bjørn

ucts designed by the practice included a cigarette lighter for Consul (a German company), kitchenware for Modernum, Morsø and Electro-Helios, an in-flight tray and cutlery for SAS, a modular kitchen for Formfac, the Silent extractor fan for Bahco, consumer electric equipment for Ericsson and Bang & Olufsen and a plastic picnic set for Husqvarna. Bernadotte & Bjorn was the first industrial design practice in Denmark and one of the earliest in Sweden, and in the 1950s became the first Scandinavian design consultancy to achieve international recognition. In 1964, Bernadotte went on to independently found the Bernadotte Design Studio, which specialized in heavy industrial design, from forklift trucks to outboard motors. As one of the most important pioneers of industrial design in Europe, Sigvard Bernadotte also became the first European designer to be elected to the American Designers' Institute.

Sigvard Bernadotte, electric iron for General Electric, c. 1965

Sigvard Bernadotte, Ginge Precision weighing scales for Ginge, c. 1952-1953 - codesigned with Acton Bjorn

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/843cf90a032c94442ac6a230c21d8a4aa43f1123f6e33ebabfe152969954a4a7.jpg)

Thorvald Bindesbell, silver beaker for A. Michelsen, 1899

Thorvald Bindesbell, chair made by J. P. Mørck for the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale"

OVERLEAF Thorvald Bindesbell, earthenware plates made at G. Eifrig's workshop, c. 1900 - with characteristic Art Nouveau cloud-like motifs

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/782579c24864c877d6f7e7954835fc238fe538a601f4b11d535b16b64e2c2d57.jpg)

# thorvald bindesbøll

## (b. 1846 Copenhagen - d. 1908 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/cbb3722c301672d6487aa1ab590f05fa897b7e5ea930d3dc4896011ef60b47e5.jpg)

The son of the Danish architect M. G. Bindesbøll, Thorvald Bindesbøll trained as an architect at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design), Copenhagen, where he graduated in 1861. From 1872 he received numerous decorative art commissions that included designs for furniture, embroideries and book illustrations. In 1882 Bindesbøll exhibited his work both in Denmark and abroad, and the following year began designing ceramics for the J. Walmann Pottery, which were inspired by the art of Japan and natural forms. Also influenced by both William Morris (1834-1896) and the British Arts & Crafts Movement, Bindesboll developed a highly individual and expressionistic style that was characterized by bold shapes and bizarre patterns that often included abstracted cloud, wave and seaweed-like motifs. From the 1890s onwards, Bindesbøll was the leading Danish designer of his generation and his decorative Art Nouveau style had much influence on many younger designers, such as F. Hegel, Molger Kyster and even Georg Jensen. During the 1890s, Bindesbøll also worked for the Københavns Lervarefabrik and the Kaehler Keramik ceramics factories - the former producing his well-known earthenware dishes decorated with incised cloud-like motifs. His first silverware designs date from 1899; with their more delicate paisley-like motifs, which are reminiscent of those found on Indian textiles, these pieces display great finesse and stylistic innovation. His silver designs for A. Michelsen were subsequently shown at the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale". Throughout his career, Bindesbøll successfully translated his individualistic and forward-looking style into a number of diverse disciplines, including metalwork, leatherwork, jewellery, furniture, textiles, glass, graphics, lighting and stained glass. As one of the leading exponents of the Scandinavian Art Nouveau movement, Bindesbøll's designs were the antithesis of contemporary industrially-produced products. His adoption of abstracted decoration and simple forms, however, can be seen as anticipating the later emergence of modern design in Scandinavia.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f6c96310ecd5ef1c8a6a31b935ac8730ea822b20dd519ee5f1a975ce392b5560.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/43526f8da1c39d23b53e2f4bc8498fa951a406e75d85c70ec285f26562208d01.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c2d81808db917a3c2937f6a79dc91c7259b91e8d61ec8eacdd044eb0783daae2.jpg)

Pietro Krohn, Heron service for Bing & Grøndahl, 1886-1887

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/169b0839adf1c858e4523d93f0f29f0765ebdddd1d439d4bf20ec27b132e3ee0.jpg)  
Effie Hegermann-Lindencrone, pierced porcelain bowl for Bing & Grøndahl, 1917

# bing & grondahl

In 1833, Frederik Vilhelm Grøndahl began an as apprentice at the Royal Copenhagen factory and six years later was promoted to the position of designer/sculptor. Eventually he was approached in 1852 by two brothers, Jacob Herman Bing and Meyer Herman Bing. The Bings were established art and paper dealers in Copenhagen and wanted to set up the first privately owned porcelain factory in Denmark. The following year the business was established in Copenhagen under the name Bing & Grøndahl Porcelænsfabrik. Initially, the factory mainly produced large biscuit Neo-Classical porcelain figures and reliefs based on sculptures by the Danish artist Bertel Thorvaldsen (1770-1844), which were retailed through M. H. Bing's various galleries. Around 1880, J.H. Bing's sons Ludvig and Harald joined the factory as business and production managers respectively. Later in 1885, Harald Bing appointed the painter and illustrator Pietro Krohn (1840-1905) as the company's artistic director, which heralded a new progressive direction for Bing & Grøndahl. Three years later, Krohn designed the Heron service, which was remarkable for its austere style and asymmetrical patterning. The design of this ser-

vice not only reflected the growing interest in Japanese subjects and Orientalism in general, it also predicted the Art Nouveau style. The majority of the products emanating from Bing & Grøndahl, such as those designed by F. A. Hallin, were either in the Beaux Arts style or were strongly influenced by the work of the Royal Copenhagen factory - indeed several artists were employed by both factories. In 1895 Jens F. Willumsen (1863-1958), who like his predecessor oversaw the production of "art" figures as well as more functional wares, succeeded Krohn as artistic director. During the 1920s and 1930s Bing & Grøndahl continued to produce art wares that included stoneware figures by Mogens Bøggild, animal statues by Knud Kyhn (1880-1969), refined classical tableware by, among others, Kay Bojesen, Ebbe Sadolin (b. 1900) and Hans Tegner, and other decorative pieces by Axel Salto, Kai Nielsen and Jean René Gauguin (1881-1961), son of the post-impressionist painter Paul Gauguin. Briefly between 1937 and 1938 Gunnar Nylund was artistic director of the company before returning to Rörstrand. After the Second World War, the company opened a new plant in Valby for the mass production of tableware, while the original factory in Vestergade continued to produce more artistic wares. In 1947, Finn Juhl received the Eckersberg Medal for his interior design of the Bing & Grøndahl showroom in Copenhagen, which highlighted the company's commitment to design excellence. During the post-war years, Bing & Grøndahl continued producing its earlier designs (including its well-known blue and white porcelain figures) alongside more contemporary wares such as Gertrud Vasegaard's oriental-style stoneware pieces and her Chinese inspired porcelain dinner and tea service. In 1961, Henning Koppel began designing for the Bing & Grøndahl factory. According to him, the "perfect, pure material of this factory invited attempts to go beyond the limits", with some of his designs being "based entirely on the characteristic of the porcelain". Other designs by Koppel, such as his Form 24 service, were translations of his work in silver for Georg Jensen. During this period, the company continued to maintain its reputation for welldesigned tableware as well as figurative sculptures, and kept its long-established studio, that not only employed artists on a full-time basis,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8e8fcff31c563fca0fb370cb355e592a4256edf3e869ded0c84912e8e10e5eb8.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1932045e703b4a30deffb00769d09ad5447f5c7bf964ced8a6d39a8e5d5b5a43.jpg)  
Thorvald Bindesbell, butterfly-pattern plate for Bing & Grøndahl, c. 1885  
Axel Salto, vases for Bing & Grøndahl, 1923-1925 & 1925

but also played host to visiting artists, such as Carl-Harry Stálhane. In the late 1960s, Erik Magnussen also designed several important pieces for the factory, including a stoneware teapot (1963), the Form 25 Termo service (1965), and a porcelain teapot (1969) which had an extremely innovative double-walled form that was incised to provide an integrated handle. In contrast to Magnussen's restrained modern style, the company also produced humorous sculptures by Sten Lykke Madsen made of stoneware. Eventually the factory merged with Royal Copenhagen in 1987 and the original Vestergade plant was closed, but the company's prestigious studio was retained. In Denmark, Bing & Grøndahl was historically second only in importance to Royal Copenhagen, and as such it played a major role in establishing the Danish reputation for progressive yet high quality design in porcelain and stoneware.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e8cfbdb2fe879c810880b1471585cac49cf46d070b49ba814eb832a86051b7d5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/12b40cf994dd25af2395e2a00ed01105d69c12e250a6c45f61a60935a6e3d18c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/64a052118b1a619235d0fca2a697d54e4ef8fc1730301b4092ffafc6aa6e67b0.jpg)  
Hrafnkell Birgisson, graphic rendering of Homobile seating/ storage unit, 1999

# hrafnkell birgisson

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a3bce81c9a7c8ea2007874488786f4ee65d035e7d3f2f78df8c08e7cd1f04d37.jpg)

The Icelandic designer Hrafnkell Birgisson studied at the Reykjavik School of Art (1991-1992), the Icelandic College of Arts & Crafts (1992-1993), and the Polytechnic Hafnarfjördur (1993-1994). In 1995 he moved to Germany, where he studied product design at the Hochschule der Bildenden Künste (Academy of Art) in Saarbrücken under Andreas Brandolini (b. 1951), Harald Hullmann and François Burkhardt, and graduated in 2000. During this five-year period he also undertook a design studies course taught by Professor Achim Heine (b. 1955) at the Hochschule der Künste (Academy of Arts) in Berlin. Working within the fields of graphic design, furniture design, product design and interior design, Birgisson (who now lives in Berlin) has forged an international reputation for his idiosyncratic work that often playfully subverts function. He has won numerous prizes, including the Derag Design Award (Munich) in 1997 for his Sleep & Go hotel pod. He also received a scholarship from the DAAD (Deutscher Akademischer Austauschdienst) in 1998. Birgisson's Homobile seating/storage unit (1999) reveals his interest in multi-functional design solutions, while his Hábolli teacup glasses (1998)

exhibit the quirkiness that is so characteristic of much contemporary Icelandic design, from graphics to fashion. To date, Birgisson's most interesting and potentially farthest-reaching design is his Sleep & Go (1997), which is an extremely innovative example of low-cost product architecture. Designed with industrial mass production in mind, the Sleep & Go could potentially be used as a form of low-cost easy-to-site emergency shelter. The design also predicts a less materialistic and more nomadic way of living. Birgisson's design solutions reflect the innovative nature of Icelandic design, while offering tantalizing glimpses of possible future alternative lifestyles.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fcf8ea4d86222c946db5fa262cf836fb95224bf45696a1660ac86a79ceae0419.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2d5574df9b97ad909614e70994528815d1e100621fe8266d4c3acadae4d83069.jpg)  
Hrafnkell Birgisson, Sleep & Go hotel pod, 1997 - projection for 2020  
Hrafnkell Birgisson, working drawing of Sleep & Go hotel pod, 1997 - projection for 2020

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/03957473248ed3965f3ccd1a0a56dd8323984dd5553e23afa7fb700f99e15325.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0025130c93e1fcc35de8cb42887b51bd46dfa20d77024897ddc7cc5e05114c60.jpg)  
Acton Bjorn, Facit Pl typewriter for Atvidabergs Industrier, 1958 - co-designed with Sigvard Bernadotte

## acton bjørn

## (b. 1910 Copenhagen - d. 1992 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d90f826ee649494d464e81f8a4bdcedd386cdbf40948d7054fad83c7fbf65f7b.jpg)

A pioneer of Scandinavian industrial design, Acton Bjørn studied architecture and town planning prior to working as an industrial designer during the Second World War. He subsequently worked with the aircraft manufacturers Douglas and Boeing on the design of aeroplanes for SAs - an experience that gave him a valuable insight into industrial design practice at one of the highest technical levels. In 1949 he met the designer Sigvard Bernadotte who shared his interests, and the following year they established one of the first Scandinavian industrial design consultancies under the name Sigvard Bernadotte & Acton Bjørn, Industridesign. This Copenhagen-based practice not only designed metalwork and ceramics, but also industrial equipment and machines. Among their best known work was plastic kitchenware for Rosti (1950) and office equipment for Facit (1958). In 1966, Bjørn won a Danish ID Prize for his Beolit 500 transistor radio for Bang & Olufsen. Produced from 1965 to 1969, this design was one of the first radios to achieve a functional simplicity comparable to that of a telephone. The same year Bjørn established his own design practice, Acton Bjørn Tegnestue, in Copenhagen, which specialized in household products and appliances as well as office furniture and packaging. He also received much recognition for his corporate identity work, mainly executed for companies involved in banking and insurance. Bjorn's product designs were classically Scandinavian in that they elegantly balanced practical function with simple sculptural forms.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a921f88114e9cf3b316ae46a67b3eab5efbbff3822e645044319fca5870bc8c0.jpg)

Jonas Bohlin, L/V-Collection pendant lamp (self-production), 1997

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ae9b5c19f774590ade258a7bca865e495745e2c8c6e3945f59c0d97159f3489e.jpg)  
> Jonas Bohlin, Concrete chair (self-production), 1981 - re-issued by Källemo

# jonas bohlin

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/cbd4ca7ff4bc819fbbcae139bb95b812787102d17afdfb5946d5bc7ba8fefb00.jpg)

Jonas Bohlin worked as a civil engineer prior to studying interior design at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design), Stockholm (1976-1981). At his degree show in 1981 he shocked the Swedish design establishment with his Concrete chair - a tubular steel and concrete construction conceived more as an art piece than as usable furniture. The Concrete chair, which made reference to Bohlin's earlier life as an engineer working on bridge building projects, completely countered the tenets of Scandinavian good design and came to epitomize Swedish Post-Modernism. Bohlin subsequently worked as a freelance designer for Källemo, which produced the Concrete chair in 1982 in a limited edition. Källemo also made available his Concave chaise longue (1983), another Post-Modern design with a segmented form and highly graphic profile. Idiosyncratic designs such as these were widely publicized throughout the 1980s and helped to establish Bohlin as one of Sweden's most avantgarde designers. Bohlin founded his own design practice 1983, and two years later established a Stockholm-based art and design gallery, Stockholm Mobile, exhibiting his own work, which played

with form, function and emotion. In 1988 Bohlin became a professor at Beckman's School of Design, Stockholm, and was later appointed head of its Form Course (1992-1996). The same year he was awarded the prestigious Georg Jensen Prize. During the 1980s and 1990s, Bohlin exhibited his numerous limited-edition designs in Scandinavia, Japan, America and Europe. One of his most notable projects known as "LIV" (the Swedish word for "life" and the Roman numeral for "54"), involved him and 53 friends of his rowing from Stockholm to Paris over a period of 116 days. This extraordinary exercise, marked by the publication of a book documenting the journey, inspired a design collection that included lighting, furniture, a vase and a rug. From 1991 to 1993, Bohlin was chairman of SIR (National Association of Swedish Interior Architects) and since then has remained one of the leading figures in the contemporary Swedish design community. On his approach to design Bohlin has stated: "I want my furniture to touch and please, to communicate with time and space, to be made by hand and heart, and to be considerate of mankind and nature."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/69d8e18e86216224c8e09a179c1acbf9bc0e26e53e88e99a8c8544abfb8cedc4.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8c7a60c77ff530ee1c643a1652e42e5966e9ccdd05050637e9e8c923c765a78c.jpg)

Installation at Jonas Bohlin Design, Stockholm, 2000 - showing LIV-Collection stools and pendant lamps (self-production), 1997

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0c25beb0ddea8bdb37721a508477fd7fda67e3e08142f9cf86ffd70c15767c5c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5514ec2a7311e4b85581505afe1fa3444b7269569334c2d6fff9359ccbee25b0.jpg)  
Kay Bojesen, Monkey toy, Kay Bojesen workshop, 1951

# kay bojesen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a6db770c8ff4595f21923a34b55d00b3cf75d07a44886d54d488b67df58ab990.jpg)

One of the most important pioneers of Danish design, Kay Bojesen initially trained as a silversmith at the Georg Jensen workshop from 1907 to 1910. He continued his studies in 1911 at the Fachschule für das Edelmetallgewerbe (Technical School for Precious Metals), Schwäbisch-Gmünd, and subsequently worked for a short while as a craftsman in Paris. After returning to Copenhagen he established his own workshop there in 1913. His early work reflected the rich decorative influence of the Georg Jensen studio, but by 1931 he had adopted a modern style that emphasized simple forms, undecorated surfaces, and suitability for mechanized production. From 1930 to 1931, Bojesen designed ceramics for the Bing & Grøndahl factory. As a leading figure of Scandinavian Modernism, he was also the driving force behind the opening of Den Permanente in Copenhagen in 1931 - one of the first design centres to be established in Europe - which acted as a permanent showcase for the best products of contemporary Danish design. During the 1930s, Bojesen also designed numerous silver flatware and hollowware pieces, including his influential Grand Prix cutlery (1938), which was notable for its elegant pared down form and flowing line. Many of his silver designs were later translated into stainless steel and industrially produced by Motala Verkstad in Motala and the Universal Steel Company in Copenhagen. Bojesen was also one of the first modern designers to begin exploring the formal potential of the three-pronged fork. Apart from manufacturing his own designs, Bojesen's workshop also produced metalware by other designers, including Finn Juhl, Ole Wanscher and Magnus Stephensen. In 1938, the Danske Kunstindustrimuseum in Copenhagen staged a one-man show of Bojesen's work and he later exhibited at the 1940 (VIl) Milan Triennale. During the 1940s and 1950s, Bojesen continued producing metalware items which brought him widespread recognition. He was awarded a Grand Prix at the 1951 (IX) Milan Triennale, and the following year was appointed silversmith to the King of Denmark. During this period he also received international acclaim for a number of landmark wooden designs, including his characterful monkey, rabbit, elephant, bear and puffin toys (which he had begun to make as early as 1935), and his functional yet sculptural teak salad bowls, such as his spherical double

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0f52aa8d19241c6ac60a50b33316a3c8a919fcfab147e15aca501eeb46b5c5d6.jpg)

bowl - a gold medal winner at the 1954 (X) Milan Trienale. Bojesen's workshop, now overseen by his son, continues to produce some of his best-known designs, which are still widely seen to exemplify the sensual yet practical style synonymous with Danish design.

Kay Bojesen, cutlery in silver, stainless steel and ebony, Kay Bojesen's workshop, 1938 (from 1951 manufactured in stainless steel as Grand Prix cutlery)

Kay Bojesen, teak double salad bowl with servers, Kay Bojesen's workshop, 1949

Kay Bojesen, silver serving dishes with ebony handles, Kay Bojesen's workshop, 1951
