![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f47b7abecfc90674cc7b2650dff8e83b12317970f009f7b0c8c4b777d49c7472.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/871c5d4d5976ffe37940c0b979a4779c938e09602f91764b346a792c9172808a.jpg)

Vivianna Bülow-Hübe, silver and rutilated quartz necklace, 1954-1959

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d57ef0eb31b5c7a048583e11a822660653ef75b3fdf7309b6e303107ece9799c.jpg)  
Vivianna Bülow-Hübe, silver bangle watch, 1962 - produced by Georg Jensen from 1967 as model no. 326

# vivianna bülow-hübe

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fbec1b1a5b2eb6b9d12437f3cb9078922bc5bbf5effba9d094a670d821a89554.jpg)

The Swedish jewellery designer Vivianna Torun Bülow-Hübe studied at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) and the Kungliga Tekniska Högskolan (Royal Institute of Technology) in Stockholm (1945-1950). In 1951 she became the first female silversmith to establish her own studio in Sweden. Based in Stockholm, she initially concentrated on the design of wooden jewellery. The same year she received a commission for silver jewellery from Orrefors and in 1954 was awarded a silver medal at the X Milan Triennale for her innovative designs in this medium. Believing that "A simple silver coil that highlights the line of the neck is more beautiful than all the diamonds in the world" and that "Diamonds are a girl's worst friend", Bülow-Hübe often created plain ring-style necklaces incorporating pendants of pebbles and fossils as well as shimmering glass amulets. Bülow-Hübe moved to Paris in 1956 and two years later established a studio in Biot, France employing five assistants. While on the beach there collecting pebbles she met Pablo Picasso, who arranged for a solo exhibition of her work in Antibes in 1958. During this period her designs, such as the exquisite free-

form Mobile necklace (1954-1959), became increasingly sculptural. In 1960 she was awarded a gold medal at the XIl Milan Triennale and the coveted Lunning Prize. From 1967 she designed an extensive range of jewellery and watches for Georg Jensen in gold, silver and stainless steel. Typically, her designs for Georg Jensen, such as her well-known bangle-style wristwatch (1962), reflected the new sculptural confidence of the period. In 1968 she moved to Germany and established a design studio first in Wolfsburg, then in Wendhausen. While in Germany, Bülow-Hübe designed porcelain for Hutschenreuther and glassware for Löhnberg glassworks. She moved to Jakarta, Indonesia, in 1978, where she opened a studio and worked as a design consultant for a social foundation. In 1983, she was named an "Honorary Smith" by the Society of Contemporary Swedish Silver. Today, Bülow-Hübe continues to produce designs for Georg Jensen that are notable for their forward-looking, sensuous and sculptural qualities.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/40a46a8bcec00e03297c4dc18f46292d646043a3e274d96468fe30786100181b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f02844968ec68293a81c235b37dcbf691b9dfa06fc0986e5fc66153cbf7b5f63.jpg)

Gunnar Cyrén, Nobel glassware range for Orrefors, 1991

Gunnar Cyrén, Pop goblets for Orrefors, 1965-1966

# gunnar cyrén(

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e4873b0a702640ee88ee1e807470ef259dd821a6d03c0e49c60fe39d3ae94379.jpg)

Gunnar Cyrén studied metalwork at the Högre Konstindustriella Skolan (Higher College of Industrial Arts), and also at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design), Stockholm, receiving journeyman certificates in both goldsmithing and silversmithing in 1951 and 1956 respectively. He also briefly trained at the Cologne Werkschulen in 1954. After the completion of his studies, Cyrén began working in 1959 as a designer for the Orrefors glassworks. In contrast to the prevailing Scandinavian taste for rusticity in the early 1960s, Cyrén designed glassware that was distinguished by its precision, refinement and use of virtuoso craft skills. This approach to design is exemplified by his elegant overlaid glass bowl decorated with bear motifs (now in the Nationalmuseum, Stockholm). Cyrén's later iconoclastic Pop goblets (1965-1966) with their colourfully striped stems and opalescent bowls were similarly radical, yet perfectly encapsulated the joyful and youthful exuberance of the period. For Cyrén, design has always been a very private activity and his work often displays "a personal touch" that has on occasions been at odds with the constraints of industrial mass production. Designing either costly one-off pieces (using Graal techniques and decorative cutting) or glassware for small-scale serial production, Cyrén has concerned himself with objects that not only function well, but also exhibit a "charming" personality. In 1966 Cyrén was awarded the Lunning Prize and during the 1970s began designing for Dansk International Designs and the Gefle Porslinsfabrik. In the early 1970s he also began designing glassware for industrial production and from the mid-1970s worked as a silversmith, at times combining both glass and silver in a single object. During the 1980s his glassware designs continued to display the restraint and elegance for which he had become so well known. In 1988 he received the Prince Eugen Medal and in 1991 he contributed glass tableware (with green, amber or gold coloured stems) to the specially commissioned Nobel service, which commemorated the 9oth anniversary of the Nobel Foundation. Today, as "the innovative grand old man of glass", Cyrén continues to design original and beautiful glassware for the Orrefors glassworks.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c502aa7b09abebdd0e4c2151d77e8328bdb319d13f7ceaadae9e05d546da1d0d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5bf30736c77be49094cb3d49f83b97348fd2e8c5cfa5f6f2d1651e27ae72dc00.jpg)  
Ewald Dahlskog, wine glasses for Kosta, 1927

# ewald dahlskog

Ewald Dahlskog studied at the Centraltryckeriet (Central Printing Office), Stockholm (1905-1908), and later at the Kungliga Tekniska Högskolan (Royal Institute of Technology), Stockholm (19081912), and the Kungliga Konsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts), Stockholm (1913-1917). Around 1920 he took several study trips abroad including visits to France, Italy and Tunisia, and from 1924 to 1926 he lived in Paris as a freelance painter, illustrator and journalist, On his return to Sweden in 1926 he began producing designs for the Kosta glassworks, such as his stemmed cutglass bowl with highly stylized motifs of minotaurs and palm trees, which showed the influence of the Art Deco style. The following year he designed a range of wine glasses with inverted cone bowls and faceted stems that had a more modern aesthetic, as well as several free-blown vases and a decanter that were vernacular in style. Dahlskog also received widespread recognition for his marquetry panel designs, particularly for those he designed in 1926 for the Stockholms Konsterthus (Stockholm Concert Hall) which were adorned with stylized scenes of shepherds and soldiers. Three years later, Dahlskog began designing ceramics for

Bobergs Fajansfabrik in Gefle, including his wellknown series of faience flower vases and garden urns. The bold geometric forms of these ribbed vessels signified the increasing influence of the Modern Movement on Scandinavian design - having been exhibited at the landmark 1930 Stockholm Exhibition they were described as "functionalist" in the Swedish press. But while these moulded earthenware pots did reveal the formal influence of the Modern Movement, their chunky robustness owed more to the strong craft heritage in Scandinavia - a synthesis of the contemporary with the traditional that is so often at the heart of Scandinavian design. Dahlskog continued designing for Bobergs Fajansfabrik until his death in 1950.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/42bd2dc8b54ae80c60cb7aeb51a25f50797c5e93e873438a4cf5e5f3b7ed1212.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b06eea6a9a4ac822429eb6e751e2d1acf20877835c3ab428f4b1c4503f464a31.jpg)

Björn Dahlstrom, BD-1 easy chair for CBI, 1994

# björn dahlström(d)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b9ba53c564fd0ec0ce60bc067bc468c546b59b5fed163c71beea92e5e3fa4767.jpg)

One of Sweden's leading furniture and industrial designers, Björn Dahlström began his career in the mid-1970s designing graphics and animations for film and television productions. After having been employed as an art director in an advertising agency in 1982, he founded his own graphic design company, Dahlström Design, and worked for a variety of clients including Ericsson, Scania and Atlas Copco. Eventually, Dahlström turned his attention to three-dimensional design - the Rocking Rabbit (1985) and streamlined Toycar (1996) for Playsam, and furniture for CBI in the early 1990s, were all influenced by his earlier work in animation and graphic design. Dahlström's aesthetically pleasing product designs are often distinguished by their soft yet elemental forms and straightforward functional practicality. Believing that "the designer has to be part of the industrial process, taking the role of free-thinking catalyst", Dahlström expanded his practice in the 1990s to encompass the design of a wide range of products including a flashlight and soldering equipment for Primus, a pneumatic drill for Atlas Copco, toys and water-play systems for Aquaplay, electric handicap aids for Gewa, bicycles for Skeppshult, and a walking stick and plastic furniture for Magis. One of Dahlström's great skills lies in the way in which he is able to make even "hard" industrial design look and feel attractive. His Cobra MK1 pneumatic drill for Atlas Copco, for example, which took four years to develop, not only set a new aesthetic precedent for such a product, but also maximized ease of handling by reducing vibrations to a minimum. Throughout the 1990s Dahlström won numerous awards for his product designs, including sixteen Excellent Swedish Design Awards and two Plus Design Awards. In 1999 he became Professor of Furniture Design at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) in Stockholm and has since also taught at Beckman's School of Design, Stockholm. Dahlström's work, most notably his highly successful Tools cookware range (1998) for Hackman, embodies the high object-integrity that has epitomized the very best of Scandinavian design since the late 1920s.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d9cec059f690da5b451790dbef413982c0664f3826d081e381275ae98e47fafa.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1ec0c49d10bf32ce3f770cdd90999aa35a1a86c3571a483e46fb4ff5a2775fdc.jpg)

Björn Dahlström, BD Relax chaise-longue for CBI, 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/77ec78e8101f6d68908bf27b4809677ba34ce2d0a5b15235a0c9079775dcab4a.jpg)  
Aga 2000 Special Edition cooking range for Aga-Rayburn, 2000 - based on the original standard model from the 1930s

## gustaf dalén

## (b. 1869 Stenstorp - d. 1937 Stockholm, Sweden)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/adde3be7ed07152d0e4eb63ce67c73f943547e8e124750c619962eeff17c0983.jpg)

In 1906 Gustaf Dalén was appointed chief engineer at the Svenska AB Gas Accumulator Company, a supplier of acetylene gas. Here, Dalén undertook much research on gases and turbines, which resulted in the improvement of hot-air turbine engines and the de Laval steam turbine. Dalén became managing director of the company in 1909, and subsequently invented Agamassan - a substance that absorbs acetylene allowing the gas to be concentrated without fear of explosion. In 1912 he won the Nobel Prize for Physics for his invention of the solventil, an automatic sun valve which uses sunlight to regulate a gas light-source so that it is turned off during daylight hours. This innovative desig was latrsenainally e of unmanned lighthouses and buoys. Tragically, Dalén was blinded in 1913 by an explosion that occurred during one of his experiments. While he was recuperating, he became aware of the problems his wife was encountering with her antiquated and highly temperamental cast-iron cooking range, which needed constant supervision to keep the temperatures from fluctuating. By applying the concept of heat storage, Dalén invented a cooker that combined a small and efficient heat source, two

large hotplates and two generous ovens into one robust and compact unit. Known as the Aga (taking its name from the initials of his company), Dalén's design achieved a high accumulation of heat that could be delivered in precisely controlled quantities, with a minimum of fuel, within a wellinsulated environment. The heat generated was radiant, so that every cubic inch within each oven was utilized. The heat was also thermostatically controlled, which meant the elimination of all knobs and dials. Since the ovens and hotplates maintained different temperatures depending on their distance from the heat source, the Aga could roast, bake, steam, simmer, fry, stew, grill, boil, broil and toast - all at once. Agas were first imported into the UK in 1929 and are now solely manufactured by Aga-Rayburn in England. All the principal castings for Aga cookers are made at the historic Coalbrookdale Foundry in Shropshire - birthplace of the Industrial Revolution. The remarkable Aga revolutionized the design of cookers; even today it has many loyal users who still consider it the ultimate appliance. About 500,000 Agas are currently in use around the world, paying testimony to the sheer brilliance of Dalén's invention, which remains

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/16d9fe1bbd78990ff8ebfea2a3ca7814b77682073a77dd67d20f84f7dd886f31.jpg)  
Gustaf Dalén, Aga Classic Special Edition cooking range for Aga-Rayburn, 2000 - based on the original standard model from the 1930s  
Archival photograph showing demonstration of Aga cooking range, 1930s  
Two Oven Aga cooking range for Aga-Rayburn, 1990s

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a1eeb7d5f1ae44c901750b345bb081745fb61525eb6ccd801738ecf18faa6b62.jpg)  
an enduring icon of early Scandinavian industrial design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0a6b89d3d1cbb2a8811da1f469a6b0f7b59d34ac722ca422d4b372f7dd5cf90d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/298a26a34d647de4894c365f6a122ec5efe5dd8ce7738327c1b971a8d571c22e.jpg)  
Enamelled plate for David-Andersen, 1950s  
> Thorbjørn Lie-Jørgensen, silver and ebony jug for David-Andersen, 1934

# david-andersen

This renowned Norwegian goldsmith workshop bears the name of its founder, David Andersen, who established the business in Christiania (now Oslo) in 1876. By the turn of the century the David-Andersen workshop had begun to forge an international reputation for its exquisite Art Nouveau enamelled wares designed by, among others, Gustav Gaudernack and Thorolf Holmboe (1866-1935). The founder's son, Arthur David-Andersen (1875-1970), also designed silverware with swirling floral motifs in the Art Nouveau style for the family firm. Between 1901 and 1952, he was the artistic director of the workshop, and during this tenure he employed a number of leading designers including Harry Sørby, Johan Lund (1861-1939), Ludwig Wittmann (1877-1961) and Johan Sirnes (1883-1966). In the late 1920s Arthur's son, Ivar David-Andersen (b. 1903), became the third generation of the family to produce work for the firm. During the late 1920s and early 1930s, the workshop's output was increasingly influenced by the Art Deco style. Thorbjørn Lie-Jørgensen's (1900-1961) silver and ebony jug of 1934 reflects this stylistic shift with its bold undecorated form, although its softened geometry also anticipated the gentle organic forms that became so characteristic of Scandinavian design in the following decade. After the Second World War, Ivar David-Andersen took over as artistic director, while his father resumed his career as a designer, winning a gold medal at the 1954 (X) Milan Triennale. Since then, the workshop has continued to produce modern silverware designs alongside objects with more traditional flatware patterns, such as the Rococo-style Telemark. The company is also noted for the design and manufacture of contemporary metalware incorporating its famed and brilliantly coloured jeweller's emalje (enamels). Using a time-consuming technique that involves the building up of layers of enamel, the workshop has produced vases, dishes, bowls and spoons made of various metals - silver, aluminium, copper and stainless steel - that perpetuate the century-old Norwegian tradition of enamelling. Today, David-Andersen proudly claims to be Scandinavia's largest jewellery store, retailing not only its own designs (including delicately enamelled jewellery) but also those produced by other Scandinavian workshops.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9618ec87485207c9c52fa40632094bfcabd841bfe83dd2939937b3d37ca1084a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4c70ee2599e3f63c6ce8cbeaac95bd849cf39a34b37deae9520c330b0da7c946.jpg)  
Nanna Ditzel, Model no. 111 silver armring, Model no. 107 silver bracelet and Model no. 333 silver brooch for Georg Jensen, 1956

# nannal ditzel

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8bad9f7c566a28c00a7efedd239b1c130c08e2965d3ab7e4806214e18358e46a.jpg)

Nanna Ditzel (née Hauberg) studied cabinet making at the Richards School in Copenhagen alongside Grete Jalk and Kristian Vedel. She later trained as a furniture designer under Kaare Klint at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Academy of Fine Arts) and also at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School for Applied Arts), Copenhagen, where she was taught by among others Orla Mølgaard-Nielsen, Peter Hvidt, Aksel Bender Madsen (1916-2000), Peter Koch, Ejner Larsen (1917-1987), and the painter Victor Isbrand. While at the latter institution, she met her future husband, Jørgen Ditzel (1921-1961), who had earlier trained as an upholsterer. In 1944, they exhibited living room furniture together at the Cabinetmakers' Annual Exhibition in Copenhagen. These designs, which included a tea table with a removable tray designed for Louis G. Thiersen, received considerable attention. Two years later, the couple married and established their own design studio in Hellerup. Initially, they concentrated their efforts on solutions for small living spaces and Nanna explored the concept of using kitchen units as room dividers. In the early 1950s, she worked as a furniture designer in the architectural practice of Fritz Schlegel (1896-1965) while continuing to design and exhibit furniture with her husband. In 1952, they designed a range of children's plywood furniture for Knud Willadsen Møbelsnedkeri, and collaborated with Gunnar Aagaard Andersen (1919-1982) on the design of a stand for the Cabinetmakers' Exhibition. During this period, Nanna also designed silver jewellery for Georg Jensen with remarkably forward-looking organic forms. In 1954, she and her husband published a book entitled Danish Chairs and in 1956 they were jointly awarded the Lunning Prize, which allowed them to travel extensively - Italy, Greece, Mexico and the United States. From 1957, they designed a range of wicker furniture for R. Wengler that included the well-known hanging egg-shaped chair. They also designed a range of solid-wood children's furniture for Kolds Savværk, which comprised their ubiquitous highchair (still used extensively throughout Scandinavia) and the simple yet elegant Toadstools children's stool. This creative partnership was abruptly ended, however, by the untimely death of Jørgen in 1961. Nanna subsequently designed several playground environments and was asked by Søren Georg Jensen to design

Nanna Ditzel, hanging eggshaped woven cane chair for R. Wengler, 1957

Period photograph showing egg-shapped woven cane chair hanging from a tree, c. 1957

Nanna Ditzel, living room installation with Oregon Pine furniture executed by Poul Christiansen, 1962

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4af83712e9dd6495bb048f430e743b45cf5d8ef4fe28f24ff27635c14e440711.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/175c60b8b5f24fc5aa9a296d355cb0cc7d1de3aa619e327659857106bb390957.jpg)

jewellery that incorporated semi-precious stones, such as agates and tourmalines, for his family's silversmithy. Eventually Nanna married Kurt Heide in 1968 and spent fifteen years living in London. After his death, she returned to Copenhagen (1986) where she established a workshop, studio and home in one of the oldest parts of the city. Driven by her natural curiosity and "appetite for change", Nanna Ditzel continues to execute innovative craft-based designs for furniture, metalware, tableware, jewellery and textiles. Her work, such as her butterfly-like Bench for Two Model no. 2600 (1989) for Fredericia Furniture, possesses a remarkable sense of lightness and texture as well as "motion and beauty"

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4e23d6ebc64d87b54602b9e74732bb8ca9d4d8ffa5329861fff245c1134fb047.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ed231469f8840339e679c5446edbab97cba90b29bd02b3dd0af6335ae65c6969.jpg)

Nanna Ditzel, child's highchair for Kold, 1955

Nanna Ditzel, Toadstools for Kold, 1962 - later produced by Krüger and today by Trip Trap

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3e9c92813c68afa7aaad5a05e0dff14b10045eced829dd39f3327f253c44a267.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7ca7aa73c2da98d63582d04285daf2b5e617699cd72220bcb74f69846be20f4a.jpg)

Nanna Ditzel, Trinidad chair for Fredericia Furniture, 1993

Nanna Ditzel, Sommerfugle Butterlychar r Fredcia Furniture, 1990

Nanna Ditzel, Bench for Two Model no. 2600 and table Model no. 2601 for Fredericia Furniture, 1989

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3b0769afd498b7da9ed858184dacf7f0849d76d37b6eff28062c5b21515e7036.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c3895c23a02f86f4c3b4596b74241e215476b53aea9109ce3f2a5341b259d3b4.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9fca9a059e5139e8dcfdac4a5eea870c6334534eccfee8901f0e1f228ffd2ca0.jpg)  
Tias Eckhoff, Maya cutlery for Norsk Stálpress, 1961

# tias eckhoff

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7f742fc942b7f7ec07154ac403d83058ba4eedb8e4e00641b2aab7695964f9ad.jpg)

The renowned Norwegian designer Tias Eckhoff initially trained in Denmark with Lillemor Clement and Inger Folmer-Larsen, and then later apprenticed under Nathalie Krebs (1895-1978) at the Saxbo pottery studio in Copenhagen from 1947 to 1948. He also studied at the Statens Handverks- og Kunstindustriskole (National College of Art and Design) in Oslo, graduating in 1949. The same year he began working as a designer for Porsgrund Porselænsfabrik, a ceramics factory that was attempting to modernize its product line by re-organizing its design department and recruiting a number of young gifted designers. One of Eckhoff's first successes for Porsgrund was his well-known Det Riflede (The Fluted One) tableware range designed in 1952, This white porcelain service recalled the soft forms pioneered by earlier Scandinavian designers such as Wilhelm Kåge in the 1930s and reflected Eckhoff's goal \*of arriving at a beautiful, simple and functional solution". He also designed the fireproof Glohane (1955) and the Regent (1962) tableware ranges for Porsgrund, as well as several other utilitarian services intended for institutional use. Eckhoff was Porsgrund's artistic director between 1953 and 1960, and in this position he had an enormous influence on the creative direction of the factory. In 1951 he won two prizes for his design of decorative and utility glassware in a competition held by Hadeland, which revealed his talent and scope for working within the contexts of both artistic and industrial design. Beyond his work in ceramics and glassware, Eckhoff also gained an international reputation for the excellence of his metalware designs. In 1953 his silver Cypress cutlery (1952-1953) won the Inter-Scandinavian Design Competition sponsored by Georg Jensen to celebrate the company's 50th anniversary. This elegant design led to him winning the coveted Lunning Prize the same year. Later, Eckhoff designed highly polished stainless steel cutlery for Dansk Knivfabrik (including the Fuga and Opus ranges) and for Norsk Stalpress (now known as Norstaal). His designs for Norsk Stalpress included Maya (1961), Una (1973), Tiki (1974), and Chaco (1990). Eckhoff also designed metalware for Halden Aluminumvarefabrik and architectural hardware, such as door-locks and mounts, for Trio-Ving, Using the same single-piece injection-moulded plastic seat-shell, he designed the Type 350 and Circle

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5d6fc521ce6df6bf5999aaa8ad88d2de8582866bfe5c28f65a5c371626492dd9.jpg)

armchairs (both 1983) for the Oslo-based furniture company Ana-Tomi. Eckhoff's rational and almost scientific approach to design was tempered by an acute artistic sensibility that ensured that his designs were both functional and beautiful. Over his career he won numerous awards, including five gold medals at the Milan Triennale 1954, 1957 and 1960, and three Good Design medals from the Norwegian Design Council (1962, 1965, 1966). As the most prominent Norwegian designer of the modern era, Eckhoff almost single-handedly helped focus international attention on his country's design output - thus ensuring that Norway was not completely overshadowed by Sweden, Denmark and Finland.

Tias Eckhoff, Regent service for Porsgrund, 1961

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/650177f30a9123031380b2f85fbed815a0902eda96ea6010a4abcd3781fe2a40.jpg)  
Kurt Ekholm, AH glazed faience tableware for Arabia, 1935

Kurt Ekholm, Sinivalko (White-Blue) tableware for Arabia, 1936

# kurt ekholm(

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2512bddbc621c6fae582aa3ac767d52175584d358d99b0c767e91da186abb7ca.jpg)

Born in Finland, Kurt Ekholm studied at the Kungliga Tekniska Högskolan (Royal Institute of Technology), Stockholm. While training in Sweden he gained an insight into the moral and social philosophic basis of modern design and was greatly influenced by the progressive "funktionalmen" (functional) movement there. On his return to Helsinki in 1932 he joined the Arabia ceramics factory and that same year set up its prestigious art studio. Around 1934 he designed a number of stoneware vases with simple forms and mustard yellow or celadon green glazes. More importantly, Ekholm also designed the practical, attractive and inexpensive AH service (1935). This warm yellow faïence range of tableware was followed a year later by his Sinivalko (White-Blue) service, which was undoubtedly inspired by Wilhelm Káge's revolutionary Praktika service (1933) for Gustavsberg. With its softened practical forms and simple banded decoration, the stackable and inexpensive Sinivalko service was similarly utilitarian and aesthetically austere in nature. Like Praktika, the design of the Sinivalko service was primarily motivated by social concerns and in particular the idea of "good design for all". In 1949 Ekholm moved to Sweden and worked for a year as a designer at the Rörstrand ceramics factory. He was subsequently appointed director of the Konstindustriskolan (School of Industrial Arts), Gothenburg, in 1950 and two years later obtained Swedish citizenship. Ekholm's practical wares, which were highly suited to industrial mass production, not only exemplified the democratizing ideals of Swedish design, but also reflected the continuous transfer of ideas from one Scandinavian country to another.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/84de8311e01e91372c11cfa1da3a839b3183e987eea6abba7beb4fc21790e3cf.jpg)

Axel Wenner-Gren, Model V vacuum cleaner, 1921 - the first Electrolux model to be fitted with runners

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/111ed52e7935be9885d85ae36455cd34421c0f8f31c3f7620940d5612cbb53c3.jpg)  
Axel Wenner-Gren, General Manager of Electrolux

## electrolux

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d4569a8497a56ab3ed2adfe184054136a744c8e3ebe1f232c08da736b021f99e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f81eba5274220c2b9ca0c17c5abdee924cbfb231643cb48ab741529b0feb6f20.jpg)

The Electrolux Group is the world's largest producer of powered household appliances, with over 55 million products - including refrigerators, cookers, washing machines, vacuum cleaners and lawn mowers - being sold worldwide each year. The ancestry of Electrolux can be traced back to the AB Lux company, which was founded in 1901. In the same year the company launched a kerosene lamp designed specifically for outdoor use, which was known as the Lux lamp. Later in 1912, Axel Wenner-Gren began a collaboration with the company that resulted in his invention of one of the world's first household vacuum cleaners, the Lux 1. The same year this revolutionary design was launched by AB Lux, the noisy car-like vacuum cleaners that trundled along the streets plying their business from one house to another were rendered virtually obsolete over night. In 1915, the company introduced the first of its revolutionary cylinder vacuum cleaners, which came with a range of tools that could be individually attached to the end of a flexible hose. Four years later, Lux merged with another Stockholm-based company, Elektromekanista, to form Elektrolux (later changed to Electrolux) and Wenner-Gren

was appointed general manager of the new entity. In 1921, the easy-to-use Mode/ V vacuum cleaner was launched with its sleigh-like runners designed by Wenner-Gren - an innovation that featured on Electrolux cleaners well into the 1960s. The following year, Swedish engineering students Baltzar von Platen and Carl Munters invented a cooling machine which converted heat to cold by applying the absorption process and which could be powered by electricity, kerosene or gas. This highly innovative refrigerator was subsequently manufactured in limited quantities by their company, AB Arctic. In 1925, Electrolux purchased the company, increased production of the refrigerator, and over the next decade grew rapidly, establishing manufacturing plants in France, Britain and America. In the late 1930s, Electrolux recognized the competitive advantage offered by high-style design and commissioned Raymond Loewy (1893-1986) to design several streamlined products, including a cylinder vacuum cleaner, a floor polisher, and the sleek L3000 refrigerator. 1940 saw the launch of Alvar Lenning's revolutionary labour-saving streamlined Assistent (Assistant) food processor, which could mix, grind, grate, squeeze and chop. Later in the 1940s and early 1950s, Swedish designers Sixten Sason and Ralph Lysell (1907-1987) produced other streamlined designs for Electrolux, including Sason's famous bullet-shaped Model 248 vacuum cleaner (1943). In 1963, Electrolux established its own in-house design department under the leadership of Hugo Lindström. Over the following decades, Electrolux increased its share of the white goods and household appliance markets with various company acquisitions, including Eureka, Husqvarna, Zanussi and White Consolidated, which owned brands such as Frigidaire, Kelvinator and White Westinghouse. One of Electrolux's design directors, Michael Green, notes: "In today's marketplace, appliance manufacturers that don't make a serious commitment to design very quickly turn into commodity producers ... this company includes design in the Values and Vision statement." Like many other Scandinavian manufacturers, Electrolux has been more concerned with offering innovative value-added design solutions than with attempting to predict fashion trends. For nearly a hundred years its products have eloquently addressed the Scandinavian desire for "practical beauty" - a strategy that remains key to the company's phenomenal success.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/94eedd7a1e1ff21df58451bb065853c0e90b490311ecd387a216f9a7d1b4dfbe.jpg)  
English advertisement for the Mode/ V vacuum cleaner, c. 1921  
Oxygen vacuum cleaner, Electrolux, 1999  
Electrolux vacuum cleaner advertising car, 1920s

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f05ac64beb60b6ce9377d7d394a6810e8aa4cba306d0488f320ea2b0ee92774b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9b5f100f94018729b9ee59a01f9756f05b43f4339bf47de032d679150f7cef99.jpg)  
Display of the model D10 dishwasher, the first "Round Jar" model, Electrolux, 1959

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c6b6691608def6838da5779be53347a43449b6b3b40ffec86de9854a87c57a5b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e7db9ae767130e7ec3ee287e1b5e649defda04355d876d5fcf992f3abdb1fa25.jpg)  
Alvar Lenning, Assistent (Assistant) food processor for Electrolux, 1940

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c1fa82ad7b6cf213a32fa6e82759faeddd0a6deff0e621996687ce70c1219ab3.jpg)  
Δ> Model L1 refrigerator - Electrolux's first air-cooled refrigerator, produced 1931-1937  
Carl Otto, City Box chest freezer for Electrolux, 1956

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3cb0252bb52f8beaade7b430f47a7933c27a1dbf3115009fde2fe3eae46b8142.jpg)  
Ergonomi Design Gruppen, welding equipment for Hörnell International, 2000

# ergonomi design gruppen

(founded 1979 Stockholm, Sweden)

Widely recognized for its work in the area of design for special needs, Ergonomi Design Gruppen (EDG) evolved from two Swedish design groups that had been founded in the late 1960s. In 1979, Designgruppen and Ergonomi Design officially amalgamated to form the 14-member Ergonomi Design Gruppen, based in Bromma. The group is dedicated to the research and development of safe, reliable and efficient design solutions based on ergonomic principles for the working environment and handicapped people. It studies usersystem problems by building full-scale models to evaluate designs in experimental situations, and collaborates with medical experts as well as patients to hone its solutions. Ergonomi's most notable design for (dis)ability is the Eat/Drink programme of cutlery, drinking vessels and plates (1980), which was manufactured by RSFU Rehab - a private company that was initially set up as a grant-aided foundation to advise on and supply various medical aids. The Eat/Drink range was designed so that it could be used by people who had only single-hand function. The programme's development and manufacturing costs were subsidized by grants from various insurance companies and public institutions, so that it could be sold as inexpensively as possible. Indeed, had it not been for this private and public funding, the programme would never have got off the ground as no independent manufacturer could be found that was prepared to produce this landmark realneed design. Apart from RSFU, Ergonomi Design Gruppen has also been commissioned by other state-funded bodies such as the Institute for the Handicapped and the Work Environment Fund to research and develop products for particular disadvantaged sections of society. The group has also designed a number of award-winning products for industrial manufacturers, including the Genotropin Pen for Pharmacia & Upjohn, an ergonomic coffee pot for SAs, the Ergo range of hand tools for Sandvik, a baby carrier for Baby-Bjorn, and an ergonomically-resolved stapler for Esco. Among the founders of the group, Maria Benktzon (b. 1946) and Sven-Eric Juhlin (b. 1940) both undertook research in 1972 into muscular ability and its relationship to the actions of gripping and holding, and have since specialized in design for disability. Ergonomi Design Gruppen is guided by the belief that, "Design is not only appearance.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b6a326cc8188e0ddcbd72ad77d9afc678baedb0745a5a63cc5b151925d295c3a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6c343e777050eaef61f3bbb379efb44db448afba6010343a2b5644a62e262f77.jpg)

It is form, function and economy. Conscientious product development can increase sales, reduce production costs, open new markets and improve quality profile. Design becomes a strategic tool for success." Ergonomi Design Gruppen is also renowned for machines, tools and protective gear designed to reduce the risk of repetitive strain injury and accidents. Central to its work is the idea of social inclusiveness - a philosophy that is at the core of Scandinavian and in particular Swedish society, where there is a progressive approach towards the provision of services and products for various disadvantaged groups of people. Like the advanced welfare state found in Sweden, the origins of this ethical approach to design can be ultimately traced to the Christian doctrine of Evangelical Lutheranism, which stresses reason and moral rectitude. It is this fundamental underlying morality - so clearly embodied in the work of Ergonomi Design Gruppen - that is such a characteristic feature of Scandinavian design in general.

Ergonomi Design Gruppen, bow-saw for Bahco, 2000

Ergonomi Design Gruppen, gripping tongs for Etac, 1985

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0f7aede8c2359e8fcdfb5be1fc3f065f4de60ff83d7d541445befa5cab1b5ef7.jpg)  
Ericsson promotional photograph, c. 1956 - showing the Ericofon designed by Hugo Blomberg, Ralph Lysell & Gösta Thames in 1954  
> Carl-Arne Breger, Ericofon 700 telephone for Ericsson, 1976 - designed to celebrate the 100th Anniversary of the company

# ericsson

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/063c2238e713a08f4ce7484fb67824fd58e0ca07607ba33c45fa98a105e49225.jpg)

A world-leading manufacturer of telecommunications equipment, Ericsson's origins can be traced to the last quarter of the 19th century when Lars Magnus Ericsson (1846-1926) founded the company in Stockholm. Initially the business concentrated on the repair of telegraph equipment, but soon its founder recognized that there was a real need for improvements to be made to existing telephone equipment. By 1878, the company was developing telephones that were based on an earlier design by Alexander Graham Bell (1847- 1922). In the early 1890s Ericsson began manufacturing telephones of its own design, and in 1892 launched its first major product, a desk instrument with a hand-held microphone. Soon after this the company began exporting telephones throughout Europe, and its success led to the building of several overseas factories, including one in Mexico. In 1909 Ericsson launched the first cradle telephone, the Model 88, which was enormously influential throughout Europe. Seeking to update this landmark design, the company commissioned the artist Jean Heiberg (1884-1976) and the Norwegian engineer Johan Christian Bjerknes in 1930 to design a telephone with a Bakelite

casing to replace existing metal types. Developed using plaster prototypes, the resulting DHB 1001 telephone was less angular than earlier models, and its coherent relationship between the handset and the main instrument body established the standard for telephone design until the late 1950s. Although it was not the first to use Bakelite in its construction, the DHB 1001 telephone was highly influential and inspired Henry Dreyfuss' (1904- 1972) later plastic model 300 (19301933) for Bell. Between 1940 and 1954, Hugo Blomberg (1897-1994), Ralph Lysell (1907-1987), and Gösta Thames (b. 1916) designed and developed another groundbreaking model for Ericsson, the Ericofon (launched in 1956). This unusual and forward-looking telephone had a completely unified form that integrated the earpiece, mouthpiece and dial. The design of the Ericofon, which incorporated new lightweight materials such as plastic, rubber and nylon, was made possible by the increasing miniaturization of technology. Fun yet stylish, it was the most popular one-piece telephone for over three decades. In the early 1970s an up-dated push-button version of the Ericofon was introduced known as the Model 700. This

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/eae9fbfe90973c3adf3305498c5deda01d39cc35880a92da1d7bbbffcfca41e8.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e06918de31436d860093508ba440f10252910e705a96fcc098154b4b3a7b9d31.jpg)  
Ericsson Model no. 88 telephone, 1909  
> Jean Heiberg, DBH 1001 telephone for Ericsson, put into production in 1932  
Ericsson promotional photograph showing the Ericofon telephone in use, c. 1956  
Hugo Blomberg, Ralph Lysell & Gösta Thames, Ericofon telephone, 1954 - launched in 1956

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f3872b37786cce1604b3e3c801b516fd83692ca4b2fb79198d29249dab972cc7.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ef5f89b304fe5ecebb3f386aefd1678fafb8d5b67219dbaaeb56b1b83cef60bd.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9fcaa55169c8bebf39cecc2c5be1f91d6ffe6d3ba80df93bb260a70ce984fd4e.jpg)  
Ericsson Model no. R380e mobile phone, 2002

modish design with its sleek yet angular form displayed the bold and graphic outline that is so characteristic of much Scandinavian industrial design. During the 1980s and 1990s Ericsson became one of the leading manufacturers of mobile telephones, producing well-designed yet at times rather conservative products. Throughout this period the company was the world leader in mobile telephone systems, connecting nearly 40 percent of the world's mobile callers and was at the forefront of the WAP (Wireless Application Protocol) revolution - the bridge between mobile communication and the Internet. As one of the leading players in the telecommunications industry, however, the company was severely hit by the stock market turmoil in the electronics sector during the early 2000s. By 2002 Ericsson's stock price had plunged to such an extent that today the very survival of this Swedish household name remains unclear.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ffcd902a651e4d48fbfd1c2c66920db7b7941cec321f4d3e2fe2647538344c87.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0a7f0d1ef011654a2d9e5f2ea966c780c75e577944ea5593661eb298e2d99b6f.jpg)

< Ericsson Mobile Com-1 communicator (concept design), 2002

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1426221d644da607c850d4257e432a630db72edecbd28a37b6a2314da6c266ec.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e7d83a65ea5f4c887cce058853a26ce19b660815aad04db419c55b2d5e5af6f5.jpg)  
Thomas Eriksson, Progetto 9208 medicine cabinets for Cappellini, 1992

# thomas eriksson

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bc08e13956c319f38982265c763b6ed7ffd4f9f411732ca5b6f3da6aa11e0b37.jpg)

The Swedish architect and furniture designer Thomas Eriksson studied architecture in Stockholm from 1981 to 1985. He began working as a freelance architect in 1988 and between 1992 and 1993 exhibited a number of furniture designs as part of Cappellini's Progetto Oggetto collection in Milan, New York and Cologne. These pieces were notable for their often symbolic, simple elemental forms, such as his E-shaped Progetto 9304 occasional table, his ladder-like Progetto 9209 wall-mounted coat-rack, and his cross-shaped enamelled steel Progetto 9208 medicine cabinet. Eriksson's later furniture for IKEA, most notably the PS television table (1995) and PS clock/cupboard (1995), had similarly pared-down forms. Apart from his furniture designs, Eriksson has also designed various domestic products and architectural fittings. In 1995, he was awarded the Swedish Design Award and three years later was commissioned by SAs to design its Euroclass Lounges in Milan and London. He has also designed SAS airport lounges in Stockholm, Copenhagen and Newark (New York) in a contemporary yet understated Nordic style utilizing blond woods and simple, unadorned, functional forms.

Eriksson's interior design clientele also includes Absolut, H&M and Virgin, while Asplund and CBI have also manufactured some of his furniture and product designs. In 1998, Eriksson founded the design company Stockholm Design Lab with Björn Kusoffsky (b. 1945) and Göran Lagerström (b. 1965) with the concept and goal of it becoming an "important platform for the creation of quality design that matters and makes a difference." The philosophy of Stockholm Design Lab is "rooted in Scandinavian tradition and based on the fundamental ideas of simplicity, clarity, openness and innovation". Throughout the early 2000s Eriksson has continued to pioneer a fresh interpretation of Swedish Modernism. His pared-down work with its clean Nordic lines epitomizes the new and dynamic spirit that has recently emerged in Scandinavian design.

Thomas Eriksson, Progetto 9209 coat rack for Cappellini, 1992

Thomas Eriksson, PS television table for IKEA, 1995

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/536cfc6588dc9b01c424f900881cc5f3daed43c613d6514166b99e21bb964b1e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1f4660a5884a1708bfffdf6873e87f685d72bdd7358a8b697ce2fb9a4836a03a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/006ddd5675cfdb14b039dbbf71f2c9dffdaa29de6668726592cea686745d404f.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dd716ef6a5bf108da6ebbcac02440c37c9f680abd7fd9f40261d3a8f860e9e04.jpg)  
Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi, Jokapoika (Every Boy) shirts for Marimekko, 1957

# vuokko eskolin-nurmesniemi

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c3f58e8f2a5328491d9595e3adbfa5ca2021d2428866324f30766211e705f61d.jpg)

(b. 1930 Helsinki, Finland)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f05b430c97a25d8ee8357d0143a9c3d2373dffefa37bb76aff6e99b72707d9fe.jpg)

Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi revolutionized Finnish design during the post-war years with her boldly patterned and coloured textiles. She began her training at just 16 years of age in the ceramics department of the Taideteollinen Oppilaitos (Institute of Industrial Arts) in Helsinki. After completing her studies in 1952, she worked briefly as a designer for the Wärtsila-Arabia ceramics factory, and in 1953 married the Finnish furniture and interior designer Antti Nurmesniemi. The same year, the founders of Marimekko, Viljo and Armi Ratia (1912-1979), "hand-picked" her to become the company's new artistic director. During the immediate post-war years there were shortages of all kinds in Finland and the lack of new clothes meant that women's dresses, which tended to be made of dark and drab coloured textiles, were endlessly adapted in the cause of "make do and mend". Around this time, however, the Ratias' other firm Printex had begun producing colourful and vibrant furnishing textiles designed by Maija Isola that were printed on inexpensive cotton sheeting. The concept for Marimekko was to use similar screen-printed textiles for clothing, and Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi was entrusted with creating the first line of youthful, fun and off-thepeg garments, which were the very antithesis of the expensive and rather staid collections emanating from the well-known Paris fashion houses. Eskolin-Nurmesniemi's textiles, such as Tibet and Ram, were patterned with large geometric motifs, and often used two overlapping colours so as to create a third colour - a technique that became a key feature of Marimekko fabrics. The design of Eskolin-Nurmesniemi's clothes was as radical as the patterning of her textiles. In her search for greater universality of appeal she eliminated traditional features such as tucks, darts and button-holes and replaced them with zippers and touch-and-close fasteners such as Velcro and press-studs (up until then only ever used on men's clothing) so as to make her garments more user-friendly and less physically restrictive. The first Marimekko show was held in Stockholm in 1957 and Eskolin-Nurmesniemi's ground-breaking designs were widely acclaimed by critics. The bright colours she used were especially appreciated in the United States and her clothes soon became highly fashionable there after Jacqueline Kennedy purchased a number of them. One of

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/81b884c7db31a7d3c14b73e89ccce2e829b7572f5bfe6d2660308f0e84bc2412.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1251e2c68271847948e51a42485cf88f412e14182c25779e371cfb0a4b802b4d.jpg)

Eskolin-Nurmesniemi's best known designs, the Jokapoika shirt (1957) with its small yet colourful piccolo stripes is still in production after 45 years - a remarkable feat given the fickle nature of the world of fashion. Her clothes and textiles were favoured in particular by designers, architects and other creatives who preferred her unconventional look, which relied on structural essentialism rather than on gimmickry or showiness. In 1964, Eskolin-Nurmesniemi founded her own Helsinki-based company, Vuokko, and continued to create loose-fitting clothing that predicted the future work of designers such as Issey Miyake (b. 1938), who regards her as his "design mother". She has received numerous awards including the Lunning Prize (1964), the Finnish State Design Prize (1969), the Prince Eugen Medal (1986), and the Kaj Franck Prize (1997). In 1977 her printed cottons and jacquards were exhibited at New York's Metropolitan Museum of Art, and in 1988 she was made an Honorary Royal Designer in London. Throughout her career Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi has created trends rather than followed them. As one of the most successful Finnish designers of all time, her

clothes and textiles helped define the unmistakable character of Finnish design.

Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi, Hood dress for Vuokko, 1966

Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi, Pyörre (Whirl) dress for Vuokko, 1965

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/88364d695242b8f4637561dc365240cbbab182922b3c870f2d842f6304fb19a4.jpg)

## fiskarS

## FISKARS

In the early 17th century Peter Thorwöste moved from Stockholm to Turku and became an agent for a prominent merchant. Having accumulated much wealth as a resuilt of his dealings in Turku, Thorwöste subsequently loaned some money to the owner of the local Antskog ironworks for its expansion. The loan could not be repaid and so by default Thorwöste obtained full proprietorship of the works in 1647. He subsequently gained permission to extract iron ore from the Ojamo mine and in 1649 was officially allowed to manufacture cast iron and forged items (except cannon). The same year he was also granted permission to establish a blast furnace and a bar hammer at Fiskars. This new ironworks was highly productive, being able to produce over 1,000 kg of iron a day. The Fiskars ironworks manufactured all kinds of products such as nails, wire, knives, tools, and reinforced wheels, which were transported to Stockholm and the provinces in the south of Finland. During the mid-18th century, the ironworks began producing small amounts of copper that was rendered into utensils by Fiskars' smiths. In 1822 the ironworks was acquired by John Jacob Julin, who during the 1830s established at Fiskars the first fine forging workshop and the first machine workshop in Finland. By now the ironworks was producing various household utensils, including cooking pots, frying pans, high-quality tableware, kitchen knives, scissors, and the famous puukko hunting knives. By the turn of the century Fiskars was manufacturing some forty different models of horsedrawn plough (in total over one million ploughs were produced at the ironworks between 1860 and 1900). During the First World War the ironworks was modernized and its product range expanded, but its further growth was impeded by the global depression of the 1930s. The post-war period saw Fiskars focus its attention on the developing American market. To this end the company produced a stylish, export-led range of flatware, known as Triennale (1957), which was designed by Bertel Gardberg and reflected the growing internationalism of Finnish design during this period. In 1967 the company launched its famous orangehandled O-series scissors (1963), which had been developed by the engineer and wood-carver Olof Bäckström (b. 1922). The prototype for the ergonomically conceived ABS handles of these scissors was carved by him from solid wood.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7c0b1d4a033fdb146a4d8d16c6554a126a4f56077a8c5b2ec0381a5e298921c0.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/39833f93b2e4ef17f52b398f1820218e271b20e8f88f3ec39b16039c90b71c7f.jpg)

Licensed worldwide, the scissors were also manufactured as pinking shears and in a left-handed version. Over the succeeding years this benchmark ergonomic design, now known as the Classic, has been developed into a family of eighteen scissors. More recently the Fiskars design team under the guidance of Olavi Lindén (b. 1946) has developed a range of garden tools that has received numerous design prizes. As the oldest existing industrial company in Finland, Fiskars has become one of Finland's most popular visitor attractions, and holds various design-related exhibitions during the summer months. Fiskars' products, which are celebrated around the world, reflect the company's "commitment to quality and systematic application of ergonomic principles, which result in products that are easy, safe and pleasurable to use".

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b44762229075b649487d443588c8cfcdaa750c27faa753d99702d07aa63c4d32.jpg)

# kay fisker 965)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/72396b11143e1cb2c259a0f0dbfb3d9461c4fdd11e30f72633bc7e8436881fc0.jpg)

Kay Fisker worked for the Danish architect and designer Anton Rosen from 1912 to 1916 before studying architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in Copenhagen. After graduating in 1920, Fisker established his own architecture practice and in 1921 was awarded a gold medal in Ghent for his work. Between 1919 and 1927 Fisker was also the editor of the journal Arkitekten, and from around 1925 he began designing silverware for A. Michelsen in Copenhagen. As a member of the younger generation of avant-garde designers in Denmark, Fisker set out to modernize Danish design by developing forms that were strongly geometric, such as those of his cocktail shakers from the late 1920s. Form and decoration were often utterly inseparable in his designs, as his bonbonnière for A. Michelsen (1926) or his beech wingback chair from the 1930s demonstrate. Fisker also designed book covers and exhibited his work at the seminal 1925 "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" in Paris. In 1928, he was awarded the prestigious Eckersberg Medal and later in 1947 he received the C. F. Hansen Medal. He became a

professor at the Kunstakademiets Arkitektskole (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts, school of architecture), Copenhagen, in 1936 and held this position until 1963. His design work was the subject of several one-man shows held in Charlottenborg (1934 and 1953), Paris (1949), London (1950), and Århus (1953). Like many other Danish designers, Fisker's skill lay in his ability to update traditional forms by striking a delicate balance between historicism and outright Modernism. As one of the most influential architect-designers working in Denmark during the 1920s and 1930s, Fisker mastered a wide range of materials so as to produce not only luxury goods, such as his exquisite silver cocktail shakers, but also more mundane yet useful products, most notably his cast-iron stove that has an understated beauty derived solely from functional purity.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/58ba0546904abfbd195bcc5adc8fa55688b030ebb12bf555ed4e93564c647ad7.jpg)

Kay Fisker, interior of the Danish pavillion at the 1925 "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" in Paris

Kay Fisker, cast-iron stove for Støbejern, c. 1920

Kay Fisker, wingback chair (unknown maker), 1930s

Kay Fisker, wine pitcher for A. Michelsen, 1926

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/04718b564302d768dab808933a853c9a9c61d55fdc6d7d10382d48dd29875ddd.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/337ae82ebf2907ce9e68fb964f446fa7f61017c507c1439f3bf3ccef83e6f73a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6bc31194e2d99e6071273186184ac9e6db3ed8fc626c750d8ec5aec5bf2bb7f3.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b80f583c46027701f061f50624622dd7da9fd61428a0e25281c77b3d31437f15.jpg)  
Kaj Franck, Kartio vases for littala, 1958

# kaj franck

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/84843c95a5a789b2c0dc8f1eba6f5a3cc9205bbd1dd6b042eddfe1deda57b70f.jpg)

One of Finland's most celebrated designers of glass and ceramics, Kaj Franck initially studied furniture design under Arttu Brummer (1891- 1951) at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (Institute of Industrial Arts), Helsinki, graduating in 1932. He subsequently worked as a catalogue illustrator for Taito Oy (1933) and the Riihimäki glassworks (1934). Between 1934 and 1938 he was employed as a decorator and interior designer by Te-Ma Oy Tema and later designed printed textiles for the United Wool Factory in Hyvinkäã in 1938. The following year, Franck was conscripted into the military where he met many fellow countrymen from less privileged backgrounds - an experience that profoundly impacted on his views of society. In 1945, he began working for the Arabia ceramics factory and a year later, designed a range of utilitarian tableware for the Väestölition (Family Welfare Association). During the 1950s, he designed several ranges of everyday modern crockery and kitchenware that were intended for multipurpose use and that used geometric rather than fashionable streamlined forms. In an article published in 1949, Franck wrote: "New conditions of life and a new lifestyle have forced us to modernize home fittings ... (however) ... You can see hardly any change in everyday porcelain ware .. There is no need for compromising on the need for appropriate utility items. Durable, solid, waterproof, easy to wash ... our times will build its design idiom on the conditions of life and forms which belong to us." Franck realized these ideals in his glazed-faïence Kilta range (1952), which was devised not as a traditional-style dinner service but rather as individual pieces with a shared aesthetic. In 1955, Franck received an Asla grant to study design teaching in America, and during his time in the USA he researched eating habits, which had a direct bearing on his subsequent design work. From 1954, Franck also designed limited-production wares, such as his Lumipallo (Snowball) series, until he left Arabia in 1973. He also designed pressed glassware for littala between 1946 and 1950 that included the practical Kartio range of water jugs and drinking glasses. In 1950, frustrated by the lack of experimentation at littala, Franck left the company and a year later was appointed design director of the Nuutajärvi-Notsjo glassworks, which had recently been acquired by the Wärtsilá group. The Nuutajärvi factory had already established a reputation for the excellence of its studio glass, and while working there Franck was able to explore the creative potential of glass by experimenting with different techniques and pigments. In 1952, he recruited two Italian glassblowers who were able to bring with them the expertise of the Murano glassworks, and following this some of Franck's designs bore the unmistakable influence of Venetian glass. Franck's blown art glass for Nuutajärvi varied tremendously in style, from delicately engraved decanters to rustic-style glasses with encased bubbles and boldly coloured bands, to "spun" glass with swirling stripes. One of Franck's last projects was the redesign of the Kilta range for Arabia. The resulting Teema range (1977-1980) of nineteen pieces based on elemental geometric forms (the circle, square and cone) reflected his belief that, "the only possible way to solve the problems of utility ware was to be radical and social". Franck's career is indicative of the almost unique working practice of Scandinavian designers, who often design functional democratic products intended for mass production in parallel with exclusive one-off or limited edition art-based pieces.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/90fa8e990cd160a0e1449bbeb8facba9b4b2a08b1172d5240e813df6da70773c.jpg)

Kaj Franck, Lilli glasses for littala, 1956

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/301009abf78b1f6af2bdbf0c982b47d906d25acd3af1bcf99468107e67caa237.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/02e84e07759e96c527a11ecbf54238e52beda009b504020e27e344ae4907a15d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6c0d61216fac132b29c56b4797b2a923e454c028df8e2f65c06ef42c9b2bed4d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/048f1a5aee40de7c008980ef7626cfecff5395cc571a3d4d1576dbf52043efbf.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9309bcfd3dd0af8ce857436904fa3586978b3aa456b7ba424a3c5b4b1e49ab0f.jpg)

Kaj Franck, Teema tableware range for Arabia, 1977-1980 - based on Franck's earlier Kilta range designed in 1948 &1951-1952

Kaj Franck, Teema tableware for Arabia, 1977-1980

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d15ade0b3e0e02da8e1cbb0abb0b55efc769bb03f7d35a100110b5ebfdcaadf0.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5c4c8cb512ef9c15441944f0ed0c922e540dc37fa936252e16e1def9a530693c.jpg)  
Josef Frank, Hawaii textile for Svenskt Tenn, 1943-1944

# josef frank

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d21fd9862156b29a606be68603ff69f003ee0ff0d785be3748f2244a7153392b.jpg)

Austrian-born designer Josef Frank trained as an architect at the Technische Hochschule (Polytechnic) in Vienna (19031910). From 1919 to 1927 he was a professor at the Kunstgewerbeschule (School of Arts and Crafts) there. Between 1925 and 1934, he also ran a home furnishings business Haus & Garten with Oskar Wlach (1881-1963). As one of the most prominent architects in Austria, Frank designed for the Wiener Werkstätte and participated in the Deutscher Werkbund's landmark 1927 "Die Wohnung" exhibition in Stuttgart. His specially designed flat-roofed reinforced-concrete double house was extremely modern, but his furniture from the same period was Neo-Classical in tone and completely at odds with the Modernist functionalist aesthetic. In 1933 Frank began designing for the Swedish interior design company Svenskt Tenn. Two years later, he moved to Stockholm, became the firm's chief designer and adopted Swedish nationality in 1938. He designed numerous textiles and furniture for the company inspired by the 19th century British Arts & Crafts Movement and by turn-of-the-century Viennese design. Although he rejected functionalist ideology and viewed personal taste and comfort as the

basis of domestic design, Frank's work was essentially modern, being distinguished by a purity of form. His furniture was remarkable for its harmonious proportions and high quality craftsmanship, as well as its practicality and comfort. From 1942 to 1944, he taught at the New School for Social Research in New York, and as a theorist wrote several books, including Architektur als Symbol (1930) and Accidentism (1958). He returned to Stockholm in 1946 and remained there until his death in 1967. His work enjoyed a revival in the 1980s and a number of his designs have remained in production with Svenskt Tenn. Like Carl Malmsten and Bruno Mathsson, Josef Frank is regarded as one of the three classicists of Swedish design. As an early pioneer of "the Scandinavian style", his contribution was immense, while his belief that, "the home does not have to be planned out in detail, just put together with pieces its inhabitants love" epitomized the Scandinavian distrust of dogmatic universalism such as that promoted at the Bauhaus.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b9380575b911eed9b1b0dcd515400040e264a71a3ce2b99e4c8d78c540d821b8.jpg)  
4 Josef Frank, Model no. B2483 table lamp for Svenskt Tenn, 1936  
Josef Frank, Model no. 881 cabinet-on-stand for Svenskt Tenn, c. 1938  
Josef Frank, interior with mahogany chest-of-drawers and Poisons textile - both for Svenskt Tenn, c. 1943

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7a8a45a66077440212a2d46d58a703caf07c0c3b89d925e7825fc9aef145db79.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2fae8c51643aef738cd8fd76febc3b0312ce628da91a79f9ece8b8a0aa242ea4.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bb14e6d02473aa437f2bcedf2dc0b8bb4d61eddfc0b95071dd9526bfe39af9c1.jpg)

Berndt Friberg, stoneware vase for Gustavsberg, 1954

# berndt friberg

The Swedish ceramicist Berndt Friberg apprenticed as a thrower at the Höganäs-Billesholms stoneware factory from 1915 to 1918. He also studied at the technical school in Höganäs and from 1918 trained at various ceramics workshops in Denmark and Sweden. In 1934 he joined the Gustavsberg ceramics factory, where for the next decade he worked as a thrower for Wilhelm Kage. In 1944 he became a member of the Gustavsberg Studio and subsequently designed his own ceramics for the company until his death in 1981. During this period he produced numerous one-off stoneware vessels that were distinguished by their delicately coloured - soft greens, pale ochres, dusky blues - Oriental-style semimatt "hare's fur" glazes. The forms were influenced by those of earlier Far Eastern wares. Although inspired by Oriental antecedents, Friberg's work was typically Scandinavian in its refinement - colour, texture and form were carefully balanced in order to realize objects that had a compelling natural beauty. His hand-thrown ceramic designs produced at the Gustavsberg Studio were awarded three gold medals at the 1947 (VI), 1951 (IX) and 1954 (X) Milan Triennale and the Gregor

Paulsson Trophy in 1960. His work was also shown on numerous occasions at Nordiska Kompaniet as well as other exhibitions held in Stockholm, Gothenburg, Karlstad, Höganäs, Malmo, and Växjö. Like his mentor Wilhelm Kåge, Friberg produced stoneware pottery that exemplified the superlative quality of Scandinavian craftsmanship. With his sensitive handling of materials and profound understanding of their formal and aesthetic potential, Friberg set an important benchmark in the design and composition of studio ceramics.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1858781cab06fb2aeb680a0a2aeb9d477464c4e5e0db0d88bf35a437d14618d5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6ef54ffe701d22e4562e96961ad077d898168763a0237a505dbb5915bdbbb218.jpg)  
Arne Jacobsen, Model no. 4130 Grand Prix chair for Fritz Hansen, 1955

# fritz hansen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e19ed895264f0703b6100cc7a04960795a15fbcd6334777582d6e2d078005336.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/32a37ef0f16960fb602e0ab5772e06412aaf920396f94d45ae9f0d26fe8f2285.jpg)

In 1872, Fritz Hansen established a cabinet-making workshop in Copenhagen that specialized in woodturning and the manufacture of wooden and iron frames for upholstered furniture. In 1915, Fritz Hansen's son, Christian, introduced various industrial processes to enable the mass production of furniture. These new manufacturing techniques, which included wood bending processes that were similar to those developed earlier by Michael Thonet in Austria, allowed significant expansion of the company during the 1920s. In 1932, Christian's sons, Fritz and Søren, joined the firm and began modernizing its product line with designs such as Søren Hansen's DAN chair (1930) - a subtly updated Thonet-style cafe chair. One year later the company became involved in a legal dispute with Lorenz-Thonet over a chromed tubularmetal cantilever chair designed by the architect Mogens Lassen (1907-1987). This was one of many legal wrangles within the furniture industry over the ownership of the intellectual rights to the cantilever format. During the 1930s, Fritz Hansen also produced a large number of other Moderniststyle designs in bentwood and tubular metal by architects such as Heilmann Sevaldsen, Fritz

Schlegel (1896-1965) and Kaare Klint. In the late 1940s, the firm began producing furniture designed by Hans Wegner, for example his Chinese Chair No. 1 (1944), which was inspired by an antique Chinese chair illustrated in Ole Wanscher's book, Types of Furniture (1932). Like other designs by Wegner, this chair was not a reproduction but an evolution of an existing type — a high-quality modern reworking that enhanced the chair's rationale for volume production. Other notable furniture that Fritz Hansen manufactured in the late 1940s included a beech armchair by Ove Boldt (a reinterpretation of the traditional Windsor chair), an upholstered beech chair designed by Søren Hansen (which anticipated the sculptural forms of Arne Jacobsen), and a beech sofa with collapsible side elements by Børge Mogensen (a modern reworking of an antique type). Fritz Hansen also developed and manufactured the landmark moulded-plywood AX chair (1950) designed by Peter Hvidt and Orla Molgaard-Nielsen, an accomplishment that signified an important shift towards a more Modern idiom for the company. In 1951/52 Arne Jacobsen designed the stackable Ant chair for Fritz Hansen, which with its continuous moulded-plywood seat section and tubular metal leg construction was a masterpiece of simplification. With its pinched waist and curvaceous form, the Ant chair went on to become iconic of "The New Look" in the 1950s. This revolutionary design was followed in 1955 by the even more successful Series 7 range of chairs, which had a similar moulded-plywood, tubular metal construction that was eminently suited to high-volume industrial production. Later in the 1950s, Fritz Hansen began production of Arne Jacobsen's seminal Swan and Egg chairs (1958). These hugely popular designs not only predicted the space-age forms of the later 1960s, they were also among the first seat furniture to fully exploit the potential of latex foam as an upholstering material. Furniture designers who employed this new material benefited enormously from the freedom it granted them - latex foam-upholstered chairs did not require any springing so their forms could become much more sculptural while at the same time more visually unified. A decade after Fritz Hansen launched these chairs it commissioned Piet Hein and Bruno Mathsson to develop a group of tables that complemented Jacobsen's earlier Ant and Series 7

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/93e9326191a10f807877549fbf3b4221b56b343ab126a9e4fbbd9ca3d78327e4.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3944b2eb1da7c5c1f33e44e052d609ce99e392a44468116abb86320b13f9883e.jpg)

chairs. The form used for this range of tables (1968) was based on Hein's mathematical concept of the "superellipse" - a shape in-between a rectangle and an oval. In 1974, Fritz Hansen also began production of Verner Panton's extraordinary 1. 2. 3. series of chairs, which comprised twenty different models. Having remained a family concern for four generations, the firm was eventually acquired by the Skandinavisk Holding Company and its offices were moved from Copenhagen to Allerød. Today, Fritz Hansen continues to produce its classic designs by Arne Jacobsen and Poul Kjærholm alongside its more contemporary collections by, among others, Alfred Homann and Pelikan Design.Throughout ts log andindustrious histy, Fritz Hansen has been one of the most prominent furniture manufacturing companies in Scandinavia, and as such it has contributed greatly to the promotion and acceptance of the simple yet elegant Scandinavian style throughout the world.

# niels gammelgaard

(b. 1944 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e0443f8ce2520d2948f700bda55c416169b40e54319392eb8ea3d487e5638de3.jpg)

Niels Gammelgaard studied industrial design under Erik Herlow at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi(Royal Danish Academy f Fine Arts) in Copenhagen, graduating in 1970. In 1969, he cofounded Box 25 Architects, which undertook the design of Sofiegárden College, Christianshavn in , and the Kvarterlygten Streetlight) or Louis Poulsen in 1976. After meeting Ingvar Kamprad, the founder IKEA, Gammelgaard began designing products for the company in 175. Since then his furniture designs for IKEA, such as the Ted folding chair (1977) and Moment sofa (1985), have not only won numerous prizes, including eight Ütmarkt Awards and two Red Dot Awards, they have also been among the company's best-selling products. In 1978, Gammelgaard co-founded it Lars Mahien  Pelikan Dn, a Copenhagen-based industrial design practice, which over the past 24 years has designed and seen into production literally hundreds of products ranging from small pencils children's trcles hospital beds.Pelikan Design is best nown however, fr the design of contract furnitureoffices, waiting halls, rest areas - and can name many of the most prestigious Danish furniture

manufacturers among its clients, including Fritz Hansen, Bent Krogh, Fredericia, and Erik Jørgensen. Gammelgaard and Mathiesen view furniture as a fundamentally industrial product and so highteh plastics tend to featurestrongly in thei pertoire of materials. Among the largest commissions Pelikan Design has received was the interior design of the Copenhagen city and suburban railway (1995), which won a Brunel Award in 1996. This project involved a radical space concept whereby asymmetrical bench seats were used to encourage passengers to move toward the windows in order to decrease congestion in the aisles. More recently, Pelikan Design was awarded the Danish Design Council's Annual Award in 1998 for the Wing screen wall (1993) and a Neocon Silver Award in 2000 for the /Q furniture series (996). With its abiding mission to establish longlasting relationships between its products and users, Pelikan Design remains at the very forefront of Scandinavian design practice.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c1de541aebcfb0c2c9fd889fc62120f08a4245ab82868511744170fab4c413af.jpg)

Bertel Gardberg, Canton stainless steel and teak pan for Hackman, 1957

Bertel Gardberg, silver and teak chocolate pot (handmade), 1955
