# bertel gardberg

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6329ff49381ff8b8f709b4779f0fa7ae3d7a90f9c23bc173509c64c80dda58b0.jpg)

The Finnish metalware and jewellery designer Bertel Gardberg studied at the Goldsmith's School, Helsinki, before training at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (Institute of Industrial Arts), Helsinki from 1938 to 1941. In 1949 he established his own studio in Helsinki and subsequently designed and produced his own silverware products, such as the cuboid teak and silver tea caddy (1955) and his silver and palisander Mocha service (1957). He also designed objects for industrial production that were manufactured by Fiskars and Hackman, among others. For the latter, his product designs include the stainless steel Carelia cutlery (1957) and the Canton cooking pan (1957) - both of which possess a remarkable aesthetic refinement that testifies to Gardberg's careful attention to detail and skilful handling of materials. The perfectionism and timelessness of products such as these earned Gardberg widespread recognition and numerous awards, including gold and silver medals at the Milan Triennale of 1954 ( 1957 (X1) and 1960 (XII), and the Lunning Prize in 1961. Between 1966 and 1971 Gardberg lived in Dublin, where he was artistic director of the Kilkenny Design Workshop (1966-1968) and technical directorof the Rionor company in Kilkenny (1968-1971). During this period, he attempted to revitalize Ireland's decorative arts by promoting the role of craftsmanship in local workshops. After returning to his native Finland, Gardberg established a silver and woodworking studio in Pohja in 1973. There he continued to produce exquisite jewellery and small sculptures set with a combination of precious and semi-precious stones, as well as ecclesiastical silver that was produced by Noormarkun Kotiteollisuus. Gardberg also designed other domestic products for industrial production such as his pewter tobacco jars, which were produced by Hopeatehdas. Known as the father of Finnish Arts & Crafts, Gardberg believed that, "It's not just brains that think, as every craftsman knows. The hands think, too, when they work with various materials. And between the hands and the brain lies the human heart, and love for the work. You have to respect the qualities of the material." Like many other Scandinavian designers, both past and present, Gardberg elegantly demonstrated through the aesthetic and functional integrity of his workthatraft aagatin

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b3c70ce8bf031eea643500ee83e44fa170f2f614b5be67cad3eacc250d536ce9.jpg)

Simon Gate, Parispokalen (Paris Cup) for Orrefors, 1925 - exhibited in the Swedish Pavilion at the Paris 1925 "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriel Modernes"

Simon Gate, Viking pitcher and glasses for Orrefors, 1917

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ffbff5b940b518ea99a69f6c29fef00948c1dba1610fc3b03004ad91abc97b8d.jpg)

# simon gate

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a08c5081df9c710c7a5bccd2ef10e6e4c6f87ce69bc2bcb1af560862a500ede4.jpg)

One of the most innovative Swedish glass designers of the 20th century, Simon Gate initially studied painting at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) and Tekniska Skolan (School of Technology), Stockholm, from 1902 to 1909, and then from around 1909 at the Kungliga Konsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts), Stockholm. In 1913, Johan Ekman purchased the Orrefors glassworks and subsequently began looking for a designer to help develop the company's products along more artistic lines. Already an accomplished figurative artist, in 1916 Simon Gate became the first "artist-designer" to join the glassworks. His product designs fell into two categories - simple functional wares that employed updated traditional forms which were produced by Sandvik, Orrefors' sister company, and finely engraved pictorial pieces with Baroque inspired decoration which epitomized the Swedish Grace" decorative style. One of Gate's most celebrated designs was the Parispokalen aris Cup) that was produced specifically for the Swedish Pavilion at the 1925 "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" in Paris, where it was awarded a Grand

Prix. This large showpiece vase measuring 75 cm in height was adorned with intricate classical motifs, which required around 450 hours to engrave. On seeing this design, as well as others by Gate and Edward Hald at the exhibition, the French critic Léon Deshair declared that the two designers "pensent en verre" (thought in glass). Certainly Gate and Hald's work from this period, though quite different in style from one another, marked a crucial turning point for Swedish glass and opened up new markets for Orrefors. Apart from his delicately engraved pieces, Gate also designed undecorated glassware such as the Slottglass (Castle glass) of 1923 - a green glass goblet with a fluted form that predicted the later and more sculptural Scandinavian style. From 1916 onwards, Gate collaborated with the master glassblower Knut Bergkvist and developed a new technique known as Graal after the Holy Grail, which involved casing clear glass blanks with thin layers of coloured glass. Patterns were then cut, etched or engraved into the objects to expose the underlying clear glass. These pieces were then reheated and coated with a layer of clear glass, and finally blown into their required form. By using this laborious and expensive method, decorative patterns could be encased to dramatic effect within glass bodies. During the 1930s, Gate's designs became increasingly simplified in both form and decoration. Often he would use broadly cut geometric motifs that gave his objects a jewel-like faceted quality. Around the same period, Gate also designed bulbous thick-walled vases which accentuated the inherent optical and reflective qualities of glass and provided a strong sense of mass. Throughout his career Gate experimented profusely with glass and developed numerous innovative techniques that were used by later designers at Orrefors. Like so many other Scandinavian designers, Gate perfected his craft by developing studio art pieces that were subsequently translated into designs suitable for industrial mass production. As one of the fathers of Swedish art glass, Gate had an enormous influence on subsequent generations of Scandinavian glass-designers, and through the sheer virtuosity of his work helped focus international attention on Scandinavian design during the inter-war years.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a4faebb2dfd6fbf95a8b6c1a088db0da1ca837c6e4ef80369b33f2e882f8765a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3fdc25de4622ca13f6b549cad0cefc5c62e661c5e6fb48dffdfe9c7929c69b93.jpg)  
Simon Gate, Thousand Windows cut-glass vase and bowl for Orrefors, c. 1930  
Simon Gate, Triton vase for Orrefors, 1916-1917

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7e50b2e612f89a5bafa0cf8399ded0e53b917a552b7a2fbac9cbf56f2bcb29ea.jpg)

# gustav gaudernack

## (active in Norway, b. 1865 Bohemia - d. 1914)

Born in Bohemia and trained at the Ceramics Vocational School in Tetschen (1885-1887) and at the Kunstgewerbeschule (School of Arts and Crafts) in Vienna (1888-1891), Gustav Gaudernack emigrated to Norway in 1891, where he became one of the main exponents of the Scandinavian Art Nouveau style. The same year he arrived in Norway he began designing glassware for the Christiania Glasmagasin in Oslo. From 1892 to 1910 he designed metalware embellished with decorative enameling for the David-Andersen company in Oslo, such as his exquisite Dragonfly bowl (1908). Gaudernack's designs epitomized the Art Nouveau style with their radiant enameling and sinuous and stylized forms derived from both flora and fauna. He received widespread acclaim for the skilful execution and imaginative style of his delicate yet sumptuous designs, which rivalled any work emanating from the main centres of Art Nouveau such as Brussels or Nancy. In acknowledgement of this, Gaudernack was awarded a silver medal at the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale" and (with David-Andersen) a Grand Prize at the 1904 "Louisiana Purchase Exposition" in St. Louis. In 1910, he

established his own workshop in Oslo and continued to design and produce objects using silver, filigree and enamel. Two years later, he became the first principal instructor for goldsmithing and enamelling at the Statens Håndverks- og Kunstindustriskole (National College of Art and Design). The work of Gustav Gaudernack reflects the considerable impact that the Art Nouveau style had on Scandinavian design during the early 20th century, especially in Norway, where swirling naturalistic motifs were adopted for furniture and textiles as well as for posters and food packaging.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/892fe32d428407cb77690f571e8db40e0ed829ad6e56c6d0f615fad968250e13.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f1b22d30746714cc5bf6de2df5432fcc22dcc22c572b924638bfea8228bd3f1e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/70413470be8a0d87816928a030e13439948d9d62872a6aa911025d0f61462d61.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/74d7da65e74bf07069d82f5d773860005a85d96103deac2cd6ebb05d3577d4f2.jpg)

Elsa Gulberg, Fish textile hanging for Elsa Gullber Textilier, c. 1950

Arthur Percy, Spaljé Trellis-work) screen-printed rayon textile for Elsa Gullberg Textilier, 1936 - Gullberg's company also produced other designers' textiles

# elsa gullberg

The Swedish textile designer Elsa Gullberg learnt the art of weaving at her family home in Scania. As a young woman she also practised carpentry, metalwork and printing prior to and during her studies at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) in Stockholm. From around 1910 she began designing textiles that were intended for either traditional craft production industrial manufacture. Aware of the design reforms advocated by the German Werkbund and well grounded in the tenets of functionalism, Gullberg was director of the testing and furniture design departments at the Svenska Slöjdföreningen (Swedish Society of Craft and Industrial Design) from 1907 to 1917. She later went on to head the Society's intermediary bureau from 1917 to 1924, where she gained first-hand experience of largescale industrial production methods. She eventually established her own textile company known as AB Elsa Gullberg Textilier in 1927, which produced knotted hangings and carpets with rhythmic folkstyle patterns. Around this time she also began experimenting with the weaving of synthetic silklike materials, such as rayon, which led to her designing a checked upholstering textile (1930)

for Nordiska Kompaniet. In 1955, Gullberg handed over the running of her company to her daughter, Elsa-Maria Gullberg, but continued to design textiles and carpets, some of which were suitable for domestic weaving techniques. During her career she also produced a number of specially commissioned textiles, including those for the Stockholm Concert Hall, Gothenburg Town Hall, Malmo City Theatre, and King's College Chapel in Cambridge. Like fellow textile designer Märta Maás-Fjetterström (1873-1941), Elsa Gulberg played a major role in the revitalization of hand-weaving that took place in Sweden during the inter-war years; but more significantly she was also an important pioneer of textile design for industrial production. Whether produced by hand or in a factory, Gullberg's brightly coloured textiles reflected her unquestionable skill and knowledge of design and manufacture. Her company also produced a wide range of modern mix-and-match upholstering textiles, as well as tapestry hangings by other designers, such as Birgitta Graf, Arthur Carlsson Percy (1886- 1976) and Einar Forseth (1892-1988).

Daniel Johan Carlsson, flintware teapot and covered dish with Viking Revival style decoration for Gustavsberg, 1877

Wilhelm Káge, green glazed vase with silver overlay decoration for Gustavsberg, 1930

The Gustavsberg workshop, 1896

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ba2345d6bd8aabe45e0c120b3a1659add4efdb96d7e9f46d82517e8e9a36c486.jpg)

# gustavsberg

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/322b226a4de6ccb99f33a9fd6772308018ac180b0c4392cff3993cebdbb9ec9c.jpg)

The Gustavsberg ceramics factory was founded in 1825 at Gustavsberg, on the island of Värmdö, on a former brickworks site. Initially the factory produced designs that were hand-painted with simple floral patterns but by the late 1830s the company had adopted various new techniques for ceramics manufacture from England, including transfer printing. At this stage Gustavsberg was also producing bone china using imported clay from Cornwall, and employed British workers skilled in china making. During this period, the patterns used often closely followed those of British wares. Reputedly, one of the owners of the factory had a double-walled suitcase in which he smuggled copper-plates between England and Sweden. Nevertheless, the factory soon became celebrated for its wares and achieved commercial success when its Parian figurines (also based on British antecedents) were awarded a gold medal at the 1867 Paris "Exposition Universelle". In the mid-1860s, Gustavsberg broadened its production of both "art" wares and tableware, but during the 1870s it came under increasing attack for the "bad taste" of its products. The management responded to this criticism by issuing ceramics that were truly Swedish in nature, and

by commissioning August Malmström (1829-1901) and Magnus Isäus (1841-1890) to produce designs in the Viking style. By 1900, under the directorship of Wilhelm Odelberg, the factory had 1,000 employees and was manufacturing delicately painted ceramics designed by Gunnar Wennerberg in the Art Nouveau style. The factory's output later came under the influence of the Svenska Slöjdföreningen (Swedish Society of Craft and Industrial Design), founded in 1845, an organization that was later inspired by the Deutscher Werkbund and similarly promoted an alliance between art and industry. Its rallying cry for the production of Vackrare Vardagsvara (More Beautiful Everyday Objects) was taken up by Wilhelm Káge, who had been appointed the artistic director of Gustavsberg in 1917. Under Kåge's leadership during the 1920s and 1930s, the factory's output became increasingly influenced by the Modern Movement. Kåge's stackable open-stock Praktika ware (1933), for example, was utterly utilitarian and epitomized the functionalist approach to design that was being promoted by the Continental avant-garde. In 1937, Kage designed a less austere and more popular service known as Soft Shapes, which predicted

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a1a7e377b30a37bf5d5b71913be8904a45c583490a902c6264fbfa4e9991e975.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1223c4f5d925b426ef0217a30e2854b9b884697e518bbd5b8cc33ebae21ba940.jpg)

Wilhelm Kåge, various stoneware vessels and plate for Gustavsberg, late 1950s

Berndt Friberg, large stoneware vase for Gustavsberg, 1959

Carl-Arne Breger, stoneware water jug for Gustavsberg, 1957-1958

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8d53a15e8afea3ad11866fe41bed7a5240da95b2cc6640def0b05bf436336313.jpg)

the organic forms that were to become so characteristic of later Scandinavian design. The same year, Gustavsberg was sold to the Kooperativa Förbundet (Swedish Co-Operative Union and Wholesale Society) and the factory was modernized. Two years later, it began production of sanitary wares, and in 1945 started manufacturing products in plastics. In 1949, Stig Lindberg took over from Wilhelm Káge as artistic director. During his tenure, Gustavsberg produced both industrial products and more limited artistic wares. During the 1970s, Sven-Eric Juhlin (b. 1940) - who founded Ergonomi Design Gruppen - and Jan Landqvist designed sanitary wares and plastic products for the company that were primarily intended for institutional use. In 1980, Karin Björquist (b. 1927) succeeded Lindberg as the factory's artistic director and in 1987 the company was sold to the Finnish company Wärtsila. Later, Gustavsberg was divided into three small companies that continued to produce ceramics in its old factory. Since 2000, the company has been part of the Villeroy & Boch group and today specializes in the design and manufacture of sanitary ware.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/292c40c9bbd3cc5a989c5a9f4fe42933079473ed4bc3b1fb3f1732b09ca84cce.jpg)

Per-Olof Landgren, plastic measuring jug for Gustavsberg, 1977
