## hackman

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2710e4f3f4d7ef235489a1b9bb9d1b7a915748b88fc6f180fa3fc1e45f86858c.jpg)

In 1790, the merchant Johan Friedrich Hackman established a trading company in Vipuri that sold wooden products, including goods made from sawn timber. This wood was lumbered from forests owned by the company and processed in its sawmills. When Johan Friedrich Hackman died in 1807 his widow consolidated the company. At the same time, his son introduced a number of industriallyproduced products that were made and retailed by the company. By the early 1860s, Hackman was using steam-driven saws to increase its productivity, and the company's subsequent expansion led to the acquisition of the Havi Candle Facty in 86 an thefunding f a cutley facry close to Vipuri, in 1876. This new, larger enterpriseundertook the manufacture  a wide range fwares, including kitchen knives, hunting knives, scissors, skates and forks. Hackman later exhibited at the 1878 Paris "Exposition Universelle", where it was awarded a gold medal for its kitchenware and cutlery designs. In 1891, the company's cutlery factory was relocated close to its sawmill in Sorsakoski. After a fire at the sawmill in 1898, the factory expanded its product-line to include handcrafted tools, a variety of household utensils, and a number of products made of sheet metal. In 1902, Hackman began using a nickel-steel alloy for its knife and fork handles - a new material that had been developed in France in 1885 and was remarkable for its strength. Later in 1923, Hackman began manufacturing products in another newly developed material - stainless steel. Like other Scandinavian manufacturers, Hackman was highly instrumental in the promotion of this revolutionary material through its production of elegant yet popular designs. During the early 1950s, Hackman introduced its first stainless steel saucepans (which were initially made by hand) and started commissioning well-known designers such as Kaj Franck to contribute to its product-line. The company subsequently received widespread recognition when it launched Bertel Gardberg's Canton cooking pan and Carelia cutlery in 1957 - designs that exemplified Hackman's pursuit of design and manufacturing excellence. Over the following years, Hackman continued to specialize in the production of stainless steel household wares, which on occasion featured handles made of exotic hard woods or plastics. One of the most iconic designs produced by Hackman was Tapio Wirkkala's

##

stainless steel and nylon-handled Puukko knife (1961), which was inspired by traditional Sami hunting knives. During the 1960s and 1970s, Nanny Still McKinney and IImari Tapiovaara also produced designs for the company. In 1969, Hackman & Co became part of a joint stock company and later in 1989, joined an amalgamated trust group known as Designor Oy (which today also owns the Arabia, littala and Rörstrand brands). Having subsequently become the leading design house in Finland, the company reaffirmed it commitment to avant-garde design by holding a large design competition in 1990, with the winning entries being put into production. During the late 1990s and early 2000s, Hackman commissioned a number of leading designers, including Björn Dahlström, Harri Koskinen, Carina Seth-Andersson (b. 1965), Antonio Citterio (b. 1950) and Ross Lovegrove b.1958), to create a new and exciting range of cutlery, utensils and cookware known as the Tools collection. The manufacture of functional, innovative and simple products such as these has ensured Hackman's position at the forefront of a major international revival of interest in Finnish design.

Δ4 Stefan Lindfors, Tools salad bowl and servers for Hackman, 1998

AΔ Bjor Dahiström, Tools saute pan and grill pan for Hackman, 1998

Renzo Piano Workshop, Tools cutlery far Hackman, 1998

Tapio Wirkkala, stainless steel and nylon Puukko knife for Hackman, 1961 - based on traditional Lapp hunting knives and produced up to the 1970s

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/894d2b11e8e99e32bb4f7e3e3177cf26c5f18af3cc5ca123af172ed49081a691.jpg)  
Gro Bergslien, apple shaped glass paperweight in opaque glass for Hadeland, 1976

# hadeland

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/693ba6e6af2f59573857630e37a6cc04a6786ca62284918bf75fcff2f4ff353d.jpg)

In 1762, the Danish-Norwegian state under the rule of Christian IV began building a glassworks on a site it owned in Jevnaker. Three years later the Hadeland Glassworks fired up its ovens for the first time and started producing colourful cobalt blue bottles, pharmaceutical glassware, and floats for fishing nets. Hadeland was an enlightened employer who granted its workforce various social rights and advantages, including the provision of schooling for the workers' children. The glassworks was sold in 1824 and in 1842 Hadeland was amalgamated with two other glassworks to form a privately owned company. After the introduction of transparent colourless glass in 1852, Hadeland began producing tableware products, including drinking glasses, bowls, dishes and vases - both plain and undecorated. In 1898, the glassworks became part of the Christiania Glasmagasin company, which had (and still has) a large shop on the main square in Oslo (or as it was known then, Christiania). In response to government pressure on factories to increase the design quality of their products, in 1929 Hadeland appointed Sverre Petterson (1884-1958) as its fs artisticdiector. Initially, Pettersons des were light and graceful, but by the mid-1930s they became heavier and more architectural, thus reflecting the influence of the Art Deco style. Under his guidance, the glassworks hired the talented designer Willy Johansson (a third-generation glass blower) in 1936. At Hadeland, Johansson learnt sandblasting techniques from an in-house apprenticeship with the sculptor Stále Kyllingstad, as well as engraving methods at the Christiania Glasmagasin. From 1947, Johansson headed the Hadeland design team and in 1954 he received a Diplôme d'Honneur at the X Milan Triennale for his glassware, which was distinguished by simple elemental forms. During the 1950s, Hadeland also produced glassware designed by Herman Bongard (b. 1921) and Arne Jon Jutrem (b. 1929), which also possessed an unadorned solidity that emphasized the intrinsic qualities of the glass. Since the 1950s, Hadeland has produced both pressed and blown glassware, ranging from the utilitarian to the artistic (such as Gro Bergslien's apple forms of 1976). In 1988 Arne Jon Jutrem became the artistic director of Hadeland's design department and during his tenure the glassworks has produced distinctive sculptural glassware designed by, among others, Maud Bugge and Lena Hansson. Although perhaps lacking the sheer brio of Finnish glassware, Hadeland's output has nonetheless been central to Norwegian design. Today, the glassworks remains responsible for around eighty percent of the country's total glass production.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4a78f229b2d7253727d7bd95a2105ee656802935322a6e5433169e27e032e76a.jpg)  
Inger Magnus, Glide carafes with shot glass stoppers for Hadeland, 1990  
Hadeland glassware from the Nostetangen collection, based on 19th century models

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4ecbd0a33a1ef56bc4885178a236568566ebe9793750eea44195281d25bc5bd1.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/459a0979f3bac389ffb500d1e5a8012f3547013aa0ac37269baae1c264a8169a.jpg)  
Edward Hald, Bollspelande Flickor (Girls Playing Ball) vessel for Orrefors, 1919

# edward hald

## (b. 1883 Stockholm - d. 1980 Stockholm, Sweden)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3f813248cb69ea138c0b6a76e674e1be8aa38c6a86e7aa826efed13c39de3962.jpg)

The Swedish glass and ceramic designer Edward Hald trained in England and Germany from 1903 to 1904 before furthering his studies at the Technische Akademie (Polytechnic) in Dresden from 1904 to 1906. At this latter institute Hald was undoubtedly inspired by the reforming activities of the Dresdner Werkstätten für Handwerkskunst (Dresden Workshops for Arts and Crafts, founded in 1898) and its advocacy of well-designed products for everyday use. In 1907, Hald went on to study painting at the Artists' Studio School in Copenhagen, and from 1908 to 1912 trained under Henri Matisse (1869-1954), whose Fauvist style had a major influence on the decoration of his later designs. Between 1917 and 1927 Hald designed a number of ceramic products for Rörstrand, including the earthenware Halda service (1919) with blue rustic-style transferprinted decoration. This tableware, which epitomized the elegant "Swedish Grace" style, was illustrated in Gregor Paulsson's influential 1919 publication, Vackrare Vardagsvara (Good Everyday Goods). In 1917, Hald also began designing modernistic glassware for Orrefors. Like his contemporary, Simon Gate, Hald executed a

number of engraved narrative pieces for Orrefors, even if his style was influenced more by trends in contemporary fine art than by historic classical styles. His Bollspelande Flickor (Girls Playing Ball) vessel of 1919, for example, was adorned with simplified expressionistic motifs that drew stylistic references from Henri Matisse's art. In response to the Svenska Slöjdföreningen's (Swedish Society Craft and Industrial Design)rallying cry for beautiful yet functional wares for everyday living, Hald also designed more utilitarian glassware, which was shown to much acclaim at the 1917 "Home Exibiion"held at he Lijvalcs galleyStcholm and was produced by Sandvik (which became an Orrefors subsidiary in 1918) from 1920 to 1930. These moulded products made of muted coloured soda glass were notable for their softened traditional forms and simple ribbed or fluted decoration. In the late 1920s, Hald began devising even more rational pressed glass designs that were suitable for large-scale mass production. It was his virtuoso engraved "art" glass pieces, however, such as his exuberant Fyrverkeriskálen (Fireworks) bowl of 1921, that brought Hald the most recognition at the time and led to him being awarded a Grand Prix at the 1925 "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" in Paris. While continuing to design for Orrefors and Rörstrand, Hald was also the artistic director of the Karlskrona porcelain factory from 1924 to 1933. He became managing director of Orrefors in 1933, and although the glassworks' experienced cuts in exports and shortages of raw materials during the Second World War, it continued to undertake both technical and artistic experimentation. During this period, Hald developed the Cut Graal and Fish Graal cased-glass techniques, while the forms he adopted became softer and simpler. In 1945, Hald was awarded the Prince Eugen Medal for his glassware designs, but two years later he decided to resume painting, while simultaneously remaining a freelance designer for Orrefors until the late 1970s. As one of the leading proponents of Modernism in Sweden, Hald's great skill lay in his ability to impart the high-object integrity of his exclusive craft-based pieces to product designs that were intended for industrial mass production.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4717402c293118e66c82ca984a2100aef1d5886eef39c8b7608581ca45fcb9f8.jpg)

Edward Hald, Fish Graal vases for Orrefors, 1937

Frida Hansen, design for Poppy tapestry, c. 1898

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/56d6563ce650b989a540749106083f20298c35052a3f64afe6417d279d5d61ab.jpg)  
Frida Hansen, Summer Night's Dream tapestry hanging, c. 1899

## frida hansen

## (b1855 Hillág-by-Stavanger - d.1931 Oslo, Norway)

The Norwegian textile designer Frida Hansen studied painting and then later established an embroidery workshop in Stavanger in 1882. During this period she became increasingly familiar with the Norwegian folk tradition of tapestry weaving - since the 18th century "peasant" weavers had produced tapestries depicting relatively primitive biblical scenes. These textiles, whose powerful artistic expression made up for all that they lacked in technical finesse, were generally viewed in Norway as national art treasures. Inspired by these antecedents, Hansen began learning weaving techniques on a standing loom at Kjerstina Hauglum in Sogn, and in 1889 completed her first woven tapestry. A year later she established her own textile studio, and then in 1892 moved to Oslo (then known as Christiania) where in 1899 she founded a weaving company under the name of Det Norske Billedvæveri (DNB). Initially she drew motifs from the ancient Norwegian sagas, but after a trip to Paris around 1885 she embraced the Art Nouveau style. From this period onwards, her tapestries were often adorned with "Mackintosh-style" roses and other highly stylized flowers with strong saturated

colours and bold swirling outlines. At the DNB workshop, Hansen not only revived the use of natural dyes and old weaving techniques, but also pioneered special new techniques including her famous "transparent weaving", which brought her widespread acclaim. This innovative process involved leaving some areas of the warp exposed so as to create a transparent background that contrasted dramatically with the richly coloured woven motifs. During the 1890s, Hansen collaborated with fellow Norwegian designer Gerhard Munthe (who designed several tapestries for DNB), and in 1899 she wrote a book entitled Husflid og Kunstindustri i Norge. She exhibited her work at the 1893 "World's Columbian Exposition" in Chicago and also at the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale", where she was awarded a gold medal. By the turn of the century, Frida Hansen was not only the most important Norwegian weaver, she had also become one of the greatest advocates of the naturalistic Art Nouveau style in Scandinavia.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/44fba0877a0f29947d94b888e2cbcac0dbbf6f8ddc29da4a2825321229644c9a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/06a6482143038f2e60aa34102cf4ca4ca1cf7955696e7aca4258daa97f8c2e6c.jpg)  
Søren Hansen, DAN chair for Fritz Hansen, c. 1930

# søren hansen97)

After the First World War, the Danish manufacturer Fritz Hansen began experimenting with the production of bentwood furniture using techniques that had been pioneered at the turn-of-the-century by the Austrian designer and industrialist, Michael Thonet (1796-1871). Eventually, Søren Hansen (a descendent of the company's founder) and Fritz Schlegel (1896-1965) came up with some designs for the company, which after several attempts were successfully put into production. As Karl Mang noted in his publication, History of Modern Furniture (1979), Søren Hansen's bentwood DAN chair of 1930 was, "One of the few truly new ideas for the bentwood process, which was stagnating in terms of formal process." The chair's continuous bent-wood element, which incorporated both the back rest and rear legs was not only a technical masterpiece, but also provided the design with an elegant line that emphasized its structural essentialism. Both Hansen and Schlegel went on to design tubular metal Modernist-style furniture for Fritz Hansen, which was similar to that emanating from Germany at the time and which was shown at the 1932 "Den Permanente" exhibition. The same year, Søren Hansen officially joined the family firm and designed a number of chairs for the company, including a painted beech upholstered armchair (c. 1949) that was notable for its curvaceous sculptural form. Like other leading Danish designe, Hansen's considerable skll and understanding of construction enabled him to re-invent and modernize existing furniture types, while balancing function and aesthetics so as to achieve optimum solutions for industrial manufacture.

Production of a bentwood element for the DAN chair, c. 1930

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/756e016a1ece93cfd4d86af21e7ec599f06c4373b8dcdc142573d6dd5b5a35c6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f841288b42c56541c14f02113089518d3920aabbe2f8ec7ea866cb35cbf35a8b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/56b0fdde92418d9e747f4bd562b9d16b20e5cfc559749220e8fa3f24cf70cef1.jpg)  
Søren Hansen, stacking stools for Fritz Hansen, 1953

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bb44cd9950ad43a5b414dba17727f595f9dc82a8ad878e15abb5f8f34619821b.jpg)  
Sixten Sason, Hasselblad 1600F launched in 1948 - the world's first single-lens reflex camera in 6x6 format with interchangeable lenses, film cartridges and view-finders

Victor Hasselblad

# hasselIblad

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d8d5e5c8d28fa91c6703bc2ef30276bb9c54b6acf8e91d8d2004a7a612f6b118.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/147a45e668ab9ffa0725ede27e649e6b9dfe9e6bed594ce7806645e190165f91.jpg)

Renowned throughout the world, Hasselblad cameras have been used by a plethora of famous photographers, from Ansel Adams to Irving Penn, to create some of the most iconic images of all time. The origins of Victor Hasselblad AB can be traced back to an earlier wholesale and distribution company, which was established in 1841 by Victor Hasselblad's (1906-1978) grandfather. This firm sold all kinds of products - musical instruments, knives, perfume, toys, oil lamps, buttons, soap, crinolines, pens, and water closets - and by the 1870s was retailing lanternes magiques imported from France and various photographic and stereoscopic pictures. Later, the company began importing photographic equipment and in 1893 it issued the first Swedish camera catalogue, which not only included the Kodak No. 1 and No. 5 cameras, but also a camera marketed under the name of Hasselblad Swedish Express. The company eventually became the official agent for Kodak in Sweden and its founder's son, Victor, became a keen amateur photographer. In turn, his son Victor also developed an interest in photography, which he combined with his love of ornithology to become a pioneer bird photographer. In

1925, he undertook a period of study at Kodak in Rochester, New York, and returned with a somewhat cumbersome Graflex, which was then the best camera available for bird photography. Although he later used a more compact 35 mm Leica, he was still not entirely satisfied with the results. In 1933, he published a book entitled, Flyttfagelstrak (The Passage of Migratory Birds) that was illustrated with his own photographs, but by this time he was already thinking about creating an entirely new camera. During the Second World War, a German bomber containing a reconnaissance camera was shot down over Swedish territory. When the Swedish Air Force took the camera to Victor Hasselblad to see if he could produce a replica, he reputedly said, "No, but I can make a better one!" The resulting handheld military aerial camera, the HK7, was the first single-lens reflex camera in 6x6 format with exchangeable film cartridges, and was manufactured for the Swedish Air Force from 1941 to 1945. This undertaking proved an invaluable experience, particularly in terms of what Hasselblad learnt about the construction of shutters for use in very cold temperatures, the benefits of motorized camera operation and interchangeable cassette systems, and the value of high-resolution optics that could be speedily interchanged. Even before the hostilities of war had ended, Victor Hasselblad began adapting the HK7 for civilian use. To this end he had the foresight to recruit the leading Swedish industrial designer Sixten Sason to work on the project. Using wooden prototypes, Sason developed a highly compact housing for the camera that had a simplified and easy-to-use arrangement of operational buttons. The resulting 1600F, which shared many of the features of the earlier HK7 camera, was launched in New York in 1948 where it met with huge enthusiasm - U. S. Camera magazine commented, "With few reservations we have found this to be the dream camera." The 1600F was an ergonomic masterpiece and became the blueprint for all subsequent Hasselblad cameras. In 1962, when NASA engineers were sketching what they thought would be the ideal space-camera, the astronaut Walter Schirra remarked, "That looks just like my Hasselblad!" A Hasselblad 500C was duly purchased and modified by NASA engineers to fit their exacting specifications. Since then, Hasselblad cameras have been used on all American space missions, including the first moon landing in 1969. Combining elegant no-nonsense styling and superlative function with high-quality robust construction, the Hasselblad camera system is among the most famous Swedish products of all time.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ffab8bb8935aa27c148826fb73b265a39969b2bc16e3c12995630241af2a4789.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/88bfee9dea73cb80e4b4648f2bbec6efd8bb0b379a88734b618186a492b02efa.jpg)

Hasselblad HK7 handheld aerial camera manufactured for the Swedish Air Force, produced from 1941 to 1945 Hasselblad 205TCC camera, 1991

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7af55c3b8ef6d8d005ad336d1a144a07f46bf463829a525282c652f6f5dfa9b9.jpg)  
Edwin E. Aldrin with the Hasselblad EDC (Electronic Data Camera) lunar camera 1969 - Hasselblad cameras have been included on every NASA manned space mission since 1962  
Hasselblad 503CW camera - equipped with winder CW and view-finder PME90, 1996

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/af3fb63a19b30a5436062f49940ca3601b6d54e190133061aee87c96bd829437.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/37105c645140aa92ac4eab77cf9e0510f4346041344394f6ad5bb7c0794e55e2.jpg)

Piet Hein, Model no. 1147A Superegg silver box for Georg Jensen, 1966

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/88f5e3d59aea7ca8b09d84378cd947483dbf1efee9ef53d4c9a827a2fd84b4b7.jpg)

Piet Hein, leather and steel bar stool for Fritz Hansen, 1961

## piet hein

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a49b9f40bdda88e6d3359888ebb7af38019a98e1f645ed075319ec40dfbd886d.jpg)

The Danish mathematician, author, inventor and designer Piet Hein studied at the Copenhagen University, Copenhagen, and the Kungliga Kunsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts), Stockholm. Throughout Scandinavia, he is best known for his thousands of epigrammatic poems, which he referred to as "grooks" and published under the pseudonym, "Kumbel". He also wrote extensively on the relationship between the arts and sciences. While working as a consultant on a town-planning project in 1959 that encompassed the design of Sergels Square in Stockholm, he devised the concept of the "superellipse" as a way of solving traffic problems. Halfway between an oval and a rectangle, the "superellipse" form was repeated in his product, lighting and furniture designs, such as the Superegg box (1966) for Georg Jensen and the Superellipse table (1964), which he co-designed with Bruno Mathsson for Fritz Hansen. For his achievements as an inventor and his work in the sciences Hein was awarded the Alexander Graham Bell Prize in 1968, and an honorary doctorate from Yale University in 1972. His pioneering work as a designer was also recognized when he was awarded the prestigious ID

Prize and the German "Die gute Industrieform" Award in 1971. Hein's product designs can be seen as an outcome of the continuous search for the "ideal" form - a defining characteristic of much Scandinavian design. Unlike most other practitioners, however, Hein used mathematics as the basis of his solutions rather than historic precedent. He was a wry commentator on design, who summarized his definition of good design in a poem entitled Form and Content: "Most design is sheer disaster/hiding what one doesn't master. True design asks one thing of us/to uncover what it covers."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bd01752a819512e1c876885cd03c90b5f81c2939d58ab74edcb345f914a69ebc.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8f309259184ac6aff4dc1c6be0e92acdf0560b7e92c0b7a639819b7cc5b2b2c9.jpg)  
Poul Henningsen, PH table lamp for Louis Poulsen, 1927

# poul henningsen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ae99c8d85f45ac2cdea73b05466d08a4ed3d6f005ae645ca0b4532fa6de8e349.jpg)

## (b. 1894 Ordrup - d. 1967 Ordrup, Denmark)

One of the greatest lighting designers of all time, Poul Henningsen studied building techniques at the Tekniske Højskole (School of Technology), Copenhagen, from 1911 to 1914, and then architecture at the Polyteknisk Læreanstalt (Polytechnic) from 1914 to 1917. While studying architecture he designed his first chandelier as part of an interior design project. After leaving the Polyteknisk, having not sat his final exam, Henningsen worked as a reviewer for the art journal Vor Tid and as co-editor of the art periodical Klingen from 1917 to 1921. He was also a journalist for the newspapers Politiken and Extra Bladet from 1921 to 1925. During this early part of his career he experimented with different materials such as copper and glass, and designed a number of light fixtures including a chandelier for the Carlsberg brewery in 1919. Three years later he exhibited a spherical light fitting with reflective planar rings at the "Artists' Autumn Exhibition", and designed the reflective Slotsholm street light for Copenhagen's municipal lighting department. As a left-wing radical who throughout his life agitated for social improvement, Henningsen used his column in Politiken as a platform for presenting his views on design - on the need for better lighting he wrote: "The aim is, by working scientifically, to make lighting cleaner, more economical, and more beautiful." In 1924, he developed a series of multi-shaded table and hanging lights that were designed to reduce the dazzling glare of the modern electric bulb. The same year, he began designing lights for Louis Poulsen and the first lamp from his subsequent PH series was exhibited to great acclaim at the 1925 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes", where he was awarded a gold medal. Henningsen's revolutionary PH series of hanging, wall, and table lamps were the result of many years of scientific study and fine-tuning. By using light diffusion graphs and reflection sketches he was able to develop a system of lights with alternately shaped and positioned shades that could direct light downward, provide good ambient light, and minimize the intensity of the reflected light and bulb - all at the same time. The logarithmic spiral configuration of the elegantly curved PH shades was reputedly inspired by a cup, bowl and plate stacked on top of one another. The PH lamps, which were designed for mass production and are still manufactured

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9c446b5f066da29e4867559309887a84b96006b9726b1ca5c4a2bbc713d8a7f2.jpg)

by Louis Poulsen, were widely exported throughout Europe, North and South America, Africa and Asia. Prior to the Second World War, they were especially popular in Germany, where they were featured in Das Neue Frankfurt magazine and Licht und Beleuchtung, the Deutsche Werkbund's architects' reference book on lighting. In the early 1930s Henningsen also designed furniture such as his Slangestolen (Snake chair, 1932), which was constructed of a single length of bent tubular metal, for the Copenhagen-based manufacturers V. A. Høffding. Throughout much of his life, Henningsen denounced what he saw as the artistic pretensions of Scandinavian design and urged for a more utilitarian approach that would bring good design to the masses. Unlike the majority of Modern Movement designers, however, Henningsen regarded traditional forms and materials as eminently suitable for the manufacture of more democratic products. On his death in 1967, he left over one hundred lighting designs, some of which have been issued posthumously.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d5cf53799dc00eebb4cef22e3fef1d412686d788a4c7f72a334a8d495220ced4.jpg)

Poul Henningsen, tubular steel and red leather Slangestolen (Snake chair) for V.A. Hoffding, 1932

Tubular steel furniture designed by Poul Henningsen for V. A. Høffding on display in Fredericia, 1932

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/df2e0afaaa8a21f96543fecb91253ac435b58cc92388465c57bdc989947b5cc8.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/43b21aa4a1bc7a0846d00e77340a563434b0ad09dd6b8ed8a294c6cc513a63d8.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4a43a53d36bd9750d7b45116131eaa036fcd7e02f5389cd1a5c6beea17024b66.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8a9fd64f6c8ca896e4d3206fa88cac3531d81c7181e83411c9d2c934eac6174d.jpg)

Erik Herlow, silver cocktail shaker for A. Michelsen, c. 1950

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/544e173d4391566c3c651dae941717bcabd5131db6c51e930be245784c11e6d3.jpg)  
Erik Herlow, aluminium water pitcher with bakelite handle for Dansk Aluminium Industri, 1952

# erik herlow

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a8a5ffd9b7de9376d52a9b75728c142bcb3001282e215d96ae16d305e20049c1.jpg)

Erik Herlow studied architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in Copenhagen from 1937 to 1944. He established his own practice in Copenhagen in 1945, and over the course of his career undertook the design of numerous architectural projects, including several state-funded exhibitions on Danish design, the United States Embassy in Copenhagen in 1952 (with Jørgen Juul Møller), an oil refinery for Shell, and various factories. Herlow is also well known for his industrial design work. From 1942 to 1949 he designed for the silversmith A. Michelsen and later designed metalware products for Georg Jensen, Norsk Stálpress, and Dansk Aluminium Industri. For the latter company he re-designed (1952-1953) a standard aluminium and bakelite coffee pot, which the firm was already manufacturing, into a sleek and streamlined product that used significantly fewer components in its construction. Between 1952 and 1954 Herløw was director of design instruction at the Guldmedehojskolen (College for Gold- and Silversmiths), Copenhagen, and from 1955 to 1979 he served as artistic adviser to the Royal Copenhagen porcelain factory. He also co-founded the Danish

Industrial Design Society, was professor of industrial design at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) from 1959 to 1979, and during the late 1970s undertook the interior design of the SAS A-300 Airbus. Herlow was a major force in the evolution of Danish industrial design, who believed that the role of the designer included working closely with production specialists so as to have an influence on all aspects of the design process, from initial feasibility studies to the final manufactured product. In this way, products could be assessed and closely evaluated during their development so as to ensure that the best possible solution was achieved. Summarizing his design philosophy he wrote, "The physical design of a product will reach the optimum of its usefulness where there is an interrelation between economy, construction, material, function, aesthetics, ethics, and use."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dc9d0a4478252d99d97715822f90e923b71cb273723554215099cd81dabdc8fc.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8fa920b24d3249dd09437444c275db6e52fc713d73f5898ef1efe9fd02140e9e.jpg)

Erik Herlow, aluminium and bakelite coffee pot for Dansk Aluminium Industri, 1956 - version of his earlier "redesigned" coffee pot from 1952-1953

Erik Herløw, silver mocha service for A. Michelsen, c. 1950

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9e99fb332383271a36c064cf8e8a5f83d9ab6320838697e7e7cfd33418b4a842.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/66552d14015b489b42ea09ceda4a1642b5225a5c1dcbb7df8dbc5274fb327026.jpg)  
Orla Juul Nielsen, Tranquebar wine glasses for Holmegaard, 1925

# holmegaard

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bb34be68a2ae1eb1a88b1208863223190dc6a5a226a9206531c5e0580284fe1e.jpg)

At the beginning of the 19th century, the progressive thinker Count Christian Danneskiold-Samsoe decided to use the peat that occurred in abundance on his large estate as fuel for a new glassworks. Having died before he could see this project through to fruition, his widow, the Countess Henriette Danneskiold-Samsøe, finally managed to establish the glassworks at Holmegaard Marsh in 1825. The first furnace for the factory was built by a Norwegian glassmaker, Christian Wendt, who had previously worked for the Hadeland glassworks. Initially, the factory manufactured cheap dark green bottles, known as bouteilles, which were used for beer or schnapps. After several years, however, the Countess bravely decided to up-grade and broaden the company's product line. To this end, she hired glassmakers from Bohemia and Southern Germany, who in 1835 began producing European-style traditional domestic stemware and art glass. In response to increasig competition from Swedish glass manufacturers, in the early 20th century Holmegaard recruited its first well-established designer as a permanent member of staff. Orla Juul Nielsen was employed by the company as part of a co-operative enterprise with Royal Copenhagen to produce glassware for its porcelain dinner services. It was noted in a company letter at the time that, "the factory has found an artist who shares our view of remaining down to earth, primarily in the shape of the drinking glass and the glass bowl, which attempt to come as close to the essential form as the nature of the material wil allow, avoiding everything wit a hit verefinement rtisthtness, but in its pure simplicity striving for the grand effect." Certainly, the idea that beauty could be achieved through simplicity was not only at the cutting-edge of design thinking at the time, but when put into practice by Holmegaard it led to an important new identity for Danish glassware. In 1924, the architect Jacob Bang joined the design office at Holmegaard and four years later he was appointed the firm's artistic director. Bang subsequently designed a variety of glassware within a modern idiom. His well-known Primula and Viola ranges were widely acclaimed and revealed his talent for producing designs that were simple and practical yet pleasurable to the eye and hand. While Bang's work was highly rational, being intended for the most part for industrial

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f5e86c60d3753c1b1b8cfb4b60d77356f5775abfa698d155234bfd1a176db09f.jpg)

manufacture, it was not devoid of the human touch. Unlike the machine aesthetic that was being promoted at the Bauhaus, Bang's product designs projected a softer and more emotionally seductive aesthetic, which predicted the Scandinavian approach to Modernism that flourished from the 1930s onwards. Bang's work for Holmegaard was also a demonstration of his belief in democratic design - he preferred "not expensive drinking goblets for the Rockefellers, but beer glasses for Mr. Hansen of Denmark" Nevertheless, Bang did design on occasion exclusive "art" glass pieces, including crystal glasses, bowls and cocktail shakers that were delicately engraved and cut with Art-Deco-style motifs. In 1942 he was succeeded by Per Lütken, who remained Holmegaard's artistic director until his death in 1998. During his 56 year tenure, Lütken not only introduced the latest developments in "art" glass, but also attempted to "renew the classical wine glass" by continuing to adopt the traditional cold cracked-off way of producing such products. His best-known design, the Provence bowl (1955), demonstrates the way in which Lütken was able to revolutionize traditional glassmaking through

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/801ef55035b710a122f0ebbee2dcd59fb15eda91d567b99b21cee727eb039145.jpg)  
Per Lütken, Winston water jug and glass (green), 1957 & Jacob Bang, opaline glassware range, 1960 - both for Holmegaard  
Jacob Bang, carafe for Holmegaard, c. 1932

Michael Bang, Saturn Platina glassware range for Holmegaard, c. 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3d55041fd518e65c6f72af64fe4b1e71a68f31a9ad97b19c374a9eeac6be82bc.jpg)

pioneering forms. Products like this epitomized the widely perceived good taste of Danish design during the 1950s and brought Holmegaard much international recognition. Believing that design should "delight our common daily lives", Lütken perpetuated the Holmegaard ethos of simplicity, classical proportion, and high-quality craftsmanship through his work. In 1968 Jacob Bang's son, Michael, joined the glassworks and subsequently designed a number of successful glassware ranges, such as the elegant Fontaine wine glasses (1987) with bubble encased stems. He has also designed "art" glass and several lights, including the Parasol table lamp (1970). Other designers who have worked for Holmegaard since the 1980s include Torben Jørgensen (b. 1945), Anja Kjær (b1956), Arje Griegst (b. 1938), Ann-Sofi Romme (b. 1957), Allan Scharff (b. 1945) and Peter Svarrer (b. 1957). Combining the traditional simplicity of Danish design with new and experimental forms, these individuals have all helped pioneer the sensuous functionalism that has come to epitomize Scandinavian design in the early 21st century.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/57d55795eee9246dffc594968e5e81565ddd70197157880d138421a6baa6b6f8.jpg)

Bent Severin, Princess glassware range for Holmegaard, 1957

From left to right: Darryle Hinz, Neptun champagne glass, 1991; Arje Griegst, Xanadu goblet, 1983; Torben Jorgensen, goblet with white filigree, 180 Michael Bang Fontaine wine glass, 1987; Michael Bang, Monte Carlo cocktail glass, 1984 - all for Holmegaard

From left to right: blue vase by Bent Severin, blue bowl by Henning Koppel, blue vase by Nanna & Jørgen Ditzel, white vase by Henning Koppel & white vase by Nanna & Jørgen Ditzel - all for Holmegaard, 1958-1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a28db94e1c38263d589920a3a4dc8a5612329401a31fce927c7a822392116b16.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/20a7d959013aa05945c97460e87b4674c1cd0f28edddd4c26794820194165f8d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e467856a44700078e9512343df14a54b2934cb955b63d336c9412eb19163278c.jpg)  
Knud Holscher, d-line door handle, 1972  
Knud Holscher, d-line staircase system, 1999

# knud holscher

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b8eea436661e1215f6325da451ff011a4f35a8d435db6a2f25ac81b158ce0fe2.jpg)

The internationally renowned industrial designer Knud Holscher studied architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, between 1949 and 1957. From 1959 to 1964 he worked for Arne Jacobsen, initially as a draftsman and then later as an associate participating in one of Jacobsen's most important building projects, St Catherine's College, Oxford. In 1964, Holscher established a design practice in Copenhagen in partnership with the British product designer and architect Alan Tye (b. 1933). Together, they co-designed the Modric range of sanitary fittings, which was "the first comprehensive range of ironmongery manufactured in Britain to an integrated design system". The Modric range subsequently received a British Design Award and the British Aluminium Industry's Prize in 1966. The following year, Holscher became a partner in the Krohn & Hartvig Rasmussen architecture practice. In 70, he won another British Design Award for an office signage system and two years later, designed his highly successful d-line range of stainless steel door furniture, which has won numerous awards and has grown to become the world's most extensive range of architectural ironmongery and bathroom fitings. The d-line range with its soft-edged functionalism is a powerful manifestation of Holscher's striving for a humanist interpretation of the rational and the presumptive". In 1975, Holscher designed stainless steel salad servers and tongs for Georg Jensen within a similar vocabulary of form. Today, Knud Holscher Industrial Design is one of the leading Scandinavian design consultancies and numbers among its clients JCDecaux, Erco, Philips, Fritz Hansen, and Facit. The practice has also won numerous competitions for street furniture and signage systems. Holscher's classically Scandinavian humanist approach to industrial design synthesizes the "rational with the irrational" so as to "provide an answer to problems that are so complex that their formulation is found only in, and with, theirsolution". Holscher has also designed numerous public and private buildings, including the Royal Danish Theatre in Copenhagen (1978), Næstved Town Hall (1981), and Copenhagen Airport (1983), and with others has published a book entitled Culture in a Crisis Time (1980).

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/eeb4373f90d4034be68fd2e1e4cf80071529de2ad30554c1f56d72e741d46178.jpg)

FriedI Holzer-Kjellberg, FK-Service tableware for Arabia, 1953

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a54ff878699ee04da11074d6c3e6d5434a49d53d885e10f41eac1979d61e4b80.jpg)

# friedl holzer-kjellberg

(active in Finland, b. 1905 Loeben, Austria - d. 1993)

Born inAustria, Fried Elfriede) Holzer-Kjelrg studied at the Kunstgewerbeschule (School of Arts and Crafts) in Graz. After her formal academic training she moved to Helsinki and in 1924 began working as a designer for Arabia, for whom she was eventually appointed artistic director in 1948. Her ceramic designs for the company were awarded a gold medal at the 1935 Bruxelles "Exposition Universelle et Internationale" and a silver medal at the 1937 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne". She experimented for ten years before perfecting her famous rice-pattern technique, which involved the meticulous cutting of rice-like patterns into an extremely thin porcelain body so as to leave delicate "open" incisions. These were then covered with a colourless glaze, which produced areas of subtle translucence. Holzer-Kjellberg's exquisite and extremely delicate rice-pattern series reveals the influence of the art of Japan, which was widely felt across the whole of Scandinavian ceramic design in the 20th century. Comprising numerous bowls and a tea service, when first launched in the early 1940s the rice-pattern tableware was an instant success. It remained in production with Arabia until c. 1974, but due to popular demand was re-introduced in 1986. Apart from her porcelain products, Holzer-Kjellberg also designed a number of one-off stoneware pieces. Having won a gold medal at the 1954 (X) Milan Triennale and the Pro Finlandia Medal in 1966, she continued designing for Arabia until 1970.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b77412194aeb859564f321c7683964b6d85d84d6f345532225fe8686d0396690.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8a4754b966f4104dbe930f0a598f7815c5a609d5868c233de0fc4c55c53d8827.jpg)  
Alfred Homann, Ensemble chairs for Fritz Hansen, 1992

# alfred homann

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9373c17bfb65d9389a93803d1f074a9dc1832442dd6db85a11433bbb039486f0.jpg)

One of Denmark's most celebrated and hardworking architects and industrial designers, Alfred Homann graduated in architecture from the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in 1976. From 1972 to 1979 he was employed in the design practice of Professor Vilhelm Wohlert (b. 1920), who like others of his generation approached architecture holistically, becoming intricately involved in all aspects of the work from the construction process to the details, inventory, and lighting, so as to ensure the highest level of quality. While working in Wohlert's office Homann designed light fittings for the first time - an area of design for which he would later become particularly well known. By the time Homann established his own architecture and industrial design practice in 1978, the holistic approach had become central to his work. Both his Homann wall lamp (1981) and Strata light series (1988), for example, were originally designed site-specific for a major building renovation he undertook in Nyhavn. Whether designing an outdoor lamp or a piece of furniture, Homan seeks "simplicity, clarity and logic", and believes that the best products are those that are initilly designed for a specific context and then later converted into standard manufactured products (Louis Poulsen subsequently put both the Homann and Strata lights into production). In 1992, Homann won widespread recognition - including Germany's Red Dot Award - for his multi-purpose Ensemble chair designed for Fritz Hansen. The undulating single-form seat shell of this chair, which is injection-moulded in a specially developed recyclable polymer, incorporates an innovative construction technique that produces air pockets in the shell so as to reduce the amount of material used and lessen overall weight. The Ensemble's seat shell not only affords good lumbar support and resilience, its surface provides a pleasurable tactility. As an efficient stacking chair designed specifically for the contract market, the Ensemble is appropriate for a number of uses including cafeterias, conference rooms, offices and waiting areas. Like his lighting products for Louis Poulsen, Homann's furniture for Fritz Hansen, Rudd International USA, and Labofa benefits from refined detailing and high-quality engineering - attributes that are synonymous with Danish industrial design. Apart from his product design work, Homann has also undertaken numer-

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dd0bdb0e35fc49019e50cd5cc4e731d491fe8811f55b0e5b8eaab922f3d9d801.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5532db433aaa3ead3d1377a0068c8e86f509582b20286ba80ffc365b9e9c31b0.jpg)  
ous architectural projects (both new-build and renovations) for private homes as well as housing projects and public buildings, most notably for DSB (The Danish State Railway).

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1cae67c41d4f09cd91dfdcccd60bdba88e28b44c0e75d9e7a45f344fb847a4ae.jpg)  
Δ Alfred Homann, Nyhavn pendant lamp for Louis Poulsen, 1986  
Alfred Homann, Nyhavn parc light for Louis Poulsen, 1984  
Alfred Homann, Homann bollard for Louis Poulsen, 1992

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fd2b66a22d6456ab54023c7c8edb3b21aa39be246684c6a1cd65b37f3c1ae2d7.jpg)  
Göran Hongell, Aarne glassware range for Karhula, 1949-1950 - now produced by littala

# göran hongell

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/014f8e4b03b5cdf8886401fc786786666932f04c888eb2ef96fd33bc4869f3bc.jpg)

The son of a master builder, Göran Hongell studied decorative painting at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu University Art and Design), Helsinki, from 1919 to 1922. At that point in time "decorative painting" studies were the forerunner of more formal industrial design courses and were intended to equip students with the necessary skills to become either craftsmen in their own right, artistic directors working in factories, workshops and studios, or art teachers. During this period in Finland there was a general concern to forge a new and distinctive national style (as a result of the country's newly-found independence) and to provetheaesthetqualityndustrially-manufactured products. In response to this climate of reform in design, Hongell became a member of Ornamo (the Finnish Association of Decorative Arists.Havingndoubtedly read r cle in the 1927 Ormano yearbook written by Arttu Brummer (1891-1951), which praised the Svenska Slöjdföreningen (Swedish Society of Craft and Industrial Design) and its promotion of "Beautiful Things for Everyday Use", Hongell and the decorative painter Gunnar Forsström established a design practice that initially specialised

in the design of murals and stained glass. Following this, in 1928 they submitted designs to the Riihimäki glassworks' competition for "useful and tasteful types of glass for everyday use" and won fourth prize. In 1932, Hongell was awarded third prize in a similar glassware competition held by the Karhula glassworks for his Congorilla vase, which was decorated with simple marine-life motifs, and the same year he exhibited a series of black moulded designs with silver enamelling at the Finnish Society of Craft and Design exhibition. Also in 1932 Hongell began working as a part-time artistic adviser to Karhula and the factory subsequently began manufacturing his unusual decorative black glass designs with classical motifs, which were exhibited at the 1933 (V) Milan Triennale. During the early and mid-1930s Hongell designed a number of cut and engraved glass pieces for Karhula that played off the optical qualities of the material. It was not until 1937, however, that he began designing pressed-glass products that were suited to large-scale industrial mass production. During the 1940s and 1950s, his glassware became increasingly utilitarian, with simplified forms and plain undecorated surfaces.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/06389ea3e3d7289c330658d7434cb5c62eb2197cc12ad35c36154420240842a1.jpg)

Among his best-known designs from this period were his "sea-green" glass vases, which were designed specifically for export, and his Aarne glasses (1949-1950), which continue to be produced by littala - having been described in Hufvudstadsbladet in 1951 as, "a compromise between the popular stemmed glasses and the cheaper unstemmed ones. They feel good in the hand and possess a 'festive' quality." In 1957, Hongell retired and a year after his death in 1973, a retrospective exhibition of his work was held at the Karhula Glass Museum. Although less wellknown than later designers such as Timo Sarpaneva or Tapio Wirkkala, Hongell was one of the great pioneers of industrial glass design in Finland and was one of the first Scandinavian designers to produce high-quality everyday glassware that was not only extremely functional but also possessed a real sense of style.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a91c11d5cf0959b8ee649c18aa1c5eaa91b23c947d52a037139726280bc25029.jpg)  
Göran Hongell, blue crystal vase for Karhula, 1930s  
American advertisement for Karhula showing a cut crystal bowl by Göran Hongell, late 1930s  
A SELECTION OF OUTSTANDING VASES AND BOS, HAND BLOWN Bowl as illustrated in clear crystal or seagreen Fetne Itwin InTERiOR DESiGn 1519 NORTH MAIN SANTA ANA, CALIF.

Saara Hopea, Pantteri vases for Nuutajärvi, 1954

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a5493625c04d01cf97e4de01980aee1b458dfe1b110db6ce7de7fade037c7596.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/26f22b986daf1eb165ed7bee637a93f553c20fd0078cb6386744467ed42a249a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bc8a5a8bac61fb0191fce06ed656441c7e7f99b3e4aec17eb5b0a46cf064367e.jpg)

# saara hopea(

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/56a50e8e33db0fec8039d60331544cd31a1c542e05b67245e2d76b2c0250588a.jpg)

The Finnish ceramicist, silverware and glass designer, Saara Hopea, trained as an interior designer at the Konstindustriella Läroverket (College of Industrial Art) in Helsinki. After her studies she worked as a freelance interior designer from 1946 to 1949. In 1952 she joined the Arabia ceramics factory and for seven years worked as an assistant to Kaj Franck. She showed her work alongside Franck's in a special exhibition held in Helsinki in 1954, and also participated in a number of other landmark exhibitions including the 1955 Helsingborg "H55" exhibition. At Arabia she designed several unusual ceramic pieces, including the Astarte hors-d'oeuvres tray, which was named after the many-breasted Phoenician fertility goddess and made up of nine "joined" bowls. In 1954 (X) and 1957 (X1) she was awarded silver medals for her "practical pottery" at the Milan Triennale. During the 1950s, she also designed glassware for Nuutajärvi - some of which was suitable for industrial production, such as her moulded pitcher and stacking tumblers, which won a silver medal at the 1954 (X) Milan Triennale. Her higher volume designs were generally distinguished by clean undecorated forms and a rainbow of muted colours (the latter being a renowned characteristic of her glassworks), while her other work, such as her exquisite spotted Pantteri vases, fell into the category of "art glass". Hopea also designed silverware for her family's business, Ossian Hopea, in Porvoo. In 1960, she married the American Oppi Untracht, and the same year emigrated to the United States where she worked as a freelance silver designer for, among others, Potter and Mellen Inc. Her husband taught her enamelling techniques and she subsequently produced numerous copper bowls and plates adorned with sweeping geometric patterns made by overlapping coloured enamels. Returning to her native Finland in 1967, she continued to produce enamelled metalware designs until 1980. Saara Hopea's work not only reveals her unquestionable mastery of materials, form and manufacturing techniques, it also stands in testimony to the significant role women have played in the history of Scandinavian design - a contribution that has facilitated the development of a more humanistic approach to design, especially with those products intended for industrial mass production.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e26bd89f5839e0dbc0f37ec462000f843fc3007bdafa2f0708fe1bff85f6915a.jpg)  
Saara Hopea, Model no. 1718 glasses for Nuutajärvi, c. 1951  
Saara Hopea, Model no. 1618 pitcher with Model no. 1718 glasses for Nuutajärvi, c. 1951

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2c752b532cd012120d470572ed8251869f261f4a29166e76a5461dd8011c2025.jpg)  
Johan Huldt & Jan Dranger, Stuns chair for Innovator Design, 1973

# johan huldt & jan dranger

(b. 1942 Stockholm, Sweden)

During the 1960s there was a commonly-held belief that group action was a more effective means of producing a result than going it alone - the espousal of this conviction resulted most dramatically in the anti-Vietnam war protests and student riots of 1968. The idea of organizing direct group action was also embraced by many young designers, especially in Sweden, a country that already had a long history of design collectives and manufacturing co-operatives. In the late 1960s, four students from the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) and Tekniska Skolan (Institute of Technology) in Stockholm, Johan Huldt, Jan Dranger, Martin Eiserman and Jan Ahlin, formed an alliance known as Dux4yra. Initially, they began developing experimental furniture made of plastics and paper. Following this, the group was invited to the factory of the Swedish furniture manufacturer Dux to conduct further experiments with tube bending machines. It seemed that bent tubular metal was the material best suited to their objectives: the realization of functional, affordable democratic furniture that comprised a "do-it-yourself" element. As Kerstin Wickman noted, "The American 'do-it-yourself' idea

(b. 1941 Stockholm, Sweden)

had now taken hold and the intention was that it should be as easy to buy furniture as to buy food in the self-service store. This was the tone of the discussions during these years." After graduating in 1968, two of the group members - Johan Huldt and Jan Dranger - founded their own manufacturing company the following year. Known as Innovator Design, the venture came to international prominence when it exhibited the Stuns chair at the Cologne Furniture Fair in 173. This highly functional tubular metal easy chair with a canvas sling and polyurethane upholstered cushions was startling for its simplicity and prefigured the new international style of High Tech. As a piece of knockdown furniture, the chair was sold in unassembled component form in a cardboard box so that it could be bought literally off the shelf. Interestingly, Johan Huldt grew up in his childhood home with an early example of Swedish knockdown design, the Trivia furniture range (1943) designed by Elias Svedberg and Erik Wørts, the latter of whom became one of the pioneers of flatpack furniture at IKEA during the 1950s. Huldt and Dranger's furniture, which was made available in five different colours - yellow, orange, green,

Johan Huldt & Jan Dranger, Tech trolley for Innovator Design, late 1970s

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2b4e2b5fb81a7e6159e84e982589fb9af9192975ca72c5d1ba499832fb83d11d.jpg)  
Johan Huldt & Jan Dranger, Slim chair for Innovator Design, 1982

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/871dcb17759a86afe0278176f65f09e08c5572344025a19f58b70665101b4a43.jpg)

brown and black - and with chrome finishes, was especially well received in France, where it was retailed by Habitat and Roche Bobois. Colourful, cheap and easy-to-assemble, their Innovator range of chairs, tables and trolleys projected a youthful image that was unashamedly industrial. While countering the natural wood aesthetic so closely associated with Scandinavian design, Innovator Design furniture could be seen as a re-interpretation of 1930s Swedish Modernism, which was itself associated with experimental applications of industrial materials such as steel and glass. Huldt and Dranger's landmark furniture designs signalled a new internationalism in Scandinavian design and were retailed anonymously so as to counter the cult of designer-names that had gained enormous currency in the 1950s and 1960s. The furniture became so popular during the late 1970s and early 1980s that eventually Innovator Design production plants were established in four different countries and IKEA began retailing pieces from the range. In tribute to his far-reaching contribution to Scandinavian furniture design, Huldt was made director of the Swedish Furniture Research Unit (1974-1976) and in 1983 was appointed chairman of the

National Association of Swedish Interior Architects. He has also consulted to the United Nations and in 1994 was made director of Svenska Form the Swedish design council. Having subsequently suffered much copyright infringement with Innovator furniture, Huldt became a leading campaigner against design plagiarism. Since the mid-1980s, Jan Dranger has collaborated with IKEA and has concentrated on sustainable design. In 1999 he received an Ecology Design Award (IF Hanover) for his inflatable softAir seating. Manufactured by News Design DFE, the softAir range includes a couch that uses 83% less resources than an upholstered version and takes 10% less space to transport and warehouse. Inexpensive, durable, and environmentally sound, this highly innovative furniture is available through IKEA.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/202e99448c249748927e97787cbce8508a954fb38391f3f2a49a61f360637174.jpg)

Model no. 90 chainsaw for Husqvarna, 1959

## husqvarna

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/26fda1b61f09e8d212d938f12c6085dbfbfae586b36a1d27bebd6d435999b8b1.jpg)

One of the highest profile Swedish manufacturing companies and one of the world's oldest brands, Husqvarna has a history that dates back over three hundred years. In 1689, King Charles XI of Sweden ordered the construction of an arms factory on the banks of the Husqvarna River, some six kilometres east of Jönköping. Initially, the factory produced musket barrels using waterpower harnessed from the Husqvarna River. The name Husqvarna means, "house-mill" (hus = house & kvarn = mill) and can itself be dated back to as early as 1529. During the late 19th century, the company - like the famous Remington Armory in the United States - diversified its product-line to include the manufacture of domestic appliances. Husqvarna's first sewing machine, known as the North Star, was launched in 1872 and two years later the Husqvarna foundry was established in order to manufacture a startling array of products, from wood-burning kitchen ranges to labour-saving household appliances such as machines for grinding corn and mincing meat. Husqvarna began manufacturing bicycles in 1896, and this led the company to become not only the largest producer of bicycles in Sweden but also from around 1903 a manufacturer of motorcycles. It was not until 1914, however, that the first Husqvarna motorcycle was actually launched. Like other Husqvarna products, the motorbikes were prized for their high build quality and for the value-for-money they offered - both central tenets of Swedish design. In the early 1950s, the company sought the design expertise of Sixten Sason, one of Scandinavia's first industrial design consultants and an early advocate of product streamlining. Sason was responsible for the design of a number of Husqvarna appliances, including a drill and sewing machine that were notable for their streamlined aesthetic and compact yet logical layout. During the mid-to-late 1950s, Husqvarna engines were incorporated into new products such as lawnmowers (from 1956) and chainsaws (from 1959). The first chainsaw was known as the Model 90 and heralded the firm's legendary status as a manufacturer of exceptional forestry equipment. A decade later, this reputation was sealed with the introduction of the Model 180 - the world's first chainsaw to have a totally integrated vibration-dampening system that made it both safer and easier to use than existing models. Since then Husqvarna has pioneered

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c5c45bf89b642afd90230e43d84b56ea37c0e823dc63658ff93ff9142519465f.jpg)  
Carl-Arne Breger, microwave oven for Husqvarna, 1969  
Gunnar Leijonberg, automatic lawnmower, 1999

many user-friendly features for its award-winning chainsaws, which are especially favoured by professional users for their excellent power-to-weight ratio. In 1977 Husqvarna was purchased by the powerful Electrolux Group. The Husqvarna motorcycle brand was sold in 1996 to the Italian manufacturer MV Agusta/Cagiva, and the sewing machine brand was sold in 1997 to the Swedish Viking company. Today, forestry, park and garden products together with white goods manufacture remain Husqvarna's core business.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bb7c0b6d6cbe52977dc05467d890d6b967d9a6075b41faa5fb07e1fe4a83b6d6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/59eef946717c9abd1c82a8f273024fdbad31345608fbb624fd545994d218e51e.jpg)  
Peter Hvidt & Orla Molgaard-Nielsen, AX table for Fritz Hansen, c. 1950

> Peter Hvidt & Orla Melgaard-Nielsen, teak and papercord armchair for Søborg Møbelfabrik, c. 1956

Peter Hvidt

# peter hvidt (b. 1916 Copenhagen - d. 1986 Copenhagen, Denmark) orla mølgaard-nielsen

(b. 1907 Alborg - d. 1993 Hellerup, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4167cc97dd78b4cfccec098baa47dd2ad5587a90f9f6cc8e4a51382e28eb96b1.jpg)

Orla Mølgaard-Nielsen apprenticed at the Nielsen Brother's workshop in Iborg and subsequently studied cabinetmaking at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts), Copenhagen. He later trained under Kaare Klint in the furnishing and interior design department of the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen. As one of Klint's students, Molgaard-Nielsen was grounded in the relatively new science of anthropometrics - the systematic collection and correlation of measurements of the human body - and taught how to apply this data to his furniture designs so as to achieve better performance and more comfortable solutions. After completing his academic studies, he worked for a number of Danish architectural firms, including those headed by Kaare Klint, Helweg Moller, Arne Jacobsen, and Palle Suenson (1904-1987). Between 1944 and 1975, he worked in partnership with Peter Hvidt, who had similarly studied cabinetmaking at the Kunsthandværkerskolen, Copenhagen (graduated in 1940 and established his own design practice in 1942). One of the earliest fruits of this collaboration was the Portex chair (194) - a teak design with an innovative construction that was one of the first modern stacking chairs produced in Denmark, and one of the first Danish furniture products conceived for industrial manufacture. Manufactured by Foreningen for Møbelexport, the space-saving Portex chair was available with or without arms and with an optional upholstered seat cushion. Hvidt and Mølgaard-Nielsen also collaborated with the well-known Danish furniture manufacturing company Fritz Hansen, and this association led to the pioneering of a completely novel form of furniture construction known as "lamella-gluing in which layers of beech wood were laminated onto a mahogany core - a technique similar to that used for wooden tennis rackets. The AX chair (1947) was the first Danish furniture product to employ this method of construction. Initially, the chair had a quilted leather-upholstered sling seat/ back that was attached to its beech-veneered mahogany core frame, but the design was later modified so that it was better suited to industrial mass production - from 1950 it was manufactured with a teak and laminated beech frame with separate moulded teak-faced plywood seat and back elements. Although most often seen as an

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1fc121ea0eab7ed7196fc0b6f018bfcb68b7b81af992ac14df3f8935d7bae363.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/35aef97116bcbf51438bfa224ea75766ec47a9a758f991d0eafd4974c9aab925.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/01018e95ee3f2edf38d0815bfd5af013c24fddaa06e02eb48f4fcbc201d1b26b.jpg)

Peter Hvidt & Orla Molgaard-Nielsen, unassembled AX chair elements for Fritz Hansen, c. 1950

Peter Hvidt & Orla Mølgaard-Nielsen, AX chair for Fritz Hansen, 1947 - this early version had a padded leather sling seat/back section

Peter Hvidt & Orla Mølgaard-Nielsen, AX chair for Fritz Hansen, 1950 - the mass-produced version

armchair, the AX chair was also available without arms and with a single armrest. Tables were also produced to match the range of chairs. Importantly, the AX series could be sold in knockdown form, which made the different pieces easy to transport and relatively inexpensive to produce. As a result, the AX armchair became one of the best-selling chairs in Scandinavia during the 1950s. It was also awarded a number of prizes, including diplomas of honour at the 1951 (IX) and 1954 (X) Milan Triennale exhibitions, and a Good Design Award from the 1951 "Good Design" exhibition in America. During the 1950s, Hvidt and Mølgaard-Nielsen also designed a number of "Teak Style" chairs for France & Son and for Søborg Møbelfabrik, which revealed the influence of Finn Juhl's sculptural furniture. Later in 1961 they developed the X lounge chair for Fritz Hansen, which featured a woven cane seat construction and utilized the lamella-gluing process. The design of this chair was adapted in 1977 to facilitate a quilted seat section and to enable it to be linked into benchlike configurations for waiting areas. From 1970, Hvidt and Mølgaard-Nielsen also collaborated with the designer Hans Kristensen. Through

their high-quality, democratic, and technically innovative furniture designs, such as their hugely successful AX chair, Peter Hvidt and Orla Molgaard-Nielsen helped disseminate around the world the casual yet modern Scandinavian look that became so popular and influential during the 1950s and 1960s.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3644aa9e4409fb7e818c62e5f0161b42df58801a93c233e081be17d5444d87b5.jpg)

Alvar Aailto, Savoy vase for Karhula, 1936 - later produced in cobalt blue glass by littala

Timo Sarpaneva, Lansetti II Lancer /) ase or littaa, 1952

## ttalad

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/42f887d7b3bcef8d0b4d8b4e48d11e3f2c1fe37d03772847afcc16c7adfb47eb.jpg)

The littala glassworks has historically contributed much to the identity of Finnish design and through its contemporary ranges of glassware continues to promote the design excellence that has become so closely associated with the country's manufacturing output. In 1881, the Swedish master glassblower Per Magnus Abrahamsson founded the littala glassworks near Kalvola. Although the company's first glassblowers were recruited from Sweden, its early glassware, which mainly consisted of simple undecorated designs for household use, revealed influences from elsewhere in Europe. After a difficult beginning, the glassworks eventually managed to gain a reputation for its high quality products, some of which were engraved by Alfred Gustafsson with patriotic motifs that reflected Finland's struggle during the early years of the 20th century for cultural and political autonomy. Having encountered financial difficulties during the First World War, the company's main shareholder was forced to sell his shares to the Karhula glassworks in 1917, which at the time formed part of the Ahlström group. Although it lost its commercial independence, littala benefited from the additional investment that came with being part of a larger industrial group. Ahlström subsequently instigated numerous improvements at the factory, and the Karhula glassworks continued to play an important role within the group's activities. Even though products from both glassworks bore the name "Karhulalittala", by the 1930s littala was mainly concentrating on the production of blown-glass rather than on pressed glass — limited-production "art" glass pieces rather than mass produced utilitarian glassware. Initially, Aino Aalto's range of functional Aalto glassware (1932) and Alvar Aalto's famous Savoy vase (1936) were manufactured by Karhula and were only later produced by littala, In 1946, littala organized a competition in an effort to develop a range of contemporary art glass, which was won jointly by Tapio Wirkkala and Kaj Franck. The unconventional nature of both these designers' work heralded a new direction for littala and a new era in Finnish glass design. Objects such as Wirkkala's famous Kantarelli vase (1946), which was inspired by the heaped piles of chanterelle mushrooms sold in the main market in Helsinki, revealed the new sculptural confidence that was emerging in Scandinavian design at the time. In 190, Franck left lttala, and the same year Timo

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/39c58e0f6afb0036783acca2807811e1a7019e9a74283ae1152ee003104ecbd2.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e142f25b4705f5b459b98c5abe48760436389a809398d990f47ea24a6f748eb0.jpg)  
Annaleena Hakatie, Ballo candleholders for littala, 1995

Sarpaneva joined the glasswork's design team. Like Wirkkala, Sarpaneva pioneered many new forms and techniques that brought the company international recognition, especially at the Milan Triennale exhibitions, and helped focus attention on Finnish design in general. During the 1950s, Sarpaneva designed several pieces of highly sculptural art glass that employed his innovative steamblowing technique. He also designed the company's first modern household glassware collection in the mid-1950s. Known as the i-collection, this product range was the result of two years intensive research and attempted to bridge the utility and art glass markets. With its delicate smoky colours and simple forms, the i-collection possessed a sophisticated yet elemental beauty. During the 1960s, littala produced a number of "frosted" glassware ranges, notably Sarpaneva's landmark Finlandia vases (c. 1964) and Wirkkala's bestselling Ultima Thule range (1968), which exerted considerable influence on mainstream glassware design in the 1960s and 1970s. In 1971, the company built a new factory for the production of light fittings and established a glass museum, but during the early 1970s littala along with other

Finnish glassworks suffered badly from the global recession and from the proliferation of cheap foreign copies of their designer collections. By the mid-1970s, however, there was growing demand for smooth-surfaced glassware, and littala designers produced a number of ranges using innovative production techniques, such as those used for Sarpaneva's Archipelago range (1978), which captured different sized bubbles within the glass. The Kaveri wine goblets (1979) designed by Jorma Vennola (b. 1943) even combined clear glass bowl with interchangeable plastic stems. During the late 1980s and early 1990s, two new designers joined the littala design team, Tiina Nordström (b. 1957) and Markku Salo (b. 1954), who have since executed a number of notable designs for both mass-produced and one-off glass objects. Tiina Nordström's Major votive lantern vase (1994) reflects her goal of finding a concrete expression for glass, which she describes as the "immaterial material". In 1990, littala was purchased by the Hackman Group and with a new international outlook the company commissioned work from non-Finnish designers such as Konstantin Grcic (b. 1965) and Marc Newson (b. 1963). Since

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6dc76e8510dd9902dd174f34f26d589a74db064c93143d6e642b105f2aad2692.jpg)

1996, the highly talented Finnish designer Harri Koskinen has also worked in-house for littala and has developed a number of notable products, including the At/as vase/candleholder (1996) and the large-scale Relations storm candleholder (1999), both of which demonstrate the innovation and artistry that have for so long been littala's greatest attributes. In order to meet changing lifestyle demands and to spread its beliefs about how to optimize the functionality of our homes, in 2002 littala's parent company launched a range of products for cooking and socializing that was brought together from its four Scandinavian brands - Arabia, Hackman, littala and Rörstrand - into one new common brand, littala.

Markku Salo, Nappi candleholder for littala, 1998

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/14a0d13dacfdbd04cff110c76f4c4e146ef939df2b58bc75bc524b9e1d9be18c.jpg)
