## ikea )

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/89c501ed71f9d9d57a0c0473b54ad45434b04f33889b4fd0c68e6979991a3901.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/32f9236681632d10ce9ea3a29f4ad959597486799496c52b2421830010e2e96e.jpg)

No other company has promoted the concept of Scandinavian design more widely than IKEA, which for over 50 years has manufactured and retailed highly democratic products based on the trinity of "form, function and affordability". From its outset, IKEA espoused a particularly Swedish philosophy born out of the socialist belief that "people who are not well off should be given the same opportunities as people who are" - which in IKEA's case has meant the possession of welldesigned yet inexpensive home furnishings. The origins of this extraordinary and now major international company can be traced to 1943, when the 17-year-old Ingvar Kamprad (b. 1926) established IKEA with a £150 loan from his father. Founded in the town of Álmhult, a few miles from Kamprad's home village, IKEA was situated in the Småland region, which had historically been one of the poorest areas in Sweden. The company originally sold a variety of products through mail order, from cattle soap to stockings. Later, during the early 1950s, IKEA began retailing furniture at factory prices through mail-order catalogues. This move was met with such resistance from the established furniture trade that IKEA was not allowed to exhibit at the large furniture trade fair held in Stockholm, and suppliers threatened to boycott the company. Kamprad observed that "most nicely designed products were very, very expensive", yet he desired to manufacture well-designed practical products that the majority of people could afford. With this social mission to democratize design, Kamprad realized that there were three essential priorities - aesthetics, function and suitability for mass production. During the late 1950s, he recruited his first designers, Gillis Lundgren, Bengt Ruda and Erik Worts who, while working within the modern idiom, were designing furniture for self-assembly - a revolutionary development in domestic furniture design. The Regal bookshelf (1959) designed by Lundgren was one of the first IKEA products sold in flatpack form. Another concept for the home IKEA's designers were developing at the time was "modular thinking", which involved the design of modular components and interlinking systems rather than individual one-off products. This led to the company's co-ordination of its product range and promotion of an integrated home furnishing look - an ey and mportant example lifestyleetailng. The advent of particleboard in the 1960s heralded

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/132ab3c793f3e8d49ae729cc34846e133d7c88106a7c9475a4807bfd320ff80f.jpg)

Karin Mobring, Diana chair for IKEA, 1972

Jan Dranger, Tärnaby chair for IKEA, 1983

v  Cover of the first IKEA catalogue, 1951

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/16a540c035f0bad954d00fa3893514c374bed90168bf1d67ce032bfab24981a0.jpg)

the arrival of really low-cost furniture and this influenced IKEA's product line immensely. An inexpensive yet relatively hard-wearing material, particleboard was used extensively by IKEA for all kinds of self-assembly furniture, as it still is today. While the 1970s were a period of general insecurity, IKEA prospered and expanded thanks to its policy of producing inexpensive functional furniture with a youthful appeal. During this time the company developed several products made of plastic, including the Telegono lamp (1970) designed by the avant-garde Italian designer Vico Magistretti (. 1920). Throughout its history, however, IKEA has mainly concentrated on producing interpretations of well-known classic designs - from the ubiquitous Thonet café chair to the plastic Polyprop stacking chair by Robin Day (b. 1915). The 1970s were also the decade of stripped pine and brightly coloured textiles, and IKEA developed this informal look within its product range specifically for younger consumers. The following decade, IKEA began producing stylish yet practical furniture designed by, among others, Niels Gammelgaard - whose Moment table (1987) won the Excellent Swedish Design Award. Other award-

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/69e1a0fc4a976a67e6a0e2df73f658d78719ba337caedfc4b97cb5e75025a692.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f9c5fee750cf18c10bc5e1d4007984a2f9b8c966ba891954bba6abdd12e6e978.jpg)

Gillis Lundgren, Tajt chair for IKEA, 1973 - could be converted into a single bed

Tord Björklund, Bogen table/trolley for IKEA, 1986

Knut Hagberg & Marianne Hagberg, Puzzel children's furniture range for IKEA, 1988 - recipient of an Excellent Swedish Design Award in 1988

> Olle Gjerlöv-Knudsen & Torben Lind, Skopa chair for IKEA, 1974

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d2b2cb9bb357917c3ad042ff51336a05fbc017d64af4b5252bf64df39bf5258d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8c317d5ef2b97e4ffd224423135e9716565f771237f8ae1612f5f97c6dbb22d6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7b63bc048a913511128e7d6c7aa17d0f9e7cfc3f1c89efa0a8f82a7cd3bbd8ef.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f5612bdc908df1093558b523f2101e233636a30969ae0c4bc4c7dc57e12e6493.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/be074ad81d2a33d86679ad01787afb15eced4348065bd3c0619bb6bcf596ff1f.jpg)

Noboru Nakamura, Poem chair for IKEA, 1977 - although designed by a Japanese designer this chair is "so strong, so blond, so typically Scandinavian that it remained in the IKEA range for years"

Δ Cover of British IKEA catalogue, 1989

winning designs included the Puzzel range of children's furniture (1988) designed by Knut and Marianne Hagberg, which combined primary colours and simple forms and became a bestseller. Throughout the 1990s, IKEA continued to expand its international operations and popularize its version of Scandinavian Modernism. Today, the IKEA Group has 143 stores in 22 countries with a further 20 stores owned and operated by franchisees in 13 countries/territories. More than 2,000 suppliers in over 50 countries manufacture its 12,000 products. In 2001, the company's turnover was 11.3 billion Euro, while more than 255 million people visited its stores worldwide. Despite these impressive figures, Kamprad is not content with just producing home furnishing and plans to diversify his product line into small pre-fabricated flats. As the design theorist Victor Papanek wrote, "One thing is certain: IKEA will continue in the forefront - ecologically, socially and culturally - of making things that work, possess beauty and are affordable."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/56d698146e09efc4e92cd2ac161971f918fc7abf8f111e7e7a6554178e435e4c.jpg)

## A best seller, in anyone's books

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2ca9c1fb72790b7402bc39ce7dce1076b9332384b23dc782a98d39f9c320c028.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8cd9e8186e684888fd0421944873a38bc7f82199a10b94746e15958954435fe4.jpg)  
InterDesign, locomotive design for ASEA-Traction, 1985

# interdesign

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fc5910281c5462fa0b4b6bc9fbf8a32bb9b9962f27d9e36b4146e709f5e91aaf.jpg)

In 1982, Tom Ahlström (b. 1943) and Hans Ehrich (b. 1942) established InterDesign as a subsidiary of their other design practice, A&E Design, which had been founded in Stockholm in 1968. This earlier design consultancy was highly celebrated for its design for disability and won numerous awards for s life-enhancing and ergonomic products. InterDesign was established for the development of more industrial-type products - from transportation to packaging to consumer products. In 1997, the venture became an independent industrial design consulting company and has since become one of Sweden's leading industrial design practices. Although mainly specializing in plastics, InterDesign has worked in a wide range of fields and materials. It has designed sanitary ware for Gustavsberg (1984), a locomotive for ASEA-Traction (1985), a range of toothbrushes for Colgate (1986), automatic injection devices for SHL Medical (1997), as well as dental floss and tooth pick dispensers for Jordan, electric circuit breakers for Norwesco, and lighting fixture components for Belysia. In 1992, InterDesign received the Swedish Environment Design Award for its returnable milk bottle (1992) designed for Arla.

In the development of its product solutions, Inter-Design uses state-of-the-art computer-aided design (CAD) systems to rapidly determine product structure, and stereolithography to validate the ergonomics, aesthetics and integration of components - all before tooling is engaged. InterDesign states: "Designing the first product for a company is not without consequences. Designing the second and subsequent products means implementing a design strategy that includes product mage in perfect coherence with user ergonomics. The whole must satisfy and attract the direct client (the distributor) as well as the client of the client (the ely and competitive differentiation." InterDesign's work exemplifies the Scandinavian approach to industrial design - the meticulous development of qualitydriven product solutions that are informed as much by human needs as by material, technological, and commercial concerns.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/86d0fbc192b82ebe3462e7556ca530cd276442ceb79d789fd1cbb51fff7c0f44.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/274fed65116f76be4832143585022875a028951bb9b9722a5b663bfb2f35be81.jpg)  
InterDesign, toothbrushes for Colgate, 1986  
InterDesign, Peninject autoinjector for Scandinavian Health, 1995  
InterDesign, returnable milk bottle for Arla, 1992

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a9afad51922bebae5aa17a08e85e2cd76ba049385d3eee4ea181de8dd2e4276c.jpg)

Alfred William Finch, earthenware vase, covered pot and jug for Iris Workshops, C. 1897-1902

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/16fb02cb272eee98f197de355f0b6292c2a608355b48ce8c2bbda2e503bc6990.jpg)  
Alfred William Finch, earthenware vase for Iris Workshops, c. 1897-1902

# iris workshops

In common with other artistic collectives established around the turn of the 19th century, such as the Guild of Handicraft in England (founded 1888), the Dresdner Werkstätten für Handwerkskunst in Germany (founded 1898), and the Wiener Werkstätte in Austria (founded 1903), the Iris Workshops produced well-designed highquality wares for everyday domestic use in the New Art" style. With its factory based in Porvoo, the workshops retailed its goods with much enthusiasm in Helsinki, but lke so many other progressive reformist design ventures its endeavours were relatively short-lived, spanning only five years. The founding of the Iris Workshops by the Swedishborn furniture designer, Louis Sparre (1866-1964) and the celebrated Swedish-Finnish artist Axel Gallén (1865-1931), who later changed his name in 1905 to the more Finnish sounding, Akseli Gallén-Kallela, together with the Anglo-Belgian ceramicist Alfred William Finch (1854-1930), a friend of the celebrated Art Nouveau designer Henry van de Velde (1863-1957), must be seen within the context of the strong nationalistic movements that were emerging in Scandinavian countries during the 1890s, which stressed a romantic involvement with nature and the historic past. In short, the founders of the Iris Workshops were not only interested in reforming design, and thereby public taste, but were also consciously attempting to forge an independent Finnish cultural identity. The Iris Workshops were in fact the very first Finnish enterprise to be set up with this specific intenreceived and fulfilled a number of important commissions from Finland and Russia. It also retailed its wares in St. Petersburg and through the two most important galleries in Paris for the dissemination of the New Art, Siegfried Bing's "L'Art Nouveau" and Julius Meier-Graefe's "La Maison Moderne" The Iris Workshops manufactured ceramics, textiles, metalwork, and furniture within the Arts & Crafts idiom - all of which was eminently suited as furnishings for the numerous fin-de-siècle Art Nouveau buildings being erected in Helsinki and its environs, the most notable of which were designed by the architectural practice of Eliel Saarinen, Herman Gesellius (1874-1916), and Armas Lindgren (1874-1929). From Finch's folk-style earthenware ceramics with their abstracted wavelike organic motifs to Sparre's elegant yet rustic

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/103036556800141ddc1df1f085d994fd0017386f10aab7bddbb373307b04a065.jpg)

template for the future direction of Scandinavian design, while also embodying a uniquely Finnish interpretation of the Art Nouveau style.

furniture, the workshops' output promoted an unpretentious aesthetic that was based on an honest expression of the materials being used - a characteristic that has remained a defining feature of Scandinavian design. As with the other well-intentioned design collectives in Britain and Germany, the Iris Workshops often based the design of its domestic wares on vernacular antecedents that had an inherent formal simplicity. Objects such as these, however, had little if any impact on the design of products intended for ordinary working class people. Indeed, Iris designs were almost entirely the preserve of the intellectual elite - the people who were not only the most likely to appreciate the philosophy underlying the objects but also those who were best able to afford their high cost. In 1900, the Iris Workshops exhibited a room installation designed by Gallén-Kallela at the Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale", which was awarded a gold medal. Although the Iris Workshops received much international acclaim, it became financially unsustainable and was forced to close in 1902. Though short-lived, the Iris Workshops' homely yet sophisticated products can be seen to have provided an important philosophical

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9619873cf4fb3cf70f08106f5815c7a1e7b467bd747eefe676f8ed37d434e01a.jpg)

Louis Sparre, design for a living room, 1903

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/040abcf2858e4fea51296a6b682be914d4aa4e5be12441b0e961814e419196d0.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/27ead829d848150b703e2732bc0ad99200e7f737d6fb1811df3f16b988cf0461.jpg)  
Maija Isola, Unikko (Popp textile for Marimekko, 1960

## maija isola(

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/69ef99e82fefeb21b86f391727c6bb35cc8215f48f852ddeeaf3603a422f8ce5.jpg)

The Finnish textile designer Maija Isola studied painting at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki from 1946 to 1949. Between 1949 and 1960, she was the principal designer for the Finnish textile firm Printex, which had been established by Viljo Ratia and was under the artistic directorship of Armi Ratia (1912-1979). During her time there, Isola designed numerous printed textiles for interior use that the company manufactured. From 1951, she also produced designs for Printex's sister company, Marimekko, which had been founded with the intention of promoting the use of Printex textiles in fashion and interior design. The inexpensive cotton sheeting used for Isola's designs was completely transformed by her bold abstracted patterns into elegant yet avant-garde textiles. Her designs for dress and furnishing fabrics for Marimekko, as well as her textiles for Printex, were included in the "Design in Scandinavia" exhibition that toured America and Canada from 1954 to 1957. In 1958, her work was included in the "Formes Scandinaves" exhibition in Paris and displayed at the Brussels' World's Fair. The following year, her textiles began to be retailed in the

United States, where they were admired for their startling originality. Isola's earliest designs were inspired by the African art she first saw on a trip to Norway in 1949. Initially, these were executed in brightly coloured crayons and later included hand-painted stripes in their patterns. By the mid-1950s, Isola was producing textiles decorated with floral motifs. She also designed fabrics influenced by Slovakian folk art in the late 1950s, and by traditional Karelian peasant motifs in the late 1960s and early 1970s. Many of her silk-screened textiles were translations of her own artwork. In the mid-1960s, she produced her most famous range of printed cotton textiles, which incorporated large-scale geometric patterns that were printed in strong flat colours. These bold designs such as Kaivo (1964), Melooni (1963), and Cock and Hen (1965), reflected contemporary artistic trends, most notably the influence of Colour Field painters in America. Isola's fabrics from this range had a strong graphic quality and came to epitomize not only Marimekko textiles but also a new direction in Finnish design. She received ID Prizes in 1965 and 1968, and during the later part of her life was partnered with Fujiwo Ishimoto (b. 1941), one of

#

the few in-house designers working at Marimekko. Isola's colour-saturated and elemental patterned fabrics, which have been widely exhibited throughout Europe, America and Australia, have had an enormous influence on contemporary textile design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/23fb37f09ddc8f91b41f6efd58e625e96a6c4e094a2ea3b1079a41f1d00a9cf7.jpg)  
Maija Isola, Lokki (Seagull) textile for Marimekko, 1961 Maija Isola, Melooni (Melon) textile for Marimekko, 1963

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1e65e8de364afb3ea1815ec5b0b0fc874be78eb1c5b96bf5bfd94a6eebd2ad82.jpg)

Maija Isola, Kaivo textile for Marimekko, 1964

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c2b7e979a565f36368fe58070942caed033d7e6ad11f368a3e6253a732be2e1f.jpg)

##

Arne Jacobsen, set of Model no. 3100 Myren (Ant) chairs for Fritz Hansen, 1952

Arne Jacobsen, Model no. 3107 - Series 7 chair for Fritz Hansen, 1955

## arne jacobsen

(b. 1902 Copenhagen - d. 1971 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/00e8d461ea2b24b70f6503222eeb6c47d8e1946573371db55dd04623115bf69c.jpg)

The most celebrated postwar Danish architect and designer, Arne Jacobsen initilly trained as a mason at the Skolen for Brugskunst (School of Applied Arts), Copenhagen, from where he graduated in 4. He went on to study architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) Copenhagen and graduated in 1927. This combination of practical and artistic training enabled Jacobsen to later produce buildings, interiors, and many different types of product designs that elegantly balanced function with aesthetics. Following the completion of his studies, he worked as an assistant in the City Architect's Office (Paul Holsøe) in Copenhagen until 1929. While there, he designed a music pavilion, shelters, and entrance gates for Enghave Park in Copenhagen. His early work was influenced by Modernists such as Le Corbusier (1887-1965), Erik Gunnar Asplund and Ludwig Mies van der Rohe (1886- 1969). Jacobsen became one of the first designers to introduce Modernism to Denmark through his projects such as the circular House of the Future, which featured all mod cons including a helicopter landing pad on the roof, and which he co-designed with Flemming Lassen (1902-1984) for the building and housing exhibition held by Copenhagen's Academic Architects' Association in 1929. For this project, Jacobsen also designed several pieces of tubular metal furniture that were aesthetically suited to their Modernist surroundings. Around 1930, he established his own design office in Hellerup and began practising independently as an architect and interior designer. One of his first important architectural commissions was the functionalist Rothenborg House, Ordrup (1930), which was conceived as a Gesamtkunstwerk (total work ar. Another important early project was the beach-fronted Bellavista apartment block with the adjoining Bellevue theatre and restaurant complex (1930-1934) in Copenhagen. The interior of the Bellevue Theatre was remarkable or its sweeing balcony and its wave-like rows of steam-bent plywood auditorium seats. In 1936, Jacobsen and Erik Moller (b. 1909) won a competition for the design of the Arhus Town Hall (1937-1942), a scheme that was distinguished by soft flowing lines that not only revealed a decidedly humanistic approach to Modernism, but also predicted the bold organic forms of Jacobsen's later projects. Aside from his architectural work, from 1945 onwards Jacobsen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/86806f0f78db68c7ace5e7b9f101dd34136f760ec8468308d8870e939f09775d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/19c646a3475dd901f95da47998487bef2375ba45f29b7df15d141f81971cfe2a.jpg)

Arne Jacobsen, Room 606 (The Jacobsen Suite) of the SAS Royal Hotel, Copenhagen, 1955-1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/41acce970dd4cab565662c337ed0832c1a01145bda3cb492f29ff023b90fb8ed.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2ff16532b2344d42fa6c709ae7d95008a955874dcfb2bb485c8ca9667de81f03.jpg)

Arne Jacobsen, entrance lobby of the SAS Royal Hotel, Copenhagen, 1955-1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/00ab53d363f7923a492fdbd0e8a38ae076de2a1f2a7c0478aa6050307fe6daed.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3e3d87c199ea8bdb19876930259bf3593f334ef1d03701419792e52e8a71ef14.jpg)  
Arne Jacobsen, Model no. 3316 Egg chair for Fritz Hansen, 1958  
Arne Jacobsen, Giraffen dining chair site-specific designed for the dining hall at the SAS Royal Hotel, 1958  
Arne Jacobsen, dining hall of the SAS Royal Hotel in Copenhagen with site-specific designed furniture and lighting, 1955-1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/15c6e9fbf016f828e5c9d2f4d70fec8c406819a46975618ef95f2e3427a1c8c2.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1c002346fba3193b8c43a4bc7ccb2e1c07ccd1c5de9d9d77c3389f99a4a2a1ca.jpg)

Arne Jacobsen, Cylinda Line stainless steel hollowware range for Stelton, 1967

Arne Jacobsen, Model no. 600 AJ stainless steel flatware for A. Michelsen, 1957 - later produced by Georg Jensen

also designed furniture for industrial mass production. His greatest successes in this field were his Ant chairs (1951-1952) and Series 7 chairs (1955), both designed for Fritz Hansen. The latter range, which remains among the most commercially successful seating programmes ever produced, comprised a standard single-form compoundmoulded plywood seat (with veneered or lacquered finish) onto which a wide range of options (different bases, arms and tablets) could be attached. Often Jacobsen's furniture and lighting designs were initially produced within the context of speciic projects and then later put into serial production. For his most holistically integrated project, the SAS Air Terminal and Royal Hotel, Copenhagen (1956-1960), Jacobsen designed every detail from textiles and light fittings tocutlery and fiue. The interiors of the SAS Royal Hotel, for which the famous Egg and Swan chairs (1957-1958) were specifically designed, were extraordinary for ther futuristic aesthetic. Apart from his furniture designs manufactured by Fritz Hansen, Jacobsen also designed the AJ range of lighting for Louis Poulsen (1955-1960), the Cylinda Line metalware collection for Stelton 1967), cutlery for

A. Michelsen, bathroom fittings for I.P. Lunds, and textiles for August Millech, Graucob Textilen and C. Olesen. From 1956 until 1965, he was a professor emeritus at the Skolen for Brugskunst in Copenhagen. During the 1960s, Jacobsen's most important architectural scheme was St. Catherine's College, Oxford - another wholly unified project for which he designed site-specific furniture (also manufactured by Fritz Hansen). Above all else, Jacobsen believed that it was essential for buildings and product designs to be well proportioned and that materials and colours should be compatible so that the optimum overall mpression could be achieved. Jacobsen combined sculptural and organic forms with traditional Scandinavian design attributes - material and structural integrity - to produce elegant, essentialist and functional designs that have a remarkable, timeless appeal. He summed up his concern for the appearance of products by saying: "Pastry usually tastes best when it looks nice  there's nothing I mind if it looks nice."

##

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f12b96cf4aea88bc1648cac306c9f4eb96ac373df95cd454589cc8133eacb184.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/29e9785e18d6fda95e22e72061fb000fb5f3200e4f8cb300b16367616e8682d9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/762f9e3e7ca10a41747cac99cd00bfbfd60f30466eeeac3f1bc5ed55ade9cc76.jpg)  
Jacob Jacobsen, Luxo L-1 task lamp for Luxo, 1937 - an early example  
Jacob Jacobsen, chromed Luxo L-1 lamp for Luxo, 1937

# jacob jacobsen (b 1901 Oslo -d. 1996 Oslo, Norway)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b5b28dbeb116351703454e7141e12e194efae2a13ab0f3131478b7ea0686533f.jpg)

The Norwegian lighting designer Jacob Jacobsen was an engineer who initially trained in the textile manufacturing industry in England and Switzerland. In 1937, he acquired the Scandinavian production rights to the Anglepoise lamp (1934), which was designed by George Carwardine (1887-1948) and manufactured by Herbert Terry & Sons in Britain. During the same year, Jacobsen designed a variation of this lamp, the LuxoL, which incorporated a similar auto-balancing system of springs. As a talented entrepreneur, Jacobsen successfully commercialized his re-designed lamp, which had a more refined connection between the shade and stand and a softer aesthetic than Carwardine's. The lamp not only exemplified the influence of functionalism in Norwegian design during the 1930s, but also predicted the soft-edged aesthetic of postwar Scandinavian Modernism. Within a few years, Jacobsen also acquired the United States production rights to the Anglepoise's constant tension principle and during the 1940s he had a virtual monopoly on the sale of task lamps in both Europe and America. His manufacturing company, Luxo, continues to produce the classic L-1 lamp, which is notable for the ease with which its arm

and shade can be articulated. There are several variations of the design, including one with an enlarged shade known as the Panoramic. The Luxo L-1 has won numerous awards and is in the permanent collection of the Museum of Modern Art. Although Jacobsen's lamp has been widely imitated over the years, its technical performance has rarely been equalled. Norway's most notable contributions to our material culture have included the universally available paperclip and the humble yet useful cheese slicer - both forms of which were derived entirely through their function. Jacobsen's Luxo L-1 shares a similar utilitarian vocabulary of form and functionality and as such has enjoyed enormous commercial success - over 25 million have been sold to date.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9b1724c2f19eac021bdb1f91525b69389eb75bd3bcd434200ea6483bb8e74b2c.jpg)

Grete Jalk, teak-faced laminated lounge chair and set of tables for Poul Jeppesen, 1963

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f5046235752be142fa10ab0bc91e122b5798753b0d1d433869e8f1789090ec69.jpg)

## grete jalk

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1debab0bcd7615dcbbef67de463fbe404cbfc7cd3ffdbca60e2e82d4dd903a8c.jpg)

The Danish furniture designer Grete Jalk apprenticed as a joiner at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts), Copenhagen, from 1940 to 1943, and then trained as a furniture designer under the guidance of Kaare Klint at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, from where she graduated in 1946. As one of Klint's students, Jalk would have studied the "ideal" forms of historic furniture types, such as the Windsor chair, undertaken research into anthropometrics, and been taught to combine traditional craft skills with rational design principles. In 1946 she was awarded first prize in the annual Cabinetmaker's Competition in Copenhagen and five years later her furniture designs were exhibited at the 1951 (IX) Milan Triennale. In 1953 she won the Georg Jensen competition, and the following year established her own design practice. As a prominent member of the Danish design community, she was co-editor of the Danish design journal Mobilia (1956-1962 & 1968-1974) and received widespread recognition for her beautifully designed furniture, which was produced by Henning Jensen, Fritz Hansen, France & Son, Søren Horn, and Poul Jeppesen. The majority of

Jalk's designs, such as her rosewood and leather armchair of 1956 and her mahogany and leather stool of 1961, were modern re-workings of traditional furniture types but were nonetheless notable for their interesting "hidden" joins and subtle curves. Her most celebrated design, however, the two-piece moulded-plywood chair of 1963, had a completely innovative form and speculated on the possibility of industrial mass production. Though beautiful and comfortable and awarded first prize in a competition sponsored by the Daily Mirror newspaper in the UK, the chair was never produced in large numbers. This perhaps had less to do with the way the small radii of its curved surfaces led to the material and bending process being pushed to the limit than with the fact that by the mid-1960s plywood was beginning to fall out of fashion with manufacturers. During the 1970s and 1980s, Jalk was a member of the jury for the prestigious ID Prize and organized several travelling exhibitions for the Danish Ministry of Foreign Affairs. As a leading commentator on Danish furniture design, she also edited the book Dansk möbelkunst gennem 40 aar (Fourty Years of Danish Furniture Design), which was published in

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b3ca9a206de915813b6d65eb2514e711a2401ee7bb985340107bf0585859b95c.jpg)  
Grete Jalk, rosewood and leather easy chair for France & Son, c. 1960  
Grete Jalk, Watch and Listen furniture group executed in Oregon pine by the cabinetmaker J. H. Johansen, 1963

1987. The pervasive ethos of social inclusiveness in Scandinavia has ensured that more women designers, such as Grete Jalk, have risen to prominence than elsewhere. This long tradition of gender equality perhaps also accounts for the particular focus within Scandinavian industrial design on products intended for the domestic environment, and the general disinterest in creating bravado design statements that have a high visual impact yet little practical function.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/eba8b512d525363d0c70c6c6acbc2b6e800a6ba1208d19305e6cb0cdfdc831d0.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8c24a8b3b203124eb1affae6fac988761ea26621995e05e67919f7c9cfa8440c.jpg)  
Georg Jensen, Model 263 silver bowl for Georg Jensen, 1918

# georg jensen

## (b. 1866 Rådvad - d. 1935 Hellerup, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dce10c0d7c7f630abbbda9f093fc747e9478f284d3f0ef0b340de330ff09624c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/727a2d75e938dd0b0d21e568678835b8872ba67d987ce6551702c038eb485d8b.jpg)

The Georg Jensen name refers not only to a man and his firm, but to a style that throughout the 20th century has epitomized Scandinavian design. Georg Jensen, the designer, was born on the outskirts of Copenhagen in the small industrial town of Radvad, which had both a steelworks and a water-driven grindery where his father worked as a blacksmith. As a young child he worked in the foundry and became an apprentice in the braziers'workshop. At the age of urteen, his parents recognized his metalworking ability and decided to move to Copenhagen where he served an apprenticeship as a goldsmith. From 1884 he worked as a journeyman, but he was not content with the prospect of remaining just a highly-skilled craftsman. Consequently from c. 1895 to 1901 he trained as a sculptor at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) where he was influenced by the designer C.C. Peters (1822-1899), who worked in the Neo-Classical style. During this period, Jensen produced sculptural ceramics in collaboration with the painter Joachim Petersen (1870-1943) for the Mogens Ballin workshop, which was just outside of

Copenhagen. After completing his studies, and unable to earn a living as a sculptor, Jensen turned his attentions to design. He began making ceramics with Christian Joachim for the Aluminia pottery and later became a designer at the Bing & Grøndahl porcelain factory in Copenhagen. In 1904, he established his own silversmithy and, rebuking the contemporary taste for historicist metalware, he began designing Arts & Crafts style jewellery and silverware that was influenced by natural forms, such as berries, leaves and swirling tendrils. Jensen's most notable designs were the Blossom (c. 1904-1905) and Grape (. 1918) ranges, which comprised a variety of pieces from tableware to jewellery. Around 1906, he persuaded the artist Johan Rohde (1856— 1935) to produce a number of designs for the workshop and these included the well-known Acorn pattern (1915) that epitomized the transition from the Arts & Crafts style to Art Deco - from naturalistic ornamentation to a more stylized and abstracted form of decoration. When the company exhibited at the 1915 San Francisco "Panama-Pacific International Exposition", the newspaper baron William Randolph Hearst was

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e8f72ba7f37473b02e856558bcd5e6b0257138ffdd127afdad8e14f3a1a8bf63.jpg)

Georg Jensen's first workshop in Bredgade, Copenhagen, 1908 - Georg Jensen is standing behind Miss Alba Lykke (later Mrs Just Andersen) and seated on the far left is Kay Bojesen

Georg Jensen, Model no. 22 amber and agate brooch for Georg Jensen, 1904-1905

OVERLEAF Georg Jensen, Model no. 407A silver pitcher for Georg Jensen, 1925

Georg Jensen, Model no. 10 silver and amethyst potpourri jar for Georg Jensen, 1910

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c7174de887040f5a906cefb526bb915a15ac873b0a1910ed1d346ad90d329ef8.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/24d8217b44d11f562ea50cdcc5f7f9037cfb546570e3bda1dd8d5068c8b7f99e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7a726db58261d65324bfaa0fb2086b0b65211b1fe534e792c48a767580c724e3.jpg)  
Georg Jensen, Model no. 324 silver candelabrum for Georg Jensen, 1919  
> Henning Koppel, Model no. 992 silver pitcher for Georg Jensen, 1952

so impressed with the quality of the work on show that he purchased their complete inventory. Eventually the company became so successful that in 1924 they opened a showroom on Fifth Avenue, New York. During the 1920s, Jensen recruited other avant-garde designers, such as Harald Nielsen, Gundorph Albertus (1887- 1970), and Sigvard Bernadotte to produce designs for the company. At the same time, Rohde introduced unornamented modern forms t the firm's line of silverware and in o doing heralded a new direction for the silversmithy. Upon Jensen's death in 1935, the running of the company was taken over by his son Jørgen Jensen (1895-1966), who perpetuated its forward-looking ethos. After the Second World War, the company began producing supremely elegant Modern handcrafted silverware designed by

Henning Koppel and Tias Eckhoff, which exemplified the functional and aesthetic purity of Scandinavian Modernism. During the post-war years, the firm also began manufacturing glassware by Finn Juhl and stainless steel products by Arne Jacobsen, such as his flatware of 1957 original manufactured by A. Michelsen). Throughout the 1960s and 1970s, the company produced a number of beautiful and sculptural jewellery and wristwatch designs by Nanna Ditzel and Vivianna Bülow-Hübe that were in tune with the more casual lifestyle of the period. In 1973, Royal Copenhagen purchased the Georg Jensen brand and since the late 1970s the silversmithy has produced gold and silver jewellery, elegant watches, and stainless steel and silver tableware, which either incorporate the company's "trademark" undulating organic forms or express a classical pared-down aesthetic. In 1997 Georg Jensen became part of the Royal Scandinavia group, which also owns Royal Copenhagen, Holmegaard, Orrefors, Kosta Boda, Boda Nova, and Höganäs Keramik brands. Today, the Georg Jensen company continues to uphold its long-held commitment to innovative design, superlative craftsmanship, and high-quality manufacturing standards - attributes that have ensured that its name has remained utterly synonymous with Danish design excellence.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8493bf065ab03056b9f72c6417657295ff99f45df302fcb150e1cdfadf07b775.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/745a047a02a313ceb37d97eba5b5f940a6e17dfda0204e18ae5c199f4ddd7bf4.jpg)  
Jacob Jensen, Beosystem 1200 record player for Bang & Olufsen, 1969

# jacob jensen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7b99dfd51278926f93731d1d43365ca26b2852c3cb2d5961aa19040e674199dc.jpg)

The Danish product designer Jacob Jensen studied industrial design at the Kunsthandværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts), Copenhagen, where he graduated in 1952. Between 1952 and 1959, he was the chief designer for the first Danish industrial design consultancy, which had been founded in 1949 by Sigvard Bernadotte and Acton Bjørn. From 1959 to 1961 Jensen lived in the USA, where he established a design practice with, among others, Richard Latham (b. 1920). On returning to Copenhagen in 1961, Jensen founded his own industrial design practice, and three years later began designing audio equipment for Bang & Olufsen. In response to the company's mission to "create a European Hi-Fi format which communicates power, precision and identity" Jensen and his assistant David Lewis (b. 1939) introduced the use of linear dial controls to its products that were modelled on a slide-rule and communicated the impression of absolute precion. With e pe carhe outs and extruded anodized aluminium casings, Jensen's sleek, high-performance designs set the aesthetic and technological standards for audio systems. His Beogram 1200 hi-fi system (1969)

was awarded a Danish ID Award for its harmonious balance between "apparatus" and "furniture", while his Beogram 4000 record player (1972) was notable for incorporating a tangential pick-up arm. Although best known for his pioneering designs for Bang & Olufsen, Jensen has also designed products for other Danish and European manufacturers, including the E76 pushbutton telephone orAlcatelKirk, aoffice chair r Labofa, ultrasound scanning equipment (1982) for Bruel & Kjær, and a wristwatch (1988) for Georg Jensen. One of the most famous Scandinavian industrial designers, Jensen has received over one hundred international awards for his innovative work. Believing that, "Design is a language understood by everyone", Jacob Jensen's revolutionary products, which synthesize functionalism and elegance within a simple yet classical vocabulary of form, have changed forever our ideas of how everyday objects can be experienced. Since 1989, Jensen's son Timothy has directed the Jacob Jensen Design Studio.

Jacob Jensen, Jacob Jensen indoor thermometer for Bell Xpress A/S, 1990s

Jacob Jensen, Beogram 4000 record player for Bang & Olufsen, 1972

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e5b4b9f66c855435ec9d76c91d9e7ae9bab58836a8ecaa1344028003056b48be.jpg)

# gocken jobs & lisbet jobs

(Swedish 1914-1995)

(Swedish b. 1919-1961)

From the late 1930s, the Jobs' family studio produced hand-printed textiles and hand-painted ceramics that epitomised the idea of Swedishness. Based in Leksand, the studio was located in the beautiful province of Dalarna - the mystic heart of Sweden, with its Falun-red houses and picturesque lakes. Here Lisbet Jobs-Söderlund decorated glazed earthenware plates and bowls with freely painted representations of wild flowers found in the surrounding countryside. These painterly designs had a spontaneity and liveliness that reflected the spiritual reverence for the natural world of Sweden, where the annual midsummer maypole festivals reveal the nation's almost paganistic love of nature. Lisbet's older sister, Gocken, also incorporated motifs of wild flowers - wild arum lilies, ferns, buttercups, dandelions, lilies-of-thevalley - and insects into her silk-screen printed textiles that embodied an unprepossessing informality and fresh originality. Certainly, Gocken Jobs' textiles were endowed with an enchanting lyricism and a delight in patterns - key distinguishing features of later Swedish textile design. Although many of Gocken's textiles were handprinted in the family's studio, other designs,

such as Trollslända (Dragonfly) of 1945, were produced by Ljungbergs Textiltryck in Dala-Floda. Significantly, Gocken's richly detailed yet unpretentious floral textiles brought a touch of much-needed colour into Swedish interiors, which had been dominated by the subdued Gustavian palette of pale blues and grey-whites. One of Gocken's most interesting designs is a textile entitled Gobelin - a pun on her name and a reference to the famous Parisian tapestry factory. This design is essentially a celebration of Swedishness with its Gustavianstyle furnishings and dancing girls - it even includes a depiction of the famous Dala wooden horse. Hand screen-printed in the Jobs' studio, the textile was presumably intended for nursery use. The almost child-like innocence of its design reflects the strong influence of Swedish folk art, and also refers to the cultural importance of children in Scandinavian society. Today the Jobs family still produce beautiful screen-printed textiles in their Leksand-based workshop.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a929e00183c664a01f272ef3a3b6eb078a93f3a74d4c483abb5065499030d7a9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f80b7061ddde90f52ad30db8e17c682e0e16ccbeb38ba10bc0df6b6618111d02.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dc0fa42df67afb9cc041ddcfa62f0e4e4debb576b95c34ad4debaaff35158186.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/839b1cf1ec159fef59c974512591e0c6f72c11cf9c912260247a94d13bba8458.jpg)  
Willy Johansson, Atlantic vases for Hadeland, 1970

# willy johansson

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/243611a0ff487a37b6c8d5e0b2a7ecc65cc3bc5bc09128210c0ffd5642a89927.jpg)

Willy Johansson's grandfather came from the Swedish region of Smland, which was renowned for its production f high-qualityglassware, while his father was a senior glassblower at the Hadeland glassworks. In 1936, Johansson followed in his father's footsteps when he began working in Hadeland's glassmaking workshop. He later undertook formal artistic training at the Statens Handverks- og Kunstindustriskole (National College of Art and Design) in Oslo from 1939 to 1942. After completing his studies, he returned to Hadeland and from 1942 to 1945 served an apprenticeship to the sculptor Stale Kyllingstad (1903-1987) the firm's sandblasting section. Johansson also designed glassware with decorative engraving for Christiania Glasmagasin from 1945 to 1947. He replaced Kyllingstad as head of the design team at Hadeland in 1947 and in so doing, became responsible for the design of both free-blown art glass and pressed-glass production wares. In 1954, Norway exhibited at the (X) Milan Triennale for the first time and Johansson was awarded a Diplôme d'Honneur for his glass designs, which included his well-known sandblasted vases that were blown into wooden moulds and had contrasti coloured cores"Tree years ater, he ed a gold medal at the 1957 (XI) Milan Triennale for a group of clear coloured dishes that were exhibited in an "aerial" framework display specially designed by Arne Korsmo. The colour of these remarkable free-blown designs subtly intensified towards their outer edges, which were contained by Johansson's "trademark" opaque white rims. Like these large-scale designs, Johansson's bluegreen vase (1958) and his Medoc decanter (1961) reflected his pursuit of simple forms and perfect proportions, which provided his work with a quiet monumentality. While Johansson's clear and smoked glass designs did not possess the bravado and expressive spontaneity of Italian glass, they did have an underlying integrity that was quintessentially Scandinavian.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/601ab81860ee3eab9f5b8896709979ff3a8fbcf3da0941df72a362f37447c136.jpg)  
Willy Johansson, free-blown vase with coloured core for Hadeland, 1963  
Willy Johansson, Svalbard glassware range (bowls, decanter and glasses) for Hadeland, 1977

Willy Johansson, Medoc glassware range for Hadeland, 1961

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0d3a8906b52c81e8bac1255a80a26e5c4a7af59266419a52b7faf5049a58ca22.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a8930b472e134eb46d480292ed6fe35fd8f21af7c06ddd373dddc3e14d980477.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/22402df96802c849217548b8b929d771def2d315ba2ff8491569a38d23a69d24.jpg)  
Lisa Johansson-Pape, chair for Stockmann, 1930-1933  
Lisa Johansson-Pape, hanging lamp for Stockmann-Orno, 1954

# lisa johansson-pape

The Finnish designer, Lisa Johansson-Pape, trained at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design), Helsinki, graduating in 1927. From 1928 to 1930 she designed furniture for Kymäkoski and from 1928 to 1937 she designed textiles for the FFH (Friends of Finnish Handicraft), becoming its artistic director in 1952. Johansson-Pape also worked as a furniture, textile and interior designer from 1937 to 1949 for the well-known Stockmann department store in Helsinki. Her furniture designs, such as the wood and lacquered armchair (c. 1935) upholstered in a textile designed with Greta Skogster-Lehtinen, were distinguished by graceful and simple forms that belied both the stability and strength of their constructions. From around 1947 onwards, she designed numerous electric light fittings for Stockmann's sister company, Stockmann-Orno, that were similarly rational in form and construction. These included her turquoise-enamelled sheet-metal hanging lamp of 1947, which was delicately pierced with holes that allowed the light to shine through, and her later more sculptural sandblasted and etched glass hanging lamp of 1954, which was produced by littala for Stockmann-Orno. In the mid-1950s,

Johansson-Pape designed a number of light fittings with enamelled aluminium shades that were sectioned in such a way as to diffuse the light as effectively as possible. Among these was the brass-coloured Orno pendant lamp (c. 1957), which stylistically predicted the more utilitarian approach to lighting design in Scandinavia during the 1970s. Her revolutionary metal and acrylic lighting designs were awarded medals at the 1951 (IX), 1954 (X) and 1960 (XIl) Milan Triennale exhibitions, and their influence on later lighting designers can not be understated. As Johansson-Pape noted, "When we started out, there was just wood, yarn and clay, but soon there was a flood of materials .. Industry gave us unlimited scope for experimentation with different colours and materials." In common with many Finnish designers, Johansson-Pape enjoyed exploring the formal potential offered by new materials and industrial techniques, while developing simple yet striking forms that were very graphic in outline.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/eef9d6167685df4fa554f4852d41332085873bb9b922e78dd9f14fafaec15dfb.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c0b5217ace517a75e389b083e3a9772a241f0e300fcc084c61e369be40e179bd.jpg)  
Finn Juhl, Model no. NV-45 asy chair or Niels Voder 1945 - this elegant design was offered in a variety of woods: rosewood, mahogany, teak and walnut

## finn juhl Coehagen Cpehagn, D

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/865247a749c548ef0d3c8cd5777414d3164474d575be0dd9c73db96b324a3130.jpg)

When he was eighteen years old, Finn Juhl visited the landmark 1930 Stockholm exhibition and was greatly impressed by the steel and glass buildings designed by the grand master of Swedish Modernism, Erik Gunnar Asplund. That same year, he enrolled at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, where he studied architecture under Kay Fisker, who was renowned for his riveting lectures. In 1934, Juhl began working as an architect in the office of Vilhelm Lauritzen (1894- 1984), who was also a prominent teacher at the academy. Although originally intended as only a summer job, Juhl remained in Lauritzen's office for eleven years and never actually graduated from the academy. He was, however, accepted as a member of the Federation of Danish Architects in 1942. During this period, he also designed several pieces of furniture that were first shown at the 1937 Copenhagen Cabinetmakers Guild exhibition. Many of his pieces, which were designed in collaboration with the master cabinetmaker Niels Voder, were remarkable for the sheer virusiy o their construction. Juh's furniture, such as the Pelican chair (1940), differed significantly from

the modern re-workings of traditional furniture types, such as those promoted by Kaare Klint and his followers, and signalled a new and important direction in Danish design towards more sculptural and abstracted organic forms. In 1945, Juhl set up his own practice and began designing solid wood chairs, tables and sofas within this visually seductive vocabulary of form, the results of which brought him much acclaim - he was awarded six gold medals at Milan Triennale exhibitions and fourteen prizes by the Copenhagen Cabinetmakers Guild. His work, such as the No. 45 chair (1945), the No. 48 chair (1948) and the famous Chieftain chair (1949), was distinguished by superlative craftsmanship and highly expressive, almost floating forms. Some of the elements of his furniture, such as the back of the Model no. 48 chair, reflected the influence of contemporary abstract sculptors such as Alexander Calder (1898-1976) and Hans Arp (1887-1966). Juhl also developed numerous constructional techniques specifically for the use of teak, which led to the material's widespread use in Danish furniture design and ushered in what became known as the Teak Style". From 1945,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/843546431a1717c1e92c8cce285331c4bc090f59d20940565b89f8e8b7899e60.jpg)

Juhl was the senior instructor at the School of Interior Design in Copenhagen and in this position he played a key role in influencing the course of Danish design. Juhl's exclusive, mainly handcrafted furniture was adapted for large-scale manufacture in America by Baker Furniture in the early 1950s. Beyond furniture, however, Juhl also designed exhibitions, interiors, wooden bowls for the firm of Kay Bojesen, appliances for General Electric, carpets for Unika-Væv, ceramics for Bing & Grondahl, and glassware for Georg Jensen. Described by the architectural critic Henrik Sten Moller as "the out-and-out dandy", Finn Juhl exerted much influence on the following generation of Scandinavian designers with his use of bold sculptural forms and ultra-refined detailing.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0a0679025dc86bb132dcaf79e91999da8c42297eff7460a4013ff0ad1590bc71.jpg)

Finn Juhl, upholstered sofa for Niels Vodder, 1939 - a very early example of the use of sculptural organic forms in Danish furniture design

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9b1ff6c66ca86fdec8569221a60a3fc624fb57e7a1218124f3a46e57b7fcb515.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7a0da10266806fd141cf4bbdfc57a01974913cdccae0bfc601129cf27be28c6b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8e2ecc6513e743f8e0e7c4471153e1974938455ea929f1c5d5d72156912dcae5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2eb2e1da80a212646755a054fcaef4eccb433d2a26a482d7f603af96042ee048.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7813faa01a223770106843ec6f58b5ed8fa2b54619945d05436f0a7598cf23bb.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/36371c59b2286224fabdcf5a70b2e50476cf60c55115a3038a7515b53f207eae.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e635a7227930d19fbb1e7f3d3d50099424bf8165900b9da3ac861ceb79b38bf3.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ff2bc3f3c7eb3a1443db41e6ef852d896cc1253cbb0b3aac6a8c4e2b1101cfa6.jpg)  
Dora Jung, Viol damask tablecloth for Tampella, C. 1968

# dora jung

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5dd68dad2d715935a5439adf81eb9dc2e14a87ddb411b47d48bf26993279db4b.jpg)

The Finnish textile designer Dora Jung trained at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design), Helsinki, and later in 1932, established her own studio in Helsinki. In 1951 she exhibited her textile designs at the IX Milan Triennale, where she was awarded a Grand Prix. She became especially well known for her work in woven damask - a material that had long been associated with naturalistic flower patterns, especially chrysanthemums. Jung, however, preferred the use of abstracted and stylized floral motifs and subtle geometric patterns while employing a restricted range of earth tones - olive, copper, grey, dusky yellow and natural white. Her wellknown Hundred Roses textile, for instance, incorporated an informal yet dense scattering of Charles Rennie Mackintosh-style roses over its entire surface, which produced a completely new and contemporary aesthetic for woven damask. In 1957, she was awarded a Grand Prix at the XI Milan Triennale for her damask design known as Linen Play, which with its strong geometric patterning of intersecting "hand-drawn" lines signalled a new and more casual approach to the design of table linen. While Jung's table linens were mass produced by Tampella of Finland, she also became renowned for her one-off wall hangings and church textiles, which highlighted her mastery of complicated weaving techniques. She also occasionally used to dramatic effect a technique known in Norway as smettevev, which combined coarse damask weaving with areas of hand-stitched colour. For her tapestries Jung would also use the weft to produce an almost three-dimensional effect. She often developed her own weaving techniques which gave her, as Eileene Harrison Beer noted, "the deepest possible insight into her craft" and ensured that her textiles achieved the highest level of artistic and manufacturing excellence. She also experimented with unusual materials - some of her upholstering fabrics, for example, were woven from threads of paper. Jung's innovative textiles exemplified the high technical standards common to both the handicrafts and serial production in Scandinavia, while her simple abstracted patterns typified the bold graphic quality that is so characterist Finnish textile design.

Dora Jung, Kyyhkysiä (Doves) woven hanging exected by the Dora Jung Studio, 1951

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7bb7ec7306eeea4ac352ea1e8cb67d9656ebc80defe84c44aa154a22b1f119fb.jpg)

Wilhelm Kage, Lilebla Blue Lily) dinner service for Gustavsberg, 1917 - This service was so affordable that it became known as the Arbetarservisen (Workers) Service)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c30272ccfc1818d7fa9f8eabf428ae596d1df6f6051a5f23636a7b29199b0e87.jpg)

Wilhelm Kàge, green glazed vase with silver overlay decoration for Gustavsberg, 1930 - This design reveals the strong influence of the Art Deco style on Scandinavian design during the 1930s

## wilhelm kage

## (b. 1889 Stockholm - d. 1960 Stockholm, Sweden)

One of the great Swedish design reformers, Wilhelm Káge initially studied painting at Valand Art School in Gothenburg. He also trained under Carl Wilhelmsson in Stockholm and with the Danish designer Johan Rohde in Copenhagen before turning his attention to graphic design at the famous Plakatschule (School of Posters) in Munich. After the completion fhis academic studies, Kage gained recognition as a poster designer before being recruited by Gustavsberg in 1917. This appointment can be seen as a direct result of the Svenska Slöjdföreningen (Swedish Society of Craft and Industrial Design) campaign to persuade manufacturers to employ artists so as to improve the design of everyday objects. Kåge's subsequent Liljeblà (Blue Lily) dinner service (1917) with its folk-inspired decoration was certainly an embodiment of the Society's catchphrase Vackrare vardagsvara (better everyday goods or more beautiful things for everyday use) and was so affordable that it became known as the "Workers' Service" The 1930 Stockholm Exhibition marked a watershed in Swedish design and heralded the new Funkis movement that had been inspired by the pioneering work emanating from the Bauhaus and by

Modern Movement architects/designers such as Walter Gropius (1883-1969) and Le Corbusier (1887-1965). For this landmark exhibition, Kåge designed the suitably functional Pyro range, which venoabl i  Gste His later Praktika earthenware service (1933), however, was a far more revolutionary design. But while it was extremely practical with its stackable, graded-sized elements, Praktika was not a commercial success - the result, no doubt of its severe utilitarian aesthetic. Design critics at the time nevertheless deemed Praktika "the" functionalist service of the era. It was also the first range of Gustavsberg tableware to be sold as "open stock" in shops (i. e. individual pieces could be purchased separately rather than as a complete service) thereby making it more accessible to those on lower incomes. This concern for social inclusiveness was (and still s) a major driving force within Scandinavian society, and historically has manifested itself most noticeably in Swedish design. In the late 1930s, Kåge adopted a less austere aesthetic with his Grá ränder (Grey stripes) service (1939). With its suble organic forms and simple grey sipe pattern, Gra ränder was a significant departure

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2692bed3cc30e027ed06ea59979043ed581c9780c73661c170579a7524f60932.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/43d539f27beb5173e12d380bb312277008fb73e34584ac09628afe7eeeb91f6b.jpg)

Wilhelm Kåge, Grå ränder (Grey stripes) service for Gustavsberg, 1937

Wilhelm Káge, Vága white stoneware vase and bowls for Gustavsberg, c. 1950

Wilhelm Kåge, Praktika earthenware service for Gustavsberg (top: decorated, bottom: plain), 1933

OVERLEAF Wilhelm Káge, Surrea vase for Gustavsberg, 1940

Wilhelm Káge, Terra Spirea stoneware vase for Gustavsberg, c. 1955

from his more austere designs and sold extremely well for more than twenty years. Like many other Scandinavian designers, Kåge also designed exquisite studio ceramics, including stoneware objects such as his Terra Spirea (c. 1955) and his well-known moulded stoneware Farsta vessels that were inspired by natural forms such as seed pods and by Chinese and Mexican ceramics. He also designed a range of pottery known as Surrea, which with its fragmented forms revealed the influence of Cubism and Surrealism. While Kage's studio pottery displayed his unquestionable artistic and craft skills, his designs for industrial production were of far greater importance, above all because they predicted the basic functional shapes that have come to dominate large-scale ceramics manufacture. From his folk-inspired Liljebla service to the utilitarian Praktika range to the Gra ränder, Kåge's work powerfully reflected, if not led, the changes of direction that occurred in Swedish design during the first half of the 20th century - from Arts & Crafts to Functionalism to Organic Modernism.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9970ce7c69969aadd0bd1c689ac6686d402797e0d1233a158ed9cafa4b8f5acc.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/cd6a11af99ee6f01681ae56d40d2095eeb94eed431897805335686bfee70e317.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1a1dacb7f51fedf800c1dc46f6502c4351974eab7929ebc47eef6255dfe04650.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/58b572f057888bb5519eb636700a178522c7dff23e5f1feecc31a13b6e025752.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/154add87909d10d260bd7b1f3ba68a3ce28e364dc62ef2009e7c5ebd0ad10309.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/aeaeb869566194823673577dbc73660ddfc981af6f1c830ef435e3624f1356b4.jpg)  
Birger Kaipiainen, Paratisi Paradise) service for Aaba, 1969

# birger kaipiainen

## (b. 1915 Pori - d. 1988 Helsinki, Finland)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bd3e818c13b14e6baa14ca9f5392f5eabeb5fc8396b70005bc5258c60354ffb6.jpg)

One of Finland's best-loved ceramicists, Birger Kaipiainen trained at the Konstindustriella Läroverket (School of Arts and Crafts) in Helsinki before joining the Arabia ceramics factory in 1937. Working in the company's art department, he decorated ceramics with colour-saturated, Byzantine and Italian Renaissance inspired motifs, which often included stylized depictions of flora and fauna - from pansies to peacocks. From 1954 to 1958, Kaipiainen designed ceramics for Rörstrand, but he returned to Arabia in 1958 and remained there until his death in 1988. Although he produced designs almost exclusively for small-scale studio production, such as his glazed earthenware charger (c. 1964), he did design one range of tableware specifically for mass production - Paratisi (1969). This well-known service with its soft functional forms adorned with bold motifs of ripe fruits and blooming flowers has withstood the fickleness of changing taste and fashion by appealing directly to the timeless Scandinavian joy in the colour and bounty of nature. Referred to by his contemporaries as "the prince of ceramics" and "the king of decoration", Kaipiainen was awarded a Diplôme d'Honneur at the 1951 (IX) Milan Triennale and

later a Grand Prix at the 1960 (XIl) Milan Triennale for his surrealistic sculptural ceramic birds, which were made with steel frames covered with strings o glazed hand-painted pottery beads. By the end of the 1960s his work was becoming increasingly three-dimensional and monumental, as demonstrated by his extraordinary Sea of Violets installation at the Expo '67 in Montreal with its giant blue ceramic flowers. Kaipiainen's idiosyncratic style brought him widespread recognition and many awards, such as the Pro Finlandia Medal in 1963 and the Prince Eugen Medal in 1982. In 1977 he was also awarded the honorary title of professor and in 1987, a year before his death, his Paratisi tableware range was re-introduced by Arabia (a black and white version was introduced in 2000). Above all else, Kaipiainen's richly imaginative pottery embodied the pantheistic love of nature that is so much a part of Scandinavian culture, while providing a brilliant glimpse into his own private fairytale paradise that was inhabited by giant flowers and exotic birds.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c272bbca1d3848628195e61777131b30a492ee5cda3a7170fced0971b616157b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/356209f4a9318fe0fd4b2074b4de1ad962a4ef20a5565a8f6c3487b963c2816f.jpg)  
Birger Kaipiainen, glazed earthenware charger for Arabia, c. 1964  
Birger Kaipiainen, glazed earthenware platter for Arabia, 1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/69c615bd95044a30ccf701f347bde1b7e64f7784d08322f724b8f1c758ff4d99.jpg)  
Mats Theselius, Chaise Longue for Källemo, 1992 - limited edition of 50

## källemo

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f93e785cd73df2113bbf527c2b5ca6d5a69786ffa65dc91582bd9b045136e8d5.jpg)

The origins of Källemo AB can be traced to a small furniture manufacturing company that was acquired in 1965 by Sven Lundh (b. 1925), who sought to produce interesting and exciting furniture made to exacting standards. Unlike IKEA, which offered a wide range of home furnishing items of good design and function, at prices so low the majority of people could afford them", Källemo took an alternative approach that centred on creative artists and designers producing expressive objects in small runs. Rejecting what he saw as the banality and blandness of mass-produced furniture, Lundh wanted to manufacture products that radiated a human warmth so that their users could establish a more meaningful emotional connection with them, in much the same way as works of art are supposed to function. Influenced by the work of American artists, such as Robert Rauschenberg (b. 1925) and Jasper Johns (b. 1930), and the "ready-mades" of Marcel Duchamp (1887-1968), Lundh attempted to bring some of the qualities of contemporary fine ar to the design of functional objects. Eventually in 1982 he found an object that met his aesthetic and practical ideals - Jonas Bohlin's controversial and iconoclastic Concrete chair (1981).

Källemo immediately produced a batch edition of this landmark Post-Modern design and so ushered in a completely new era of furniture design and manufacture in Sweden. Bolstered by the success of the Concrete chair, Källemo subsequently began working with other young up-and-coming designers and artists, such as Mats Theselius (b. 1956), Ernst Billgren (b. 1957), Rolf Hansson (b. 1953) and Fredrik Wretman (b. 1958), who created highly individualistic products in a diverse range of styles. One of the most widely published of these furniture pieces was Theselius' National Geographic Magazine bookcase (1990), which not only invoked the global cultural symbolism of the magazine but also provided enough storage space for 25 years' worth of editions. As one of the greatest protagonists of Scandinavian Post-Modernism and the driving force behind Källemo, Lundh has stated, "It cannot be dangerous for man to have a creative nature. The only danger is that one becomes noncommercial. The danger of society becoming less commercial is that it becomes more human."

Harri Koskinen, Pick Up aluminium shelves for Källemo, 1999

Mats Theselius, National Geographic Magazine bookcases for Källemo, 1990

Sigurdur Gustafsson, Take Off stainless steel and hemp rope chair for Källemo, 2001

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/be0618cebff66756971ebb2c63f708db3923e4537230a9cb5750941ca9cf6212.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b22d9f41fd15efd87c7325f7b05d0fc81cbfc491aa081c515ebad27000fa2848.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6d8d80c726f1118801c9affd0c64ecbccf63b19d33051cf890538fb3d577edfe.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a2dd926089a2b6c23ab2612b15820ce84dbf5d62d2a7406fbcffec9e38a79750.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6fce5a6441f19e0cac34df19a3fd5a74b1f9237a6a775b7be80cd042399397bd.jpg)  
Poul Kjærholm, Model no. PK24 Hammock chaise longue for E. Kold Christensen, 1965 - reissued by Fritz Hansen

## poul kjærholm

(b. 1929 Øster Vrå - d. 1980 Hillerød, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0549db53c0d86a59b5774fc97a0f04ae1e5d4fc53b0360fc7862e3b60ad513e4.jpg)

The celebrated Danish architect and furniture designer Poul Kjærholm trained as a carpenter before studying furniture design and cabinetmaking at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School of Arts and Crafts), Copenhagen, from 1949 to 1951. During this period the school ran an industrial design programme for a few months, and while on the course Kjærholm was taught by Jorn Utzon (b. 1918) - the designer of the Sydney opera house - who found that "Poul had a great talent. Nothing was difficult for him. It was child's play for him to design, to choose materials and use them correctly. He needed no instruction." Certainly his graduate project, the PK25 steel chair (1951) with its halyard seat and back, demonstrated a remarkable sureness of handling and was immediately put into production by the furniture manufacturer E. Kold Christensen (later produced by Fritz Hansen). Around 1952, Kjærholm designed another constructionally innovative chair, the PKO, which was made of only two moulded plywood sections. Obviously inspired by Charles and Ray Eames's earlier moulded plywood chairs of 1945-1946, this remarkably progressive design could be considered even more rational because of its reduced number of elements. However, for reasons presumably to do with either cost or sales appeal the chair was not put into production until 1997. Throughout his career, Kjærholm concentrated on the design of furniture for mass production and from 1955 he mainly worked in conjunction with E. Kold Christensen based in Hellerup. Unlike many of his Danish contemporaries, Kjærholm adopted steel rather than wood as his material of choice - his designs being inspired more literally by those of Modern Movement designers, especially Le Corbusier (1887-1965), than by the Kaare Klint school, which continued to advocate the reworking of existing "ideal" types. Although Kjærholm's elegant and rational furniture designs, such as the PK22 chair (1956) and the Hammock PK24 chaise longue (1965), were conceived within the Modernist idiom, they managed to avoid the alienating hard-edged aesthetic so common to the work of the Modern Movement. Kjærholm achieved this through the high-quality construction and realization of his designs, which retained a sense of traditional craftsmanship even though they utilized modern industrial materials and were mainly produced by machine. Kjærholm was awarded a

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/58ebd12ffd2a38f825aff16ba3c3d26cc8135d90187e62e46b7053e5ee67cd16.jpg)

Grand Prix at both the 1957 (XI) and 1960 (XII) Milan Triennale and also received a Lunning Prize in 1958 for his furniture, the manufacture of which was taken over from E. Kold Christensen by Fritz Hansen around 1970. Although Kjærholm was one of the Scandinavian designers most influenced by the functionalist approach to design, his aesthetically pure furniture was to a great extent humanized by a traditional Scandinavian sensibility based on the mastery of materials and technical construction. Of his choice of materials he stated, Steel's constructive potentials are not the only thing that interest me; the refraction of light on its surface is an important part of my artistic work. I consider steel a material with the same artistic merit as wood and leather."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e63498fa6c0eec0e299f8a9e3517f7a5efaca99fb57bdb54c3c514dffc758783.jpg)  
Poul Kjærholm, Model no. PK25 steel and halyard chair with Model no. PKO moulded plywood chair for Fritz Hansen (produced) by Fritz Hansen from 1997), 1951 & 1952  
Poul Kjærholm, design for the Model no. PKO chair (produced by Fritz Hansen from 1997), 1952

Poul Kjærholm, Model no. PK20 chair for E. Kold Christensen, 1967 - reissued by Fritz Hansen

From left to right: Poul Kjærholm, Model no. PK41 folding stool, Model no. PK33 stool and Model no. PK8O day bed for E. Kold Christensen, 1961, 1958 & 1957 - reissued by Fritz Hansen

Poul Kjærholm, Model no. PK22 chair for E. Kold Christensen, 1955 - reissued by Fritz Hansen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/84dd830ca14a182736ea8b4aed344803e387826beb4e7c5aa464d5dec5303f8e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/14166a08a5fe10fe0384b291a62c9dd0d648ad2cb4f94bfc2532ebfd4751ff30.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7e90d6497f7d9bae1b3bdcdb0b55f67db3cc86cc201c720d037dca1740c3db73.jpg)

Kaare Klint, Safari Chair for Rud. Rasmussen, 1933 - This design was based on the earlier knock-down Indian chair used by the British Army overseas and retailed by Maples & Co. from c. 1904

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4e6c89c91501d3d11645c1fe7f610198306303511457a594045420f2431ba633.jpg)

## kaare klint

## (b. 1888 Frederiksberg - d. 1954 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ad1db7bdef6d906578b52a45ea41ec9d93e86c4916374544f747dee08c13b71d.jpg)

Kaare Klint was the son of Peder Vilhelm Jensen-Klint (1853-1913), who is generally considered to have been the leading Danish architect of the early 20th century. Klint initially studied painting at the Polytechnic of Frederiksberg from 1903, before serving an apprenticeship as an architect under his father, who believed in the importance of studying earlier styles and traditional materials and whose own work carefully balanced 19th century historicism with 20th century expressionism. Kaare Klint later worked with the Copenhagen architects Kai Nielsen from 1911 to 1912 and Carl Petersen (1874-1923) from 1914 to 1917. From 1917, he began practising as an independent furniture designer, establishing his own design office in 1920 and working for among other manufacturers Fritz Hansen and Rud. Rasmussen. Klint's furniture designs, such as his Deck Chair (1933), were largely reworkings of earlier types drawn from a variety of influences including Scandinavian vernacularism, Shaker design, Regency furniture and oriental cabinet making. Many of his designs were notable for the high quality of their wood finishes and for their well-considered proportions. In 1924, he established the furniture department at the Kongelige

Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, and together with his students undertook important pioneering research into anthropometrics - the systematic collection and correlation of measurements of the human body - in an attempt to generate data that would enable the development of furniture that corresponded better with users' physical characteristics. As an influential design theorist and commentator, Klint also urged the combining of the attributes associated with traditional craftsmanship, such as attention to detail and in-depth knowledge of materials, with rational design principles. Like his father, he additionally stressed the importance of studying earlier styles and encouraged his students to take precise measurements of "classic" furniture forms - such as the traditional English Windsor chair - so as to gain an understanding of the principles of proportion, construction and function. In 1944, he became professor of architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi in Copenhagen, and the same year designed his Fruit lamp, which was constructed of folded paper. These inexpensive paper lamps were manufactured by Le Klint, a company that had evolved from a cottage industry established by his father, who had also made and designed paper lamps. Klint believed that design should serve the public and that the best way of achieving this was through the adoption of rational design principles and the study of anthropometrics. Klint's teaching laid the foundations for the renewal of Danish design after the Second World War, and inspired a number of furniture designers, especially Hans Wegner and Børge Mogensen, to create "classic" modern designs that were essentially reworkings of historical furniture types.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5612a79f552b37a59690e739c6e941b384fb1cd3453caaa9d651b2b52db064c1.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/47a3b4c96a5486837da01fb306a65923507a88f48c80bb82859963cb3bd87cd5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bf55197d26e8d02b933890690fbac9c4ab3cfb7659950841ce5dae5dd4f3f35d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/74c6c6edb6f6df9b45e48116744d7b8260870c1ccadc390b218df14ca6b27273.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8550d7f63d8664eef9747f9990abcc123b2fcc59202ef287593dd3016aa36e56.jpg)

Kaare Klint, Model no. 3758A Red Chair for Rud. Rasmussen, 1927 - This design is also known as the Barcelona Chair as it was awarded a prize at the 1929 "Exposición Internacional de Barcelona"

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e6b80f4322351501f96b1c0da190ac054d9c5bdc49c0d3baaf1d51caeb06d1ac.jpg)
