Vibeke Klint, red carpet with zig-zag pattern, 1953

Vibeke Klint, striped woven textile, 1980

# vibeke klint

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b71fb3124ca3bbcd5bebeb76d3bd98068c750d4f3706d6a25ecd1262d553851d.jpg)

The Danish textile designer Vibeke Klint studied weaving under Gerda Henning at the Kunsthndværkerskolen (School of Arts and Crafts) in Copenhagen, graduating in 1949. From 1949 to 1950 she worked in Henning's workshop and in 1951 went to France to continue her training as a weaver under the guidance of Jean Lurçat (1892-1966) at the renowned Aubusson tapestry school in St. Céré, and also with Jean Wemaire in Brittany. On her return, Klint took over the direction of Henning's workshop and by the early 1950s was regarded by Lis Ahlmann as "indisputably the most competent of the young Danish weavers". Using simple zigzag and striped patterns, Klint remained true to traditional materials and weaving techniques, yet her designs radiated a contemporary freshness through her use of bright saturated colours, such as deep reds, dark blues, unbleached whites and warm golds. Inspired by Indian and American First Nation textiles, Klint's designs projected an almost "primitive" aesthetic that contrasted strongly with the refined craft skills used to produce them. This combination of bold geometric patterning with masterful weaving techniques gave Klint's work a distinctive character

that revealed, according to Jörgen Schou-Christensen, the "noble simplicity of genuine handcraft". Apart from her hand-loom textiles in cotton and silk, from 1956 onwards Klint also translated her designs into patterns suitable for industrial manufacture, such as her carpet collection for C. Olesen. In 1957, she executed tapestries for the Egmont H. Petersen College in Copenhagen after cartoons devised by the painter William Scharff (1886-1959), and three years later was awarded the celebrated Lunning Prize. Vibeke Klint not only promulgated the Scandinavian tradition of hand weaving but also revitalized it with her bright yet simple textiles, which she often adapted so as to meet the requirements of mass production.

Mogens Koch, MK Safari chair for Rud. Rasmussen, 1932

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bb317979729e70315c9509da6e53748f0382fc03d019b042a306828ae3cb3231.jpg)

# mogens koch

(b. 1898 Copenhagen - d. 1992 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9769d78cde88aee1151ab72d73716818dc8dd8d0f1adb78a30f5a668284c8a13.jpg)

From 1921 to 1925 the Danish furniture designer Mogens Koch worked as an assistant to the architect Carl Petersen (1874-1923) while studying architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, from whence he graduated in 1925. Following this, he went on study tours throughout the United States, Mexico and Europe, and from 1925 to 1930 he worked as an assistant to Kaare Klint. Later in 1932 Koch designed his MK Safari chair, which like Klint's slightly later Safari chair (1933) was based on the design of a traditional campaign chair. Although Koch's chair is now considered a classic Danish furniture design, when it first appeared it was deemed too radical to be put into production. Eventually, the chair was manufactured by Interna and Rud. Rasmussen in 1960. In 1934, Koch established his own design practice and four years later was awarded the prestigious Eckersberg Medal. Influenced by Kaare Klint's evolutionary approach to design, Koch's furniture - like Klint's - can be seen as essentially modern reworkings of traditional types. His Wingback chair of 1936, for example, was an homage to Georgian chairs made in Great Britain during the 18th century. Apart from Interna and Rud. Rasmussen, Koch also designed furniture for Danish CWS and N. C. Jensen Kjær which exemplified the Danish look. Beyond furniture, Koch designed silverware, textiles and carpets in connection with various Danish church restoration projects. His work was regularly exhibited at the Milan Triennale exhibitions and was included in the "Arts of Denmark" travelling exhibition that toured America in 1960. From 1950 to 1968, Koch was a professor at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi and was also a visiting lecturer at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology from 1956 and at the Industrial Art Institute, Tokyo, from 1962. As one of the leading pioneers of modern Danish furniture design, he was awarded the C. F. Hansen Medal and the Cabinetmakers' Guild Annual Prize in 1963 and 1964 respectively. Koch's subtle yet fundamentally modern approach to problem solving, which relied on the refinement of "ideal" forms, exemplified Danish design during the inter-war years.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/23efe753f3ca0cdc323f20bfaa9c3912bdce28904fe4e8f952bbde7638bc77b7.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b14f0cae19d81cf02e0d6ff0c47d3673586e6a9344613d27edd4ce93b8e82e22.jpg)  
Mogens Koch, mahogany sectional chairs, foot stools and tables for Rud. Rasmussen, 1961  
Mogens Koch, two-piece mahogany and rosewood table executed by N. C. Jensen Kær, 1952

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f60232ae5febfa9e5865dd3ca836a3f08f530547edd70d9a02f7194cbb30255b.jpg)  
M100/Classic rocking spring seat for Kompan, 1970

# kompan

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/81431dacabe5f5eed046c07da0da360fb6728d32715fceeee8aa0898b1519921.jpg)

Kompan is one of the world's leading manufacturers of playground equipment. Its familiar, colourful yet simple designs populate public spaces throughout the world, from small villages in the Spanish countryside to shopping malls in North America. The company was founded by Tom Lindhardt Wils and Hans Mogens Frederiksen in 1970, and the first product they launched was an abstract animal-shaped toy on a rocking spring known as the M100/Classic (1970). Another early product was a wave-like ladder known as the M401 Sprout (1972) - a relatively simple yet ingenious design that required only a small area for its installation. An improved version of this design was later introduced with two platforms and a small crawl space at its bottom. Kompan was the first manufacturer to develop play equipment that was proportioned specifically with safety issues in mind. Among Kompan's most successful products is the Sandworks M581 988), which like the majority of its designs is constructed of laminated wood sections with simple bold forms and brightly painted colour combinations. This multi-activity play centre was designed for children between the ages of three to eight and has numerous features - sand can be formed, scooped, poured and lifted by two crane-like elements. Its sculptural form and cheerful palette of eye-catching primary colours encourages interaction and provides play that is both fun and educational. Like other Kompan designs it possesses both an engaging simplicity and a highly recognizable visual identity, which exudes a playful cheerfulness even on the bleakest winter's day. For older children aged between six and twelve, Kompan launched the Sirius GXY 8011 "jungle-gym" in 1998. Constructed of galvanized steel and polyurethane, it has a more sombre aesthetic than the equipment for younger children yet still invites interactive play. Guided by a design philosophy that recognizes play as an important part of life, Kompan enters the world of play from the child's perspective in order to work with issues o great importance to the child: "Play Value, Play Rights, Play Safety and Play Together". In recognition of its innovative products, Kompan was awarded the European Community Design Prize in 1994 and the Industrial Designers Society of America Award in 1999. As a Danish company, it is not surprising that Kompan has become such a dominant force within the playground equipment sector for Scandinavian firms and design groups have historically led the way in terms of implementing "design for safety", while Scandinavian society has long prided itself on its culture of trans-generational inclusiveness.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b5bba0b7924132b846c22c3afbe4c6eac2a74515d18b7544110a3628de96a89c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1f2fd40fd2bcf4cab912771ee15b9436314e05ccb8ec7b921c6d2c453cf23dab.jpg)

Δ GXY8011 Sirius play equipment for Kompan, 1998

GXY8011 Sirius play equipment for Kompan, 1998

Playground with Kompan's sculptural and clourl py furniture", c. 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c4c76a48a39e3e0ea98062a5f9dbe580d93556015d3bac348ad35946dfe7adb6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ebec58548aa92b57d9ac01a25851b28c79560def8ad8ec17ef57b0fa469962f1.jpg)  
Henning Koppel, Model no. 323 silver and enamel brooch for Georg Jensen, 1956

# henning koppel

## (b. 1918 Copenhagen - d. 1981 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7a0719f50bea587a0b6872509e826fbfb5a0095787f3711c55c86b223fc777f0.jpg)

The Danish designer Henning Koppel studied drawing under Bizzie Høyer in Copenhagen from 1935 to 1936 and then sculpture under Anker Hoffmann at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi, (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, from 1936 to 1937. Following this, he trained at the Académie Ranson, Paris, from 1938 to 1939, where he became aware of currents in avant-garde sculpture. During the Second World War, Koppel was exiled in Stockholm and while there produced designs for the Orrefors glassworks and Svenskt Tenn. Around this time, he also began designing gold and silver jewellery. In 1945, he returned to Denmark and initiated his long association with the Georg Jensen silversmithy, which lasted until his death in 1981. Although he worked in other areas of design and for other companies, it was his beautiful sculptural silverware designed for Georg Jensen that attracted the greatest international attention. Inspired by the contemporary abstract sculptures of Hans Arp (1887-1966) and Constantin Brancusi (1876- 1957), Koppel designed biomorphic silver jewellery, such as his Model no. 89 bracelet (1946), which was exceptionally forward-looking and

functioned as wearable sculpture. Despite the fact that Koppel worked within the Danish craft tradition, his jewellery and silverware products such as the Model no. 978 wine pitcher (1948) and his fish dish (1954) were fundamentally modern because they articulated a design rhetoric that rejected historicism in favour of a universal language of organic form. His concepts for product designs were initially worked out in sketches and then modelled in clay, which enabled him to perfect their forms before they were translated into silver. In 1957, Koppel introduced a more expressive mode of design to Georg Jensen's flatware collection with his Caravel cutlery, but during the 1970s his work became increasingly geometric and less sculptural. One of his last designs for Georg Jensen, which reflected this shift towards a more rational and pared-down aesthetic, was the Vejrstation (weather station) series (1980), which included a barometer, thermometer, clock and hydrometer. Between 1961 and 1981 Koppel also designed a number of ceramics for Bing & Grondahl, including his undecorated porcelain Form 24 service (1962), which is sometimes referred to as the Koppel White service and

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b55508d0b2af26c795fbb08ba32181eb519048bc1c992562b269ca3d802fc434.jpg)

featured a tea pot with an unusual teak handle jutting from its side. From 1971, Koppel resumed work as a freelance designer for Orrefors. He also designed clocks and lighting for Louis Poulsen and postage stamps for the Danish Post Office. During his illustrious career, he was awarded three gold medals at the 1951 (IX), 1954 (X) and 1957 (XI) Milan Triennale, the Lunning Prize (1953), and the International Design Award of the American Institute of Designers (1963). Koppel's masterful understanding of the intrinsic nature of materials enabled him to produce exquisite metalware that was remarkable for its sculptural plasticity and sensuous organic form. With their highly polished reflective surfaces and asymmetrical lines, these designs came to epitomize the "New Look" of post-war Scandinavian design.

Arne Korsmo & Grete Prytz, silver and Perspex teapot for Jacob Tostrup, 1947-1948

Grete Prytz

# arne korsmo & grete prytz

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d5ee7fe35e54f52b8abbf23423a3471ebde6fe4a79a97b8f5182e9d7756681cc.jpg)

## (Norwegian 1900-1968)

Arne Korsmo studied at the Norges Tekniske Høgskole (National Polytechnic) in Trondheim and then established his own design practice in 1929. He subsequently designed metalware for the goldsmith and jewellery firm Jacob Tostrup (founded in 1832), which was headed by Jacob Tostrup Prytz (1886-1962) who became his father-in-law when he married his daughter, Grete Prytz. In 1937, Korsmo designed the Norwegian section at the "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne" in Paris. He later received considerable recognition for the silver cutlery he designed in 1949 (produced by Jacob Tostrup from 1953), which was remarkable for its soft, ergonomically-inspired melting forms and was awarded a gold medal at the 1954 (X) Milan Triennale. From 1936 to 1956, Korsmo was director of the Statens Handverks- og Kunstindustriskole (National College of Arts and Crafts) in Oslo and later became a professor at the Norges Tekniske Høgskole. Apart from his metalware, Korsmo also designed several pieces of wooden and tubular metal furniture. During the post-war years, Arne and Grete Korsmo were Norway's "design couple" - equivalent to Charles and

(Norwegian b. 1917)

Ray Eames in America and Robin and Lucienne Day in Britain - and as such, had enormous influence on the Norwegian design community. Coming from a well-known goldsmithing family, Grete Prytz was the daughter of Jacob Tostrup Prytz, who was the fourth generation to head the family's Oslo-based goldsmith firm, Jacob Tostrup. From 1936 to 1941 she studied at the Statens Hándverks- og Kunstindustriskole (National College of Arts and Crafts) where her father, who was one of the leading advocates of functionalism in Norway during the 1920s and 1930s, taught and became rector in 1945. After completing her studies, Greta began designing jewellery and metalware for the family firm, which often involved enamelling - a technique that had become a Norwegian specialty during the early years of the 20th century and one for which the Jacob Tostrup company was especially well-known. Learning from her family's long-held expertise in this field, Grete revived the technique by using it for contemporary abstract motifs and vibrantly coloured decoration. Between 1949 and 1950, Grete was a Fulbright scholar at the Chicago Institute of Design, and on her return to Norway she collaborated with the Sentralinstituttet for Industriell Forskning (Central Institute for Industrial Research) on the development of techniques that would allow large pieces of silver to be enamelled. The results of these experiments, which included her chunky Domino rings (1952), were shown at the "Norsk emaljekunst i lyset" (Light on the enamel art of Norway) exhibition held in Oslo in 1952. Now known as Grete Korsmo (after her marriage to Arne Korsmo), she was awarded the Lunning Prize for her advancement of enamelling techniques and in particular for her introduction of freehand engraving on the "to-be-enamelled" surface using a cutting tool that was similar to a dentist's drill. This innovative process pioneered by Grete enabled a greater degree of artistic freedom than had previously been obtainable. After the death of Arne Korsmo in 1968, she remarried and became known as Grete Prytz Kittelsen. During the 1970s she designed a number of enamelled stainless-steel bowls that were industrially produced by Cathrineholm and which reflected her commitment to the design and manufacture of inexpensive yet high quality products. From 1975 to 1978, she was president of the Norwegian National

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e9e64d1f85d83e766c4f48d130f4726a7bef209a9d6f8707fd9f6c2c9ac2aa7b.jpg)

Association of Arts & Crafts and in 1984 was elected an honorary member of the World Crafts Council.

Arne Korsmo, Korsmo cutlery for Jacob Tostrup, 1947-1952 - produced from 1953

Arne Korsmo, silver tea set with Perspex handles for Jacob Tostrup, c. 1950

Grete Prytz, Ty textil for Sissi Bjønnes, 1955

Arne Korsmo & Grete Prytz, silver and enamel candelabra for Jacob Tostrup, c. 1950

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/82c24925020881c7e9a78dcc4a082448d26822eb88093d1f955bcc81645f12e7.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3b514a33d43de8b589059d7f67a02d9df130fc811d536446c0a75744c3d281db.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0cb11ae35f13b440bb1162b5c6fa6a487704cc857f173658e313a1403e6fc64d.jpg)  
Harri Koskinen, Block Lamp for Design House Stockholm, 1997

# harri koskinen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a551a999993ca888dcae7bff36d3ca1fb6b4e9acea310eb562d99c8bb369328c.jpg)

One of the leading contemporary product designers in Finland, Harri Koskinen studied at the Lahti Design Institute and graduated in 1993. The following year he took an MA in Product and Strategic Design at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design), Helsinki. While there, he collaborated with the littala glassworks on a project for a wedding present for the year 2021 - a pair of red schnapps glasses that rested in a box of cast glass. Although not particularly satisfied with the resulting design, he was fascinated by how the molten glass glowed when it was being poured into the mould, and as a result set to work developing a cast glass lamp. For this, Koskinen carved a graphite mould using the shape of a regular-sized light bulb as a template and then once the form had been cast in glass, sandblasted the void so that the smaller-sized bulb inserted inside as the light-source would not be seen. The Block Lamp as it became known was exhibited in Helsinki in 1997 and was put into production by Design House Stockholm, which has to date sold over 15,000 units. The Block Lamp also led to Koskinen winning a placement at Designor Oy in 1996, where he initially worked at the Nuutajärvi glassworks. Two years later, he was transferred to littala (also part of the Designor group), which has historically employed many of the greatest Finnish glassware designers, from Alvar Aalto to Tapio Wirkkala. Since 1996, Koskinen has created a number of important designs for the company, including the innovative Atlas candleholder (1996), which when inverted transforms into a vase, the Klubi range of drinking glasses (1996-1998), which have as Koskinen puts it "good glass mass", and the Relations storm candleholder (1999), the largest glass object ever made at littala. Koskinen has also produced designs for the other companies in the Designor group. For Hackman, he designed stainless steel utensils for outdoor cooking (200o), which combine simplicity and functionalism and reflect the influence of having grown up on a farm in the Finnish countryside - an experience that Koskinen says gave him "a belief and courage to rely on the fundamental things in life". For Arabia, his Air storage containers (2001) are similarly practical yet beautiful in a subtle, almost elemental way. He has also designed several furniture prototypes and his work has been included in numerous exhibi-

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/71e5493ce8bfebdc8d320f21e1e2275f5f84f9561ec949fe61bf41e7bb60b599.jpg)

tions throughout Scandinavia, Europe and the Far East. As an heir to the remarkable legacy of design achievements in Finland, Koskinen's work embodies the key values so famously associated with Finnish design - innovation, functionalism, simplicity, and a touch of refined artistry.

Harri Koskinen, Atlas candleholders for littala, 1996 Harri Koskinen, Tools outdoor cooking utensils for Hackman, 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8dba19a9654402d13bfc0eda35d0cf632349d7567f306b98512674b8699d704a.jpg)

Harri Koskinen, Klubi glassware range for littala, 1996-1998

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/751eb0917da8418cdd14debb7f6e7ccc6d7352d63b7664c8bcbd3f0d98dd4e65.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2601dc201c16f8fedfe7a1992409a2d522c1dc99c711957f590003277872ca74.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/386f2cb5114f87b96154fff9b1df877ded984aa0b0a550da9a2d734319dd5211.jpg)

# kosta boda

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4286a7121863aa15139bfc237836c5388adb4f7cf087ceb966d99b85f295a466.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d1ab123eed4b6d3db3f4b7e194e86ba648c67947048d46d42c2cd7efc73570c4.jpg)

Kosta Boda originates from three Swedish glassworks, Kosta, Boda and Åfors, and has only been used as a trademark name since 1976. The beginnings of Kosta - Sweden's longest surviving glassworks - can, however, be traced back to 1742 when two county governors and generals in King Karl XII's army, Anders Koskull (1677-1746) and Georg Bogislaus Staël von Holstein (1685-1763), joined forces and founded a glassworks on the boundary between their counties of Kronoberg and Kalmar in the forested Småland region - an area that is now sometimes referred to as the "Kingdom o Crystal". The name of the factory and the village in which it was sited, Kosta, was derived from an acronym of their surnames. Attracting skilled glassblowers from Germany, the Kosta glassworks initially produced high-quality household items such as traditional German-style drinking vessels. The company also became renowned for its manufacture of chandeliers (in the English style) and architectural glass, and became the main supplier of window-panes to the Royal Palace (completed in 1753), which was built by Carl Gustav Tessin in Stockholm. From 1756 Kosta was under the financial control of the Wickenberg family, but

eventually it became a publicly-traded company in 1875. Around the turn-of-the-century, the factory began producing more artistic wares, especially Art Nouveau style cameo glass inspired by the work of Émile Gallé (1846-1904), which was adorned with naturalistic floral decoration and was designed by prominent Swedish designers such as Gunnar Wennerberg and Alf Wallander (1862- 1914). Following the establishment in 1915 of an agency to promote the employment of artists in industry, the Svenska Slöjdföreningen (Swedish Society of Craft and Design), and the society's appeal for Vackrare vardagsvara (better everyday goods), the Kosta glassworks employed a number arsts toevitalizeits product in ncl Edvin Ollers (1888-1959) - a member of the Optimists art group - and Edvard Strömberg. Ollers' designs for Kosta were distinguished by simple forms with decorative motifs, and were stylistically similar to the objects manufactured by the Daum glassworks in France during this period. Strömberg's designs were similarly distinguished by their restrained yet well-proportioned forms. During the 1920s and 1930s a number of leading designers worked for Kosta, including Ewald

Elis Bergh, cut glass vase for Kosta, 1937

Elis Bergh, glasses and decanters for Kosta, c. 1936

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9f52ee0b16eafed0e09daef0d74cf85f0ddfccfb8372a626328dc4d0b632032e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7ccbaf34db4615068d203fd8714d11d578b4ac8366e82717e0de7dc9384d257d.jpg)  
Early advertisement for Kosta

Dahlskog from 1926 to 1929, Sven Erik Skawonius (19081981) from 1933 to 1935, and Elis Bergh (1881-1954) from 1929 to 1950. As artistic director of the company, Bergh was responsible for the graceful yet modern approach to design that epitomized Kosta's product line during the inter-war years. This included the production of cut glass products with simple outlines that were mostly executed in clear glass and engraved with either stylized figurative or geometric motifs. In response to the increasing popularity of the Art Deco style during the late 1930s, Kosta's glassware became increasingly angular, chunky and deep cut - as exemplified by the designs of Skawonius and Tyra Lundgren (1897-1979) that were embellished with highly stylized fish and bird motifs. Although Kosta's output during the interwar period was of generally high quality, it did not match the artistic and technical innovation of the Orrefors glassworks. The Kosta glassworks were not creatively energized until 1950 when Vicke Lindstrand was appointed its artistic director. Having worked previously for Orrefors, Lindstrand brought with him several techniques that he had developed there, including the so-called Ariel

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d3923277a9ca0f1e171be3c3ccb6c3b8d88ffecc361fcd75864ba73159c162df.jpg)

Vicke Lindstrand, Träd i dimma (Trees in the Fog), vase for Kosta Boda, 1957

Vicke Lindstrand, Negress vase for Kosta, 1953

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/25afcdf76a697fd85daa65b6d60ea9ea257c70e961843800db189e4318375c40.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/73ba4a656f2e471e0001480dc3214689c5de284507a2ec258abaf33affc14790.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/454a16e811e596ea20f2801aa9b6038f80bc9b2694ed66a421a5320e15e17795.jpg)  
Anna Ehrner, Atoll bowl from Artist Collection for Kosta Boda, 2000

technique, which involved trapping air between a sandblasted exterior pattern and the final casing layer of clear glass. At Kosta, Lindstrand produced some of the most sculptural vessels of his career. These were distinguished by asymmetrical forms, soft undulating lines, spiralling stripes of colour as well as cut and sandblasted abstract motifs. Under his direction the firm manufactured a diverse range of glassware, including Erik Höglund's bubbly rustic-style coloured soda glass vessels, Mona Morales-Schildt's polychromatic faceted Ventana series, and Ann (b. 1937) and Göran (b. 1933) Warff's highly expressive art glass, which was frequently embellished with humorous cameo decoration. In 1972 the firm acquired the Johansfors glassworks and in 1976 it was renamed Kosta Boda. In 1990 the company was taken over by its long-time competitor, Orrefors, and since then it has built on its reputation for artistic and colourful glass. Today, the company has a stable of eight inhouse designers - Monica Backström (b. 1939), Anna Ehrner (b. 1948), Kjell Engman (b. 1946), Ulrica Hydman-Vallien (b. 1938), Bertil Vallien (b. 1938), Ann Wahlström (b. 1957), Gunnel Sahlin (b. 1954) and Göran Wärff - who design new products and additions to existing collections that "communicate their artistic messages with warmth and light-heartedness" While Kosta Boda endeavours to connect users not only with its cultural roots but also with the "ever changing present", its glassware products continue to be widely acclaimed for their inherent craftsmanship and artistry.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b95a73e57b45e4c7d3eb952192b49dcdd304e20de05f7d7384e189c74c6b6386.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/711bfa1c643503d58937e30ced09ad87be1c856eb91f54e845a5185548cda21d.jpg)  
Herbert Krenchel, Krenit enamelled steel bowls and salad servers for Torben Ørskov, 1953

# herbert krenchel

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1f2916bdc2d4285684bcfc6319508911a064b99ba04f4a04f3090ddde311c9ab.jpg)

The Danish metalware designer Herbert Krenchel studied civil engineering at the Tekniske Højskole (Polytechnic) in Copenhagen. In 1953, he established his own design practice and began working as a freelance designer for the metalware manufacturer Torben Ørskov & Co. The same year, he designed his well-known Krenit bowls, which won a gold medal at the 1954 (X) Milan Triennale. These highly rational enamelled vessels were machine-pressed from millimetre-thin sheet steel and were remarkable for the benchmark they established in the industrial mass manufacture of domestic ware. During the mid-1950s, Krenchel also designed mass-produced tableware using newly developed "hard" plastics, such as his black melamine salad servers (c. 1956) that were also manufactured by Torben Ørskov. Slightly later, he designed the innovative enamelled steel Krenit frying pan (c. 1957), which was made available in three different diameters (6, 8, and 10 inches) with a different colour for each size. With its detachable bakelite handle, this product could be used directly from the stove-top to the servingtable. Like many other Danish designers, Krenchel concentrated his efforts on the creation of highquality democratic products for the home that exemplified the Svenska Slöjdföreningen (Swedish Society of Craft and Industrial Design) catchphrase, Vackrare vardagsvara (better everyday goods).

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/81c25024e98f77dd73a74987d3a250da78e6a6b2ffe769296788425761f47d03.jpg)

Arnold Krog, Kobra (Cobra) earthenware vase for Royal Copenhagen, 1914

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b8a005688a8cb9ac42d995cdc86d93a58094d0161e67afae17ac3126b45f8bce.jpg)  
Arnold Krog, porcelain vase for Royal Copenhagen, 1907 - Krog decorated this vase repeatedly with various different Oriental-style motifs

# arnold krog

The Danish ceramicist Arnold Krog studied architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, from 1874 to 1880. While still a student he assisted in the refurbishment and interior design of the imposing Frederiksborg Palace west of Copenhagen (1878-1881), and then in 1883 worked briefly in the design office of the architect Henrik Hageman. In 1884, he began designing ceramics for Royal Copenhagen and a year later was appointed the firm's art director. Krog's work received much praise when it was exhibited at the 1888 "Scandinavian Exhibition of Industry, Agriculture and Art" in Copenhagen. The following year, he visited Paris as a member of the jury for the 1889 Paris "Exposition Universelle" and while there saw the extensive Oriental art collection of the dealer and design entrepreneur Siegfried Bing (1838-1905). Krog subsequently produced a number of designs with undecorated forms that were strongly influenced by Japanese antecedents and featured Oriental-style crystalline glazes specially devised by Valdemar Engelhardt. One of his most influential designs for the Royal Copenhagen factory was the Marguerite service (1897), which incorporated handles that were inspired by dragonflies and bees. Krog's designs were exhibited at the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale", where he was awarded a Grand Prix. During the 1910s, he moved away from the delicacy of his earlier Japanese-inspired work and began designing objects in the Art Deco style, as exemplified by his vase Kobra (Cobra) of 1914, which with its twisting snakes and zigzag motifs showed the influence of primitivism and traditional earthenware forms. Krog was one of Royal Copenhagen's most influential designers, having produced ceramic designs for the factory until 1916. Like so many other Danish designers, Krog was strongly influenced by the art of Japan and throughout his career sought to replicate ideal forms - a classically Scandinavian preoccupation.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/98e5622735fac7d56b05a4750edde212628ddc05a2498a47e421fb6b3481c831.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f33fd52ca71b0a87d0176e1f91a7b6588d167923b58d0cc5d63fe851f9e573c9.jpg)  
Yrjö Kukkapuro, Karuselli (Carousel) chairs for Haimi, 1964-1965 - reissued by Avarte

Yrjo Kukkapuro, Experiment 401 chair for Avarte, 1983

# yrjo kukkapuro

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1b4eacf57ef26ab7885bf26c10e9f2332d827dff8da9aeb0d0861d1a981105c1.jpg)

One of Finland's leading furniture designers, Yrjo Kukkapuro studied interior design at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design), Helsinki, from where he graduated in 1958. A year later, he established his own design studio in Kauniainen and began designing furniture within a functionalist vocabulary. His early work was greatly influenced by llmari Tapiovaara, who taught at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu in Helsinki and who was a pioneer of knockdown furniture. During the late 1950s Kukkapuro began designing chairs with moulded plywood seat elements and tubular metal frames - not because these were his materials of choice, but because they were readily available. As Kukkapuro later noted, "I was already dreaming of making fibreglass chairs, but the dream was little short of utopia in the Finland of those days." He did, however, eventually get the chance to experiment with plastics thanks to the advent of sintering in 1961. With this relatively simple technique, a heated steel form is dipped into powdered plastic so as to form a layer of melted material that can then be removed from the mould once it has cooled down sufficiently. Kukkapuro developed a couple of prototype chairs using this technique, but he was unable to find a manufacturer or the necessary financial backing to put the designs into production. In early 1964, Kukkapuro made a second attempt at designing a chair in plastic and constructed a prototype from wire mesh that had been moulded to fit the shape of his own seated body. He then attached this shaped element to a tubular metal frame, covered it with sacking that had been dipped in plaster, and smoothed and scraped it into the required form. It took nearly a year of modelling before a working prototype of the chair was finally completed. The resulting fibreglass and leather Karuselli (Carousel) chair (1965) featured an innovative cradle-like construction and a comfortable single-form seat shell that embodied Kukkapuro's belief that "a chair could also be a reflection of the human form. As softly shaped as people are. And if at all possible, just as beautiful." In 1966, the Karuselli chair was featured on the cover of Domus magazine, testifying not only to the importance of this particular design but also to the increasing influence of Finnish design in general. During the late 1960s and early 1970s, Kukkapuro designed other chairs and tables that were constructed of moulded plastic, such as his Saturnus tables (1987). By the post-oil-crisis years of the mid-1970s, however, the plastics era with its experimental and expressive forms had been replaced by "the golden age of ergonomics and ecology". As a result, Kukkapuro undertook various "physiometrical examinations" of different human sizes so as to generate the anthropometric data necessary for the development of a form-pressed plywood office chair. The subsequent Fysio chair (1978) was completely revolutionary, being one of the very first office chairs to have a form based almost entirely on ergonomic considerations. Kukkapuro was a professor at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki from 1974 to 1980 and the institution's rector from 1978 to 1980. By the 1980s, with the ascendancy of Post-Modernism, Kukkapuro began producing more expressive designs, such as his Experiment chairs (1982-1983). Of this high style he declared, Post-Modernism has once again put us in touch with the vital element that the French call joie de vivre'". Kukkapuro's furniture was originally manufactured by Haimi, but in 1980 a new company, Avarte, was established to take over its production. Kukkapuro's work can be seen as classically

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/195eeeb9843c87df6c8047c6194dae7172e84e15ae6cdbf69d4346845108fe6c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/28835fe16233fbb7d1ece95f264a91b8b928cb5f73e9be516b799b24a40d592b.jpg)  
Yrjö Kukkapuro, Model no. 817H-1K Remmi chair for Avarte, 1970  
Yrjö Kukkapuro, Model no. 455F Fysio chair for Avarte, 1978

Finnish, being distinguished by strong graphic outlines, technical innovation, experimentation and the use of high-quality materials and construction.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/43b4e39af012bba0a802dbf4c79f65745e141347f09a4cb7d8c11cd2ecb503bc.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f30390c9b496c773cef74dec2f593897c88a3c4e5a30d58c3860d2e0e4f395ec.jpg)  
Borge Lindau & Bo Lindekrantz, Model no. S70-3 bar stool for Lammhults, 1968

## Iammhults

## LAMMHULTS

The internationally renowned Swedish furniture company, Lammhults, was founded in 1945 with meagre financial resources and an empty barn. Initially, the firm supplied various fittings to local furniture manufacturers and it was not until 1955 that it began making its own furniture. The earliest Lammhults products were designed by one of the company's founders, Edvin Stáhl (1896-1971), and included the tubular metal and plastic 282 stool (1955), the stackable and linkable Pyramid chair (1955), and the Kongress 57 armchair (1957). Stahl also designed furniture for trans-Atlantic ships and the Spindeln chair that was used for the outdoor restaurant at the landmark 1955 "H55" exhibition at Helsingborg. In the mid-1960s, the company commissioned two of the most avant-garde designers working in Sweden, Börge Lindau (1932-1999) and Bo Lindekrantz (b. 1932), to design a stacking chair for use in assembly halls. What they came up with were two designs, the Ritz (1965) and the Royal (1965), which were named after the two best-known cafés in the Lammhult's region. These chairs enjoyed much success and were followed in 1968 by the Bauhaus-inspired S70 range of furniture also

designed by Lindau and Lindekrantz, which included wall-mounted and floor-standing ashtrays, a hat stand, two tables, a bed, a sofa-bed, a sofa, two armchairs and the well-known reversed cantilevered seat that was available in three different heights - tabouret, armchair and barstool. During the 1970s, Lindau and Lindekrantz designed a wide range of furniture that was influenced by 1930s Swedish Modernism. In the 1980s, they designed furniture in the Post-Modern style, such as the Plakan chair (1985), which was intended as an homage to Gerrit Rietveld's Red/Blue chair of 19181923. In 1989, Lammhults introduced designs by Gunilla Allard (b. 1957), who was the first female designer to contribute to Lammhults' furniture range, Her minimalist designs, such as the Casino and Chicago chairs (1997 & 1998), were especially favoured by German architects, who appreciated her re-interpretation of functionalist Bauhaus principles. During the 1990s, Lammhults also produced designs by Love Arbén (b. 1952) and Jonas Bohlin that articulated a more expresulaveas fyr, Lammhults has produced furniture that has been strongly influenced by both Modernism and Post-

Modernism, yet ts products have avoided stylistic extremes, epitomizing the measured Scandinavian approach to design that generally precludes aesthetics from overriding considerations of practical function.

Gunilla Allard, Cinema Sport armchair for Lammhults, 1999

Gunilla Allard, Cosmos chairs for Lammhults, 2000

Gunilla Allard, Cinema easy chair with footstool for Lammhults, 1994

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a9f7dd50ea46ae741f356d288d2b6966fcc6c255f5adecda57c2365c46ec28b9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7b5115c28385a8bdabc8002b69fe44b4b7b1fedef9e6a6fd7c815e69434c065a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dc194ac8d8bb5c2b28e0a6687f8f81047cb0d4f320458221b71f7b9076f28713.jpg)  
Nils Landberg, Tulpan (Tulip) glasses for Orrefors, 1957

# nils landberg

The Swedish glassware designer Nils Landberg studied at the Konstindustrieskolan (School of Arts and Crafts) in Gothenburg from 1923 to 1925 and trained at the newly established school of engraving at the Orrefors glassworks from 1925 to 1927 - as did his compatriot, Sven Palmqvist. Landberg later studied in Italy and France before returning to Sweden, where he began working under Edward Hald as an assistant and engraver at Orrefors. Around 1935, he began creating his own designs for the company and became a member of its in-house design team, producing elegant classical decorative motifs for glass shapes devised by Simon Gate. His own engraved glassware from this period was shown at both the 1937 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne" and the 1939 New York World's Fair. During the 1950s, Landberg experimented with abstracted and attenuated forms using both clear and delicately tinted glass. The resulting designs possessed an inherent tension due to his pushing the limits of the medium's tensile strength as far as possible. His famous Tulpanglas (Tulip glass) of 1957 with its slender waist was blown as a single piece and received a gold medal at the 1957 (XI) Milan Triennale. This exceptionally refined design not only epitomized the elegance of post-war Scandinavian organic design but also predicted the bold and colourful nature of Swedish glassware in the 1960s. Revered as a classic Orrefors' design (it remained in production until 1981), the Tulpan range's fragile poise testified to the sheer technical brilliance of Swedish glassblowers. Landberg's Illusion glassware line (1957) similarly emphasized according to Orrefors the "inherent purity and softness of the glass". Apart from glassware, Landberg also designed lighting and decorative doors and windows. In 1972, he retired from Orrefors and in 1986 the Orrefors Museum held a retrospective exhibition of his glass designs. Landberg's work not only perpetuated the "grace" that had been historically associated with Swedish glassware, but with its exaggerated organic forms it also exemplified the "New Look" of post-war Scandinavian design.

Nils Landberg, Tulpan (Tulip) glasses for Orrefors, 1954

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f7b78190f2af28e29be056cf317e106b5ebf9472b1b40f61e18bd847a4ba7b18.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7e357e1c6b41c94e631243393528b737a5febda46d971c2f08571f28d7ea46ae.jpg)  
Axel Larsson, living room furniture, 1939  
Axel Larsson, chair and magazine cabinet for Svenska Möbelfabrikerna, 1933

# axel larsson8-1975)

One of the greatest proponents of Swedish Modernism during the inter-war period, Axel Larsson studied at the Tekniska skolan (School of Technology) in Stockholm. In 1925, he began designing furniture for Svenska Möbelfabrikerna (Swedish Furniture Manufacturers) that was suitable for mass production. Around this time he also established his own workshop in Stockholm and designed a dining room interior for the 1930 Stockholm Exhibition. This installation revealed the strong influence of German Functionalism, but the overall effect was somewhat lightened by the inclusion of potted plants and a boldly patterned geometric rug. Like his fellow countryman and contemporary Sven Markelius, Larsson initially designed furniture with simple, pared-down forms that was primarily informed by functional concerns. By the mid-1930s, however, his work became softer and more organic, much in the manner of Bruno Mathsson's landmark Eva chair (1934). In 1939, Larsson designed a living room interior for the Swedish Pavilion at the New York World's Fair, the furniture for which adhered to Modern Movement principles while also demonstrating the distinctly Scandinavian concern for superlative

craftsmanship and ergonomic form. From the 1930s until 1956, Larsson was the principal designer for Svenska Möbelfabrikerna in Bodafors, one of the first firms in Scandinavia to pioneer the manufacture of aesthetically pleasing mass-produced furniture. Larsson's designs for the company, such as his light stained-ash side chair and shelved cupboard (c. 1934), exemplified the subtle detailing of modern Swedish design, and possessed an elegant functionalism that rejected the hard-edged Bauhaus style for a warmer and more inviting aesthetic. Like many other Scandinavian designers, Larsson also designed exclusive handcrafted furniture. During his career he was commissioned to design a number of public and private interior schemes, including those for the Gothenburg Concert Hall (co-designed with Nils Einar Eriksson) and the Foreign Office in Stockholm. Although Larsson's furniture - both handcrafted and massproduced - was essentially modern, it was not born out of a dogmatic adherence to Functionalism and as such presaged the softer and more humanistic approach to design that flourished in Scandinavia during the post-war years.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8b0aa5b403eb07432ea6766490cadf1a291461fb51f87df837e70333e18573e6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0f8f358ca39b76cd6e2d704c2b9d022312ee007357e088eb00d3de2120c9ebf3.jpg)  
The library at Lilla Hyttäs, with tablecloth woven by Karin Larsson

# carl & karin larsson (b. 1853 Stockholm -

d 1919 Sundborn, Sweden & b. 1859 Örebro - d. 1928 Esbjörn, Sweden

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e4851a22bac14c37d3b9a556bf0ba6332c6b2c78ce062f277d0dc6340f906d2b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/689437d9d7e8cb3bc3e614cae9e5cec88215ba715eedc70a3cd003ae4dc2e447.jpg)

Carl and Karin Larsson were important pioneers of the concept of "design as lifestyle". Together, they formulated an informal eclectic style that focused on the idea of warm familial domesticity, which became hugely influential especially in Scandinavia and Germany. Carl's artistic talents as a youth led to him winning a place at the Academy of Art in Stockholm, where he subsequently studied classical art and life drawing. During this period, he contributed caricatures to the journal Kasper and graphics to the newspaper Ny Illustrerad Tidning. In 1877 he moved to Paris and in 1882 settled in Grez with a number of other Swedish painters. While there, he produced watercolours that had a poetic realism and strong narrative quality. In 1879, he met the Swedish artist, Karin Bergöo, whom he married in 1883. During the 1880s, Larsson joined the Swedish art group, Opponents, and worked as an illustrator. In 1888, Karin's father gave the growing Larsson family a small house, Lilla Hyttäs in Sundborn, which they used as a summer residence until 1901, when it became their permanent home and the central focus of their lives. Karin decorated its interiors in a simple folk style, which included white painted and built-in furniture, wooden floors, embroidered textiles and pots of red geraniums, while Carl portrayed their daily life and their six children in his watercolours. His brightly coloured stylized studies, which captured this idyllic, rural, self-sufficient and carefree lifestyle, were widely reproduced in an album, Ett Hem (Our Home, published in 1899), so as to "reform taste and family lie" The house was sited in the picturesque village of Sundborn in the Dalarna region - the mystic heart of Sweden - and was remodelled and embellished with a refreshing simplicity and characterful warmth by the Larssons in an attempt to evolve a new concept of Swedish domesticity. Believing in the importance of "art in the home", the Larssons decorated practically every surface of the house including the backs of doors, which they adorned with portraits of their children, and walls which they inscribed with heart-warming mottos such as "Love Each Other Children, for Love is All. The Larsson house was a complete realization of the Arts & Crafts goal of "a rural ideal" and was also an important and hugely influential manifestation of the Scandinavian concept of hygge - a joyful domestic cosiness. The integrated and simple lifestyle - moted by Carl and Karin Larsson formed a key part of the foundation from which modern Swedish design evolved, and can be seen as the spiritual progenitor of the IKEA aesthetic.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7fd4a2c9efcd997a3980ce323fbb80a547573845daa27460b4b178050f634357.jpg)

The best parlour at Lilla Hyttnäs - This light, bright and airy room shows a marked contrast to the general stuffiness of bourgeoisie turn-ofthe-century interiors

Carl Larsson, drawing of the best parlour at Lilla Hyttnäs, c. 1900

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0d2d85a7ede1f26e6199e867d06458ba7f28d72ea7540c2cb3b826137a8359db.jpg)

OVERLEAF The dining room at Lilla Hyttnäs - with the most remarkable paper lampshades designed by Karin Larsson

The working room at Lilla Hyttnäs

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6d62620e0551196f1897c70f80942a60bc47abd6fe3905ff41fa87558ba6cc09.jpg)

Poul Christiansen, Model no. 172 Sinus hanging lamp for Le Klint, c. 1972  
Peder Vilhelm Jensen-Klint, originator of Le Klint

## Ie klint

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/227dff03cb2d52093962647dcf8209bafbbf77ac4e06b108bb95a56e2603227c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4eb3b5c17cfdc6ee16b6f335a5c1ec92244611e3de22ada14a5104f6a12f9e6f.jpg)

In 1901, the renowned Danish architect, engineer and craftsman Peder Vilhelm Jensen-Klint (1853-1913) designed a rustic-style stoneware oil-lamp for himself, which he wanted to fit with a simple pleated paper shade. Unable to buy a suitable readymade lampshade, he set about making his own from a piece of parchment. Folding the paper into small pleats along its length, Jensen-Klint devised a "collar" that not only helped to retain the shape of the shade but also kept it at a safe distance from the heat of the lamp's glass chimney. Soon, making lampshades out of folded pleated paper became a pastime enjoyed by other members of his family and in time evolved into a small cottage industry. During this period the architect's eldest son, Tage Klint (1884-1953), devised a method by which the paper-pleated shades could be fitted onto an ordinary lampshade fitment without the need of an additional supporting frame, and in 1943 he founded the Le Klint company to commercialeploit e my obby. The olly his architect-brother Kaare Klint designed the pendant Fruit lamp, a drawing of which is still used as part of the company's logo. Kaare

Klint's "cross-pleated" designs for the company were highly suitable for use in modern interiors, being not only simple and sculptural but also relatively inexpensive to produce. Later, his son, Esben Klint (1915-1969), designed several well known lamps for the company. Other designers were also recruited to design new lamps, including Peter Hvidt and Orla Mølgaard-Nielsen. While still a student at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Poul Christiansen (b. 1947) began developing a hand-folded lampshade that incorporated sine curves, which he first presented to Le Klint in 1967. Immediately, the company's director Jan Klint saw the potential of Christiansen's innovation and put his first lamp design, the Model no. 167, into production. Following this, Christiansen added a new sine curve pendant lamp to the Le Klint range on an annual basis. With their sculptural mathematical curves Christiansen's designs captured the spirit of the late 1960s and early 1970s and made the earlier classic cross-pleated shades look positively dated. Today, Le Klint lamps are still made by hand at the company's Odense factory and

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/15a28a12c165913c14018d48964c7413fc1e16aa26e4ad413d24b98bc6c5b7c9.jpg)  
are internationally recognized as a "shining example of world-class Danish applied art."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0562a3666fc12f8984ce11fd06df54f2176803e4fd5adc6fe50dd13b995e150b.jpg)  
Esben Klint, Model no. 107 hanging lamp, and Andreas Hansen, Model no. 157 hanging lamp for Le Klint, 1950s  
Peder Vilhelm Jensen-Klint, stoneware lamps with folded paper shades, 1901 - the first Le Klint lamp

LEGO bricks with the famous "stud-and-tube" coupling system, invented in 1955

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/657f7063774649feafb47b5717a6f0eabf2d6b875d2d302be616d8eef3781317.jpg)  
Ole Kirk Christiansen, founder of LEGO

## legO (d ar)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/49e0958a7ec5fda1274e1e59a396def2f6f7280fd2f9b6630e72c4bcadbb77f5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/451f95c49666019af828e88bee2f5bb710d615f6f4241a7889629c9d8b2f5f15.jpg)

The origins of the Danish toy company LEGO can be traced to 1932, when the carpenter Ole Kirk Christiansen (1891-1958) founded a workshop to manufacture stepladders, ironing boards and wooden toys. Two years later, he came up with a name for both his toys and workshop, LEGO - a contraction of the Danish words "Leg godt" meaning "play well". It was a remarkable coincidence that the name can also mean "I put together" in Latin. During the 1930s and 1940s, the company produced wooden toys, which were well built and reflected the company motto, "Only the best is good enough". In 1936, LEGO was manufacturing 49 different designs, which were sold for 36 Danish krona per dozen. During the Second World War, the LEGO factory was razed by fire, but its production of toys resumed quickly. mportantly or the future of the company, the war effort accelerated research into new materials and production-methods, including injection-moulded plastics. In 1947, LEGO became the first Danish company to invest in this technology for high-volume plastics manufacture. Two years later, the forerunner of today's LEGO bricks was introduced under the name Automatic Binding Bricks, but was only sold in Denmark. This remarkably simple yet innovative toy mirrored the interest in constructional techniques in the immediate post-war years of reconstruction. In 1954, the toy was renamed LEGO Bricks and a year later, the LEGO name was officially registered. In 1955, the well-known "stud-and-tube" coupling system for the bricks was invented and patented (1958). This new method of connecting enabled both greater stability for models built from the bricks and unlimited building possibilities. Over the succeeding years, the range was expanded to include sloping roof bricks (1958), wheels (1961), the LEGO family (1974) and mini figures (1978). For pre-school children, a larger and easier to use brick system was developed, known as Duplo (1967). LEGO is a unique and hugely successful toy - since 1949 approximately 320 billon LEGO elements have been sold. Appealing to both genders and to a wide range of ages, LEGO provides stimulating play that stretches the imagination and improves dexterity, while teaching children the fundamental principles of construction and, therefore, design. LEGO toys not only exemplify the inclusive child-centred

Automatic Binding Bricks with original packaging, 1949

v The first LEGOLAND theme park opened in Billund (Denmark) on the 7th June 1968 - attracting 625,000 visitors in its first season

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/eb732ccb009a25af8741f35c804e550f1d5d3585c187ee882ae579ce41ba83dc.jpg)  
nature of Scandinavian society, but also epitomize the high-quality and functional integrity of Danish design in general.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9e38d7a0ccdeafe3ad657e3269d3a7b484bc72eac7a3f7ba2543747f06bb9db3.jpg)

# marie gudme leth

(b. 1910 rhus - d. 1997 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e088cea2f42b7c92faba1a1b9a6e4c5d4ba921e4ca10c7324d14f7e2cccfca48.jpg)

The renowned Danish textile designer Marie Gudme Leth studied at the Industrial Art School for Women and the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in Copenhagen. Following this, she trained at the Kunstgewerbeschule (School of Arts and Crafts) in Frankfurt prior to heading the textile department at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts), Copenhagen, from 1931 to 1948. In 1935 she co-founded the textile company Dansk Kattuntrykeri, remaining its director for the next five years, and in 1940 established her own textile workshop, where she specialized in the production of colourful silk-screen printed fabrics. One of her earliest designs, Jagten (1932), shows the strong influence of oriental art, while her slightly later Landsby / (1935) and Landsby // (1936) textiles, which depict scenes of houses, windmills and trees, were inspired by traditional folk art. In these landscapes Gudme Leth also included factories and in so doing acknowledged the increasing industrialization of the Danish countryside. Her Brener textile 937) with ts unfurling fers gnalled a move away from "busy" pictorial scenes. She went on to design numerous textiles with

floral motifs that became increasingly stylized and complex. Her beautiful Kirsebær textile of 1946, for example, would have been difficult to print with its six-colour design. During the 1950s, Gudme Leth's work became increasingly abstract and block-like, with textiles such as Bølger (1955) and Izmir (1955-1960) stylistically predicting the bold and colourful fabrics produced by Marimekko in the 1960s. She was awarded a gold medal at the 1951 (IX) Milan Triennale and her textiles were included in the landmark "Design in Scandinavia" exhibition that toured the United States and Canada from 1954 to 1957. In 1995, a major retrospective of Marie Gudme Leth's work was held at the Danske Kunstindustrimuseum (Danish Museum of Applied Arts) in Copenhagen.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/140a24559ea119c8c68c2e9369bdddf10c0637aedec8a84ee1e0d6e11e2cf91c.jpg)

Stig Lindberg, Pungo Pouch) vase for Gustavsberg, C. 1953

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/914f8fd1ec4931028509afe6afaa28ac6e4f346317108976e2a6d1f3686a0e38.jpg)  
Stig Lindberg, Pottery textile for Nordiska Kompaniet, c. 1947

## stig lindberg

## 1916 Ume, Sweden  d 1982 San Felice Circeo, Italy)

The Swedish designer, Frederick Stigurd (Stig) Lindberg studied in Jönköping and later at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) and the Kungliga Tekniska Högskolan (Royal Institute of Technology) in Stockholm from 1935 to 1937. Between 1937 and 1940, he trained under Wilhelm Kåge at the Gustavsberg ceramics factory and executed a number of geometric designs in the Art Deco style, and at least one asymmetrical piece that presaged his later organic approach to design. During this period, he also briefly studied in Denmark and at the Académie Colarossi in Paris, the latter of which he claimed wasutterccial to his artistca. Lindberg's ceramics were first shown publicly in Stockholm in 1941, and during the post-war years they became widely recognized as exemplars of Scandinavian Modernism. He launched his first range of painted earthenware in 1942 at an exhibition entitled "Fajanser målade i vår" (Faience painted this Spring). His subsequent faience designs introduced whimsical elliptical forms and decorative patterns with bands of colourful stripes. From 1947 to 1949, he designed glassware for Malerás glassworks and textiles

for Nordiska Kompaniet, while also working as a book illustrator. In 1949, Lindberg succeeded Wilhelm Kåge as Gustavsberg's artistic director and over the following years introduced several new ceramic ranges, which included his beautiful Pungo (Pouch) vase (1953). Epitomizing the organic forms that came to typify post-war Scandinavian design, the elegant and sophisticated Pungo vase contrasted strongly with his homely and at times humorous earthenware designs. Lindberg also designed faïence wares painted with scenes inspired by the paintings of Marc Chagall (1887-1985), which were produced in the Gustavsberg studio. His work inother areas of design also possessed a similar pictorial quality, especially his textile designs, such as Pottery (c. 1947). In 1957, Lindberg left Gustavsberg and began teaching at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) in Stockholm (1957-1970). He also began designing glassware for both Holmegaard (1959-1960) and Kosta glassworks (1965). From 1971 to 1980 he resumed his directorship at Gustavsberg and following this established his own studio in Italy. Like many other Scandinavian designers, Lindberg skilfully

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/13e6af69fefd998ab14d8fedaa0d21ad4575216b4827da286f582c142ffe703d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4e8746a07d5002d7830f59189bdefbfb559f52d624944d751dca6a3386d77651.jpg)

Stig Lindberg, Terma stoneware frying pan for Gustavsberg, 1955

Stig Lindberg, Model nos. 243, 245 & 236 vases for Gustavsberg, c. 1949

adapted his style to suit the nature of the material he was working with. His work possessed a classical Scandinavian character and warmth of appeal, which was mainly derived from his use of elegant asymmetrical forms and folk-inspired decorative patterns.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8fe548cd5d8bdd4c3c9ef01dc8268e45102ea7694eb51d7aaa0527b3adb60fc2.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6b5f70abaf2a7a78ae7dfc9933b38b38a1924fcf259347cef94645e46a955870.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2c1ab64adccbc094021cf2a58af0eff9ca0824d7980d32dbf2914c7afd4db1a7.jpg)  
Stefan Lindfors, Boy glassware range for littala, 1999

Stefan Lindfors, Scaragoo table lamp for Ingo Maurer, 1988

# stefan lindfors 2 Maarianhamina, land, Finland

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8a7e1b37a877bf40f7f0d19421d42217a555f3e69676649be3a91320c43d5eb1.jpg)

The Finnish designer and sculptor Stefan Lindfors studied interior and furniture design at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki from 1982 to 1988. While still a student he received a silver medal for his work at the 1986 (XVII) Milan Triennale. After graduating, one of his first commissions was to design the News Studio for Yleisradio (the Finnish Broadcasting Company). In 1988, he designed his widely publicized Scaragoo table lamp, which was constructed of aluminium, steel, plastic and concrete. This curious insect-like halogen lamp, which is activated by touch, became one of the best known Post-Modern Scandinavian designs once it was put into production by the German lighting manufacturer Ingo Maurer in 1990. As a subversive design that blurred the boundary between functional objects and works of art, the Scaragoo led Lindfors to become widely recognized as one of the leading protagonists of avant-garde design in the late 1980s and early 1990s. In 1990, he designed the interior of the café at the Taideteollisuusmuseo (Museum of Industrial Arts), Helsinki, and three years later the interior for the Marimekko shop in Helsinki. Apart from his various interior projects,

Lindfors has also produced numerous product designs for among others, Martela, Asko, P.O. Korhonen, Vitra, Skanno, Kalevala, Belus and Alessi. In 1992, he received perhaps the highest Nordic cultural accolade - the Väinö Tanner Trailblazer) Award. Four years later, he created a sculpture entitled Winged Victory for the Swedish Pavilion at the Olympic Games in Atlanta, and also designed the "Freedom of Speech" sculpture exhibition at the Finnish Embassy in Washington DC. In 1997, Lindfors designed his EgO range of ceramics for Arabia and the following year contributed designs to the first collection of Hackman tools, for which he won a Design Plus Award in 1999 and a Good Design Award in 2000. By brign highlytt artsility design of functional objects, Lindfors produces work that is quirky but nonetheless distinctly Scandinavian in character.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5985b87d1c208c5f1758b8f1aba9f04bbdf32b3f48075ecdc5368185a4459dbc.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/487e802ed48c99be33c66687e55d67b4f13615bd2d14c0ae0b94c0f117e22f01.jpg)  
Vicke Lindstrand, Fishing Net vase for Kosta, 1951

# vicke lindstrand

## (b. 1904 Gothenburg - d. 1983 Smland, Sweden)

The renowned Swedish glass designer Victor Emmanuel (Vicke) Lindstrand studied graphic design at the Konstindustriskolan (School of Arts & Crafts) in Gothenburg from 1924 to 1927. From 1922 to 1928, he also contributed cartoons and illustrations to two local newspapers and briefly studied in France and Italy. In 1928, he began working for the Orrefors glassworks designing glassware that was classical in form and often engraved with stylized figurative motifs in the Art Deco style. Objects such as these together with other painted glass pieces by Lindstrand were exhibited at the 1930 Stockholm Exhibition and received much critical acclaim. Several of his designs were adorned with muscular female nudes, which reflected the continuing and pervasive influence of Cubism. He later designed a window for the Swedish pavilion at the 1937 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne" and a glass fountain for the 1939 New York World's Fair. Around this time, he began developing a softer and more fluid style, with his engraved decoration becoming more rhythmic. During this period his modelling of figurative motifs also became increasingly gentle

and more naturalistic. Although he continued producing designs for Orrefors until 1941, Lindstrand also worked for the Karlskrona Porslinsfabrik from 1935 to 1936 and Upsala-Ekeby from 1936 to 1950, where he was art director from 1943 to 1950. Between 1950 and 1970, Lindstrand ran his own studio in Århus and was also the design director of Kosta Boda. During the 1950s, the glassware he designed for this company had a strong and identifiable character and predicted the bold forms that became so fashionable in Swedish glass the following decade. While at Kosta, he produced some of his most sculptural work, much of which was influenced by the work of contemporary Venetian designers such as Fulvio Bianconi. Unlike the Italians, however, Lindstrand's designs were distinguished by less expressive organic forms and by a more restrained use of colour. During the early 1960s he experimented with engraving polished cullets of waste glass, the results of which possessed a raw and icy character that suited his motifs of Laplanders and reindeers. Lindstrand's transition from engraved formal glassware to sculptural free-blown vessels to "art" glass sculptures testified to his

Vicke Lindstrand, vase for Kosta, c. 1954

Vicke Lindstrand, Cirkus Circus) vase for Orrefors, 1930

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/08bc9e781f3de71f5415ce1fbd6efef54f4009ad1f4619172bf6014dbd2ed7ce.jpg)

complete understanding and mastery of the medium. As one of the leading figures in Swedish glass design, Lindstrand was among the first designers to work within a Modernist idiom. His greatest legacy, however, was his later, pioneering, organic-form glassware that came to typify the "New Look" of Scandinavian design during the post-war years.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/398f93c643008ae372174ef5eaa3c3912791d9095c896435faa1fd4ef728bdc9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2ccc62d39d1ee362ff896ff308c2f794a807fd1be10c4196f70e427f646e745d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b63e939c9dba9a7249f37963fb76f2c471ce63b522060e02c9511463c532988e.jpg)  
Poul Henningsen, PH hanging lamp for Louis Poulsen, c. 1927

# louis poulsen

The well-known Danish lighting company, Louis Poulsen evolved from a cork-trading and general ironmongery business, which from 1892 had an "electrical department" that supplied carbon arc lamps. Louis Poulsen (1871-1934), the nephew of Ludwig R. Poulsen (1846-1896), the founder of the original enterprise, headed the business from 1906 and two years later took over premises in Nyhavn, Copenhagen. The turning point in the then wholesale business occurred in 1911 when Sophus Kaastrup-Olsen (1884-1938) acquired a 50% stake in Poulsen's electrical division. Olsen was a true believer in electric power, as well as a political radical. In 1917, Louis Poulsen retired and Olsen became the sole proprietor of the company. Almost immediately, he began benefiting from the increase in demand brought about by the end of the First World War and the return of the long overlooked Southern Jutland region to Denmark. By 1918, Louis Poulsen had a considerable turnover of 5 million krona, of which 600,000 krona was clear profit. Although the 1920s were a financially turbulent period for the company, it gained a valuable market share after Olsen came into contact with the lighting designer Poul Henningsen, who once claimed that Olsen "was just as mad as I was". From 1924, Henningsen produced lighting designs for the company that attempted to soften the dazzling effect of electric carbon arc lighting by means of multi-shade configurations. As he noted: "The fact is that electric light is defective and ought not to be condoned in a room where people gather. Its defects are also so profound that electric light, despite its ease of use, low price and abundance, is still unpopular and considered a necessary evil by civilized people." To solve the inherent problems associated with electric lighting, Henningsen's revolutionary light fixtures reflected light in such a way that the glare was effectively defused. These remarkably modern designs, which were both beautiful and functional, won a gold medal at the 1925 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes". A year later, Henningsen designed the famous PH series of lamps, which remain in production today. The PH lamps were startlingly modern for their time and appealed especially to architects and interior designers - Alvar Aalto, for example, incorporated them in his design for the auditorium at Turku in Finland. These "shadow-free" lamps with their three highly effective light-diffusing shades were also used in large public buildings, such as the Cologne Railway Station in 1929, as well as in smaller domestic and commercial premises. By 1931, 30,000 PH lamps had been sold around the world. Louis Poulsen managed to survive the Second World War, and during the post-war years continued to expand by establishing international subsidiaries and commissioning other well-known designers, such as Arne Jacobsen, King & Miranda and Alfred Homann, to design domestic and contract lighting products for the company. By 1997, Louis Poulsen had a workforce of 1,090 and wholly-owned subsidiaries in Germany, Sweden, Norway, Finland, Britain, Holland, France, Switzerland, Australia, Japan and the United States of America. Today, the company continues to produce Poul Henningsen's once-revolutionary designs, alongside classic designs by Arne Jacobsen and Verner Panton, as well as some of the most innovative architectural and street lihting currently available.

# ingeborg Ilundin

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/327532c6a04b3c6a629cba4a1bedcdb6b6af40613b091e8cba1e8d038905c984.jpg)

The Swedish glass designer Ingeborg Lundin trained at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) and the Kungliga Tekniska Högskolan (Royal Institute of Technology) in Stockholm from 1941 to 1946. After completing her studies, she began working at the Orrefors glassworks in 1947. Around this time the older and more established glass designers at Orrefors were pioneering various new techniques, such as Sven Palmqvist's Ravenna technique and Edvin Öhrström's Ariel technique, which brought a new kind of artistry to Swedish glass production. Lundin rejected this new aesthetic and technical approach, preferring instead to use both simple forms and techniques. Her resulting designs, such as the Timglas (Hour-glass) vase (1954) and the well-known Äpple (Apple) vase (1955), were remarkable for their pure forms and informal elegance. The Apple vase is essentially a large bubble of glass that emphasizes the inherent qualities of the material - transparency, lightness and fragility. In 1954 Lundin was awarded the prestigious Lunning Prize and the following year exhibited her glassware at the landmark "H55" exhibition in Helsing borg. The 1950s were a period of relative prosperity that could financially support the proliferation of objects of high-quality craftsmanship. As a result, designers such as Lundin became widely acclaimed for their one-off studio pieces. Her work was often engraved with abstract "jottings" that provided a sense of informality and artistry. During the 1960s, however, Lundin and other designers working outside the industrial process suffered from the economic downturn, as manufacturers looked for ways of reducing costs. As a decorative artist rather than an industrial designer, Lundin eventually left Orrefors in 1971 - times had moved on and studio craftsmanship was no longer considered as important as it had once been. Ironically, however, Lundin was a master of simplicity - a crucial aspect of design for industrial production - but she never abandoned her production of studio pieces in favour of objects for mass manufacture. The aesthetic purity of Lundin's work countered the dominance of dazzling yet complicated industrial techniques employed by many post-war Swedish glass designers, and in so doing reflected a return to the core attributes of Scandinavian design - modesty, beauty, clarity of form and practical simplicity.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dd510b76158ab3aba4bdab9d6400be8a25f369598be7aafa1bdf4b614b61e942.jpg)

Per Lütken, vases for Holmegaard, 1956 - The soft undulating form of this design not only reflects a natural empathy with the material being used but characterizes the Scandinavian idea of "living glass"

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9ecd6dd89fc43c69c2463f117f9a17c5e80bdbd791d5018bbdec417d42e0455a.jpg)

## per lütken

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a0a9230d0b688a50437625303998dde081237d2be85291f9e50b467d4dd56bdf.jpg)

The Danish glassware designer Per Lütken studied painting and technical drawing at the Kunsthndværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts) in Copenhagen. Following the completion of his studies in 1937, he worked as a freelance designer and displayed his first glassware products at the 1942 "Danske Kunsthåndværk" (Danish Arts & Crafts) exhibition held in Stockholm. This early work was distinguished by the use of simple classical forms and colourless glass. In 1946, Lütken succeeded Jacob Bang as art director and chief designer at the Holmegaard and Kastrup glassworks, and initially he perpetuated the classical modern aesthetic of his predecessor. After visiting the (X) Milan Triennale exhibition in 1954 on a study tour of Italy, however, Lütken pioneered a new aesthetic direction for both companies by introducing soft colours and free-flowing forms into his work. The resulting designs, such as his well-known Provence bowls (c. 1956), Beak vases (1951) and Martini jug (1957), epitomized the fluid and gentle organic forms that came to dominate Scandinavian Modern design during the post-war years. Throughout the early 1960s, Lütken experimented with clear and coloured

glass in an effort to achieve dramatic swirling colour combinations. In 1969, he introduced his innovative Lava glassware, which was blown into wet clay moulds in such a way that the impurities were picked up and retained so as to produce a then-fashionable rustic finish. During the 1970s, Lütken's work became increasingly experimental, as his multi-coloured egg-shaped glass sculpture Form (1970) demonstrated. Later in 1978, he introduced his Vintergæek series, which was characterized by the combination of colourless glass flecked with green and white opaline glass. Lütken stated that glassmaking demanded an "intimate, imperative and exciting collaboration based on mutual acquaintanceship between designer and glassmaker" and believed that "the more the finished piece of glass tells us about this process and bears witness to it through the vigour of its form, the better". During his long career at Holmegaard, Lütken redefined the aesthetic of the company's output and produced a number of unique experimental pieces that had much influence on glassware intended for serial production. This hands-on approach to glass design is characteristically Scandinavian - even today it is not uncom-

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5d296858e998e59ad7ff53f5102ed738b4a877db0c64a796339d5585ecfd9ba9.jpg)  
mon for innovative one-off studio pieces to have a major bearing on products designed for highvolume industrial manufacture.  
Per Lütken, shallow dish, Beak vase and self-blown vase for Holmegaard, 1952, 1950 & 1955  
Per Lütken, bowls for Holmegaard, 1942 & 1944

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/03f1a55a766805ce6dfa8984363faf7d75ad3f3f2afd761e6dfdd9a0b8b9c76b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5bc8ee0d10a1ec9a1c1ed0cf49cf5c5ee4058cecbed69f89998a8957bb17ce52.jpg)

Erik Magnussen, salad bowls and salad servers for Stelton, 1986

Enk Magnussen, Thermal Orange) for Stelton, 1976

# erik magnussen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1f9367dad44c53f94eaacc2224ff812d2274456f25c17064fbbdc48749d058a8.jpg)

The Danish designer Erik Magnussen trained as a ceramicist at the Kunsthandvaerkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts) in Copenhagen until 1960. From 1962, he worked as a designer for the porcelain manufacturer Bing & Grøndahl designing simple geometric tableware such as his stoneware teapot with integrated strainer (1963) and his stackable Form 25 Termo service (1965). In 1967, Magnussen was awarded the Lunning Prize for his ceramic designs, which clearly demonstrated his talent for combining artistic sensibilities with an understanding of industrial production techniques. The following year, he designed the highly functional tubular metal and canvas folding Z-chair for Torben Ørskov & Co., which revealed his ability to work within different product categories. In 1969 he designed an innovative teapot for Bing & Grøndahl with a handle that was incorporated into the main body of the vessel.
