Around 1970, he co-designed with Per Kragh-Muller a range of school furniture, including a stacking chair with a frame constructed of a single piece of bent tubular metal onto which elliptical moulded plywood seat and back elements were attached - an extraordinarily rational solution that was reminiscent of Poul Henningsen's

Snake chair (1932). Magnussen also worked for Stelton from 1976 and designed a number of well-known products, including the Thermal Carafe (1976), an extremely elegant and practical insulated jug that was made available n both stainless steel and brightly coloured ABS plastic. This hugely successful thermos, which was intended to complement Arne Jacobsen's Cylinda Line metalware collection (1967), exemplified Magnussen's design principles: functional and aesthetic durability, formal siplicity,easfus, cost-effec iv manufacture. From 78, Magnussen designed hollowware for Georg Jensen and the silversmithy also produced a sterling silver version of his Thermal Carafe. During the 1980s and 1990s, his product designs included the Model no. 301 kerosene lamp for Harlang & Dreyer (1988), a clock and barometer for Georg Christensen, and a table and chair for Paustian (989), the frame of which comprised a continuous piece of bent tubular metal. As a designer who seeks perfection even in the smallest of details, Magnussen's work epitomizes the high object integrity of Danish design - especially in terms of consumer products intended for industrial mass production.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/cd64fd5b0c33cf1be71e1ed7f80c44219ad9a7115265fdf7518dfbfc23aba2c4.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a2e8d7fd0ba75f6c5495b2becd493fa1092b5b04c4dbc03088c1531db06cac25.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b940b6aa420cde75f26ad5dd8611cfd32fda6679b7c8e5382341490bc65a5e95.jpg)

Gunnar Magnusson, Inka nca) chair for Nyvirki Ltd., 1963

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/35cc36bffff75a1773eb100769ce0aefc227c7f7cb93944cc8179889d967dae7.jpg)  
Gunnar Magnusson, Apollo chair for Kristjan Siggeirsson, 1967

# gunnar magnússon (. 13 lau cad)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/95997bd10acc90a8f605691738644a52aaf9212da5038930038d35a9eb2cdfc3.jpg)

One of the leading Icelandic furniture designers, Gunnar Magnusson studied carpentry at the Reykjavik Polytechnic, graduating in 1955. He worked as a carpenter in Reykjavik and Copenhagen until 1959, when he began studying furniture and interior design at the Skolen for Brugskunst (School Applied Arts), Copenhagen. After graduating in 1963, Magnusson began designing furniture for a number of Danish manufacturers and architects in Copenhagen. The same year he also designed the upholstered pine Inka (Inca) chair, which with its exposed construction and squared form projected both directness and purposefulness - common characteristics of Icelandic design. With its use of uncompromising geometric shapes, this design predicted the trend towards more rational forms that took place in Scandinavia during the1970s. In the early 1960s, Magnusson received a number of awards for beds and tables (1962) that he had designed while still a student, and was also awarded fourth prize in the Daily Mirror's first "International Furniture Design Competition" for a suite of bedroom furniture that was notable for its strong geometric forms. In 1964 Magnusson returned to Reykjavik and established his own office specializing in the design of furniture and interiors for offices, banks and homes, as well as for ships and airplanes. In 1967, he designed his moulded plywood Apollo chair, which was inspired by the staging sections of NASA's Saturn V moon rocket used for the Apollo space missions, and was based on the constructional concept of "a central point and two types of curve". As the leading Icelandic designer during the 1970s, Magnusson was commissioned to design the chess table used for the famous 1974 World Chess Championship match between Bobby Fischer and Boris Spasski held in Reykjavik. Magnússon's furniture designs reflected the Icelandic penchant for elemental constructions and functional directness and were included in a number of design shows both at home and abroad, most notably the prestigious Copenhagen Cabinetmakers' Guild Exhibitions.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/46bac0f21c93d0628bf8b829ffacfd584b89c935db5dfdb845d07a64a6d874b0.jpg)

Christian Joachim & Arno Malinowski, tureen for Royal Copenhagen, 1925 - The form and decoration of this design reflects the strong influence of the Art Deco style on Danish design during the mid-1920s. This design won a Grand Prix at the 1925 "Exposition International des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" in Paris. Its shape was designed by Christian Joachim, while its decorative motifs were devised by Arno Malinowski

Arno Malinowski, Model no. 283 Butterfly silver brooch for Georg Jensen, 1942

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bdba0934cb96f34fadab11c95875b214d995c5b0ace01ba267288a4453d1a1d6.jpg)

# arno malinowski

## (b. 1899 Copenhagen - d. 1976 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fe421e81af2dc656fea94a32afb17fe11eb4c3df0b772cfe73a1140dbb43b0d0.jpg)

The Danish sculptor and designer Arno Malinowski served an apprenticeship with the Danish Royal Court engraver, S. Lindahl, from 1914 to 1919 and then studied at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) between 1919 and 1922. From 1921 to 1935, he designed various ceramic objects for the Royal Copenhagen factory, including a number of scantily clad porcelain figurines, which featured oriental blanc-de-chine glazes. His figurines such as the De fem verdensdele (The Five Continents), Islandsk pige (Icelandic Girl) and Bruden (The Bride) were produced as limited editions and were remarkable for the delicacy of their modelling. In 1925, he also decorated a dinner service, the Neo-Classical form of which was designed by Christian Joachim and was adorned with painted and gilded animal motifs, such as a leaping ram and a rather curious heron-like bird in the Art Deco style. This tableware was shown at the 1925 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" and was awarded a Grand Prix. Malinowski also produced a number of stoneware pieces for the Royal Copenhagen factory, which all had a robustness that contrasted strongly

with the extreme delicacy of his porcelain designs and which were based on abstracted natural forms, such as seedpods or fruit. In 1933, he was awarded the coveted Eckersberg Medal and three years later began designing for the Georg Jensen silversmithy. Malinowski's well-known pierced silver Faun and Butterfly brooches (1959) epitomized not only the strong linearity and graphic quality of his work, but also that of the Art Deco style in general. Malinowski also designed the silver and enamel Kongemærke (King's badge brooch) emblem commemorating the 70th birthday of King Christian X that was worn as a sign of patriotism by literally thousands of Danes during the Second World War. The form and decoration of Malinowski's work revealed the pervasive influence of Modernism in Scandinavia during the 1920s and 1930s, while the exquisite refinement of his designs - whether in porcelain, stoneware or silver - demonstrated his superlative craftsmanship and masterful handling of materials.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a9e8cc42dc6cd876eea3fca9d329649201783a70b955af49149bfdfca551c411.jpg)

Carl Malmsten, walnut armchair made in Carl Malmsten's workshop in Stockholm, 1915 - With the design for this chair, Malmsten won a competition for the interior design of Stockholm City Hall thus establishing himself as a major force in Swedish furniture design

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c4cf17bdb6953810c2940dc3f4067fcc357aea0a689efce73eab032fcd92a5c5.jpg)

Carl Malmsten, design for chairs and desk, c. 1912

# carl malmsten (b. 1888 Stockholm, Sweden - d. 1972)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/deb30352f21c46e7b317e5bfcd43f71dd9a0d4a3d69c5427c07edf63df0fb93d.jpg)

One of the leading Swedish furniture designers of the 20th century, Carl Malmsten trained at the Pahlmanns Handelsinstitut and the Stockholms Högskolan (University of Stockholm) in Stockholm until 1908. He also briefly studied economics in Lund in 1910. Between 1910 and 1912, Malmsten served an apprenticeship with the cabinetmaker Per Jönsson and from 1912 to 1915 he studied architecture and handicrafts with the architect Carl Bergsten. In 1916, Malmsten established his own studio in Stockholm and began working as a freelance furniture and interior designer. The same year, he won first and second prize in a competition for the design of furniture for the Stockholm City Hall.The following year his furniture designs were displayed at the Blanchs Konstsalon exhibition in Stockholm. During the late 1910s and 1920s, Malmsten was one of the leading avantgarde designers in Sweden. His work, which mainly comprised modern reworkings of Gustavian forms, was distinguished by functional simplicity and formal clarity. Although he was convinced of the need for a modern approach to design, he also believed that it was important to remain true to the cultural roots of Swedish peasant art, which he saw as

a means of providing his product solutions with warmth and character. While the austerity of his furniture anticipated the Modern Movement, Malmsten rejected the stark and alienating Modernism of the buildings and interiors at the 1930 Stockholm Exhibition, declaring "Moderation lasts. Extremism palls ... and in any event there is always a place for some of both, and every conceivable degree of variation between the two." The same year as this landmark exhibition, he founded the Carl Malmstens Verkstadsskola (Handicraft School) in Stockholm, presumably in opposition to the pure functionalism being championed by designers such as Erik Gunnar Asplund. Apart from his design and teaching work, he also headed his own companies, Firma Carl Malmsten (from 1933) and Carl Malmsten AB (from 1944). Throughout his career Malmsten promoted a humano-centric approach to design - near the end of his life, in the late 1960s and early 1970s, he co-designed with his son Vidar a range of furniture suited to the needs of geriatric users, which included chairs with raised seat heights, sraight backs and upward curving armrests to facilitate sitting, resting and rising. By synthesizing traditional European styles with

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/34012fb2fad03cfab410eabaa4cc6357ae0b207730f90f7b17668fe9365a4583.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3803142712f541927cf088a360951d001cd12efbd898cc086fccb15696bd9f2b.jpg)

Swedish vernacularism and simplified, functionalist forms, while stressing the importance of production quality, elegant proportions and practicalutility, Malmsten developed a hybrid idiom from which modern Swedish design would evolve. The influence of his work on later Swedish designers, such as Bruno Mathsson, cannot be overstated.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b21501178b3e39062483eda6d289aea4f2afcb53fec391454cf796ad802818aa.jpg)  
Marimekko store at Kalevankatu 4, Helsinki, early 1960s

# marimekko

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6ad19b089472d8de236a1cb4b2fc39700e1b348f59ef8927bfe2d90e30f173c4.jpg)

The origins of the internationally celebrated Finnish textile company Marimekko can be traced to a textile printing company known as Printex, which had been established by Viljo Ratia (b. 1911) in 9 in response to the dire shortage of clothing in Finland during the immediate post-war years. His wife, Armi (1912-1979), who had trained at the Ateneum School of Art, decided that it was important to manufacture textiles that were different from what was already on the market, which for women at the time mainly comprised rather drab printed florals. Armi subsequently designed a couple of patterns that sold relatively well, but she was not particularly keen on becoming a designer and so began looking for someone else to fill this role. To this end she approached her old friend, the rector of the Ateneum School of Art, Arttu Brummer (1891-1951), who suggested that the company hire Maija Isola. Having been duly appointed the principal designer at Printex, Isola devised a number of bold non-figurative silk-screen printed patterns using inexpensive cotton sheeting. In May of 1951, Armi and Viljo Ratia founded a new company, Marimekko - which means "Mari's dress" or, in other words, "a simple everyday dress without frills" -, with the aim of promoting Printe textiles through it for use in interiors and clothing, because although people liked the company's textiles "they did not know how or exactly where to use them." In the Official Register of Trading Associations it was noted that, "The purpose of the (new) company is the production of all kinds of clothes and clothing accessories, including madeto-order, as well as their wholesale and retail, import and export." Marimekko's first collection, which was exceptionally bright and colourful for its time, was launched in the spring of 1951. Although the fashion show was a critical success, it was not a financial one. Worse was to follow over the next few years when the parent company Printex went into bankruptcy. Marimekko struggled to keep afloat, despite receiving huge press interest in its products. In 1953, Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi joined the design team at Marimekko and subsequently devised the company's "anti-fashion" design policy. Setting rather than following trends, Nurmesniemi's landmark clothing designs were born out of her belief in the primacy of form and its relationship to material. The outlines of her clothes were as bold and elemental as the fabrics she

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2c43ded39994da8ee6480447247c83d3395d80ba24d9a8f68687560c955f1e0b.jpg)

Maija Isola, Melooni (Melon) textile for Marimekko, 1963

Various dress designs created for Marimekko by Annika Rimala - clockwise from top left: Chickweed (using Buckwheat textile - co-designed with Oiva Toikka), 1961; Plinth (using Hatch extile), 1963; Circus (using Petrooli textile), 1963; Glitter (using Packet textile), 1965; Outfitter (using Big Square xtile 65 Line Sig sig Gallery textile designed by Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi in 1954), 1965; Balcony (using Garden textile), 1963; Lupin (using Garden textile), 1963; Seaweed (using Oasis textile), 1966

Fujiwo Ishimoto, Korsi textile, 1989 - inspired by Annika Rimala's boldly striped Tasaraita textile that had become synonimous with the Marimekko name during the late 1960s and early 1970s

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9fa3e9e952b06ef1e59351ccfc54f70c6623edf5da914dc0eefa199896d20134.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e2c7fbfa55d88810157cfd01a40c299e4d93ff1848cd795cdd4adbf633b7cbd7.jpg)  
Magazine cover showing Marimekko dress designed by Annika Rimala, 1966

designed for them. Marimekko's projection of its brand image was similarly progressive - the models used in its promotional photography had a very natural look, while the clothes "appeared to take a back seat". It was not until 1955, however, that the company was able to purchase its own sewing machines, which eventually allowed it to turn the financial corner. In 1958, the international reputation of Marimekko was established when the company exhibited at the World's Fair in Brussels. This exhibition led to a number of export contacts including Benjamin Thompson who began importing Marimekko clothes into the United States in 1959. A year later Jacqueline Kennedy during her husband's presidential campaign bought seven of the company's dresses at Thompson's Design Research Store in Cape Cod, which helped Marimekko gain considerable publicity. As Viljo Ratia remembered, "Jacqueline was criticized for her expensive Paris fashions. So it was a choice morsel for the Kennedy press (team) when they could tell their readers that Mrs Kennedy had just bought some inexpensive Finnish cottons." Certainly Marimekko bucked the trend by insisting on the use of 100 % cotton rather than on the new

wonder material - nylon. In 1959, Armi Ratia met the designer Annika Rimala (b. 1936), who the following year took over from Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi - who left Marimekko to start her own company, Vuokko Oy - as artistic director of Marimekko. Rimala's early textile designs, such as Hilla (Cloudberry), were rather subdued, but these were soon followed by bolder and more brightly coloured textiles that had an attention-grabbing, youthful appeal. The simple, chic yet casual clothes pioneered at Marimekko by Rimala, including the rather curious Ryppypeppu dungarees (1965) with their ruched behind, were similarly unconventional. Appealing in particular to professional creatives, Marimekko clothing was described by the influential American Women's Wear Daily as virtually a "uniform for intellectuals". Just as the swinging Sixties were drawing to a close and about to be overtaken by the more thoughtful and conformist 1970s, Rimala introduced her versatile Tasaraita collection in 1969. Made from her trademark evenstriped tricots, this landmark range of clothing (comprising T-shirts, nightgowns, underwear and shirts) was intended to complement the growing fashion for blue jeans. Easy-to-wear and casual,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9889130951a17aba3128d42ca2060c126de23bdec80467f8a6ed48da3dbd5771.jpg)

the Tasaraita collection was an instant success with its basic blue-and-white and red-and-white stripes, and in some ways mirrored the colourful yet no-nonsense simplicity of the emerging High-Tech style that was being pioneered in Scandinavia by among others Johan Huldt and Jan Dranger. From the mid-1960s to the present, Marimekko has also produced innovative textiles and clothing by other designers, including Liisa Suvanto (1910- 1983), Fujiwo Ishimoto (b. 1941), Katsuji Wakisaka (b. 1944), Pentti Rinta (b. 1946), Marja Suna (b. 1934), Ristomatti Ratia (b. 1941), Albert Turick (b. 1953) and most recently Antti Eklund (b. 1960). Although Marimekko was sold after Armi Ratia's death in 1979, the company enshrined her core belief that "You have to be different" - a principle that has stood the company in good stead for over fifty years. Through its pioneering textiles and clothes Marimekko not only brought Finnish design to a wider international audience, but also played an instrumental role in defining some of its key attributes - bold outlines, bright colours and innovative forms.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7853b653947b1f03666267f23604a96e7a653ee90ceb6a47e44d6a0edb27638a.jpg)

Annika Rimala, Petrooli textile for Marimekko, 1965

Marimekko dress and hat featured on the cover of Mobilia, January 1964

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8d3bd2968e230e666bf11f5991469ec6aae1405eed5e4927c37e67b244fda97e.jpg)  
Sven Markelius, textile for Nordiska Kompaniet, 1958

# sven markelius

## (b. 1889 Stockholm - d. 1972 Stockholm, Sweden)

The Swedish architect and designer Sven Markelius studied at the Kungliga Tekniska Högskolan (Royal Institute of Technology), Stockholm, from 1909 to 1913 and later trained at the Kungliga Akademien för de fria Konstrena (Royal Academy of Fine Arts) from 1913 to 1915. He then served an apprenticeship in the architecture practice of Ragnar Östberg (1866-1945), where he assisted in the design of the façade of Stockholm's City Hall. Markelius's language of design was initially inspired by Romanticism and then later by Neo-Classicism. After becoming aware of Le Corbusier's (1887-1965) architecture and the ideas emanating from the Bauhaus, he began to embrace Modernism. For the 1930 Stockholm Exhibition, he designed exhibition buildings as well as an interior that included an innovative orangelacquered beech and linoleum writing desk (1930), which was manufactured by Nordiska Kompaniet. He also exhibited a lounger at the Stockholm Fair that was remarkable for its pioneering undulating organic form. In 1932, he designed the concert hall in Helsingborg, which included site-specific functionalist stacking chairs. He also designed the Swedish pavilion for the 1939 New York

World's Fair, which brought him widespread international recognition. From 1938 to 1944 he was a member of the Building Administration in Stockholm, and after the Second World War was elected to the United Nations Planning Board and was a member of the UNESCO Arts and Building Committees. From 1944 to 1954, Markelius headed the Stockholm Planning Office and devised a scheme for expanding the city, which included suburban communities known as "town sections". During his career, he designed numerous buildings in Sweden, including the Building Union's headquarters and the People's House in Stockholm, and also assisted in the design and planning of the United Nations building in New York. During the 1950s, Markelius also designed a range of printed textiles with Astrid Sampe for Nordiska Kompaniet, which were marketed by Knoll Textiles. His Pythagoras textile (1952) reflected his continuing interest in strong geometric forms. As one of the most important pioneers of modern design in Sweden, Markelius' work exemplified the less dogmatic Scandinavian approach to Modernism - his wooden furniture, for example, while plain and undecorated, rejected the functionalist

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6d1fe375eab8af088908dd9f3f3e328785da1c3828a0a6c45fa1dfae29fb73c7.jpg)  
machine aesthetic of the Bauhaus in favour of a more humanistic language of design.  
Sven Markelius, writing desk for Nordiska Kompaniet, 1930  
Sven Markelius, sitting room installation for Nordiska Kompaniet that was exhibited at the 1930 Stockholm Exhibition - This landmark exhibition established him as one of the foremost exponents of Swedish Functionalism or "Funkis" during the 1930s

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8cdc062ec6bcccda0e4b5a0d0500ca878052cdb16bbdfa3fedbffb6cbbba4bfa.jpg)

Bruno Mathsson in his glasshouse in Värnamo, 1950

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4b4739f49d68cdbbd890e1313dc83a4bd825e175c84ba31ade6252bff16dba0b.jpg)  
Bruno Mathsson, Eva chair for Karl Mathsson, 1933-1936 - originally known as the Arbetsstol (Working Chair)

# bruno mathsson

(b. 1907 Värnamo - d. 1988 Värnamo, Sweden)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0fc2affff777b72c82d6153d704aa6b138b4a209788632532e2ef45bddaa7b2b.jpg)

Alongside Carl Malmsten and Erik Gunnar Asplund, Bruno Mathsson is recognized as one of the great pioneers of modern Swedish furniture design. He trained as a cabinetmaker with his father Karl Mathsson, and from 1933 designed furniture mainly for his family's business Firma Karl Mathsson in Värnamo. During the early 1930s the "Funkis" movement dominated Swedish design practice with designers such as Axel Larsson and Sven Markelius, producing functionalist furniture designs in wood with strong geometric forms. In contrast, Mathsson promoted a new approach to the design of seat furniture that involved the use of soft undulating organic forms, which were better suited to the shape of the user. Believing that furniture should be designed for how people sit naturall, Mathsson designed his seating from an ergonomic perspective with its contours closely echoing the form of the human body. For added flexibility and comfort he also made great use of breathable jute or hemp webbing, which did away with the need for bulky upholstery. The first chair Mathsson designed using these principles was his Arbetssto/ (Working Chair). Developed between 1933 and 1936, the Arbetsstol not only afforded the sitter greater comfort but both aesthetically and philosophically it represented an important generational shift in Swedish design, from geometric Functionalism torganic Modernism. Mathsson's furniture signalled a new sculptural confidence in Scandinavian product design, though this became a defining feature more strongly associated with Danish and Finnish design than Swedish design during the postwar years. It is often overlooked that Mathsson's highly organic Arbetsstol and Pernilla chaise longue (c. 1934), both with bent laminated wood frames, woven hemp webbing and solid beech seat frames, predate Alvar Aalto's similarly constructed Model no. 43 chaise longue (1936) and Model no. 406 chair (1936-1939). Although Mathsson's designs were less utilitarian than Aalto's, they were more ergonomically resolved and were somewhat more forward-looking. In 1936, Mathsson was given a one-man show at the Röhss Museum for Design and Applied Art, Gothenburg, and a year later he participated in the 1937 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne". Between 1945 and 1957 he mainly concentrated on architecture,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/da6b0795a34cc04d6f78de05cfed1dd08480b2d943f22b98d82c2281050c428b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dbf77df4faa9c7ace4ca8600538e599811e4159175efb73b48226a030cd64d87.jpg)

Living area in Bruno Mathsson's own summerhouse in Frösakull, 1961

Bruno Mathsson, Pernilla lounge chair for Karl Mathsson, c. 1934

designing several simple structures in glass, wood and concrete for use as summerhouses and schoolrooms. He took over the management of the Firma Karl Mathsson in 1957 and from 1958 began developing furniture in collaboration with the mathematician and designer Piet Hein, which included the famous Superellipse table (1964) that was later manufactured by Fritz Hansen. Mathsson also designed furniture for DUX Industrier in Trelleborg, including most notably the Karin chair (1968) - a proto-High-Tech design with a tubular metal frame and hemp canvas seat/back sling with upholstered cushions. As one of Sweden's leading mid-century designers, Mathsson was awarded the Gregor Paulsson Trophy in 1955 and his furniture was exhibited in numerous one-man shows and a number of hugely influential group exhibitions, including the 1939 New York World's Fair, the 1946 Copenhagen "Svenska Form" (Swedish Form), the 1955 Helsingborg "H55", the 1957 Berlin "Interbau" and the Paris 1958 "Formes Scandinaves". Throughout his career, Mathsson devoted himself to the continual refinement of ideal types based on structural purity and humano-centric logic. The fact that his

chairs remain in production today must be seen as a vindication of his guiding belief in the success and abiding appeal of these values. When in 1978 DUX Industrier took over Firma Karl Mathsson and the production of Mathsson's furniture, it changed the name of the Working Chair to Eva.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e465f23f3cebd7a5c5a8dea18a54c8c5f95ee24e3f147d021dfb8163f1a8394b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c33a1a962950f74ea77b6ec71e94a804aaedd2306f2bfdc104a9156d3e76b163.jpg)  
Grethe Meyer, Firepot range for Royal Copenhagen, 1976

# grethe meyer

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c6e85d8d2379c99aaf66b4c43ce2e7f72992b6b2acdb30c2e583b452a845f1d6.jpg)

The Danish architect and designer Grethe Meyer studied architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) and from 1944 to 1955 worked on the editorial staff of Byggebogen (The Building Manual). From 1955 to 1960 she worked at the State Institute for Building Research, where she undertook research with Paul Kjærgaard and Bent Salicath on the standard dimensions used in the design of housing and consumer products. During this period she also designed the Boligens Byggeskabe for C. Danel and Öresund cabinet-storage systems (1957) with Børge Mogensen for Karl Andersson & Söner Möbelfabrik, and collaborated with Ibi Trier Mørch on the design of stacking glassware (1959) for the Kastrup glassworks. The practical and formal simplicity of her early utilitarian glassware was informed by her influential research into the standardized sizing of products. In 1960, she began designing tableware for the Royal Copenhagen porcelain factory and the same year established her own design practice. Although she subsequently dedicated herself to a career in architecture, she gained an international reputation for her glassware and ceramic designs. Her earthenware Bla Kant

(Blue Rim) service (1965) and ovenproof Ildpot (Firepot) range (1976) not only demonstrated her understanding of form and mass, but also her knowledge of industrial production techniques. With the aim of creating things people can afford" Meyer's product designs combined simple forms with material tactility and were intended to be as functional as possible. Her Firepot range, for example, was stackable for ease of storage, graduated in size according to the dimensions of the average oven and standard food portions, and could even withstand being put into a hot oven directly from the freezer. High-integrity household products such as these can be seen as a material expression of the "cult of domesticity" common to Scandinavia and reflected Meyer's belief that "one should buy fewer items of better quality." Around 1982, she designed the basic shapes of the Picnic tableware range for Royal Copenhagen, which were remarkably pure in form but somewhat marred by Ole Kortzau's (b. 1939) brightly coloured printed motifs of bicycles, crashing waves and striped parasols. As one of the leading designers of her day, Meyer was awarded the Danish ID Prize in 1965 and the Scandinavian Industrial Art and

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5158343b9f8d9898f667cfc93876f7172b299e16000dbd95e1c3ff5867377ed0.jpg)  
Grethe Meyer, Blue Line earthenware tableware for Royal Copenhagen, 1965 - This service received a Danish Industrial Design Prize

Design Award in 1973. Meyer's utilitarian ceramics exemplified her detailed analysis of use, and although they were industrially mass-produced, they were humanizing products that projected the soft yet rational aesthetic that is so characteristic of Scandinavian industrial design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e3f313aeceafdf9f501bf43eb35005ea0a353483fbb9f8d5cfe45bec9ca4a0bb.jpg)  
Grethe Meyer, Model no. 340 Copenhagen cutlery for Georg Jensen, 1991

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b11d1cac07ea8eed2bb89812d3664ef75ff725c6f4c7c2368014e29dbcb10b52.jpg)  
Børge Mogensen, Model no. 2226 Spanish chair for Fredericia Furniture, 1959- Based on a vernacular Spanish chair, this design delights in the natural and humanizing qualities of its materials

# børge mogensen

(b. 1914 Aalborg - d. 1972 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/efa4a49f01ab17e1620585e852b83959a4b93c4bd40341caeaf5ed7300762000.jpg)

The well-known Danish furniture designer Borge Mogensen studied at the Kunsthåndværkerskolan (School of Arts & Crafts), Copenhagen, from 1936 to 1938. He later trained under Kaare Klint at the furniture school of the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, from 1938 to 1941. Under Klint's guidance, Mogensen studied the proportions of a number of "ideal types", such as the traditional English Windsor chair, and was encouraged to update forms such as these within a modern idiom. Klint's pioneering studies into anthropometrics - the systematic collection and correlation of measurements of the human body - also had a significant formative influence on Mogensen's work. In 1940, he began designing furniture specifically for young people - a completely novel endeavour at the time - under the "Hansen's Attic" and later "Peter's Bedroom" labels. Between 1942 and 1950, Mogensen headed the furniture design section of the Association of Danish Cooperative Wholesale Societies and designed simple utilitarian pieces. In 1944, he designed an armchair closely based on a traditional English Windsor chair and the simple yet refined beech and papercord J-39 Shaker chair, which was strongly influenced by the purity and integrity of American Shaker furniture. Importantly, this latter design incorporated standardized components and was one of the first Danish furniture designs to speculate on industrial mass-production. A year later, Mogensen designed his well known Model no. 1789 sofa that was similarly inspired by earlier precedents. Between 1945 and 1947, he also worked as an assistant to Klint at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi. In 1950, he established his own studio and subsequently designed furniture for Søborg Møbelfabrik, Fredericia Furniture and Karl Andersson & Söner. Mogensen also undertook ergonomic research, which resulted in the Öresund (1957) and Boligens Byggeskabe (1956) cabinetstorage systems designed in collaboration with Grethe Meyer. Like his mentor, Kaare Klint, Mogensen's approach to furniture design involved the reworking of traditional types, as his Spanish chair (1959) and Asserbo chair (1964) most notably demonstrate. His furniture was distinguished by the frequent use of attractive blond beech wood, masculine proportions and superlative craftsmanship, which reflected the skill of the

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/437ee2cf88ab2b046449410b3cfa7b702b02d5192055eda941bfa3e0831e4ff8.jpg)

Danish cabinetmakers he worked with, most notably Erhard Rasmussen. From 1953, he also co-designed with Lis Ahlmann several furnishing textiles for C. Olesen. Above all else, Mogensen's well-designed and well-executed furniture epitomizes Danish design - the use of natural materials, simplicity, high-quality construction and the re-interpretation of historically successful forms.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2595da055206ba425c687a818d7f9827a52b1ea72a0695d02c63a6ab5a1cd96c.jpg)  
Børge Mogensen, Asserbo chairs for Karl Andersson & Söner, 1964  
Børge Mogensen, Shaker J-39 chair for FDB, 1944

Børge Mogensen, room with teak furniture designed 1947 - The sofas were upholstered with a textile that was designed by Lis Ahlmann

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3537c241bb9f5e765748b1e6c2ac08e0c0adea5a44a1db45240ba484bae3f9d5.jpg)

Børge Mogensen, teak chair executed by Erhard Rasmussen, 1949 - A contemporary commentator noted of this design, "Børge Mogensen has created a product which will serve as a model for future chairs."

Børge Mogensen, drawing for Model no. 1789 sofa, 1945

Berge Mogensen, design for teak chair, 1949

Berge Mogensen, Model no. 1789 Spindle Back sofa for Fritz Hansen, 1945 - Like many of Mogensen's designs this sofa was a modern reinterpretation of a traditional seating type

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/015583394ecd088fb2fc7c0a3da34059ebe2b087361b939a1b8b5fd0ab714762.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7fe902528dd3265fff80d52a4377e2d4cbf4dac7655e3f1c28e99f3055466c9e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/816402ece3af3651398cae0c4643d078a089593bee8183ffc515d4ed504e57c0.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f43ece8a2ed628de2800364ba330fea8beb3257350a145c7e1885375e1e62a21.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/34a62942e591a2afbf367dc8e05f09d88cb3eaed389d136821a92422e3a37b5e.jpg)  
Gerhard Munthe, Den Klogefugl tapestry,1903

# gerhard munthe

Gerhard Munthe studied painting under J.F. Eckersberg (1822-1870), Knud Bergslien (1827-1908) and Julius Middlethun in Oslo. He also trained in Düsseldorf in 1874 and from 1877 to 1882 lived and worked in Munich. In 1886, Munthe with fellow artists Christian Skredsvig (1854-1924), Harriet Backer (1845-1932), Kitty Kielland (1843-1914), Eilif Petersen (1852-1928) and Erik Werenskiold (1955-1938) established an artists' collective at the Fleskum farm in Dælivannet, some fifteen kilometres west of Oslo (then known as Christiania). During what has become known as the "Fleskum Summer", the collective pioneered a Neo-Romantic style that reflected Norway's desire to assert its own cultural identity. The group was also inspired by Japanese art thanks to the recent publication of Karl Madsen's Japansk Malerkunst (1885), which was the first Scandinavian book to explore Japanese painting. During the late 1880s and early 1890s, Munthe's textiles and wallpaper designs (many now in the collection of the Oslo Museum of Applied Art) and under-glaze ceramics - such as his Blue Anemone service produced by the Porsgrund porcelain factory (1892) - were strongly influenced by the art of Japan, with some incorporating Mon style motifs while others were inhabited by swirling koi carp and stylized cranes. Inspired on the one hand by Japanese art and on the other by the cultural strength of the artistic community in Denmark, in the winter of 1890-1891 Munthe developed the idea of revitalizing the decorative arts of his own country by pioneering a distinct Norwegian aesthetic. He subsequently drew inspiration from ancient Norwegian legends as well as his country's indigenous flora and fauna. In his designs he also incorporated motifs found in medieval "dragon-style" woodcarvings and folk art tapestries. His bold Neo-Viking narrative tapestries were exhibited at the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelleet Internationale" and alongside work by other Scandinavian designers - led a commentator in the Studio magazine (1901) to note that, "In these remote countries a powerful art movement is forcing its way into the general art development of Europe." Munthe believed that tradition was not "ancient romance or history" but a crucial element in "the developing power of the nation itself". He attempted to bring about a dynamic synthesis of the past and the present so as to realize objects that were infused with a vital national spirit. As the greatest proponent of the Viking Revival, Munthe's work was imbued with a strong symbolism that reflected the Norwegians' overwhelming desire for cultural, social and political autonomy. His decoration of the medieval Hákon Hall in Bergen was generally acknowledged as the culmination of the National Romantic movement in Norway - sadly, however, it was destroyed during the Second World War.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/55e224274e7c88eed325545f2f9846ad4bb1cd630aabf80263e7d1510ce4f297.jpg)

Gerhard Munthe, chair from the Fairy Tale Room at Holmenkollen Tourist Hotel, 1896

Gerhard Munthe, Fairy Tale Room at Holmenkollen Tourist Hotel, 1896

Gerhard Munthe, Blue Anemone service for Porsgrund, 1892

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5f777520d812ebc5bb739caac407bfde49e3414103b0f7e515dd22f02bc44630.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3acd9da0c8809df2f27ade2665c6590fff723401f3f628ef1d9dae5f766e74cd.jpg)

Harald Nielsen, Mode/ no. 600B silver teapot for Georg Jensen, 1930

Harald Nielsen, Model no. 15 Pyramid silver cutlery for Georg Jensen, 1926

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/98b54d460618992d64d802ff98d2c3a69c9b2731be4efcf51212c733e6255b8c.jpg)

# harald nielsen

The Danish metalware designer Harald Nielsen was the brother-in-law of Georg Jensen and one of his closest colleagues. He began an apprenticeship at the Georg Jensen silversmithy in 1909 and initilly worked as a chaser, later gaining the espnsibilitytranslating Geor Jensen and Johan Rohde's rough sketches into highly finished objects. During the inter-war period, Nielsen worked as a silverware designer, producing numerous pieces of hollowware as well as a few flatware patterns for the Georg Jensen firm. Preferring stylized ornament to naturalistic decoration, Nielsen's Art Deco designs were frequently based on quasi-classical forms, yet he imbued them with a sense of modernity. His silver Pyramid cutlery of 1927 demonstrated a remarkable formal simplicity and like the work ofJohan Rohde, reflected the silversmithy's shift towards more modern forms. Reputedly, upon seeing the Pyramid flatware for the first time Jensen remarked, "It's neat but it won't sell", ut it nonetheless went on to become one of the firm's best selling designs. Working alongside Jensen and Rohde, Nielsen made a significant contribution o thedevelopment of the Georg

Jensen style during the 1920s and 1930s with his smooth-surfaced yet softened geometric forms. After the Second World War, Nielsen became the firm's artistic director and as such, was resons ible for the introduction of sculptural rganic wares created by a younger generation of highly talented designers. During his career, Nielsen executed numerous designs for jewellery and tableware bowls, trays, dishes, candlesticks, coffee pots and tea services - that epitomized the bold forms an sculptural fluidity of the Danish Art Deco style.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/055863c6ef481a6a51ce8dd90fc30ac9bd6adcdf84bc47b9da9eca5d8702b83b.jpg)

Wiwen Nilsson, silver, rock crystall and onyx pendant for Anders Nilsson, c. 1935

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/41a4ed70306df0e25d8f34c5bfe4e603ba55d28996ac68e1a0b749883eaa1982.jpg)  
Wiwen Nilsson, silver and ebony pitcher for Anders Nilsson, 1941

# wiwen nilsson

The son of the Swedish silversmith Anders Nilsson, Karl Edvin Wiwen Nilsson trained in his father's workshop in Lund and later studied in Hanau at the Königliche PreuBische Zeichenakademie (Royal Prussian Drawing Academy). He undertook further artistic training at the Académie de la Grande Chaumière and at the Académie Colarossi while working in the Georg Jensen studio in Paris. In 1923, Nilsson began designing for his family's silversmithy and five years later took over the management of the firm. He was awarded a gold medal at the 1925 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" for his designs, which were notable for their unadorned surfaces and pure geometric forms. The angularity of his cubistic designs was, however, often softened by a gently curving handle or a simple edge of reeded moulding. His large-scale vessels were distinguished by the use of faceted surfaces and by the adoption of streamlined hemispherical or cylindrical forms. Although never intended for large-scale manufacture, the simplicity of Nilsson's silverware designs would have made them eminently suited to serial production. During the 1930s, Nilsson and fellow countryman Erik Fleming (1894-1954) produced a large number of beautifully executed Modernistic designs that epitomized the Swedish Art Deco style and reflected the growing international influence of functionalism. Nilsson's work was exhibited at the 1939 New York World's Fair and included in the landmark Helsingborg 1955 "H55" exhibition. He also won numerous awards for his modern silverware designs, including the Gregor Paulsson Trophy in 1955 and the Prince Eugen Medal in 1958.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0b91f02227e5da8cfcb8b4a912eb826ee58e076afd7e8d8c9f23e353b85dbe35.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b80ed5132f8ca2baf3fc608530126d8e1eac06eb74e78a745e2afdabfeb82134.jpg)  
Nokia 5510 mobile phone/communicator, 2002

Frederik Idestam, founder of Nokia

## nokia (

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3cb2592c9aaa5587f94819797732f7106f7066918faff0609f58bfc1e9c69638.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/82c32ed7541963b1ea7a8d87003e801dec22b019c30ff6a9433829c720ab26ac.jpg)

A global giant within the telecommunications industry, Nokia was an important pioneer of mobile telephony and is today the world's leading supplier of mobile telephones. The origins of this remarkable Finnish company can be traced back to 1865, when the engineer Frederik Idestam established a wood-pulp mill on the banks of the Emäkoski River in southern Finland. Christened by Idestam, the Nokia company benefited from the increasing demand for paper and cardboard that accompanied the rapid industrialization of Europe during the latter half of the 19th century, and exported its products initially to Russia and then later to Britain and France. Soon, a thriving community grew up around the pulp mill (which eventually also became known as Nokia) and in 1898 a neighbouring manufacturing company was established known as Finnish Rubber Works, which produced a wide range of products including rubber bands, industrial components, raincoats, rugs, balls and other latex toys. During the 1920s, this second firm began selling its products under the Nokia brand name. In 1922, the Finnish Rubber Works company acquired a majority shareholding of the Helsinki-based company Finnish Cable Works

(est. 1912), which supplied the cable required for telegraph and telephone networks. Over the succeeding decades the three firms' business activities - paper, rubber and cable - became increasingly integrated and eventually in 1967 the companies merged to form the Nokia Group. Importantly for this new venture, the Finnish Cable Works had previously established its own electronics department in 1960. From 1962 onwards this department dedicated itself to the research and development of radio transmission equipment - including radio telephones that were made for the army, public utilities and emergency services. These important precursors of the cellular telephone utilized pioneering semiconductor technology. It was an extremely opportune time for the Finnish Cable Works to be entering the telecommunications field because semiconductor technology was "making its way from the lab to the industry", and "not only newcomers, but also the industry leaders at that time had just begun learning the ABCs of new technologies". In 1969, the recently merged Nokia Group became the first company to introduce transmission equipment based on Pulse Code Modulation (PCM) that met the requirements of the CCITT (Consultative Committee on International Telegraphy and Telephony). Significantly for the company's future, PCM converted analogue sound signals into digital form and because of this, Nokia was able to establish itself at the very forefront of digital technology. During the early 1970s, Nokia developed a digitalized switch, the DX 200, for telephone exchanges - the majority of which were still electro-mechanical and used analogue switches. Thanks to its use of "high level computer language" and Intel microprocessors, the digitalized DX 200 equipment had a remarkable capacity that could handle the requirements of whole networks and could be easily extended by adding more computers in parallel to the system. Importantly, the DX 200 digital switch developed in the early 1970s remains the basis of Nokia's mobile and fixed networks. In 1981, the world's first common cellular network known as the Nordic Mobile Telephone (NMT) service was established, and Nokia developed a number of designs for this large network, which spanned several countries, including the first-ever transportable phone, the Mobira Senator Talkman car phone (1982) which weighed a hefty 9.8 kilos. During the early 1980s, Nokia

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e9247d358e15318c753f62e7e3a0d432f1265421df3aca2f20b1bb32ac92c96d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ea1a744a43adf950776e6947087eb9bfc4229fc30e95baffb056ebe35da0c7c0.jpg)

The predecessor to the mobile phone: the Radiophone in 1964

Nokia Electronic's testing ground, 1971

Nokia Mobira Senator Talkman 450 car phone for NMT 450 networks, 1986

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e19d581e97961c32e82a5d22f6930ab63ebe9c16ec0029d6098d1e8cbdff1e58.jpg)

also developed phones for other standards in Europe and America, and in 1987 launched its first hand-held phone, the Mobira Cityman 900, which weighed only 900 grams with its battery. Despite its phenomenally high price (FIM 24,000), this revolutionary design became a must-have status symbol for the "yuppie" generation. Since then, the size of Nokia's mobile handsets has increasingly diminished thanks to the miniaturization of technology. Significantly, Nokia determined early on that it was not just cutting-edge technology but usability and design that would ensure the success of their mobile phones. As a very personal form of technology, Nokia realized that the mobile phone must not only be user-friendly but must also suit the personality of its owner. Because of this, the company pioneered the introduction of large graphics displays, coloured covers and different ringing tones so that each phone could be effectively customized. In 1994, Nokia launched the world's first telephone handset that could be used within all major digital standards, thereby heralding the advent of truly global mobile communications. Two years later, the Nokia 9ooo Communicator became the first all-in-one integrated communica-

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1f1d4bd695bb7251870656bf5eedfa10107abc550c0276ad840bca7543806b5e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1b6d2fd4b4bac8d24bc2b42d9c41a4035cb148c8a09a1e1f68117a747b59a839.jpg)  
Δ Nokia Mobira Cityman 900 mobile phone, 1987

Δ Nokia 101 mobile phone, 1992

tions device offering digital voice and data services, Internet access, short-messaging, e-mail, fax access and personal organizer functions. As Finland's leading company, Nokia has been partly responsible for the recent Finnish "economic miracle", while the design excellence of its products has been recognized by numerous awards including an IF Seal of Quality for Good Industrial Design (1998) and a SMAU Industrial Design Award (1998). The firm's success lies to a large extent in its classically Scandinavian approach to design, which seeks to simplify and humanize technology; and in the appeal of its stylish products, which so neatly embody the attributes that have come to define Finnish design - strong graphic outlines, nicalvaion, hehiualy materials and construction.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/efcfe910967d1e5cf03cc507ada03624d2d0b3c3e1c68c4aa5636f448f6e797b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6ecd81c3cc9f95466007bdf3a791e8fda22917188b456f0a5c007f534be6db2f.jpg)  
Espen Voll, Evol stools for Norway Says, 2000

## norway says

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/50554c6d60c21b47d91a611293f5f50ed6b27356feb1849d0a55ff5ed8d524bb.jpg)

While Scandinavian design is renowned the world over, the term is usually associated with products from Denmark, Finland and Sweden rather than Norway, which is better known for its folk art than for designed products. One of the reasons why modern design has not flourished in Norway to the same extent that it has in these other Nordic countries is that since the 1970s, Norway has enjoyed unprecedented prosperity through oil revenues, which in turn has meant that the economic importance of design has been overlooked. In an attempt to readdress this situation, especially now that Norway's oil reserves are predicted to become seriously depleted within the next twenty years, the Norwegian government has at long last begun actively promoting Norwegian design abroad. Against this background of state support, the design group Norway Says was founded in 1999 by five young furniture designers who were studying at, or had recently graduated from either the Statens Hándverks- og Kunstindustriskole (National College of Art and Design), Oslo, or the National College of Art and Design, Bergen. Its five members, Torbjørn Anderssen (b. 1976), Tore Borgersen (b. 1966), Andreas Engesvik (b. 1970), Frode

Myhr (b. 1974), and Espen Voll (b. 1965), initally formed the group with a view to collectively showing their work at the Salon Satellite at the Milan Furniture Fair in 2000. Sponsored by among others the Norwegian Ministry of Foreign Affairs, the group was eventually "able to put their own prototypes in a van and drive the 2,500 kilometers down to Milan."This first group exhibition included a number of innovative and elegant designs, such as the Dock chair (2000) designed by Espen Voll and Tore Borgersen, the Comfort Living multi-functional chaise longue (1998) by Andreas Engesvik, the 24 Meccano modular storage system (2000) by Torbjorn Anderssen, and the Kazu ceiling lamp (2000) by Frode Myhr. This work consciously rejected "the over-representation of cabin-looking furniture all made of pine" and was born out of the belif that "design is an essential tool in manufacturing good everyday products." Later in 2000, the Norway Says group participated in the London "Designers Block" exhibition. Efforts such as these to promote Norwegian design both at home and abroad may well enable the "little brother" of Scandinavia to finally assert its own distinctive cultural identity within the international design community.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/defed90e0297c801982626e9119ee35b029e1d44eeb7b27311b1a80dcb67b6c6.jpg)

Torbjørn Anderssen has summed up the goals of the group: "I want to make everyday objects for everyday people. Needs and demands are important. Personal expression is not a goal, but a way of getting there" - sentiments that are in complete accord with the philosophical precepts of Scandinavian design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/469ff6fed436764908ca599defc9b85642d252f5cdf01359b63af420c47347da.jpg)  
Espen Voll, Hole table for Norway Says, 2000  
Tore Borgersen & Espen oll, Dock chair or Norway Says, 2000

Yki Nummi, white opaline glass pendant lamps for Stockmann-Orno, c. 1963

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/679261306816e93c3f75831ff330c698b9d676881ce3dfd7a62450068e99ccd4.jpg)  
> Yki Nummi, Modern Art acrylic table lamp for Stockmann-Orno, 1955

# yki nummi

Born in China, Yki Nummi was one of the leading Scandinavian lighting designers of the mid-20th century. During the immediate postwar years he studied mathematics and physics in Helsinki and Turku (1945-1947) and also trained as a "decorative" painter at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki (1946- 1950). From 1950 to 1975, Nummi designed numerous lamps and light fittings for Stockmann in Helsinki - the largest department store in Scandinavia. Many of his lighting designs produced by Stockmann-Orno were remarkable for their use of new materials - acrylic resins, opaline glass, and aluminium - such as his best-known design the Modern Art table lamp (1955), which incorporated a section of clear acrylic tubing that functioned as a "transparent" support for the translucent shade. His designs were also often distinguished by their use of innovative forms, as demonstrated by his space-age white opaline glass pendant lights from around 1963. Nummi was also highly regarded as a colour specialist, and from 1958 he headed the colour section of the paint manufacturing company Schildt & Hallberg. He was responsible for the colour schemes of the cathedral in Helsinki and the new garden city of Western Tapiola, and collaborated with fellow lighting designer Lisa Johansson-Pape on colour selections for Stockmann-Orno in Kerava. His innovative designs won gold medals at the 1954 (X) and 1957 (Xl) Milan Triennale, and his work was included in numerous international design shows, including the influential "Design in Scandinavia" exhibition that toured Canada and the USA 1954-1957, and the landmark 1955 Helsingborg "H55" exhibition. As an extremely versatile designer and gifted draftsman Nummi designed a wide range of products, from silverware and floorcoverings to radio cabinets and furniture. He also wrote several articles on lighting design, colour theory, and the philosophical principles governing design practice. Through their innovative use of new materials and production techniques and adoption ofunusuallyorward-looking frms, Nummis lighting designs exemplified the alternative and rather elitist approach to Modernism that was common in Finland in the 1950s and early 1960s.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8d711bcf80c6fe9906a2f949728a753536b755a5e92b785947543de845adc1dc.jpg)

Antti Nurmesniemi, Sauna stools for G. Söderström, 1952 - This horseshoe-shaped birch and teak stool was designed site-specifically for the Palace Hotel, Helsinki

> Antti Nurmesniemi, enamelled steel coffee pot for Wärtsila, 1957

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/808bc8e8c4c284a90092cab5f181516b8035c3b2db22b6880f44934cf1c5b6c0.jpg)

# antti nurmesniemi

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e92da2dc5254db12b542557d3dbe547888d91ed918d6bab275ffcd15f1f56b5e.jpg)

(b. 1927 Hämeenlinna, Finland)

The well-known Finnish designer Antti Aarre Nurmesniemi designed and constructed model airplanes as a child and later undertook a training course in gliding at the Jämijärvi school of aviation. During the Second World War, he worked in a metal workshop and an aircraft factory repairing planes for the war-effort. This early handson work experience at the Kuorivesi plane factory gave Nurmesniemi a crucial understanding of the inherent properties of wood and metal that would later inform his work as a designer. After the cessation of hostilities, Alvar Aalto brought the New York Museum of Modern Art's "America Builds" exhibition from Stockholm to Helsinki. According to Kaj Kalin, this landmark show gave Nurmesniemi a new, modern view of the world". In fact, he was so inspired by what he saw that he enrolled at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design), Helsinki, in 1947. Significantly for Nurmesniemi's development as a designer, the school had adopted the "Nordic Line" approach to design, which concentrated on both social and technical issues. Between 1949 and 1950, he worked at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki and also in the Stockmann

department store's design office, which was then headed by Werner West. To celebrate his graduation in 1950, Nurmesniemi took his first trip abroad to Stockholm and Copenhagen, where he was inspired by the exquisite craftsmanship and organic forms of Danish design. On his return he worked from 1951 to 1956 in the architecture practice of Viljo Revell (1910-1964) and Keijo Petaja (1919-1988) in Helsinki, which was pioneering a new rationalist approach to architecture in Finland that reflected the continuing influence of the Modern Movement. Nurmesniemi designed various interiors and furniture for the firm, including his well known horseshoe-shaped Sauna stool, which was designed site-specific for the Palace Hotel (1951- 1952). In 1953, he married the well-known textile designer Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi and later worked for six months in Giovanni Romano's design practice in Milan, where he was insped by functional yet stylish products designed by, among others, Marco Zanuso (1916-2001) and Roberto Sambonet (1924-1996). In 1956, he established his own Helsinki-based design practice and a year later, designed his well-known enamelled coffee pots for Wärtsila - the colours of which (red, yellow

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5bff0bf6cd27c8db262d29cb7b0a915e4fec1d56cedaeb2c69c4569a9f5e17c1.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e45c32f3c0664f58853ca540fdafd1febc1a4390273ac6385f8a74e47bef502c.jpg)

Antti Nurmesniemi, Model no. 001 chaise longue for Vuokko, 1968 - upholstered in a textile designed by Vuokko Eskolin-Nurmesniemi

Antti Nurmesniemi, Triennale chair for J. Merivaara, 1960 - This elegant design won a gold medal at the 1960 (XII) Milan Triennale - reissued by Arvo Piroiinen

and light blue) were chosen by his wife. The design has since become an acknowledged "icon" of Finnish Modernism because it rejected the Baroquestyle rounded forms of traditional coffee pots in favour of a more up-to-date industrial aesthetic that also predicted the colourful nature of Scandinavian design in 1960s. Intended for every-day use, Nurmesniemi described his coffee pot as one of only a "few household objects of its day (that) communicated the growing gulf between town and countryside." Later, he designed furniture for Artek, Tecta and Cassina, kitchenware for Arabia and Kymi, and trains for the Helsinki metro and Valmet. Among his most successful and characteristic designs were the Antti and Yleispuhelin telephones (both 1984) for Fujitsu, which reflected his belief that, "An object should not be designed simply to fulfil a purpose (not to mention manufacturing), but for the user. .. A simply designed object is wrong, if its use is complicated. ... A designed object does not need to reflect the times. It im  ih. Nurmesniemi was awarded a Lunning Prize in 199 for his refined designs, the distinctive formal vocabulary of which was derived from his synthesization of modern European forms with Scandinavian craftsmanship. Like his compatriot Alvar Aalto, Nurmesniemi was guided by the concept of shared responsibility - both social and environmental - and promoted a humanistic approach to Modernism, which revealed itself in the practical beauty and utility of his products.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4a8ddd3f051b0af5b48822fa19ac1e6d724115fd354b04f30f965e12af9257e6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/192122ddc4af5d6243c87f77158a9f8ab2862863122c8cf05bc4ff5529ca55a8.jpg)  
Kerttu Nurminen, Lumikuru (Snow Canyon) bowl for Nuutajärvi, 1985

# kerttu nurminen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4c59701dae555faa9f86256add7557295a262fe4cab806c0732bf8bef4384371.jpg)

The Finnish glass designer Kerttu Nurminen studied at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki from 1966 to 1970. In 1972, she began designing glassware for the Nuutajärvi glassworks and received the Coburger Glass Prize in 1977. Later in 1985, Nurminen designed her colourless Lumikuru (Snow Canyon) range, which included various bowls and platters as well as a small fondue dish and an eggcup. This line of partly sandblasted pressed glassware was designed as a replacement for the Luna range (1971) that had been designed by Kaj Franck. For the Lumikuru glassware, which was intended for industrial mass production, Nurminen skillfully combined simple functional forms with the subtlest use of decoration - a simple band of glass contrasting with the almost velvety quality of the sandblasted finish. The range was also sold with a smooth rather than sandblasted finish under the name of Kuru (Canyon). After Nuutajärvi was merged with littala, Nurminen produced designs for both brands. For the Nuutajärvi Pro Arte collection she designed her well-known Lampi glasses and decanter (1993 & 1995), which through their subtle bands of green and blue embodied "the

lucidity of northern nature, water, sky, and the o ehy a ila glass sculptures were also part of the exclusive Pro Arte range of studio glass, but with their leaved stems and petal forms they were more literal and playful in their representation of nature and, like the flowers they portrayed, had a fragile fleeting quality. In contrast, Nurminen's production glassware for littala was more functional, while retaining a sense of originality and creativity. When launched in 1988, her industrially-produced Mondo glassware brought Nurminen international recognition, and with its flame-like striations of fluid colour can be seen as an evolution of her earlier Lampi studio glass. With a "passion and unwillingness to compromise", Nurminen brings the artistic sensibilities associated with studio glassmaking to the realm of industrialized factory production so as to realize unpretentious yet high-quality, democratic, and timeless objects - a quintessentially Scandinavian approach to industrial design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1b9da6ee24122d341fd92b4ee8bdced0be0cd0925476561808d90d429613505d.jpg)

Kaj Franck, Prisma vases for Nuutajärvi, 1954

Kerttu Nurminen, Palazzo glass range from the Pro Arte collection for Nuutajärvi, 1998 - This collection pays hommage to the craftsmanship and traditions of Italian glass blowing, which has so inspired Finnish glass designers working at Nuutajärvi since the 1950s onwards

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ab9fb21829b8b5dbe737ce142c16c66cea6753ff566f04cdbae69fbac0163da2.jpg)

## nuutajärvi

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bf183313fdb3af8fca29a1f41a61090d7c88ad82f6d5e3613c51b666c004225b.jpg)

Nuutajärvi, Finland's oldest surviving glassworks, was founded by two Swedish entrepreneurs, Captain Jakob Wilhelm De Pont and Harald Furuhjelm, in 1793. Initially, the venture operated under the Swedish name Notsjö (later becoming known as Nuutajärvi Lasi), and during the early years it manufactured simple household glass, bottles and window panes. Around 1851, the factory was purchased by Adolf Törngren, who subsequently modernized it by implementing more efficient methods of production. After touring a number of European glassmaking centres, he decided to introduce pressed glass processes to the manufacturing operation and also began using more refined raw materials so as to enhance the quality of the company's products. Additionally, Törngren recruited the French glass blower Charles Bredgem under whose direction the company began manufacturing both pressed and filigree glassware. The Nuutajärvi-Notsjö factory went on to become one of the leading Nordic producers of glassware and in 1858 it opened its own sales outlet in Helsinki, which was named after, and run by the glassworks' accountant, G.F. Stockmann (now the largest and best known department

store in Scandinavia). From 1862 to 1878 Nuutajarvi also briefly manufactured ceramics. In the early 1900s, the company decided to acquire its own designs for contemporary glassware. To this end, it held two glass design competitions in 1905 and 1906 and subsequently received submissions from a number of Art Nouveau designers, including Valter Jung (1879-1946), Helena Wilenius, Carl Johan Boman (1883-1969), Gustaf Strengell (1878-1938), and Jussi Paatela. It was not until after the Second World War, however, that the firm began manufacturing truly innovative production and studio glassware. Between 1946 and 1948, Gunnel Nyman designed a number of influential art glass pieces for the factory, such as her Calla vase (1946), which predicted the expressive natural forms of the later work of Timo Sarpaneva and Tapio Wirkkala. Often, Nyman would incorporate trapped air bubbles or swirls of opaque glass in her thick-walled crystal vessels, as in her Serpentiini (Serpentine) vase of 1947, or use simple organic forms with faceted edges, as in her Facett 1 dish of 1941. Over her brief career at Nuutajärvi, which was cut short by her untimely death in 1948, Nyman established an important

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/49959f82219654d7f161c5465d53fa1e8780963d2f07c212c61178bcefa7992e.jpg)

Kaj Franck, handled flared glass vase for Nuutajärvi, early 970s

Kaj Franck, Pop goblet for Nuutajärvi, c. 1974

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/41cb2cefc225f556990916bb7ad4b3eea8d5fcd69b724f2ed8e49d1cee511ba9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1fbe1e1a8925a26455da4a4d9c0a0f615de57ff739a276d995dd7ff17271c839.jpg)

tradition for the company in which designers worked closely alongside skilled glassmakers in small workshops. After a devastating fire the glassworks was rebuilt in 1950 and acquired by the Wärtsila group, which also owned the Arabia ceramics factory. The same year, the head of the utility ware design department at Arabia, Kaj Franck, was given the responsibility of re-designing Nuutajärvi's utility glassware products. The following year, he was appointed the design director of the glassworks, and under his careful guidance the company's reputation for creative excellence was solidified. Franck had previously designed glass for littala, but while there had been frustrated by the company's lack of technical experimentation. At Nuutajärvi, however, he was able to freely explore new techniques and coloured pigments and indulge his desire for greater creative expression. To this end, he recruited two Venetian glass blowers from Murano whose technical know-how and expertise greatly influenced the glassworks' output. Franck's colourfully striped "spun" plates, for example, show the unmistakable influence of Italian post-war glass. During the 1950s, Nuutajärvi set itself apart from the other two main

Finnish glassworks, littala and Riihimäki, through its abundant use of bright colours and simple yet often expressive forms. In 1952, Saara Hopea became Franck's assistant and shortly thereafter designed her well-known stackable Model no. 1718 tumblers and Model no. 1716 pitcher (1953), which were highly functional yet radiated a lively aesthetic through their bright colours. During the 1950s, Franck revived the production of filigree glass and designed a number of rusticstyle pieces using bubbled glass. At Nuutajärvi, Franck was also able to pursue his interest in pressed utility glass, while enjoying the freedom of experimentation offered by the development of art glass pieces, such as his small bird sculptures. During the 1960s, the emphasis moved away from utility blown-glass towards high-quality pressed glass products, especially after the factory purchased new state-of-the-art pressing machinery in 1968. Oiva Toikka's Kastehelmi (Dewdrop) range eloquently testified to this change of direction, while his curious Lollipop sculptures and candlesticks denoted the factory's continued interest in producing expressive and boldly coloured art glass. In 1968, Heikki Orvola joined the glassworks and later executed a number of important designs, most notably his elegant Aurora glasses (1972). Around the same time, Kerttu Nurminen began designing both art and production glassware for Nuutajärvi, often using sandblasting techniques, as in her Lumikuru (Snow Canyon) range of 1985. In 1981, Nuutajärvi art glass began to be marketed under the Pro Arte trade name, and from 1988 to 1990 the glassworks was a subsidiary of littala. Like littala, Nuutajärvi became part of the Hackman Group in 1990 and three years later, in recognition of the factory's bicentennial anniversary, a new range of art glass was introduced known as the Pro Arte collection. Today, Nuutajärvi is solely concerned with the small-scale production of exclusive yet exquisite art glass pieces designed by, among others, Sami Lahtinen (b. 1967), Tina Nordström (b. 1957), Markku Salo (b. 1954), Rolf Svartström (b. 1966), Harri Koskinen, and Oiva Toikka. The factory's output continues to exemplify the successful collaboration of talented designers and skilled craftsmen, which for decades has been such a defining feature of Finnish design. This creative synergy between artist and artisan has

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ebf3c58bc276bf906df5663b03d9ed4b670cb9790891adc96d1f2f4b90e901c2.jpg)

led to a high level of technical and aesthetic achievement, as so cogently demonstrated by Nuutajärvi's Pro Arte collection.

Kaj Franck, Ring mouldblown glass plate for Nuutajärvi, 1966

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6e1c43f370cde217c033a202d5306457386c555c19253a8ba718a7f9ae72f64e.jpg)  
Gunnel Nyman, Model no. GN7 pitcher and Model no. GN23 glasses for Nuutajärvi, 1947

# gunnel nyman

## (b. 1909 Turku - d. 1948 Helsinki, Finland)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/02c29df76697cffdc26fd7a5caf539a40644730ab8e925da5fd722034dfe4359.jpg)

The Finnish glass designer Gunnel Gustafson Nyman studied furniture design under Arttu Brummer (1891-1951) at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki from 1928 to 1932. In 1932 she joined the Riihimäki design studio and subsequently won third prize in the firm's glassware competition of 1933 and second and third prizes in its second competition of 1936. During the 1930s, she explored the expressive potential of roughly bubbled glass - as did a number of other glass designers, most notably the French glass-maker Maurice Marinot (1882-1960), whose chunky bubbled vessels were highly influential on the younger generation of designers, including Nyman. In the early 1930s, she pioneered an innovative technique in which glass was blown into a pinstudded mould, removed from the mould and then encased in another layer of glass, so that the indentations from the mould would produce a regular pattern of small bubbles. Her thick-walled pieces from the 1940s were not only characterized by her signature use of tightly grouped bubbles, but also by her use of soft and often very fluid forms. The gentle undulating contours that she

adopted for her glassware during this period foreshadowed to some extent the bolder and more asymmetrical forms that became so fashionable the following decade. Nyman's innovative yet simple technique of casing tiny bubbles was relatively inexpensive to implement and could therefore be adopted for everyday tableware such as jugs and drinking glasses. Although her bubble technique was widely copied, other lesser designers were never able to replicate Nyman's virtuoso handling of the materials. In 1946, she designed her well-known Calla vase, which not only marked a stylistic departure in her own work but also signalled the movement in Finnish design in general towards the use of more expressive and abstracted organic forms. Apart from her renowned work for Riihimäki, Nyman designed lighting for Taito (1932-1936) and production glassware for Karhula (1935-1937). After the Second World War, she also briefly worked for littala (1946-1947) and Nuutajärvi-Notsjö (1946-1948) and designed furniture for Idman (1946-1948) before her untimely death at the age of thirty-nine. Although Nyman's career was all too brief, her work had a profound impact on subsequent generations of

Gunnel Nyman, Serpentiini vase for Nuutajarvi, 1947

Gunnel Nyman, Model no. GN27 vase for Nuutajärvi, 1947

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d8b89ffe3531b0b2847b013db563e5c4d7fe1860ebc22ffa2aee9153a9d30b66.jpg)  
Scandinavian glassware designers. In 1951 she was posthumously awarded a gold medal for her glassware at the IX Milan Triennale.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/49ae017f611f5b3d0caba154a9d5e89cbb2a8f00baf9029b568f6ba688940170.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e511ed2db8f34c5bf5a0b02f163fd9e76b701d46397832e1c6d1ff0057f4a190.jpg)

Gunnel Nyman, cased glass vases and Pearl Necklace vase for Nuutajärvi, 1947 & c. 1950

Gunnel Nyman, Calla vase for Riihimäki, 1946

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/965a91431a23521d3c1ba020664fcffbe49792f988bc7b1a9b2752b9f412c869.jpg)
