Edvin Öhrström, Ariel vase for Orrefors, c. 1

# edvin öhrström

The Swedish glassware designer Edvin Öhrström studied sculpture and graphic design at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design), the Tekniska Skolan (Royal Institute of Technology) from 1925 to 1928 and the Kungliga Konsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts) from 1928 to 1932 in Stockholm. He also studied drawing in Paris from around 1932 to 1934 at various art schools, including the Maison Watteau, the Academie Colarossi, and the Académie de Art Moderne under Fernand Leger (1881-1955). From 1936 to 1956, Öhrström worked at the Orrefors glassworks on a part-time basis - two months a year - which allowed him time to pursue a career as an independent glass sculptor in his own studio using space and technical assistance provided by the Lindshammar glassworks. Upon joining Orrefors, he quickly discovered "a deep affinity with the new medium", which led him to begin evolving soft organic forms that wnfn yhs evis rts Öhrström developed a novel glass-making technique in which the interior wall of vessels could be decorated by spinning a fluted wooden mould on a wheel. The raised teeth-like elements of

the turned mould produced a ribbed pattern of internal refractive rings, the optical effects of which were further enhanced by the faceted cutting of the vessels' outer surfaces. Although the A tech-s it as esibed y hrstr, was hhly innovative, his Art Deco style vases that incorporat ed it were not a commercial success and only a limited number were produced. Later in 1937, working alongside Vicke Lindstrand and the master glass-blower Gustav Bergkvist, Öhrström developed another technique for art glass known as Ariel. This innovative production method was developed from the earlier Graal technique that had been pioneered by Simon Gate at Orrefors 6. Like Graal, Ariel was an overlay technique in which several layers of glass made up the blank Some of the layers were engraved, etched, cut or sandblasted to form decorative motifs, which were then sandwiched between layers of clear glass. With the Ariel technique, however, bubbles of air were trapped between the hollowed section formed by the incised pattern and the external layer of transparent glass. Named inhomage o the y sprit in William Shakespeare's The Tempest, the Ariel technique was used extensively by Öhrström for his very expressive work, which demonstrated the virtually unique sculptural sensibility he was able to bring to glass-making. Many of his thickwalled Ariel vases were decorated with abstracted figurative motifs o animals, people and flowers, which revealed the influence initially of the work of his mentor Fernand Léger and then later that of Joan Miró (1893-1983) and Hans Arp (1887- 1966). Periodically, these designs also incorporated purely abstract decoration. Öhrström's Ariel vases were not only aesthetically pleasing but also managed to convey something of the molten material they were rendered from - a number of examples almost resonating with the drama of their fiery birth. His work was shown at both the 1937 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne" and the 1939 New York World's Fair, where it won much critical acclaim. Although best known for his superlative art glass pieces, Öhrström also designed glassware for domestic use that incorporated simple undecorated forms, while retaining a sculptural boldness. After leaving Orrefors in 1956, Öhrström continued to work as a glass sculptor, producing both private and public work, most notably his

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/67738b5ec85c2837329f2e3ca8461f5119632917602fea333b974512a19de443.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/433184641f8ab0bec67b5797140cece3decae0c2179bd597d598db1dc60d0b96.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7ae0486482711261fe6f79f50afeadebe1d08963c76b0618f826745507ba4a69.jpg)

0 feet Kristallvertikalaccent Crystal Vertical Accent) sculpture (1974) in Sergels Square, Stockholm.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/78b027c27a357ee032207ff26a7373cac29a3bb52243eccc6fbcc538febdb512.jpg)

# peter opsvik

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/129dcd19d5c271bde545929187c68fe0265c164d1bf5151a563edc6e347a8584.jpg)

The leading Norwegian furniture designer Peter Opsvik studied at Bergen College of Applied Art from 1959 to 1963 and the State College of Applied Art in Oslo from 1963 to 1964, prior to working as an industrial designer for the Tanerg Radio Factory in Oslo from 1965 to 1970. In 1967, Opsvik won a design scholarship from the Norwegian Credit Bank and two years later was awarded another scholarship from the Norwegian Design Centre. He began working as a freelance furniture designer in 1970 and designed the innovative Mini Max desk and chair, which could be height adjusted to meet the needs of both adults and children and was manufactured by Stokke. Using the same revolutionary concept of Generationenstuhl (a chair that grows with the child), Opsik later designed he bestselling riap high-chair/chair in 1972. This remarkable design not only elevated the child to a normal table height, but with its foot-rest also ensured that the necessary support was provided so as to allow enough freedom of movement "to explore the world in comfort and safety". In 1974 Opsvik designed his Model no. 2010 office chair for Håg and five years later, having collaborated with Hans Christian

Mengshoel (b. 1946) on the development of better performing ergonomic seating solutions, introduced the revolutionary Balans Variable sitting tool. With its balanced rocking base and provision of an ergonomically beneficial kneeling position, the Balans Variable afforded the user a good upright posture while working at a desk or table. During the 1980s, Opsvik continued to produce ergonomic seating designs for both Stokke and Hag, including his two Post-Modern products - the Balans Supporter (1983), which provided support for a semi-seated position, and the Garden Seating Sculpture (1984), which was more of an aesthetic exercise than a particularly functional solution. Since the mid-1980s, Opsvik has produced both conventional style ergonomic seating, such as the Sitti child's chair of 93 and the Actulum rocking chair of 1995, as well as more experimental and expressive furniture designs, such as those shown at the "Dimension in Wood - Sculpture or Furniture?" exhibition held at the Applied Art Museum in Oslo in 1986. Some of this latter type of furniture, which is distinguished by quirky forms and ambiguous function, has been put into limited production by Cylindra. Although the majority of

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/22a539dd1f4dd916b0e44e71fdf53ee482c0b62382b22e47a5a0511cfb85fc15.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/65cca2f4245ee79fc0a3ddd32ecbfc3783bd7083b0e4a37527caaa6b127fc287.jpg)

Opsvik's furniture designs exemplify the Scandinavian concern for social inclusiveness (children's furniture) and healthful living (ergonomic products), there is another side to his work that is more concerned with expressive form, which is perhaps a reflection of the Norwegian penchant for quirkiness within the visual arts.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7b9fccbcdf0f2eaa623a812dfdefcf5331aa77c42870ebb74d1b9eb65bd6900c.jpg)

Δ Photograph showing the correct ergonomic position afforded by the Balans Variable sitting tool, 1979 - This innovative design was developed with the assistance of Hans Christian Mengshoel and formed the blueprint for later ergonomic designs by Peter Opsvik

Peter Opsvik, Sitti adjustable child's chair for Stokke, 1993

Peter Opsvik, Tripp Trapp adjustable high-chair for ok, 1972

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7d429a5b064694f3f968328917811ea424ed14cbdf4fd7d0f43a73f6c56bc40e.jpg)  
Simon Gate, Parispokalen (Paris Cup) for Orrefors, 1925 - exhibited in the Swedish Pavilion at the "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriel Modernes" in Paris, 1925

## orrefors

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6081ddc9f927a0934963c90b4b570db6a134f7e0ee666877c11a3e135fe9ceb9.jpg)

The Orrefors glassworks was founded in 1898 on the site of an ironworks that had been in operation since 1726. By the late 19th century, the forge at Orrefors was in terminal decline and as a result the local economy was becoming increasingly reliant on timber. After the ironworks' eventual closure, the glassworks was established on the same site and began manufacturing medical and domestic glassware, such as glass bottles and jars. In 1913, the Gothenburg industrialist Consul Johan Ekman bought the Orrefors glassworks from Johan August Samuelsson and appointed Albert Ahlin its manager, thereby heralding a new era for the company. A year later, Orrefors began manufacturing crystal glassware, but it soon became apparent that there was a need for accomplished artists to design new forms and patterns. To this end, Simon Gate and Edward Hald were recruited in 1916 and 1917 respectively. Gate immediately instigated a thorough refinement of both overlaying and etching techniques. Working with the master blower Knut Bergkvist, he also developed a new technique that he christened Graal, in which glass blanks were decorated with cutting, etching or engraving, then reheated and coated with an outer layerofglass before being blown into their required final form. In 1917, both Gate and Hald began designing figurative patterns for glass engraving, that included a series of modestly draped nudes which were a complete departure from traditionally engraved motifs. By the mid-1920s, both designers had thoroughly perfected the art of engraving and were producing exquisitely decorated pieces, such as those designs shown by Orrefors at the 1925 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes". Gate and Hald had quite different approaches to decoration, however - the former being described as "a bounteously figurative artist" borrowing baroque motifs, while the latter was "a vigorous modernist" inspired by the art of his mentor, Henri Matisse (1869-1954). Apart from the artistic wares of this early period, Orrefors also produced domestic glassware by Hald and Gate in response to the Svenska Slöjdföreningen's (Swedish Society of Craft and Design) rallying call for the manufacture of beautiful and practical objects for everyday use. Around this time, the factory also began manufacturing glass light fittings for public buildings and later for domestic interiors, both of which proved a commercial success. In 1928, Vicke Lindstrand joined Orrefors and two years later made his debut at the 1930 Stockholm Exhibition with a series of thick-bodied Art Deco pieces that were modern in form and decorated with engraved figurative and geometric motifs. These new designs by Lindstrand signalled a general shift towards Modernism as well as a new and more sculptural direction for Orrefors. During the 1930s, both Sven Palmqvist and Edvin Öhrström developed innovative techniques at Orrefors known as Kraka and Ariel respectively - the former involved sandwiching a grid-like pattern of white or coloured glass between layers of clear glass, while the latter entailed sandblasting the surface of a blank which was then sheathed with a transparent exterior layer or layers of glass so as to produce pockets of air bubbles between the different layers. During the Second World War, the glassworks experienced a severe shortage of materials, but its designers continued to experiment with new techniques such as Cut Graal and the popular Fish Graal with marine motifs. In 1947, Ingeborg Lundin joined the firm and the following year the glassworks showed new designs by her as well as a number by Nils Landberg and Edward Hald at the NK department store in Stockholm. These post-war designs were notable for their simple, soft forms and subtle use of beautiful colours. During the 1950s, Orrefors widely exhibited its new products throughout Europe, including the 1954 (X) Milan Triennale and the landmark 1955 Helsingborg "H55" exhibition, where Sven Palmqvist introduced his range of Fuga domestic glassware, which employed a new manufacturing technique that utilized centrifugal force. If the 1950s at Orrefors were exemplified by the soft, undulating organic forms of Ingeborg Lundin's Apple vase, then the 1960s were epitomized by the bold, colourfully striped Pop glasses by Gunnar Cyrén. During the early 1970s, Orrefors began promoting a rustic aesthetic, while in the 1980s the revival of interest in limited edition studio pieces led the company to the decision in 1986 to no longer produce designs from its former catalogue and to concentrate instead on the production of new designs. A year later the glassworks also made the decision to only manufacture glass designed

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7f85fc7e4244c873cf6a8a8878edab56ea1e148d0234dc501d0598401ebcba7d.jpg)

· 福 #发 # #

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/caebe96db6517c376cb1d33ab777e868bd0662b73cb58929f50874047d941cbd.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b25ab30f04d33d81fdc9f79ba7c0266c633880c91bc593e9d12e31c17e286a61.jpg)  
Helén Krantz, Labyrinth Blue vase for Orrefors, 1998  
Anne Nilsson, Clara carafe for Orrefors, 2000

by its own in-house designers, such as Eva Englund (b. 1937), Anne Nilsson (b. 1953), Erika Lagerbielke (b. 1960) and Helén Krantz (b. 1953). During this period, Orrefors designers were on numerous occasions presented with a new design award known as Utmärkt Svenska Form for their stylistically diverse work. The 1990s saw a revival in simple yet elegant table glass, such as the designs produced by Gunnar Cyrén and Erika Lagerbielke. The mid-1990s also saw the arrival of new designers, including Lena Bergström 1961), Martt Rytkönen (b. 1960) and Per B. Sundberg (b. 1964). These designers have produced highly sculptural and technically innovative o while pioneering new forms and techniques for art glass as well as objects for everyday use. In 1999, the glass artist and ceramicist Ingegerd Raman joined the company. For over a century, Orrefors has "maintained an ambition to develop the expressive power of glass, both artistically and technically", while striving "to ensure that Swedish glass will always be elegant, exciting, and of superb quality in both craft and design".

Jan Johansson, Abina vase for Orrefors, 1998

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c2d8f9bfb08136265c2893ce4fe604245f6a9cc193c5222df1b480a05f0dc2f6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a6278f00f939578f18ccd9d0f0cef09d58f1bd75f16d96d19c505fd5c3a7b43c.jpg)  
Heikki Orvola, Aurora glassware range for Nuutajrvi, 1972 - later produced by littala

# heikki orvola

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/82c919c7af44da0cbd43b77323e05747a59364f037796f057b9f9f6a3ae8eb7c.jpg)

The Finnish glassware designer Heikki Orvola studied ceramics at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki between 1963 and 1968. Following this, he was offered a summer placement at the Nuutajärvi glassworks by Kaj Franck. He was also offered a position in the product design department at Arabia, but opted instead for the greater creative freedom afforded by Nuutajärvi. As both companies were part of the Wärtsila group, Orvola's frst design for Nuutajärvi was a range of glassware that was intended to complement a ceramic tableware service that Richard Lindh (b. 1929) had designed for Arabia. Throughout the 1970s and 1980s, Orvola designed several ranges of utility drinking glasses, such as the elegant Aurora (1972), Lili (1980) and Saturno (1984), as well as bowls, vases and a candle lantern that were massproduced using blow-moulding or pressing techniques. In 1976, he studied at the Pilchuck Glass Workshop in Stanwood, Washington, which had been founded by the renowned glass artist Dale Chihuly (b. 1941) and which was, according to one of its students, "an artist's heaven, and you don't even have to die". While in America, Orvola also became friends with the glass artist Marvin Lipofsky (b. 1938), who introduced him to the glass artist community in the San Francisco area. The highly creative and artistic nature of American art glass profoundly influenced Orvola, as did his travels to Mexico, China, Russia, Nepal and India. During this period he produced some of the most expressive art glass created by any Scandinavian designer working at the time. He used sandblasct techniques and casting, all to dramatic effect. His designs ranged widely in style and execution, from his delicate swirling filigree plates (1980) to his free-blown lidded bowls (1983), which had curious abstracted vegetal forms and incorporated the Ravenna technique that had been pioneered by Sven Palmqvist. His later limited edition pieces, such as the Maya vase (1994) for Nuutajarvi's Pro Arte collection or his sculptural totemlike Grand Canal bottles (1998), were explorations of material, form and decoration that clearly showed the influence of Post-Modernism. As Orvola noted, abundant decoration is not a typical phenomenon in Finnish design. I reckoned it was time to offer people a few colourful items of tableware." In

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9b0106044bd162671abc5c73ce720fc0eac6e023b7a249c0a3828f8d6d0ef218.jpg)

contrast to his one-off glassware, Orvola also designed production ceramics for Arabia, such as the Moreeni (1992) and the 24hr (1996) tableware ranges, which have basic, undecorated functional forms that project a sense of homely simplicity. Orvola's career, which is typical of many Scandinavian designers, demonstrates his extraordinary ability to produce designs of high integrity for both one-off studio manufacture and industrial mass-production. Beyond glassware and ceramics, Orvola has also designed textiles for Marimekko and enamels for Järvenpäa. He received the Pro Finlandia Medal in 1984 and his work was the subject of a major retrospective exhibition at the Helsinki Museum of Applied Arts in 2000 - ranging from glass sculptures to well-honed, practical tools for living. As Tapio Periäinen has observed, Heikki Orvola is a tangible example of the way in which a designer or artist can express and thus show what kinds of industrial utensils are durable, timeless, and conserve nature, and in spite of this are beautiful.. Orvola helps to point out a way for us all to what is called a sustainable future, an environmentally friendly way of life."

Heikki Orvola, Maalais filigraani (Rustic Filigree) goblets for Nuutajärvi, 1972

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7fff0308402eb8fed734cbebe043c16a063c021c069b50421d30aa9985c9283d.jpg)  
Steen Østergaard, Cado 290 stacking chair for Poul Cadovius (Cado), 1968

# steen ostergaard

Steen Østergaard trained as a journeyman joiner, receiving a Silver Medal in 1957 and graduating from the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts), Copenhagen, in 1960. The same year, he was awarded first prize at the Saddlemakers' and Upholsterers' Guild anniversary competition. Between 1962 and 1965, he worked in the design office of Finn Juhl, where he assisted in the development of several chairs with exceptionally highquality solid wood frames. In 1965, Østergaard established his own design practice and later designed his first plastic product - the Cado 290 chair (1968), which was manufactured from around 1970 by Poul Cadovius. Importantly, this design was one of the first single-material, single-form plastic chairs to be injection-moulded in polyamide (nylon). The elegant cantilevered form of this chair was obviously inspired by Verner Panton's earlier Panton stacking chair (19591960), which had originally been manufactured in injection-moulded Baydur (a polyurethane hard foam) in 1968 and then from 1970 onwards in injection-moulded Luran-S (a polystyrene thermoplastic). Unlike Panton's design, however, the Cado 290 was available with optional seat cushions, which were screwed directly onto the chair's frame. Østergaard's chair could also be linked to form rows and twenty-five units could be stacked onto a specially designed trolley. There were also armchair and high-backed variations of the design. In some ways the Cado 290 chair was a more economic design than Panton's chair, for its scooped out lower section ensured that less material was used. The thickness of its wall construction was also thinner, which minimized the chair's overall weight. Østergaard has since designed a number of other chairs, but none have matched the Cado 290 - a functionally and aesthetically exciting processdriven design that fully exploited newly developed high-tech plastics technology.

Steen Østergaard, Cado 290 stacking chair with Cado 291 armchair, Cado 265 lounge chair and injectionmoulded plastic lounge chair with footstool for Poul Cadovius (Cado), c. 1970

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/18dbb9e7e55a4206d43003240e0fbdc3c76c5d85894b399417de695dae549d45.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/72b1687f66d6b9c481295caa0f4e83cce3a81a306a13ffb03f474075ef1a517b.jpg)

Sven Palmqvist, Fuga bowls for Orrefors, 1954

>> Sven Palmqyist, Kraka vase for Orrefors, 1944

# sven palmqvist

(b. 1906 Lenhovda - d. 1984 Orrefors, Sweden)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fbab03a2966b99ae6891979c514612629c95d30963dd5d4e4f23bbca508fe706.jpg)

The Swedish glass designer Sven Ernst Robert Palmqyist trained from 1928 at the glass engraving school that had been established at the Orrefors glassworks. Following this, he studied at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) and the Tekniska Skolan (Polytechnic), Stockholm, from 1931 to 1933 and then later attended the Kungliga Konsthögskolan (Royal University College of Fine Arts), Stockholm, from 1934 to 1936. During these years, he also studied under Paul Cornet and Aristide Maillol (1861-1944) at the Académie Ranson, Paris, as well as in Germany, Czechoslovakia, Italy and America. In 1936, Palmqvist returned to the Orrefors glassworks, where he developed the Kraka technique, in which a grid-like pattern of white or coloured glass was sandwiched between layers of clear glass. Later he exhibited his work in Paris at the 1937 "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne" and showed his designs at the 1939 New York World's Fair. In 1954, he invented a method of centrifugally spinning molten glass in a mould so as to eliminate the need for hand finishing, and at the 1957 (X1) Milan Triennale he was awarded a gold medal and a Grand Prix for

his Fuga domestic glassware, which utilized this highly innovative technique. During the late 1940s and early 1950s, he developed his Ravenna glassware in which coloured geometric glass segments were surrounded with sand-sprinkled air channels to produce a mosaic-like effect. He used this technique for a free-standing glass wall entitled Light and Dark, which was designed for the Union Internationale des Télécommunications, Geneva, and comprised two hundred blocks of Ravenna glass. Palmqvist remained at Orrefors until 1972 creating exquisite "industrial studio glass", and later worked as a freelance designer for the glassworks. Palmqvist's art glass epitomized the graceful functinalist forms  characteristic of Swedish design, while his development of pioneering techniques ensured breathtaking creative innovation, Captivated by the visual poetry of glass, Palmqvist stated, With glass you can sculpt with light, paint with spectral colours with the aid of the sun. Glass will express so much more than other materials; it will never be effected  it is eternal, unchangeable."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3f6e41d4f459ac52f9899e7bc40ea41b65b817549584afa7c9cb56c6472222fb.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b0b786679c8f291933d17c2a74996f329ea8aba8c16e7a2179517fa8e451c298.jpg)

Sven Palmqvist, Ravenna bowl for Orrefors, c. 1948 Sven Palmqvist, Ravenna bowl for Orrefors, c. 1948

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c2134903196a976de478251bf2be5473dd715d28105b75042608e2dddcb7d4b7.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/15f24cb35615cad007af65d4ba338d72d524a0e62613fdbe0882f1a96b48f75b.jpg)  
Ole Palsby, Classic No. 1 thermal jug for Alfi, 1985

# ole palsby

One of the most prolific Danish industrial designers working today, Ole Palsby originally trained in banking and international finance. In 1960, he decided to pursue his artistic instincts and began producing furniture developed in collaboration with several leading Danish designers. Four years later, he established a specialist gallery selling modern design objects and furniture, contemporary painting, sculpture and photography. It was not until 1968, however, when he opened another shop in Copenhagen specializing in high-quality kitchen equipment, that he began to seriously contemplate a career in design practice. Around this time he was invited to design a series of barbecues, which went on to win the prestigious Danish ID Prize in 1971. Bolstered by this success, in 1975 Palsby decided odedicatehimself full time toworking as an independent design consultant. Since then, he has specialized almost entirely in the design of household items, from stainless steel cookware and porcelain tableware to nylon kitchen utensils and plastic thermos flasks. Unlike the majority of industrial designers, Palsby has never had a studio or a stable of assistants, but instead works by himself wherever he happens to be. Although sketching is an important part of his method of working, he has stated that, "no-one can just draw the right solution". Because of this, he often finds that it is only when he is in a factory handling materials that the creative process really begins to kick in. Guided by his central belief that "Design should be for the eye and the hand", he evolves essential forms that have an innate simplicity and beauty. In 1977, he was awarded another Danish ID Prize for his famous Eva Trio kitchenware collection. In 1980, Palsby visited Japan for the first time and this had much influence upon his subsequent work. Five years later, he designed his best-known product, the Classic thermal jug for Alfi - the shape of which was reminiscent of a traditional silver creamer used on the Channel Islands. This commercially successful design was followed by the launch of his classic white Eva Trio dinner service, which was born out of his belief that the presentation of food should never be marred by unnecessary decoration. More recently he has designed various products for WMF, most notably his CombiNation cutlery series, which comprises fifty-eight different items. His success, especially in Germany, can be attributed to his use of classical geometry and his logical approach to the design process, in which he attempts to imbue products with a "look as natural as if the design had just occurred of its own accord". In keeping with the long-held traditions of Scandinavian design, Palsby believes that "design is a political statement", which must balance aesthetics and function in order to realize natural and ideal solutions.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/703b2003ee951c888f73491a68934de0b58221ef517285c100a8c40e67bf2022.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8acfd21dabdc92195e394a9eb57a08840b2caa6045fec1a334fcba24055532b9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1fd5908cbe70d7182647fa02e55f49fdbd40c798f5b1d129de9e19778ec01658.jpg)  
Verner Panton, Panton chair, 1959-1960, manufactured by Vitra  
Marianne Panton seated on a Panton chair with matching table, c. 1970

# verner panton

## (b. 1926 Gamtofte - d. 1998 Copenhagen, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/bdd91a70e85d07f9cf40f7a9ab0a03a1905ab0a3539e357bdab22f9a953ec72e.jpg)

With a rainbow of saturated colours and spaceage sculptural forms, Verner Panton playfully yet radically transformed design not just in Scandinavia but throughout most of the Western world. The son of an innkeeper, he spent his childhood on the Danish island of Funen before training at the Odense Tekniske Skole (Polytechnic). He later studied architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademie (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in Copenhagen, graduating in 1951. Between 1950 and 1952, he worked in the drawing-office of the celebrated Danish architect and designer Arne Jacobsen. During this period he assisted Jacobsen with a number of experimental furniture designs, including the well-known Ant chair (1951- 1952). After leaving Jacobsen's studio, Panton began making furniture models, designing auto equipment, and arranging exhibitions. In 1955, he established his own design practice and became well known for his innovative architectural proposals, including a collapsible house (1955), the Cardboard House (1957), and the Plastic House (1960). For Panton, the mid- to late 1950s was a period of imaginative exploration and playful experimentation. Around this time he began to receive

increasing critical acclaim for his numerous designs for seating, lighting, textiles, carpets and exhibition installations. In 1958, he received his first major architecture/interior design commission from Baron Berner Schilden Holsten. The brief was to rebuild and expand the Baron's Komigen Kro (Come Again Inn) sited in the small town Langesø on Funen. For this project Panton broke with all conventions and designed an all-red interior that included his famous Cone chair (1958) - a design that had an eye-catching ice-cream cornet form. This unusual seating solution brought Panton yet more international attention - when it was exhibited in the window of a corner-sited shop in New York it distracted drivers so much that the traffic police had to ban its exhibition in that particular location for fear of any more car accidents. The Cone chair and the slightly later Heart chair (1959) a "cornet with angel wings" - were eventually put into production by Percy von Halling-Koch's newly established firm Plus-Linje. In 1959, Panton continued to confound the press and the public alike when he designed an exhibition at the Købestævnet trade fair, which literally turned the world upside down - the ceiling was carpeted and all

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e4128aa613900e125e5de700498ef3ed2150374ec1bc178e0f42fefbccdb8658.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6cd529f9a5c0c86ef151ff38b3f2b0186951a25a77d547ef2fdefe9a2f7c0226.jpg)

Verner Panton, lobby of the Varna Restaurant, Arhus, 1971

Verner and Marianne Panton's home in Basel-Binningen, 1973 - showing Ring Lights designed for Louis Poulsen in 1969

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9a8e323c8c489bcf9518a20b79c08f8e6a1fb448ce76abcef767b2a12f154712.jpg)

Vv Verner Panton, Cone K1   
chair for Plus-Linje, 1958

Verner Panton, Series 100 Pantonova chair (model no. 125T) & ottoman (model 6Sor Frit Hann 1971

Verner Panton, Wire Cone K2 chair for Plus-Linje, 1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/61ffbe36a893938ac0d24cfef4136150580dd5c1448bcef4d7e8232e4496acec.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c14957d0dc66a54d99484c89d3c0b0d04c5cd378c37bf4575037b87337b49a53.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d278e98b9a474ed0e4a13656affa77f9e6d704663011e5b06479a216357b1683.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/428a81ef2a090e1285c930566f564d1329ae11f61aceb8c10dec5e57c6af7a99.jpg)  
Verner Panton, Heart Cone K3 chair for Plus-Linje, 1959

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/34ebb1b00d8c6cc404e10bac379d16db8048bcc2bb6668d325ed03bf6c610966.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/411159e6f2cc3b574b91107d33620a2362b5c3bf73b0f4877e2a4475e278694c.jpg)

Verner Panton, Geometri 1 carpet for Innovation Randers, 1960 - designed specifically for the Hotel-Restaurant Astoria

Δ Plus-Linje Collection, sales exhibition held at the Pfister furnishing company, Zürich, 1960

the furniture and lighting was inverted and attached to it. The reasoning behind this extraordinary scheme was that the fair was normally so crowded that people could often only see the back of the person in front of them, whereas if the furniture and lighting were above them everyone could see them more easily. In 1960, Panton produced a similarly unconventional installation at the Cologne Furniture Fair with the ceiling covered in silver foil. During the same year, he was commissioned to redesign the Astoria Restaurant in Trondheim. The resulting interior was shocking not only because of the unusual forms adopted, but also because of the brightness of the colours used. Poul Henningsen remarked of the project, "Verner Panton's Astoria restaurant is a square peg in a round hole. It is something different, because it combines a tenacity in sticking to an idea with due regard for the comfort of the guests. ... I feel that this is the first solution to the big question that has been tormenting us ever since functionalism got its big breakthrough: How do we make a restaurant a nice place for guests?" Certainly, the interiors transported the restaurant's diners into a fantasy space-age world with their Op Art inspired wall-

coverings and globe-like lamps. Apart from creating groundbreaking interior schemes, Panton also explored designs for the first-ever single-form chair in plastic. He had previously designed the singleform cantilevered plywood S-chair, which was developed in conjunction with Thonet GmbH in 1955-1956. For a number of years afterwards, he attempted to translate this design into plastic. From 1957 to 1960, he refined his ideas, sketches and models in order to achieve this goal and by 1962 his concept for the revolutionary Panton chair was fully formed. The same year, Panton left Denmark and stayed briefly in Paris before establishing a design office in Cannes. During this period he tried unsuccessfully to find a manufacturer for his landmark design, until he came into contact with Willy Fehlbaum, a Basel-based furniture manufacturer who was producing Herman Miller products under license. Although George Nelson, the design director of Herman Miller, was not convinced of the viability of the design, Fehlbaum decided that his own firm, Vitra, would risk developing the chair. Eventually, Panton moved to Basel to assist Willy Fehlbaum with the five-year-long production development of the Panton chair. In 1967 a small

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9002dbf6ebcfb80206bbd2fa63344e1417d401aa7ae70853a5b52f0f825faca9.jpg)

number of chairs (100 to 150 units) were produced from cold-pressed fibreglass re-enforced polyester (FRP) and the following year the design was launched to international acclaim at the Cologne Furniture Fair. That same year a new plastic known as Baydur (a polyurethane hardfoam) was used and as a result the Panton chair became the first single-material, single-form chair to be injection-moulded. Although this material allowed the chair to be produced in greater numbers, it still required a significant amount of finishing (including painting). In 1971, a new plastic known as Luran-S (a polystyrene thermoplastic) was used instead, but despite its gleaming surface finish the plastic did not have sufficient strength and eventually the production of the chair was discontinued in 1979 (only to be later revived by Vitra in 1990 using polypropylene and also polyurethane hard-foam). Having established a design office in Basel, Panton decided to remain there and set about cultivating a wide-ranging clientele, including, among others A. Sommer, Kaufeld, Haiges, Schöner Wohnen, Nordlys, Kill, Wega Radio, Thonet, Knoll International, Lüber and Bayer. Panton also designed psychedelic Phantasy Landscapes for Bayer at the 1968 "Visiona 0" and 1970 "Visiona II" exhibitions in Cologne, which combined fantastic sculptural shapes with pure saturated colour. Each room for the "Visiona O" installation contained different furniture and sitting sculptures" - one room had large orange Draloncovered foam balls, while another had "threedimensional" carpets on the ceiling. As one commentator noted, "Every room had its own sounds and odours, so that the sensations from nearly all the human senses added spice to the unique perception of space. ... You went through the exhibition and forgot time and place." The installations designed for "Visiona II", however, were even more other-worldly, with walls, ceiling and furniture melting together to form space-age grottoes. These imaginative and sensuously disorientating rooms epitomized the experimental character of the psychedelic era with their kaleidoscope vistas of shape, colour and texture. From 1969 to 1985, Panton produced geometrically patterned textiles for Mira-X, which also used his characteristically bright rainbow palette. During the late 1960s and early 1970s, he designed a range of seating for Fritz Hansen, and a system of storage units

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0e4504d947fa669c8743a3b28e22e29c592cf01637cce93982de51d3dd983b9d.jpg)

Δ Verner Panton, S-Chair Model 275 for Thonet, 1955- 1956 - produced from 1965

Verner Panton, Big Flowerpot pendant lamp for Louis Poulsen, c. 1971 - showing the strong influence of Op Art on Panton's work

#

Verner Panton, Onion cotton velour textile from Collection Décor () for Mira-X, 1976

Δ Verner Panton, System 1-2-3 chair for Fritz Hansen, 1973

> Verner Panton, VP-Globe lamp for Louis Poulsen, 1969

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d149f0092ab5858b691b33852ca5454b791c117c110811e8921db2960c094406.jpg)

as well as floor and desk lamps with chromed wire constructions for Lüber. These designs were inspired by Op Art and were developed from his earlier wire seat furniture of 1959-1960. In 1973, he designed the comprehensive 1-2-3 seating system for Fritz Hansen, which comprised twenty different models with four different seating heights for resting, dining and working. His interest in systems solutions was also explored in the colourful block-like Pantonaef toy (1975) for Naef, Switzerland. During the 1970s, Panton designed several lights for Louis Poulsen, including the spherical VP-Globe and Panto lamps (1975). During the 1980s, he designed his Art Chairs-Chair Art series (1981), which was made up of sixteen plywood single-form chairs with unusual amorphous cut-out shapes. He also experimented in 1985 with pure geometric forms - cubes, spheres, and cones - for a proposed seating range. Unlike many other Danish designers, Panton took a revolutionary rather than evolutionary approach to design. Throughout his career, he produced highly innovative and bold designs that often utilized state-of-the-art technology and reflected both his optimistic belief in the future as well as his playful imagination. With his uniquely colourful and synthetic vision of the future, Panton rejected the Danish tradition of refining existing furniture types and using natural materials, but at the same time held onto the Scandinavian belief that design should first and foremost be about durability, unity and integrity.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/880127ca0fbbef97b1bfc3b0f4ebe3d6e411d082b85588b3d105672b874a33f2.jpg)

Verner Panton, Living Tower for Herman Miller/Vitra, 1968-1969 - reissued by Vitra

Living Tower in Panton's house, c. 1970

Verner Panton, System 1-2-3 partitions for Fritz Hansen, 1973

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/886ce785f9b2f73cf89168fd16a2c418f2ecc9829407ef0a41030be386a0a017.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6adca1a00962b15fc85e93f3df0447fc121137b3efafbcde9dabb00e523b7082.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7a42af51f87f3a424baea4d8cd18df7d0a1facde95768205953f107c84b663e6.jpg)

Verner Panton, Phantasy Landscape, Visiona II exhibition by Bayer AG at the Cologne Furniture Fair, 1970 - The housing shortage during the 1960s prompted designers and radical design groups (from Joe Colombo to Archizoom Associati) to explore the idea of space-age microenvironments. Panton's remarkable out-of-this-world installations exhibited at the Visiona II epitomized this fascination with alternative living spaces and futuristic forms. Not only did Panton playfully experiment with form, function, colour and space but also blurred the long-held distinctions between furniture design and architecture, with his sculptural 3-D carpets and cave-like passageway of melting seating forms.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8e54b12caac46007f85bdf589ed2c01dc12f49d428a53873228e7121686f8ed9.jpg)

Sigurd Persson, gold and amethyst bracelet, 1963

# sigurd persson (1914 Helsingborg, Sweden)

The renowned Swedish designer Sigurd Persson trained under his father, Fritiof Persson, as a silversmith in Helsingborg from 1928 to 1937. He gained a journeyman's diploma in 1937 and subsequently studied at the Akademie für Angewandte Kunst (Academy of Applied Arts), Munich, from 1937 to 1939, and at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) in Stockholm from 1939 to 1942. He established his own workshop in Stockholm in 1942 and began designing cutlery and jewellery. Seven years later, he founded an industrial design practice in Stockholm and designed most notably sculptural stainless steel vegetable dishes (1953) for Silver & Stål, and the Jet Line range of cutlery (1959) for the Kooperativa Förbundet (Co-operative Society of Sweden). In 1959, he won a competition to design in-flight cutlery for SAS, and later designed matching grey plastic tableware for the airline. Between 1968 and 1982, he worked as a freelance consultant to the Kosta glassworks producing brightly coloured minimally decorated designs. During the late 1960s and early 1970s Persson also worked as an independent sculptor, stablishing hiswn galery  He borg in 1970. In 1976, he designed uniform badges for the Swedish Army, and together with nine other silversmiths opened the Argentum Gallery in Stockholm. He also designed a range of strong and boldly formed saucepans for the Kooperativa Förbundet in 1978, which exemplified hs approach the desi practical, evday objects. Successfully bridging the worlds of handcraftsmanship and industrial design, Persson believed that design had to be truthful in order to be successful, and that, "In every well-designed object, it is not just the function that is described in the form. Something else is there, too - we might call it an expression of the times." Whether designing a saucepan or a silver candlestick or a piece of jewellery, Persson imbued the object with sculptural monumentality and economic functionalism. His silverware was a source of inspiration to many American silversmiths and as such, assisted in the revitalization of metal smithing as a craft at numerous American colleges and universities. Like so many other Scandinavian designers, Persson proved extremely versatile, being able to design mass-produced utilitarian products alongside exclusive limited-edition pieces. Above

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1dc06ea4315643c8bb359984fc6951096d55715ad0e6e7abacf5039e7ee86eab.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1e200328b17ed7c7be5e9f0b04aebb29d63334e16c549ab38e83d61b2af440ca.jpg)  
Sigurd Persson, silver coffee service, c. 1950  
Sigurd Persson, double stainless steel vegetable dishes for Silver & Stal, 1953 - These dishes were not only designed to stack but could also be inverted to form lids  
all else his approach to design embodied the fundamentally moral and democratic philosophy of Scandinavian design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/126e25e52a7d88a63125d585228586eee277d8bd1e34559cb2999ec7c5a4cd3d.jpg)

Ulf Hanses, Streamliner F1 (model no. 21151 Red) toy for Playsam, 1984
