## playsam

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/552bd3b7acf26a53161864fdc4637a8cd396cbcdf02d5634879bc6f82a9cda24.jpg)

In 1984, the Swedish company Playsam was founded by Carl Zedig (b. 1941) with the aim of producing well-designed toys that embodied "colour, form and function, all combining to make an aesthetically pleasing whole". Around this time Zedig met Hans-Christer Ericson (1945), who subsequently created a total graphic identity for the company, which included a new font. In 1985, Playsam was awarded a prestigious Excellent Swedish Design Award for its eye-catching graphic programme. The toys manufactured by Playsam were similarly innovative and were designed by Ulf Hanses (b. 1949), who had previously apprenticed to the well-known industrial designer Sigvard Bernadotte. Hanses had also designed a range of toys for handicapped children during the early 1980s, the rights to which were acquired by Playsam. His later well-known Streamliner car (1984) for the company, with its clean lines and bold use of colour, was conceived while Hanses was sketching freely, and its "obvious" aesthetic and functional purity has meant that he has never had to alter the design. As Hanses noted, "Playing with shapes, volumes and proportions is the basis of my design work. I am constantly returning to

geometry. ... When I work with toys I can concentrate on expression without being disturbed by some complicated technical idea." Hanses' toys revived and updated the age-old tradition of wooden toy making in Sweden, and it is not surprising to learn that he comes from the Dalarna region, which is famed as the place where the colourful wooden toy horses originate from. Acknowledging the importance of craft-folk heritage, Hanses' toys have a stripped-down and essential aesthetic that is akin to that of Shaker design. Hanses believes that it is the ability to convey "soul" and "expression" within a product that differentiates design from draughtsmanship, and certainly his diminutive Streamliner has an endearing character that is as engaging for children as it is for adults. As Carl Zedig has observed, "Most important is the simplicity. When you look at a Streamliner, your imagination adds details." Apart from his Streamliner range, which includes cars, boats and planes, Hanses has also designed a simple rocking horse for Playsam that possesses a similar elemental quality. The well-known Swedish designer Björn Dahlström has also produced a number of designs for the company, including a toy car, a rock-

Björn Dahlstrom, Rocking Bunny children's toy for Playsam, 1985

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/684c6082d6f9d4cc43f8651b4784ad0128f9ae4d3cace8b411e3619b7e4fb9c0.jpg)

ing duck, and a rocking rabbit that complement the other products in the range. Playsam toys exemplify the commonly held belief in Scandinavia that all sectors of society can benefit from good design. Ultimately, the company's international success lies in the innate simplicity of its products, which offers a welcome respite from an age of increasing technological complexity.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e2fc8419fc24868de44f21ec0ebbf3aa6d7611e528bcbe233e9f7cc54fc0f702.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2fa9b6db06c9ddacc93b993c5167bce1361c419f71a24317f30d6afe9fe765ab.jpg)  
Henrik Bull, Plate with Nordic motifs for Porsgrund, c. 1900-1905

# porsgrund

In 1886, Johan Jeremiaessen established the Porsgrund ceramics factory in Porsgrunn with the financial assistance of his wife's family, the Knudsens. Shortly after this, he appointed the German ceramicist Carl Maria Bauer as the company's first technical director, and the following year the first firing of ceramics took place. Initially, the factory produced underglaze-painted and transfer-printed wares using traditional patterns originating from the Royal Copenhagen and Meissen factories. By the turn of the century, however, the factory was fully operational and making a profit on the sale of its ceramic wares, allowing Porsgrund to begin producing its own Nordic style patterns. Products such as these were not only conceived as a means of differentiating the company's output from that of more established Scandinavian manufacturers, but were also intended as a material expression of Norway's desire for independence from Denmark, and as a means of asserting its own wholly autonomous artistic identity. It is ironic, therefore, that in 1901 the factory appointed a Dane, Rose Martin, as its new technical director, who then went on to introduce a form of underglaze painting that had been developed earlier in Denmark. The

Nordic style wares produced by the factory during this period were designed by, among others, Gerhard Munthe and Henrik Bull (1864-1953), who worked in a Neo-Viking style using intertwined and intricate strap-like patterns. Around this time the illustrator Theodor Kittelsen (1857-1914), who was famed for his depiction of fairies and trolls, also designed ceramics for Porsgrund that were embellished with scenic Nordic landscapes, as did Thorolf Holmboe (1866-1935), whose Art Nouveau style designs featured simple floral motifs painted in underglaze blue. Following this early flowering of artistic excellence, the factory did not produce any significant work until the appointment of the Danish designer Hans Flygenring (1881- 1958) as artistic director in 1920. Flygenring had previously studied under Johan Rohde as well as under Arnold Krog at Royal Copenhagen. He remained at Porsgrund for seven years, during which time he produced an extensive number of decorative and functional wares that were characterized by Oriental-inspired floral and linear motifs. Between 1928 and 1945, the Norwegian designer Nora Gulbrandsen (1894-1978) was the factory's artistic director, and while there completely modernized the company's approach to the design and manufacture of utility ceramics. Her geometric and rather architectural product designs reflected her interest in the work of the international avant-garde, and in particular the Russian Constructivists and German Functionalists. The colours she used, such as those for her well-known banded designs - hot orange, gold and black - were very often influenced by the Art Deco style. By the late 1930s, however, Gulbrandsen's work became less hardedged and began to embrace a softer and more humanizing modern aesthetic. After the Second World War, Tias Eckhoff designed a number of ceramic product lines for Porsgrund, including the Det Riflede (The Fluted One) range (1949) and his oven-to-table Glohane range (1951), which were awarded gold medals at the 1954 (X) and 1957 (XI) Milan Triennale respectively. Another wellknown design from Porsgrund's post-war "golden era" was the Spire tableware range (1953) designed by Konrad Galaaen (b. 1923). During the 1960s, 70s and 80s, the factory manufactured porcelain and stoneware products designed by Eckhoff, Eystein Sandnes (b. 1924), Leif Helge Enger (b. 1934) and Grete Rønning (b. 1937) that were mainly distinguished by soft functional forms. Today, Porsgrund remains Norway's largest ceramics company, producing a wide range of quality products from dinner services to sanitary ware.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/cc3fd6347512d0339074fd84439eb2bcda3ad601121b9687eeb8ee088b1a8d7e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b732f4afd49181e5c1149fcbcd91f57663c0dbb40b047a9fa6338600f65dd76d.jpg)  
Nora Gulbrandsen, Model no. 1848 coffee service for Porsgrund, 1930s

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/df72c6d27a4d10b7b2a3186c3c458d4dcb05e1aa54cd5e693f069d6d8c3ebeeb.jpg)

Nora Gulbrandsen, porcelain vase for Porsgrund, c. 1931-1933

Nora Gulbrandsen, porcelain tureen for Porsgrund, 1927

Tias Eckhoff, Det Riflede The Fluted One) porcelain service for Porsgrund, 1949

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ba024785b716d8de9f44cebf5ec07f4955363fbc0ef01f3114cfb4da726f39f1.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/34c3803058a219709cc8bb72cd49bb1bec1bc6301ef943fcc1ae0ccba8f23b98.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/57d8003ecd28d79b2e32381661ad5fb9743c4e283350e49802ffab36a6be4dad.jpg)  
Ulla Procopé, Valencia tableware for Arabia, c. 1960

# ulla procopé

The Finnish ceramicist Ulla Procopé studied at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki. In 1948, she joined the designplanning studio at Arabia, which was then under the directorship of Kaj Franck. Working alongside Saara Hopea and Kaarina Aho (b. 1925), she designed a number of utilitarian ceramic products such as her Oriental-style GA teapot (1953) and covered marmalade jar (c. 1957), which although industrially produced had strong handmade qualities. In 1957, she designed her popular flameproof, stackable and multi-functional cooking set known as Liekki (Flame). This dark brown stoneware range was extremely innovative, with the pot lids doubling as serving dishes. Procopé also designed the dishwasher-proof "oven-to-table" Ruska service in 1960, which had a mottled brown glaze that gave the feeling of handcrafted pottery even though it was industrially mass-produced. Around the same time, Procopé designed her well-known Valencia tableware range (c. 1960), which was inspired by traditional blue and white artisan pottery from Spain. More decorative than her earlier designs, this folk-style earthenware range remains in production today and is generally regarded as a classic Arabia design. After Franck, Procopé was the most prominent designer of industrially produced ceramic wares at Arabia, and as such was awarded a Diplóme d'Honneur at the 1957 (XI) Milan Triennale. Throughout her career, Procopé demonstrated through her best-selling product designs that industrially produced ceramics did not have to embody an impersonal machine aesthetic, but through the use of earth tones, semi-matt glazes and soft yet functional forms, could possess an engaging and characterful allure. In doing so, she had a significant impact on the public perception of Scandinavian Design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/982256448338c62c817ab22d8753da851ae73c023f9e5f0a901536df6ea1ec1f.jpg)

Jens Quistgaard, cast-iron candleholder for Dansk International Designs, c. 1955

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6d83fdd97f18973f65f20bb0952cf5d23b8f4d19101102bcba9cb69e68420677.jpg)

# jens quistgaard

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9d65f5e2b61e9bee3a1b2f340a350fd941f42674ea1e0d0dfc4ff99c54fad593.jpg)

One of Denmark's leading designers, Jens H. Quistgaard was the son of the sculptor Harald Quistgaard, who was a professor at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen. At a young age he learnt the art of woodcarving and later recalled, "I could not keep away from it [wood]; it fascinated me." He later trained and worked as a sculptor, carpenter, ceramicist and draughtsman, utilizing diverse other materials - clay, silver, bronze, iron and steel - but wood remained his material of choice. Quistgaard also served an apprenticeship with the Georg Jensen silversmithy. Following the Second World War, he became an independent designer and designed a variety of products, including a stoneware teapot with a brass and cane handle (1949) that was inspired by Oriental antecedents. In 1954, Quistgaard was awarded the Lunning Prize and the same year co-founded with the American entrepreneur, Ted Nierenberg, a manufacturing company, Dansk International Designs. He ran the firm with Nierenberg until 1984, and during their thirty-year partnership was responsible for the majority of the designs produced by this company. Quistgaard's products were distinguished by the use of subtle organic forms and by a super lative handling of materials. His work, such as the enamelled steel Kobenstyle cookware (1954) or the teak and steel Fjord cutlery (1954), allowed the materials used to express their inherent qualities. The sculptural form of Quistgaard's famous teak ice bucket (1960) was inspired by his study of the wooden hulls of Viking ships, while its bridge-like handle recalls those found on early Japanese ceramic vessels. Since 1984, he has operated a studio in Copenhagen and has designed buildings, furniture and jewellery for both European and American companies, Quistgaard's well-designed and well-executed products typify the Danish Modern style and reflect his interest in both the traditional crafts and earlier historical precedents. The soft and fluid outlines of his designs - often copied but rarely equalled - also attest to his belief that "the craftsman who designs for mass production must first of all know and appreciate his material. And man must manage the result, not the machine. ... He should create an object of sculptural worth with good proportions."

Jens Quistgaard, Koben-International Designs, 1954

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/527efb0c8a65031a8841b13243337061e4f35bf3978856f8d7d93395ddcfc282.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c9a75e92f6d5d7b50fef4c49ac3e818834150228c54bb727351f5bc9592e358c.jpg)

Jens Quistgaard, Fjord cutlery for Dansk International Designs, 1953

Jens Quistgaard, teak icebucket for Dansk International Designs, 1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5e22640a8144bccb60b752558673e3ddb85faee960252d0c9cb2fae6231e9d74.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7960f97df504629ed94070f393ce86491553604babe975fc646ecd0d60a629c5.jpg)

Ingegerd Raman, Domino stoneware range for Gustaysberg, 1998

Ingegerd Råman, Slowfox glass vases for Orrefors, 2000

# ingegerd ráman

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/baad2b71e5557f1fc5e3039e269b0a61d0b8cc60dca1efcf99d110ba8f398d1d.jpg)

One of Sweden's leading glassware designers, Ingegerd Ráman studied at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) in Stockholm from 1962 to 1968, and at the Istituto Statale D'Arte per la Ceramica in Faenza, Italy. After completing her academic training, she worked as a designer at Johansfors glassworks from 1968 to 1972, prior to establishing her own ceramics studio in 1972. From 1981 to 1998, she was one of the principal designers at Skruf glassworks, producing a number of notable designs, not least her Bellman range (1992), which re-popularized the use of small schnapps glasses, and her widely published Mamsell carafe (1990). Her glass designs for Skruf possessed a remarkable simplicity of form, which enabled the intrinsic nature of the material to emerge, producing a sense of lightness and fragility. Like that of earlier Scandinavian glassware designers from the 1930s and 1940s, Ráman's work has an unpretentious quality, which contrasts strongly with the colourful and at times bravado work of other contemporary Swedish glass designers. Her belief that design should synthesize "simplicity, function and aesthetic values" is demonstrated by her Oil

Bottle of 1990, which has a spherical stopper that is slightly, though imperceptibly, flattened so that it will not roll off a table. Râman continually varies her product designs so as to create "family groups" of vases, bottles, bowls and jugs. Since 1999 she has produced art glass for the Orrefors glassworks, which like her earlier designs for Skruf is notable for the purity of its forms. These pieces, however, such as the Tanterella, Skyline and Slowfox vases of 200o, are embellished with simple, non-figurative etched motifs that enhance the inherent qualities of the material. She has also designed ceramics for Gustavsberg, worked as a design consultant to Trussardi in Milan, and advised the Portuguese government on design education. In recognition of her unquestionable mastery of glassware design, Raman has received numerous accolades for her work, including a total of nineteen Excellent Swedish Design Awards and the coveted Prince Eugen Medal in 1998.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3221fced5b5262b849b423be67d58e57ba15705a9af68238732364377ae10e25.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/71686f25ccdc8acfde46fce0bf155995454a9b8b53bbb21b58bfab7b90e96b58.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/647491c1afe9630cf0a8ce04f65234d4c4dfc5c63b128301bf058cce398ec3b6.jpg)

Ingegerd Ráman, Skyline vases for Orrefors, 2000

Ingegerd Ráman, Tanterella decanter for Orrefors, 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/29b2cbba36da01c867f4d143104a36e2126f210d6a2167f0b42ece9c5ee9e51d.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8271d56bbf09b07dcf24f001040f67b99ea60a2af40b06c241c2007613577991.jpg)  
Aino & Alvar Aalto, 244 Series Riihimäki Flower bowls for Riihimäki, 1933

## riihimäki

The Osakeyhtio Riihimäki (Riihimäki Joint Stock Company) was founded by M. A. Kolehmainen and H. G. Paloheimo in 1910 as a glass manufactory. Initially the glassworks concentrated on the production of domestic glassware, but in 1911 a second factory was established for the production of container glass (bottles and jars). In 1919, the company began the manufacture of blown window glass, and by the 1920s it had become the largest glassware manufacturer in Finland. Around this time it began commissioning domestic product designs from, among others, Tyra Lundgren (1897-1979) and Eva Gyldén (1885-1973). In 1928, the glassworks sponsored a glass competition that was won by Henry Ericsson (1898-1933), who was a close friend of Alvar Aalto (having shared a studio with him). Riihimäki subsequently hired Ericsson, who designed engraved glassware that was shown at the 1929-1930 "Exposición Internacional de Barcelona" and at the 1930 "Exposition Internationale, Coloniale, Maritime et d'Art Flamand" in Antwerp. In 1932, upon Ericsson's insistence the company hired Gunnel Nyman, who remained at the glassworks until 1947, designing both production glass and art

glass. Riihimäki sponsored other public competitions in 1933 and 1936, for which it received submissions from, among others, Alvar Aalto and Arttu Brummer (1891-1951), both of whom later designed products for the company. After the factory was modernized in the mid-1930s, the company was re-christened Riihimäki Lasi Oy in 1937. Two years later, it began the production of crystal glassware and in 1941 merged with the Kauklahti and Ryttylä glassworks. In 1945, Brummer designed his Finlandia vase, which with its broken-ice-like form predicted the more expressive approach to glassware design that flourished in Finland during the post-war years. The following year, the company was joined by Helena Tynell (b. 1918), who similarly explored the expressive and optical qualities of glass with sculptural asymmetrical forms. In 1949 Nanny Still McKinney also began designing glassware for Riihimäki, which included blown art glass as well as moulded utility glass that distinguished itself by its unusual forms and textured surface decoration. Still McKinney remained at Riihimäki until 1976, when the glassworks discontinued its production of blow-moulded glass to concentrate solely on the mass-manufacture of commercial glass, such as bottles and jars. Although it was the largest producer of household glassware in Finland from the 1920s to the 1960s, and was also the first Nordic company to manufacture lightweight bottles in 1983, Riihimäki eventually merged with the Karhula glassworks in 1988 and was finally closed down in 1995. In testimony to Riihimäki's proud heritage, the Suomen Lasimuseo (Finnish Glass Museum) is now housed in one of the factory's buildings, designed by Tapio Wirkkala.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7acb312025b26c1c3b86158d6202e98f5475f5fc7e625af10c4065da685e25ef.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2828730f18f3a8c5103c238491281bf4de98809a9ddb7e29497efe45a5b5af52.jpg)  
Nanny Still McKinney, decanter and glasses for Riihimäki, c. 1970  
Arttu Brummer, Finlandia vase for Riihimäki, 1945

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/35a21849462292a40e499d3082a960ab1bcd271f7a58d2f3973648d63180daf5.jpg)  
Johan Rohde, Model no. 62 Acorn flatware for Georg Jensen, 1915

# johan rohde95

Having initially trained in medicine as a young man, Johan Rohde developed a strong interest in the arts and in 1881 enrolled at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in Copenhagen to study painting and graphics. The following year, he established the Kunstnernes Studieskole (Artist's Studio School), where he taught anatomy classes to the art students. Six years later, his paintings were exhibited for the first time at the Royal Danish Academy. In the mid-1890s, he met Georg Jensen while he was still working as a foreman in the metalware workshop of Mogens Ballin (1871-1914). Rohde subsequently commissioned Jensen in 1903 to fabricate a number of objects that he had designed for his own personal use. Slowly a friendship developed between the two men and around 1906, Jensen persuaded Rohde to produce several designs for his newly established silversmithy. The following year, the Danske Kunstindustrimuseum (Danish Museum of Applied Arts) in Copenhagen staged a one-man show of his work, and from 1908 to 1912 Rohde headed the Kunstnernes Studieskole. In 1915, Georg Jensen introduced Rohde's most famous flatware design, known as the Acorn pattern, which remains one of the firm's best-selling products today. The highly stylized Acorn pattern can be seen as an evolution of Jensen's more naturalistic Arts & Crafts style designs. Although Rohde was Jensen's closest collaborator, it was not until 1917 that they entered into a formal contractual agreement together. During the 1920s and 1930s Rohde's silverware designs, such as his exquisite pitcher from around 1925 or his spunsilver teapot with ebony handle of 1933-1936, became increasingly simplified and organic in form. Apart from his well-known silverware patterns for Georg Jensen, Rohde also designed textiles and furniture that reflected his own highly personal and idiosyncratic style. Much of his early furniture, including his sumptuous sofa of 1897-1898, was distinguished by a simplified geometric vocabulary of form. Like many turn-of-the-century Scandinavian designers, Rohde mastered a number of different materials by initially creating objects for his own home. His work not only marked the important transition from the Arts & Crafts style to Scandinavian Modernism, but also predicted the soft organic forms that came to typify the work of the next generation of Danish designers.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e1aa0d8c44597fc76a639be1abfc0bdaf8f59684abbfa327a7ac988a38ceee7c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3fa2f769ce3975ba1c93364c4a0403d536c5a353982130078a7530575de59e6c.jpg)  
Δ Johan Rohde, sofa made by H.P. & L. Larsen, c. 1900  
4 Johan Rohde, Model no. 478 silver box for cards and gambling chips for Georg Jensen, 1926

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e808aba4303fb18ce6073153701aabf9b5e828862900d8498cbbde44a2f679fc.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e887bec7ae619bc10789aabde7a60eda763a61a519fba3c629fa3717ab30593d.jpg)

44 Johan Rohde, Model no. 432 silver pitcher for Georg Jensen, 1920

Johan Rohde, Model no. 45 silver tea service for Georg Jensen, 1915

Johan Rohde, Model no. 787 spun silver tea service for Georg Jensen, c. 1933-1936

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/25d319ce61ceddb0b31cd7275ca5e579c6658973ecafbf2d0a45556aadb3d69e.jpg)

Alf Wallander, porcelain vase for Rörstrand, 1901

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f0b1585f121e6fba85f91e9d29702d339458ceb9ce60310a1e9c885a3767fe89.jpg)  
Per Algot Eriksson, Cyclamen vase for Rörstrand, c. 1900

# rörstrand

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ba90e6f9d09ff5ba35f61df2e284688bae04f0142434069cacb6524ba004d60f.jpg)

In 1726 the Swedish Chamber of Commerce founded a factory at Rörstrand in Stockholm, making it the first Scandinavian manufacturer of ceramics to be given crucial government support. Johann Wolff, who had previously worked in the Copenhagen potteries, initially managed the works, which during his two-year appointment began manufacturing cobalt blue and white faience. During the early 18th century, Rörstrand became especially noted for its bianco sopra bianco (white on white) painting. Later in 1790 the factory began producing an English-style cream-glazed earthenware known as Flnt-porslin. In 8 the actory acquired its main competitor, Mariebergs Porslinfabrik, and in1873 established the Arabia factory in Finland in order to take advantage of the burgeoning Russian market. Throughout most of the 19th century, Rörstrand manufactured ceramic designs that were based on successful models developed by other factories, such as its well-known Blå Blom service (1870), which emulated an earlier English design. It was not until 1895, when Alf Wallander (18621914) was appointed its art director, that the factory began producing truly original and innovative ceramics of its own. Pri-

marily responsible for the introduction of the Art Nouveau style into the factory's æuvre, Wallander designed numerous vases decorated with highly stylized motifs of flowers and leaves or abstracted female figures painted in subtle dusky under-glazed shades of pink, blue and brown. These Art Nouveau designs were distinguished by the use of soft modelling and melting organic forms. Wallander also designed a number of sculptural high-relief vases that were stylistically inspired by Japanese ceramics, such as his Magpie in a Tree vase of 1897 (now in the National Museum, Stockholm). His designs for Rörstrand were awarded a Grand Prix at the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale", and were also retailed by Siegfried Bing (1838-1905) in his influential Paris store, L'Art Nouveau. Other designers, such as Anna Katarina Boberg (1864-1935), Karl Lindström (1865-1936) and Nils Erik Lundström (1865- 1960) also modelled Art Nouveau style vases for the company. During this period, Rörstrand diversified its output by manufacturing various art ceramics, including figurines of animals and birds. From 1917 to 1927 Edward Hald worked as a freelance designer for the factory, producing

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0894eb8472bbfcc58026c0e63921836d52c92907617e616d3f8b86cc238d6f26.jpg)

Blà Blom service for Rörstrand, 1870 - a design tht was rgially prouced by Gustavsberg and copied an earlier British model

Louise Adelborg, Swedish Grace tableware for Rörstrand, 1930 - This range of domestic tableware with its simple repeating pressed pattern representing ears of wheat (a symbol of nourishment) was first exhibited at the landmark 1930 Stockholm Exhibition. Simple yet elegant, this design came to epitomize "Swedish Grace" - a term coined at the 1925 Stockholm Exhibition by the British critic Morton Shand in the Architectural Review

Ostindia tableware for Rörstrand, 1931

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0487ce671489b64be576722ae7966968d6fdcfa2599131d1d209cac9a3fb6a60.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/852ece7667c993c081814cbcfe30c4e969293935881173a2750b6b811fb5dc42.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c3cc97886783de2ae3b2410b61b6a062a4168617b3969876b185bc609eb0b578.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/65eee323c8827d0cd9fa77f88176251b233d640554dba3c0f140f430c4efddfe.jpg)

numerous earthenware designs that combined rustic motifs with classical forms. In 1926 Rörstrand moved from Stockholm to Gothenburg, and four years later exhibited at the landmark 1930 Stockholm Exposition. The factory's most notable exhibit was a domestic tableware range designed by Louise Adelborg (1885-1971) decorated with a simple repeating pressed pattern of ears of wheat. In 1931, Gunnar Nylund (1904-1989) was appointed Rörstrand's artistic director, and the following year the factory's operations were expanded to include a new site in Lidköping. Five years later the whole factory was relocated there. During the post-war period, Rörstrand produced simple stoneware designs by, among others, Gunnar Nylund and Hertha Bengtson (1921-1995), which reflected the contemporary taste for abstract organic forms. During the 1950s the factory also produced a number of informal tableware ranges designed by Marianne Westman (b. 1925), including Mon Amie (1951), Pomona (1956), and Picknick (1956), which incorporated colourful printed patterns of highly-stylized flowers, vegetables and fish. Later in the 1960s Rörstrand manufactured the porcelain Pop service (1968) by Inger Persson

Inger Persson, Pop stoneware teapot for Rörstrand, 1969

(b. 1936) available in a variety of colours, the most popular option being a vibrant orange-red. Later Persson won a Design Plus Award for the Spisa range (c. 1978-1980) of high-fired earthenware, which with its brown earth-coloured glaze and simple fluted decoration exemplified the somewhat bland functional yet homely aesthetic of industrially produced Scandinavian ceramics from this period. In 1985, Rörstrand issued a limited edition of teapots that were retailed by Rosenthal's Studio House stores. This collection comprised eighty variations by four different designers, including Signe Persson-Melin (b. 1925), who worked as a designer for Rörstrand from 1980 to 1987. In 1983 Rörstrand was purchased by Arabia, then four years later merged with Gustavsberg, and in 1990 was taken over by the Hackman Group. Since then Rörstrand has manufactured innovative ceramics by various Scandinavian designers, most notably Jonas Bohlin and Pia Törnell (b. 1963), which testify to the continuing Swedish delight in functional simplicity and beautiful forms.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/65e96a3a1eb923228bab7b6318d199ebb008313e9c8671dc7790be83bbcf8bbe.jpg)  
Jonas Bohlin, Qvint tableware for Rörstrand, 2000  
Pia Törnell, Arcus vase for Rörstrand, 1995  
Pia Törnell, Grade tableware for Rörstrand, 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/64c9b6583906c10b2546638127477a921673df4aae9099cc87bdcd13fd6824cf.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7d63fc9bbfc7d2dc99fb33345f8c919a8b201673fe52fb7861793a6e13ad16ec.jpg)

Anton Rosen, detail of chair designed site-specifically for the Palace Hotel, c. 1909-1910

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/46383b732af0e38d35b916679a240a5041ae4b31826d47af273f5447ed07af3f.jpg)  
Anton Rosen, stoneware tobacco jar, 1925

# anton rosen

The Danish architect and designer Anton Rosen was the principal architect for the Landsudstillingen (Danish National Exhibition) held in Århus in 1909, and was also responsible for the design of the Palace Hotel in Copenhagen (1907-1910), which was intended to provide luxurious accommodation for officials visiting the nearby Rådhuset (Town Hall). For this latter project, he designed site-specific furniture, including a mahogany armchair (now in the collection of the Danske Kunstindustrimuseum in Copenhagen), which incorporated decorative metal fittings that shared a similar aesthetic to Johan Rohde's stylized floral designs for Georg Jensen. Rosen would certainly have been aware of the metalwork being produced by Georg Jensen, as later in 1917 he designed a ceremonial silver cup for the Butchers' Guild that the silversmithy executed for him. This unique design was richly encrusted with hand-sculpted figurative elements and incorporated decorative seals suspended from stylized rams heads and an Arts & Crafts style band of galleons with billowing sails. Around this time, Rosen also designed various other silver objects for Georg Jensen that were notable for their use of coral, malachite,

amber and other semi-precious stones. During the 1920s, however, he adopted a less ornate style, as demonstrated by his celadon green glazed tobacco jar designed in 1925. Like other Danish designers at the time, Rosen was influenced on the one hand by the emergence of Modernism in Western Europe, and on the other by the art of Japan. With his well-crafted and thoughtfully detailed work, which formed the transition between the New Art of the early 20th century and the Modern Movement, Rosen sought to enhance the beauty of everyday living while reviving a national Danish identity in the handcrafts and design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9445a925f7f82953b8b011c364443904dd1beb53fc2b3762bfd3022565b158b3.jpg)

Blue Fluted (Plain) service for Royal Copenhagen, pattern introduced c. 1775 - This design was inspired by oriental precedents and more specifically a pattern by Meissen. It was re-modelled by Arnold Krog in the 1880s.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dfc90feeb4c01e1eb32a9c469a5f81006413116ec6b6ebf219352bbda0063709.jpg)

# royal copenhagen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8db349760ab5a75512a9cedec916c58d3a7a35f0450bff9cc6e815b36c46ca67.jpg)

(founded 1775, Copenhagen, Denmark)

The origins of Royal Copenhagen can be traced back to 1775, when Denmark's first porcelain factory to enjoy royal patronage was established. Following this, over the next four years the factory's chemist Frantz Heinrich Müller (1732-1820) experimented with Bornholm kaolin in an unsuccessful attempt to produce high-quality biscuitware. Around the same time, the factory's debts were mounting to such an extent that in 1779, King Frederick V stepped in to assume personal financial responsibility for it. The rationale for saving the factory stemmed from his hope of establishing Denmark as one of the leading cultural centres in Europe, and the production of fine porcelain - then known as "white gold" - was one of the surest ways of achieving this. The factory was subsequently re-named Den Kongelige Danske Porcelains Fabrik and a ban was imposed on all imports of European porcelain into Denmark. During the 1780s and 1790s, the factory mainly produced blue-painted services for everyday use, often copying or adapting shapes and patterns from Meissen and other European manufacturers. It also manufactured multi-coloured wares and designs in the Rococo style that

were more experimental both technically and artistically. The most notable design to emerge from Royal Copenhagen during this period was the exquisite Flora Danica (Danish Flowers) service, which was commissioned by the Danish court in c. 1789 for presentation to Catherine the Great of Russia. This famous service, which was ultimately retained by the King of Denmark, was painstakingly decorated with flowers and plants copied from botanical engravings that illustrated G. C. Oeder's Flora of the Danish Kingdom. The engravings had been devised by a Nuremberg artist living in Copenhagen, J. C. Bayer, who was employed by the factory from 1776. Impressively, Bayer virtually single-handedly painted all the highly detailed naturalistic floral motifs on the service's pieces, the forms of which were designed by F. L. Bradt. When the Danish court finally ceased production of the service in 1802, it comprised a staggering 1,802 items (1,530 surviving today), making it one of the largest services in the world. During the early years of the 19th century, the patterning of the factory's blue-painted porcelain was simplified in order to make it easier and less expensive to produce. In 1813, Denmark was facing an

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/16580ec5cde3964a0d3b8a3169f34c4f6b60c08c38d5ef628077ea95190c5cef.jpg)  
Flora Danica service for Royal Copenhagen, c. 1790  
Bla Blomst (Blue Flower) service for Royal Copenhagen, 1779

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f5f5adc7adbfe82c6e7773dfe8fdd1b84efa615367737c1c21a5b764e79bfa0f.jpg)

economic crisis, and the following year its confidence was dented still further by the loss of Norway. During this period, the factory put the development of new products on hold and relied on the production of Rococo style dinner, coffee, tea and chocolate services with its famous Blue Fluted and Blue Flower patterns. It was not until the 1820s, on the instigation of the architect and designer G.F. Hetsch (1788-1864), that Neo-Classical wares in the new Empire style began to be manufactured by the company. Apart from these, during the 1830s and 1840s the factory also produced a number of biscuitware figurines and reliefs modelled on works by the Danish Neo-Classical sculptor, Bertel Thorvaldsen (1770-1844). The superlative quality of Royal Copenhagen's biscuitware products led the factory to become one of Europe's most respected porcelain manufacturers. In 1868, the company passed into private ownership, and over the following years the once state-subsidized enterprise gradually became a profitable commercial venture. Royal Copenhagen was subsequently acquired in 1882 by the engineer Philip Schou, who had previously founded the successful earthenware

works Aluminia in 1863. Two years later, the factory was moved from its premises in Købmagergade to a larger purpose-built site in Frederiksberg, then a suburb of Copenhagen. Having dealt with the production side of the business, Schou set about revitalizing the artistic aspects of the manufactory. To this end, he hired Arnold Krog in 1884 as a designer, and the following year appointed him as the firm's artistic director. The same year, Krog and Schou went on a study tour of Holland, Belgium, England and France, and while in Paris made the acquaintance of Siegfried Bing (1838-1905), who owned an oriental warehouse in Paris that catered to the increasing demand for Aesthetic Movement objets d'art. On his return to Copenhagen, Krog not only oversaw a renewal of the company's Blue Fluted pattern but also introduced a new decorative technique in which cobalt blue pigment was combined with glaze and then applied to unfired porcelain blanks. The pigment was then gently scraped with a knife to reveal areas of the underlying white material. This layered underglaze painting technique was mainly used for designs that were decorated with motifs inspired by Japanese engravings and woodcuts, such as those illustrated in Siegfried Bing's journal, Le Japon Artistique. Krog was also responsible for the introduction of other eclectic and revivalist styles, including Rococo, Renaissance, Viking, Persian, Chinese and Russian. It was, however, the formal simplicity and delicate abstracted decoration of his Japanese-style wares of the 1880s that had the greatest stylistic influence. Apart from soft cobalt blue, other muted coloured pigments, including a dusky pink, a dark green and a misty grey, were developed to withstand the high firing temperatures used for Krog's innovative underglaze painting technique. During his tenure, Krog recruited a number of progressive artists to produce designs for the factory, including Carl Frederick Liisberg (1860-1909), Carl Mortensen (1866-1945), Marianne H0st (1865- 1943), V.T. Fischer (1857-1928), Gotfred Rode (1830-1878) and Bertha Nathanielsen (1869- 1914). During the early decades of the 20th century, Royal Copenhagen established workshops dedicated to the design and manufacture of stoneware products, such as Krog's Cobra vase (1914), and the development of special glazes. Between the World Wars, the factory became renowned for its production of sculptural fgures designed by artists such as Arno Malinowski, Jais Nielsen (1895-1961), Gerhard Henning (1880-1967) and Knud Kyhn (1880-1969). In 1933, Axel Salto joined the design team at Royal Copenhagen and subsequently developed numerous stoneware art pieces with expressive sculptural forms that predicted the post-war Scandinavian fascination with organic shapes. During this period Nils Thorsson (1898-1975) also designed a number of stoneware objects as well as practical tableware for the company. From the post-war period to the present day, Royal Copenhagen has continued to produce its well-known historic designs, such as Flora Danica and Blue Fluted, alongside more contemporary products, such as the Blakant (Blue Rim) service (1965) by Grethe Meyer, the shell-shaped Konkylle service (1977) by Arje Griegst (b. 1938), and the Ursula faience dinnerware (1993) by Ursula Munch-Petersen (b. 1937). Over recent decades, Royal Copenhagen was amalgamated with the Holmegaard glassworks (1975), the Georg Jensen silversmithy (1986), and the Bing & Grøndahl porcelain factory (1987), and today is part of the Royal

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c22b5c3513bcec1725d1296dd3990b52616f71f79f47ab110ef2080e0272a858.jpg)  
Patrick Nordström, stoneware jar for Royal Copenhagen, c. 1915  
Royal Copenhagen, wall plate decorated by Bertha Nathanielsen, 1903

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a71b4c8031e2af4a2988d217de4a3a7e32d4a6a413c89b592d22cd5fb6d997c9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4d30287ade0b645c1f21b4fcb6f60b7b1868a5ba106a26c775372c3bf374e0d1.jpg)  
Ole Jensen, Ole citrus press/jug for Royal Copenhagen, 1995  
Ursula Munch-Petersen, Ursula service for Royal Copenhagen, 1993

Scandinavia Group owned by Carlsberg - the brewery that has dominated Danish cultural lfe for over a hundred years. Like so many other Scandinavian design-led companies, Royal Copenhagen manufactures high quality industrially produced domestic wares, such as the Ole citrus press/jug (1997) by Ole Jensen (b. 1958), alongside exclusive art pieces. For over two hundred and twenty-five years, the company has been at the forefront of the Scandinavian manufacturing industry and has played a vital role in defining and promoting a Danish identity in the applied arts.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3e88126774ba5b84d889e7972577c8d8905508a7f5cba001a9953dbce9f47eac.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0c79383d266300086258df6de4de55bbfe15f9d065e7396816612bbdc9bbdd88.jpg)  
Sixten Sason, Saab 92.

## saab n  in

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b286af305d28e3829477109945fa77773c77da810efce3e9378c9257075b4d98.jpg)

The history of the well-known Swedish car manufacturer Saab can be traced back to the Svenska Aeroplan AB (Swedish Aircraft Company), which was founded bythe financiers Marcus Wallenberg and Axel Wennergren in response to the Swedish government's re-arming initiative prior to the outbreak of the Second World War. Having recruited some forty engineers from Boeing, this early manufacturing venture initially produced versions of Northrop and North American aircraft before developing its own designs, such as the Saab 17 bomber, the Saab 21, which featured the world's first ejector seat, and the Saab 29, which was the first European swept-wing fighter. In 1945, the Swedish Air Force stopped purchasing aircraft, so Saab decided to transfer its skils and expertise to the design and production of automobiles instead. Thecopan ssigfifteeircraft ivelent  o. Because the engineers had never worked on such a project before, they were "entirely unfettered by the conventions and traditions of the established automobile industry", and as a result, developed a completely different kind of car with front-wheel i and an aerodynamically shaped stabilizing wing. This remarkable prototype - now known as the Saab 92001 (1947) - formed the basis from which Saab's first production car evolved. With a truly revolutionary streamlined monocoque body that was styled by Sixten Sason and engineered by Gunnar Ljungström, the Saab 92 was wholly influenced both formally and technically by aeronautical design. It was also the first small fourseater "people's car" to be produced in Sweden and reflected Sason's design philosophy that while form should follow function, products should not look too utilitarian. The Saab 92 evolved over the following decades into other Saab models, including the well-known Saab 95 estate car (1959) and the highly successful Saab 96 (1960), which was one of the company's best-sellers with more than 547,000 sold within a twenty-year period. Later, Sason and Ljungström developed a new car, the Saab 99, which was compact yet spacious inside, aerodynamically efficient, and economical to run. Launched around 1968, the Saab 99 introduced anumber fnnovative eaturesover the followig years, such as headlamp cleaning (1970), selfrepairing bumpers, heated seats (1971) and sideimpact door beams (1972). Bjorn Envall, who assisted Sason with the styling of the Saab 99, once tellingly remarked, "t's a very dangerous thing if you're getting too serious. The exterior of the car should tell you something. It should speak to you somehow." This remark reveals a very Scandinavian perception of design - one that understands the importance of imbuing a product with a gentle aesthetic that communicates fundamentally humanist goals. The Saab 99 also became the blueprint for the company's later cars, including the Saab 90 (1985) and the Saab 9-5 SE (1997). During the 1970s the company began producing "medium segment" cars, and later in the 1980s began manufacturing even larger vehicles for the executive market. Having previously merged with the truck manufacturer Scania in 1969, Saab eventually came under the aegis of General Motors in 1990, when the American automotive giant acquired a 50% stake in the company. Typical of a Scandinavian company, Saab is committed to sustainable development - from the reduction of emissions to the minimization of "resource use". The company also operates a highly ethical social programme, which includes the sponsorship of

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2b19beab0c09c3aa73eef3ece0017a94c7850e11b7e906604d52311fb16504a0.jpg)

various environmental projects and the running of its own rehabilitation centre, known as the Component, for staff who "fall victim to disease, accidents and work-related injuries" Throughout its history, Saab Automobile AB has combined design, performance and safety in the development of its high-quality and unmistakable vehicles, while its sister company Saab Aircraft AB manufactures both commercial and military aircraft that are based on the continuing application of innovative engineering concepts.

Δ Sixten Sason's Saab 92 in front of the Saab J21 plane, C. 1948

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/34ebead510b07393a4d643ecd733b467579c2fd4708860ecbb01bb1edacb0bce.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b2a1253461434ba6f1e21375a5569ea2cba9cfdbedc870c1eecd59dd083fd79f.jpg)  
Saab 90, 1985 Saab 9-3 convertible, 2002

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8158cc50a407a76137276575549623ee802f863b1c36ec67bc50308faa01a0ac.jpg)

99g qes

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/779a6fcaec2989f35f843c96f07aca2776052ad39a88b00bd4e690a80e33bac8.jpg)  
Saab J-29F Tunnan, Swedish Airforce F3 Wing, introduced in 1948 and operational from 1951 to 1956  
Saab 2000 (SE-003) airplane demonstrated at Farnborough Air Show, 1992

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/da483725596ffd0b9268a70f4377e4f052cf244ab66ca3d513a3be5fbb55d973.jpg)

v Saab Viggen with Sidewinder and Sky Flash missiles, c. 1982 - This plane was introduced in 1967 and has been operational since 1971

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8f3bbfbee2cf01c92cd96de37e7a3cf70548aa91ca8bfc09cd2c2dec4edeaec2.jpg)

Eliel Saarinen, wooden chair with heart motifs, 1909 - This design shows the strong influence of the British Arts & Crafts movement, especially the work of Charles Rennie Mackintosh and Charles oysey

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ca88cab13c8dc42014e7c40b232aff07f8d27092e6963c1e2e09b2607663d3fe.jpg)

# eliel saarinen

(b. 1873 Rantasalmi, Finland - d. 1950 Bloomfield Hills, Michigan, USA)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8633df47461501c3eb2a964020546bf81f768e4ce9a2739d7067bf96f9e66bd8.jpg)

One of the great figures of Finnish design, Gottlieb Eliel Saarinen studied fine art at the University of Helsinki and later architecture at the Helsinki Polytechnic, alongside Herman Gesellius (1874- 1916) and Armas Lindgren (1874-1929). After graduating in 1898, the three young architects formed a partnership and were subsequently commissioned to design the Finnish Pavilion for the 1900 Paris "Exposition Universelle et Internationale" With its Karelian-style roof-line and ceiling painted by Akseli Gallén-Kallela (1865-1931), the resulting building exemplified the emerging National Romantic Movement. In reaction to the political suppression of Finland by Russia and the intensifying programme of "Russification" of Finnish culture, Saarinen and other protagonists of National Romanticism sought to define and promote an autonomous national character withiall the s. Lookg to ancient Karelian culture for an untainted Finnish identity, they embellished and romanticized historical fact in order to create the mythology of "Karelianism", which was a projection of their own nationalistic aspirations. Gesellius, Lindgren and Saarinen were also strongly influenced by the Glasgow School and the Wiener Sezession, as the bold

massing of elements in their buildings demonstrates, for example the Finnish National Museum (1902-1912) and the Helsinki railway station (1904-1919), the latter of which Saarinen alone designed. By the turn of the century, the practice was inundated with architectural commissions and the three architects decided to flee Helsinki for a rural retreat. Their destination was a sixteen-acre site on a forested ridge above the shores of Lake Hvitträsk, where they each built a house around a central grassed courtyard and a large communal studio that connected the Saarinen and Lindgren houses. Each house was an intensely personal Gesamtkunstwerk - the interiors of the Saarinen House included furniture, lighting, textiles, wall paintings and carpets, all designed site-specifically by Eliel Saarinen. In 1905 Saarinen subsequently purchased Lindgren's share of the estate, and later in 1916 he acquired Gesellius. During this period, Saarinen continued to promote the National Romantic Movement, and in 1912 he joined the Deutscher Werkbund. In 1922, he won second prize in the Chicago Tribune Tower competition and a year later, the Saarinen family emigrated to the United States (although they returned almost every summer to Hvitträsk until 1949). Initiall, they resided in Evanston, Illinois, while Saarinen worked on the development of the Chicago lake front. The family later settled in Ann Arbor, where Eliel Saarinen was appointed a visiting professor of architecture at the University of Michigan. In 1923, he met the wealthy newspaper baron and philanthropist George C. Booth, who asked him to draw up plans for the development of the Cranbrook Educational Community. In 1925, Saarinen moved to Bloomfield Hills and in 1932, the Cranbrook Academy of Art was officially founded with Saarinen as its first president. Under his directorship the Academy became the premiere design institution in America and produced many of the country's most talented designers, including Charles and Ray Eames. As director of the Cranbrook Academy, Saarinen promoted the humanist ethos of Scandinavian design and had an enormous impact on the evolution of modern design in the United States. Saarinen's only son, Eero, became an architect and was one of the greatest pioneers of American post-war design.

## timo sallil

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/79fdb841bc523d59891cfc1f3960f4a2dadb3add14eb7d347c0be273b1f54963.jpg)

Well-known among the international design community for his innovative and thought-provoking design work, the Finnish designer Timo Salli began his working career as a welder. He later studied furnituredesig athe Laht Design nstitute, ating in 92, and then studied craft and design at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki, graduating in 1996. While still at college, he worked in the design office of Stefan Lindfors before establishing his own practice in 1993. Three years later, he designed the Tramp chair for Cappellini, which was constructed from a two-piece steel frame covered with a transparent nylon net bag. Although large in scale, this design "gives a light and immaterial illusion". In 1997 he designed the quirky Jack-in-the-Box television that can be closed away into its box when not in use, so as to produce "space for human action without TV's dominating, catching eye". The same year he also designed the collapsible and semi-transparent Zik Zak chair as a one-off for the Snowcrash exhibition in Milan. Following this, in 1998 he collaborated with llkka Suppanen on the design of the Nomadhouse exhibition. Salli's work, which playfully contests the conventions of daily living, continues to exemplify the cutting ege of contemporary Scandinavian design. Describing his work as "low-tech choices for a digital era" Salli seeks to make more direct and meaningful connections between objects and users, particularly within domestic realms, where according to him our homes have become "so ful of things we no longer even see". His designs are intended to challenge our perceptions of everyday "relics", with many being multi-unctional, such as the Lamp-Lamp (2000) - a mirror that also functions as a light. Like other contemporary Scandinavian designers, Salli is very aware of changes in living patterns, especially within the home environment, and so he also promotes his designs as "basic living equipment for the urban nomad". As one of the designers associated with Snowcrash, Timo Salli demonstrates through his idiosyncratic designs an originality and inventiveness that verge on the poetic.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/58bb6bd688ff4c744f06c3b8aa904815114b5cbd2ab00a45e4b1f37dbed9a70b.jpg)  
Timo Salli, ScreenLamp light (prototype), 1998  
Timo Salli, Jack-in-the-Box television (self-production), 1997

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/72833875b229cf1cf66d7938b03514d0efa886d042e846e05146df6b3639c009.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/acfae837232a856e3d6020b80767e1d6d9ce9a889c10087c9c8befe5a9876008.jpg)  
Timo Salli, Firecase fireplace (prototype), 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/34ee9dfc34a642ed4abbd0ca7ca288b2672c881afc71aa516a617f0e9c075552.jpg)  
Axel Salto, stoneware vase with Solfatara glaze for Royal Copenhagen, 1943

## axel salto oaFderik

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8f5343234bea533d9b4732caec3ee98a6ceaa03724f604453586077beb36c0b2.jpg)

The Danish ceramicist Axel Salto studied painting at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in Copenhagen before co-founding the art periodical Klingen in 1917. From 1923 to 1925, he worked as a designer for Bing & Grøndahl alongside Jean Gauguin, the son of the celebrated French Post-Impressionist artist. At Bing & Grøndahl, Salto executed several biscuit porcelain designs embellished with enamelled decoration, which were exhibited in the Swedish Pavilion at the 1925 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes". These objects generated much interest, especially in the French press. Around the same time he began collaborating with the ceramicist Nathalie Krebs (1895-1978) on stoneware art pieces, which were produced by Saxbo - a small independent pottery that Krebs had established in 1930. These stoneware vessels were instrumental in the development of the classic Saxbo style that relied on simple forms and mottled oriental-style glazes. Like other Danish designers, Salto was deeply influenced by the formal and aesthetic qualities of antique Chinese ceramics. In 1933, he joined the design team at the Royal Copenhagen porcelain factory and worked alongside the skilled potter Carl Halier. Over the succeeding years, he modelled numerous powerfully expressive stoneware designs that possessed distinctive formal and decorative qualities. His bold ceramic pieces were distinguished by simple yet sculptural and often organic forms. Some recalled the abstracted shapes of pine cones and seed heads and featured speckled litchenous Solfatara glazes that produced a real sense of mossy, damp and earthy nature. Others possessed soft "ideal" forms decorated with incised and repeating fluted patterns. Apart from being one of the leading ceramic designers in Denmark during the inter-war years, Salto also devised patterns for book-binding papers (1934), designed a number of printed textiles for L. F. Foght (1944), and directhe restoration he rizesignd by Jren Sonne in 1846) that adorns the Thorvaldsens Museum in Copenhagen. He exhibited his work widely and received several prestigious awards, including two Grand Prix at the 1925 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts Décoratifs et Industriels Modernes" and the 1937 Paris "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9dda4acb6dd53ae718d7fd9d8f8f3265a98a04f8e4314fde7b0e10a23ecd77bb.jpg)

Moderne", and another Grand Prix at the 1951 (IX) Milan Triennale. He also wrote and illustrated a number of books, including Axel Salto's Keramik (1930), and illustrated several publications by other authors. Salto's evocative stoneware designs, with their organic forms and mottled glazes, powerfully expressed the abstract essence of nature and signalled a revival of studio pottery within Scandinavian ceramic design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9f6dfbc8339d8a48dcf3eda8832590b104c063d77db19f5b1c92844312f78186.jpg)

Axel Salto, unglazed stoneware vessels inlaid with differently coloured chamotte clay for Royal Copenhagen, c. 1950

Axel Salto, stoneware vase with Solfatara glaze for Royal Copenhagen, c. 1943

Astrid Sampe, detail of Jobs Blommor (Jobs' Flowers) textile - blue version, c. 1950

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3d956785bf1c43c36f606e55c6ddbaa3a2c0509e4052e6695329e586735a7a44.jpg)  
Astrid Sampe, Versailles polyester and viscose textile for Almedahls, 1972

# astrid sampe(

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f56e12bafce087d4bf0b7d31592cb4fcf77ed9d9db5e1b9938773b520147b53c.jpg)

The Swedish textile designer Astrid Sampe trained at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design) in Stockholm and later at the Royal College of Art in London. In 1937 she began working as a designer for Nordiska Kompaniet and exhibited at the "Exposition Internationale des Arts et Techniques dans la Vie Moderne". A year later, she became head of Nordiska's newly-founded textile design department, where she produced a number of influential designs that testified to her skill as both a colourist and a pattern maker. She later worked with the architect Sven Markelius (who had also produced designs for Nordiska) on the design of the Swedish Pavilion for the 1939 New York World's Fair. Like other designers working in the 1940s and 1950s, Sampe revitalized the long Scandinavian tradition of woven "art" textiles with her vigorous abstract patterns designed within a modern idiom. Sampe is also credited with being the first textile designer to introduce glass cloth to Sweden (1946), and was particularly influential in the development of textiles designed specifically for architectural settings. She was awarded a Grand Prix at the 1954 (X) Milan Triennale and two years later was the recipient of the

Gregor Paulsson trophy. In 1948 she co-wrote with Vera Diurson the book Textiles Illustrated, and the following year was elected an honorary member of the Royal Designers for Industry in London. She also designed textiles for Knoll International in New York and Donald Brothers in Dundee, as well as for companies in Sweden. During the 1950s, Sampe executed striking patterns for the Wohlbeck and Kasthall carpet factories, which were instrumental in revitalizing the industrial production of woven rugs. In 1955, she produced several designs for domestic linens that employed repeating geometric patterns and folk-inspired motifs that could be used in an interchangeable manner. In 1972, Sampe left Nordiska Kompaniet and established her own studio in Stockholm so as to concentrate on the design of textiles and interiors. Alongside a number of other prominent Scandinavian designers, Sampe adapted the craft ideals of the past for the industrial reality of the present and in so doing preserved and extended the traditions of Nordic textile design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7d47c2abbe32d0c288919a1693300ae8c92e8924ebcdea871d5972b1a8f36592.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2f9dc779e9a86fbbee6c3b95e880b468fddcb0558d9ab76511b3dea78c27c3cc.jpg)  
FRamHLid.  
Gudjon Samuelsson, architectural design for Yfirbygd Sundlang in Reykjavik, 1925  
Gudjon Samuelsson, white painted chair, 1926-1930

# gudjón samúelsson(150)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9053bc0f541c48c9a062ef4ddd92a71d121919f6012a719132049957eb2c0233.jpg)

The greatest protagonist of Modernism in Iceland, Gudjon Samuelsson studied architecture at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) in Copenhagen under Martin Nyrop (1849-1921), the leading figure behind the National Romantic movement in Denmark. In 1919, Samúelsson was appointed the first National Architect of Iceland and subsequently designed a number of important public buildings. These were chiefly built using reinforced concrete - a material that had been imported into Iceland from the 1900s due to the lack of local construction materials. Samuelsson's buildings were constructed in a modern style that in Iceland became known as Steinsteypuklassík (concrete classicism). For his building projects, which included the Landsbankinn (National Bank, 1922-1924) and the Lansspítalinn (National Hospital, 1925-1930), Samúelsson designed site-specific furniture, such as his white painted chair (1930) made from imported pine. This design epitomized his curious synthesizing of Neo-Classical forms with Modern Movement functionalism. In the mid-1930s, Samúelsson also designed furniture for the Icelandic Parliament Building, including chairs whose rather austere

forms were enlivened by subtle vernacular detailing that made reference to traditional Icelandic woodworking. Samuelsson's furniture was manufactured locally and because of the lack of available materials, was somewhat stylistically constrained. Based on rationalist principles, his buildings and furniture must be seen as distinctive national interpretations of Modern Movement precepts. Inspired by the functionalist approach to design, Samuelsson pioneered, along with others, such as the architect Sigurdur Gudmundsson (1885-1958), a new modern identity for Icelandic design that subtly embraced rather than rejected both national vernacularism and Scandinavian Classicism.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c636483b3b4f36d21bc0a317b96fe37fc807f8a6d33150e79b4f4e129f5c3c2b.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dac671242d65d124d986a8900fdaa6daac5a760f68e3ab5085fb81371597507e.jpg)

Thomas Sandell, T. S. chairs for Asplund, 1994

# thomals sandell

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/163179607c25ca3aa8432ccad192b28bb6b4982c663bc1cb46bdcc19a3afd616.jpg)

Thomas Sandell was born in Finland but his family moved to Sweden the same year. Between 1981 and 1985, he studied architecture at the Royal Institute of Technology in Stockholm and then worked in the architecture practice of Professor Jan Henriksson. In 1986 he executed illustrations for the Metallarbetaren magazine and worked on a number of saloon car projects for Volvo. The following year he headed a consulting group for the rebuilding of the Royal Library in Stockholm, and a year later collaborated with fellow Swedish designer Jonas Bohlin on the design of a Shaker-style interior for the Rolfs Kök restaurant. Having founded his own architecture and design practice in 1990, Sandell has since worked on numerous building and interior design projects, notably the Villa Börjesson in Aspö (1990), the café for the National Museum of Cultural History in Stockholm (1993), and interiors for the Modern Museum (1995) and the Architecture Museum (1996) in Stockholm. Apart from his architecture and interior design work, Sandell has also designed furniture for a number  leading manufacturers, including Artek, Asplund, B&B Italia, Gärsnäs and Källemo. Since the early 1990s Sandell has also

designed furniture and various domestic products for Cappellini, including his wall-mounted Key Cupboard (1992), which takes the form of an out-sized escutcheon, and his well-known polished aluminium 9235 candleholders (1995). In 1995, he was one of several designers who contributed to the popular PS series of furniture for IKEA - his PS Armoire and PS Bureau with their cut-out drawer/ door pulls and wheels for easy transportability epitomize the strong functional and aesthetic clarity nt only of his own work but that of contemporary Swedish design in general. Also in 1995 he cofounded with Ulf Sandberg the SandellSandberg partnership - a concept company with a broad approach to communication that unites advertising, branding, architecture, design and the Internet. More recently, Sandell has designed the London headquarters of Ericsson (1997), the "Insideouthouse" for Wallpaper\* magazine (1998), and interiors for the Swedish Parliament building in Stockholm (2000). He has received a total of five Excellent Swedish Design Awards and has acted as chairman of SAR (National Association of Swedish Architects) since 1999. Along with Thomas Eriksson and Jonas Bohlin's work,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c1e3f3dc391ea4625488fbbdd87e37bff769ff1f2a4c39d953cc5c27f6e6b116.jpg)

Sandell's simple, practical yet stylish designs have greatly influenced the new-found confidence and vitality of contemporary Swedish design.

Thomas Sandell, Snow cabinet storage system for Asplund, 1993 - co-designed by Jonas Bohlin

Thomas Sandell, Wedding stools for Asplund, 1992

Thomas Sandell, Air carpet for Asplund, 2001

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4a11723c660689eaa2d8ddc29230c5582fed2e31dc9caf5e0234c64741c73755.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c46a332b7d0982e74b7e15e5a1ebfcabcec7d8bac80dc60afb0455245d0b1c54.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ff060525dbd54476e198d2e5184a2788060dcd06f7d82eefb168f3e10f8ef896.jpg)  
Timo Sarpaneva, Festivo candleholders for littala, 1966

# timo sarpaneva

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ed3d7f79c5d0e46dbb60eeb57f5a1659c37356e50683d7f80f2816bd8a60bb68.jpg)

One of Finland's most prominent designers, Timo Sarpaneva studied at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design), Helsinki, from 1941 to 1948. He won second prize in a glass competition held by the Riihimäki glassworks in 1949 and the following year became a product designer and head of the exhibition section at the renowned Karhula-littala glassworks. During the early 1950s, Sarpaneva also worked extensively as a textile designer, receiving a silver medal for his textile designs at the 1951 (IX) Milan Triennale. He also taught textile printing and design at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu from 1953 to 1957, and briefly worked as art director for the Porin Puuvilla cotton mill from 1955 to 1956. It was, however, his ground-breaking glassware designs that brought him the greatest international acclaim. One of his first technical innovations at littala was a steam blowing method, which was used for his early work, such as the Kajakki (1953), Maailmankaunein (954) and Linnunsilmä (1953) vessels, as well as for his delicately colour tinted thin glass plates known as Aquarelles. These remarkable art glass pieces reflected a new sculptural confidence and creative vibrancy in Finnish design. During the mid-

1950s, Sarpaneva introduced his i-Glass range, which attempted to bridge the gap between expensive art glass and utility glassware. This industrially produced product line comprised seventeen items that were available in several colours, including lilac-grey, blue-grey, smoke-grey and green-grey, which could be used together to produce different effects. The 1950s and 1960s saw a great dynamic synergy at the littala glassworks, with Sarpaneva and Tapio Wirkkala vying good-naturedly with each other to create new forms and techniques. One of Sarpaneva's most interesting and influential glass designs from this period was his Finlandia series of vases (c. 1964), which incorporated a technique using moulds made from slabs of roughly hewn and carved alder wood, which were charred by the red-hot glass. The patterns resulting from this technique bore the close imprint of the wood's structure and powerfully evoked a sense of icy rawness. The nature of this process also ensured that each design was unique, as each time a mould was used it was burnt further, meaning that the patterns would be changed. As Leena Kattunen noted in the catalogue that accompanied the 1992 "Make Glass Not War" exhibition at the

Timo Sarpaneva, Model no. i-401 and Model no. i-403 decanters from the i-Glass range for littala, c. 1955 & c. 1956

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5ee0ff6ca53635a4036365ea4ec3bff18112a36038f3a78196fd154a37fe040e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/602965b58b2954e6b95f69abbf33a5f985e7e0371ce43bef75004ff8562b145e.jpg)

Timo Sarpaneva, Suomi teapot for Rosenthal, 1974

Timo Sarpaneva, enamelled cast-iron casserole with teak handle for W. Rosenlew & Co., 1960

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/973b89b528314e59eda7799c12b19d741a9d240d27774dfde8cc9873ee0402bb.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/78ff760426e08b9a4d6676cebac41dd65e6ec0f263098db8b66cc0565aca4d76.jpg)

Timo Sarpaneva, Arkipelago candleholders for littala, 1981

Suomen Lasimuseo (Finnish Glass Museum), "In the vases of Timo Sarpaneva's Finlandia series .. Sarpaneva has liberated himself from the strict discipline of design and progressed to self-awareness." The charred wood technique was also used to produce Sarpaneva's well-known Festivo candleholders (1966), but because of the expense of continually replacing the wooden moulds, metal moulds were eventually used instead. Both the Finlandia and Festivo ranges were highly influential and heralded the fashion for industrially mass-produced "frosted" glassware in the 1970s. Sarpaneva later developed other glassmaking techniques like that used for his Archipelago range (1978), in which different sized "trapped" bubbles produced a solid ice-like effect. For his Claritas range (1984), he used a similarly innovative technique that trapped and suspended large bubbles within a clear glass casing, while his elegant Marcel series (1990) incorporated a new blow-moulding process that pushed the material to its very limits. Many of Sarpaneva's designs used closed "round-square" forms that relate to the smooth pebbles of the Indian Tantric cult, most notably his Claritas vases for littala and his cast-iron cooking pot with wooden handle (1960) for W. Rosenlew & Co. Other designs, such as his large Lasiaika glass sculptures, incorporated more open and expressive forms. In 1963, he was made an Honorary Royal Designer for Industry in London and in 1967 received an honorary doctorate from the Royal College of Art, London. In tribute to his achievements he also received a State Award for Industrial Art from the Finnish Government in 1985. Beyond his exquisite glassware for lttala, Sarpaneva has also designed ceramics for Rosenthal, metalware for W. Rosenlew & Co. and Opa, textiles for Kinnasand, Finlayson Forssa and Tampella, plastic wares for Ensto, and other glassware for Corning and Venini, as well as books and theatre sets. Like so many other Finnish designers, Sarpaneva has worked with equal skill in the realms of both exclusive one-off studio production and industrial mass-production. Through his supreme mastery of materials and artistic sensibility, Sarpaneva's work evokes the spiritual essence and raw beauty of nature, and in so doing has had a crucial influence on the development of Finnish glass design for over fifty years.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a05f303b2e45df1d6bfd6daede97c0777571beff6ae485d579960c7a6b545b51.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/857d31531fb0bb9310f96cde184e1e9e4cbc58bcb427654738363545a56b237f.jpg)  
Δ Timo Sarpaneva, Finlandia vase for littala, 1964

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/087d4623e43db8433d25dddc6881688fe42f23a4d1cb6caed87d91ae2a982b7f.jpg)  
Timo Sarpaneva, Finlandia vases for littala, 1964 - These remarkable vases were made using wooden moulds that were charred by the moulten glass. Basically cast from "life" these designs captured the essential spirit of the natural world and were hugely influential on mainstream glass design during the 1970s.  
Timo Sarpaneva, Finlandia vase for littala, 1964
