Thomas Sandell (Stockholm Design Lab), new livery for the SAS fleet of aircraft 1998

## sas

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3f30e6719789d20b2eb48dc2ac666b6d9137b3ccb5436186a13b0c12a18b76d4.jpg)

SAS (Scandinavian Airlines System) was established in 1946 as a consortium of three national Scandinavian air carriers - the Danish airline Det Danske Luftfartselskab (DDL); the Norwegian air carrier Det Norske Luftfartselskap (DNL); and the Swedish airline AB Aerotransport - for the coordination of joint transatlantic operations. In September of the same year the "Dan Viking" (the first DC-4 to be painted in the distinctive SAS colours) made its maiden transatlantic flight from Stockholm to New York via Copenhagen. Later in the 1950s SAS became a high-profile ambassador of "Scandinavianess" with its eye-catching blue and white logo and the chic uniforms of its aircrew. The company helped promote its image even further by commissioning Arne Jacobsen to design a new landmark terminal-cum-hotel adjacent to the central railway station in Copenhagen. The resulting Royal Hotel and SAS Terminal (1956-1961) was a flagship International Style building, which incorporated state-of-the-art communications systems and interiors that exemplified the cool understated elegance of post-war Scandinavian Modernism. Jacobsen conceived the project as a fully integrated Gesamtkunstwerk (a total work of art), which included numerous site-specific furnishings, from his well-known Egg and Swan chairs (1957-1958) to specially designed door handles and cutlery. In the late 1950s SAS restated its commitment to progressive design by commissioning Sigurd Persson to develop a forward-looking grey plastic in-flight service. One of the greatest pioneers of Scandinavian industrial design, Sigvard Bernadotte was also responsible or thedesign of an in-lght tray and cutlery for the company. Later in 1988 Ergonomi Design Gruppen designed an innovativand stylish plastic in-flight coffee jug or SAS that minimized the possibility of spillage. The following decade, SAS began a comprehensive programmhange withhtentioni its levels of competitiveness and profitability, and a new updated corporate identity programme was instigated in order to strengthen the airline's brand recognition. Introduced in 1998, this corporate image makeover included new-look aircraft (both inside and out), new Euroclass lounges designed by Thomas Eriksson, a modernized logo, and new typography, graphics, colours and uniforms. All of this was intended to convey "the basics of andinaviaesplicitynalit mal elegance and a respect for materials and resources" and to accentuate the airline's profile. In 2001, the new SAS corporate identity won the main category of the Core Design-Corporate Design Awards, testifying to the airline's continuing patronage of contemporary Scandinavian design and its commitment to "developing new products and concepts to increase the focus on customers in the areas of quality, service and comfort".

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/aab3d924f0dd19af54f60099fee8c37389407f98c232681c74e826053a74b7bb.jpg)

Arne Jacobsen, entrance lobby of the SAS Royal Hotel, Copenhagen

Ergonomi Design Gruppen, SAS in-flight service coffee jug for SAS, 1988

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8720e69c7378152c131ac8f516258a6ca8a79b3c6417ae69a7d21102f29d9f15.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6483713f5da36a084460f22b463481a865aab526bbe4704eb008173f77446ecf.jpg)  
Sixten Sason, Zig-Zag sewing machine for Husqvarna, 1953

# sixtensason(

One of the first Scandinavian industrial design consultants, Sixten Sason initially trained as a silversmith and studied with Swedish artists while living in Paris. He later trained as a pilot and then worked for Husqvarna making motor-cycles and small arms. In the 1930s he worked as a graphic designer and magazine illustrator. During the Secod World War he joined Saab, then a fledgling aircraft manufacturing company, where he was initially employed as a technical illustrator. Later, he was appointed the company's designer-inchief - a position he held until his death. Around 1945 Saab decided to diversify, and its chief engineer Gunnar Ljungström and his team began developing the company's first car, the Saab 92 (1947). Inspired by aeronautical design and American streamlining, Sason transformed this frontwheel-drive vehicle into a streamlined vision with integrated side panels, bonnet and boot - an innovation that was later adopted by Pininfarina and Raymond Loewy (1893-1986). The Saab 92 was Sweden's first small car and became the blueprint for the company's subsequent models, such as the best-selling Saab 95 estate car (1959), which Sasson also designed. He later developed with

Ljungström the ground-breaking Saab 99 (1969), but sadly died before it went into production. As one of the earliest European proponents of streamlining, Sason also designed several highly influential products for Electrolux, including the bulletshaped Model 248 vacuum cleaner (1943). In 1948, he developed the compact yet elegant housing for the 1600F Hasselblad camera through the use of simple block-like wooden prototypes. This landmark design, which featured an easy-to-use arrangement of operating buttons, was the world's first single-lens reflex camera with interchangeable lenses, magazines and viewfinders, and when first launched at an exhibition in Cleveland it caused a sensation. Ergonomically refined and superbly functional, the seminal 1600F quite literally set the highest standards for camera layout and handling and quickly became a favourite choice among professional photographers. During the 1950s, Sason was also responsible for the design of various appliances for Husqvarna that were similarly aesthetically and functionally resolved, such as his Zig-Zag sewing machine of 1953. Unlike many designers in America, Sason used streamlining not solely for aesthetic purposes, but as a means of

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c8b63466f1670d39e848fda7363dafc1088703b492bcd5ab2174faf4dc656ff5.jpg)  
Sixten Sason, design for industrial washing machine for Electrolux, 1950  
Sixten Sason, concept design for Saab 92 automobile, 1947

enhancing his products' performance. By skilfully synthesizing engineering and styling with a concern for high build quality and product durability (both functional and aesthetic), Sason was instrumental in developing a distinctly Scandinavian approach to industrial design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8bb466144c7a7d941ee5dc85f5c5b3e3ed2fa9e829764c1b8606ffda650bad06.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7df8ff2c88de2d42b1cd89b087b1ea914bce59b210fd0ccbb46bf329636ee5d7.jpg)

Sixten Sason, design for vacuum cleaner attachments for Electrolux, 1944

Sixten Sason, design for Model no. 248 vacuum cleaner for Electrolux, 1943

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e86cfe02f72ce08a31d33fb2959b1ce9a6016465427ba6e2f0609abdf776d9f2.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f38a0f3a96599132f85ab220e286701acd01ee090270c51810fb0a23125916e9.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/10c54ea8a62d8b945132b29672f06dc8c4dcdf206dd55cf74b587f35fefc1162.jpg)  
Teppo Asikainen & Ilka Terho, Netsurfer computer work station for Snowcrash, 1995

# snowcrash

The Stockholm-based Snowcrash design company is renowned for its innovative and experimental products, which are born out of an approach that aims to go "beyond the obvious to explore contemporary needs and equip contemporary lifestyles". The business evolved from an earlier design group known as Valvomo (Crisis), which was founded in 1993 by a collective of young Finnish designers and architects who had known each other since their student days. The original members included Teppo Asikainen (b. 1968), Vesa Hinkola (b. 1970), Markus Nevalainen (b. 1970), Kari Sivonen (b. 1969), llka Terho (b. 1968), Jan Tromp (b. 1969), Rane Vaskivuori (b. 1967), and Timo Vierros (b. 1967). In 1997 the group exhibited their work together with designs by llkka Suppanen and Timo Salli at the Milan Furniture Fair under the name of Snowcrash - reflecting not only their Nordic origins but also their desire to overthrow the "bent plywood" heritage of Scandinavian design in favour of visionary things for a network age" The following year Snowcrash was officially founded in Helsinki as a design-led organization that would oversee the development of furniture and lighting by both in-house and independent designers. Since then Snowcrash has launched a number of radical products, including the Netsurfer computer work station (1995) by Teppo Asikainen and Ilka Terho (a tongue-in-cheek attempt at reconciling computer technology and furniture within the domestic environment); the Globlow lamp (1996) by Vesa Hinkola, Markus Nevalainen and Rane Vaskivuori (a "living light" that inflates when switched on and deflates when switched off; the Airbag chair (1997) by Ilkka Suppanen (an inflatable chair/mattress for indoor/outdoor use); and the Soundwave panel system (1999) by Teppo Asikainen (a system of sculptural wall panels designed to enhance the acoustic properties of interior spaces). The Snowcrash brand was purchased by Proventus AB in 1998 and its operations were subsequently moved from Helsinki to Stockholm in 2000. The same year Snowcrash became part of Proventus' Art & Technology group (which also owns Artek and Kinnasand), and since then has built up an international network of collaborating designers, including the Paris-based Israeli designer Arik Levy (b. 1963) and the Swedish designer, Monica Föster (b. 1966). Apart from considering practical needs, Snowcrash "aims to address values such as well-being, creativity and even playfulness", which its members consider to be equally important for a humane workspace.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/abfc6f1d3c4281af23ddb1029984f4b00aca49140c81424fb2f1775a1ce0e5e0.jpg)

Vesa Hinkola, Markus Nevalainen & Rane Vaskivuori, Globlow floor lamp for Snowcrash, 1996

Teppo Asikainen, Chip lounger for Snowcrash, 1996

Ikka Suppanen, Airbag chair for Snowcrash, 1997 - codesigned with Pasi Kolhonen Teppo Asikainen, Soundwave sound-proofing panel system for Snowcrash, 1999

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/903d7c01fef40a13b94b7f1487836d2991bf7a92f87707fab0fb3201117b747e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/287f153af349298183f409e6b7367fefb19d20227196b7b641b6d53971a4ddb5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6b2ecd3e3c59fc30c22505faa224d5ed6e2be257346c86b48abe0056712cbc47.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/74edeac545c58f06e2459ca72d82e5a13d95ec667c97db02f3a553d0ac21c0d7.jpg)

Carl-Harry Stalhane, Unic glazed stoneware vases for Rörstrand, c. 1950

Carl-Harry Stálhane, glazed stoneware vase for Rörstrand, c. 1948

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/aea7d17d599087212bc7f286528c1b24468cbc3f88f6d00167305b28c2301189.jpg)

# carl-harry stålhane

## (b. 1920 Mariestad - d. 1990 Lidköping, Sweden)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f18aae2d12a995502007b2152486f5d2c00a508b8d6ece52cb2fd029a4510841.jpg)

The Swedish ceramicist Carl-Harry Stalhane trained as a sculptor under Ossip Zadkine (1890- 1967) at the Académie Colarossi in Paris. His studies also took him to Spain, Greece, Turkey, Egypt, Mexico and the United States. In 1939, he began working at the Rörstrand ceramics factory, initially assisting the painter Isaac Grünewald (1889-1946) in the production of colourful tin-glazed earthenware objects decorated with Fauvesque motifs. By 1948 he was designing his own ceramics, which were inspired by Oriental art and which explored, like the work of other contemporary ceramicists at the time such as Berndt Friberg, the ultimate in "classical" pottery forms. His soft-edged vessels with mottled matt glazes marked an important stylistic departure for Rörstrand and typified the new understated style of ceramics that began to emerge in Scandinavia during the immediate post-war years. In 1951 he was awarded a gold medal at the (IX) Milan Triennale for his highly influential work. By the early 1950s, Stålhane was also designing ceramics with geometric motifs, such as his Abstrakt series, which reflected the influence of contemporary French and Italian abstract art as well as Japanese raku pottery and calligraphy. Apart from his studio pieces, Stálhane designed functional domestic tableware for mass production, such as his SB, Blanca, and Vieta dinner services. During the 1960s, his work became increasingly sculptural and larger in scale, often functioning more as ceramic sculpture than as domestic pottery. Between 1963 and 1971 he taught in the ceramics department at the Konstindustriskolan (School of Arts & Crafts) in Gothenburg and in 1973, having designed ceramics for Rörstrand for forty years, established his own studio, Designhuset, in Lidköping, where he was assisted by Kent Ericsson. Beyond his work for Rörstrand, Stálhane also designed a simple hand-painted decorative motif for the Delfi tableware range manufactured by Bing & Grondahl, the shapes of which were designed by Martin Hunt (b. 1942). Like so many other Scandinavian designers, Stálhane skilfully straddled the worlds of both craft manufacture and industrial mass production in pursuit of "ideal" forms.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a09a3a95b6bac87a49508ad6d63db6ecd9284763d6a457c4f1c306c54f217579.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4393a4fbe621dcf139a8bf182159ce5b72ba1b4ec0e7145598798d2c517fad38.jpg)  
Arne Jacobsen, teapot from the Cylinda Line stainless steel hollowware range for Stelton, 1967

## stelton

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/432c4164d011f178e2e13bbb6c3eacb043580fdd758e54caab76546be45df4ab.jpg)

Renowned for its elegant metal and plastic products, the domestic ware manufacturer Stelton was founded in 1960 to sell the stainless steel hollowware - sauceboats, platters, salad bowls etc. - manufactured by the Dansk Rustfrit company based in Fårevejle, a little town in Northwest Zealand. The products, which were of mediocre design, were intended by Stelton to form the basis of a range of contemporary housewares aimed primarily at the Danish market. Salesman Peter Holmblad (b. 1934) was hired to market the line and in 1964, with a view to raising the company's profile, he persuaded the well-known architect and designer Arne Jacobsen to design a range of hollowware for the firm. Jacobsen subsequently produced some sketches of a few cylindrical forms that were "terse, logical and functional", but the technology needed to translate his ideas into threedimensional products did not yet exist: stainless steel is an extremely demanding material to work, as its surface can become easily distorted if it is not handled in exactly the right way. Consequently Stelton first had to develop new machines and welding techniques, before it could produce Jacobsen's Cylinda Line, which was eventually

introduced in 1967. In recognition of the superior quality of its design, the Cylinda Line was awarded an ID Prize by the Danish Society of Industrial Design in 1967, and the International Design Award by the American Institute of Interior Designers in 1968. The Cylinda Line initially comprised around 18 pieces, but Jacobsen continued to add new designs up to 1971, while others were introduced posthumously from drawings. Today, the range comprises 34 pieces in total. Described as "products which live up to the demand for a beautiful, functional, and practical hollowware range in contemporary design", the success of the Cylinda Line can be largely attributed to Jacobsen's ability to express the inherent qualities of stainless steel through highly rational yet aesthetically pleasing, timeless forms. From 1976, Erik Magnussen also worked for Stelton and designed a number of well-known table pieces in both stainless steel and plastic, including his classic threepiece vacuum jug set of 1977. The understated elegance and high object integrity of Magnussen's products, has like Jacobsen's, enabled them to remain utterly impervious to the whims of fashion. The company's range today also includes designs by Peter Holmblad (the managing director and owner of Stelton since the late 1970s), such as his Model no. 100-15 house plant watering can, which has a similar rational aesthetic to the Cylinda Line. Stelton's high-quality products exemplify Danish design and are represented in the permanent collections of museums around the world, including the Museum of Modern Art and the Cooper-Hewitt Museum, New York, the Victoria & Albert Museum, London, the Neue Sammlung, Munich and the Kunstindustrimuseum, Copenhagen.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/3326d60837b7b51620e8a9b0d8782e80a6cb6f8c984281f32b4a449dda4eb2be.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/09a742d4b1d194267aa59af8a37ec23079482e16d657ee7b53372cab07dbad2d.jpg)  
Erik Magnussen, Model no. 460 cake knife, Model no. 462 pizza servers and Model no. 463 fish server for Stelton, 1980, 1990 & 1990  
Arne Jacobsen, ice bucket and ashtray from the Cylinda Line stainless steel hollowware range for Stelton, 1967

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4d0309fdc8f4b6edb301620060a68144fce357af313914897628ea225a114d09.jpg)

Erik Magnussen, Model no. 2000 vacuum jug, Model no. 2001 creamer, Model no. 2002 sugar bowl and Model no. 2005 serving tray for Stelton, 1999

Erik Magnussen, Mills spice jars and Model no. 580 salt & pepper grinders for Stelton, 1999

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4bd10da88607702fa0ebdd1a868ecd139ce4f13124ff51d8d30aa65143c44e05.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7bc67572f2acea925fd08749e0f03ea96629f2eaa9b7bb10d697af2f62579531.jpg)  
Magnus Stephensen, armchair for Fritz Hansen 1932

# magnus stephensen

1903 in Frederiksberg - 984 n Helsingør, Denmark)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0f7c9ecace8dd6ce6be88a17012c7e535c884f659916586f3566f40ea107650c.jpg)

Magnus Stephensen studied at the Teknisk Skole (Polytechnic), Copenhagen, until 1924 and later trained as an architect at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts), Copenhagen, graduating in 1931. He also briefly studied architecture at the French School in Athens. Following his training he designed a number of buildings, but mainly concentrated on domestic architecture, which revealed his preference for precise and uncluttered lines. Between 1938 and 1952 he designed tableware, including several flatware patterns and a teapot, for Kay Bojesen's workshop, that was remarkable for stylih ophistication eteriable tl He also began designing metalware for Georg Jensen in 1950, which led to his Tanaquil and Frigate cutlery patterns. In 1954, he designed his exquisite sterling silver saucepan, the handles of which were particularly notable for the way they made carrying easier. The same year he also designed a stainless steel coffee set as well as a range of high-quality stainless steel casseroles and saucepans for Georg Jensen. These beautifully crafted objects were made by drawing the steel plate over rotating steel cores and then hand

polishing the forms so as to produce the highest quality finish. Because the steel plate used was of a sufficiently thick gauge, the rims of the pieces did not require additional reinforcing and so were left straight-edged. Later, Stephensen designed the Patella range of oven-to-table ware 1957) for Royal Copenhagen, which featured a speckled brown glaze inspired by the colour of a withered oak leaf. The glaze also had the added benefit of effectively covering any iron spots in the earthenware, thereby reducing the number of rejected seconds" in manufacture. Similar to his stainless steel hollowware for Georg Jensen, this product range was distinguished by soft yet rational forms. T Patella range also heralded the trend towards more informal mix-and-match multi-functional tableware. Like many other Danish designers at the time, Stephensen was strongly influenced by Japanese art (to such as extent that he even wrote a book on the subject), and because of this many of his products possessed a quiet and understated beauty. His domestic ware also exemplified the Danish traditions of high quality craftsmanship, precision engineering and practical utility. Over the course of his long career Stephensen won numer-

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7891ba8f47d79cc51bc81b7eb9c0d0de9c3399457f5c95f1e6bcf73fddade0fd.jpg)  
ous awards for his excellent product designs, including three gold medals and two Grand Prizes at the 1954 (X) and 1957 (XI) Milan Triennale.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f59c1e3218cf71984fc757298d61efdccb3ef1215891a2d33e0072e9268e66fd.jpg)  
Magnus Stephensen, silver entrée dish and coffee pot with plaited wicker handles for Kay Bojesen's workshop, 1938  
Magnus Stephensen, silver tea-pot with wickerwork handle for Kay Bojesen's workshop, 1938.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8df52f96d7965357e330fdfa2acee8d532f5139e8c846076c91cf84b3ec3398f.jpg)  
Nanny Still McKinney, Harlekiini (Harlequin) glassware range for Riihimäki, 1958

# nanny still mckinney

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9bb8b1ef36b7aa995b52461e8bc2cdebcdc9c6ed477659d859b867a7548bcd78.jpg)

The Finnish designer Nanny Still McKinney studied at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki from 1945 to 1949. Upon completing her studies she joined the design team at the Riihimäki glassworks. Her work there included both blown "art" glass and moulded production glassware that was distinguished by unusual forms, textured surface decoration and functional utility. As Still McKinney noted, "Practicality has always been extremely important to me. I cannot make impractical objects. You must be able to wash them, and a beer glass must sit comfortably in the hand." At Riihimäki she also felt a special urge to create colour", so in collaboration with the chemical laboratory there she developed a number of memorable colours, including a brilliant turquoise that had been inspired by a shard of glass she found on a beach while holidaying in Capri. Her subsequent product designs, such as the cobalt blue Harlekiini range (1958), which included covered jars, jugs and glasses, and the warm amber coloured Ambra candlesticks (1961), which were included in the "Finlandia" exhibition that toured Europe 961-1962- nalled a new and brilliantly coloured phase in

Finnish glass-making. Some of Still McKinney's later blow-moulded glass designs, such as her Fantasma vases (1968-1971) and her daisypatterned decanter and shot glasses (c. 1970), were remarkable for their highly textured surfaces and bold relief decoration that reflected the informality of the period. She continued designing glassware for Riihimäki until 1976, when the glassworks discontinued its production of blow-moulded glass to concentrate solely on the mass-manufacture of commercial glass, such as bottles and jars. Although Still McKinney adopted glass as her material of choice, stating "It has many more aspects than anything else  stone, marble or bronze", she revealed her extraordinary versatility as a designer by working in a number of other materials, such as metal, enamel, plastics and wood, and other disciplines, including ceramics, graphics, jewellery and lighting. Her polished teak and cane salad servers of 1954, for example, testify to her remarkable sense of line and handling of materials, while her Good Morning breakfast set (c. 1964) for La Porcelaine de Baudour - with its "tongued" lids to enable better control while pouring - reveal an innovative approach to functional problem solving. Still McKinney also designed ceramics for Cerabel-Porcelain, Heinrich Porzellan, and Rosenthal, glassware for Val Saint-Lambert, plastic products for Sarvis, and cookware for Hackman. In 1972, she was awarded a Pro Finlandia Medal for her original designs "that came straight from the heart", and which also exemplified the uniquely stylish yet informal approach to product design in Finland.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/6942f7bbd11641949f74ec5850d89c2da01e05b1fe166f796929341c3ce40e9c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1d5fd129f065014c161e4e971ae48b7ddd8a84ecf42f414b17d824efbe8685ff.jpg)  
Nanny Still McKinney, teak and cane serving spoons and forks (self-production), 1954  
Nanny Still McKinney, Good Morning flame-resistant breakfast tableware for La Porcelaine de Baudour, c. 1964

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a237d0113087f455a81b0d0e4089a419823d5f01e085de52e8d3c9ad85facfa7.jpg)  
Peter Opsvik, Balans Duo chair for Stokke, 1984

## stokke

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d0f127322807f8682e1c08e62b32335f508659afee422764a8fcefff19f44a4a.jpg)

Stokke, the largest and most prestigious furniture company of Norway, evolved from a small business that was founded in 1932 by Georg Stokke and Bjarne Møller. Its "humble production" of chairs and sofas was significantly upgraded in 1936 when a new factory was established that enabled much higher rates of production. In 1955, Georg Stokke acquired sole ownership of the company and it subsequently became known as Stokke Fabrikker AS. A sister company, Westnofa, was founded in Åndalsnes in 1968 to specifically cater to the domestic seating market, while Stokke concentrated on the development of other types of furniture. In 1972, Stokke launched the Tripp Trapp high-chair/chair designed by Peter Opsvik, which could be height-adjusted so as to in effect "grow with the child" from the age of six months to eight years old. Since its introduction, over three million of these revolutionary chairs have been sold worldwide. Later, Opsvik began research with Hans Christian Mengshoel (b. 1946) into dynamic seating solutions based on ergonomic principles, which resulted in the introduction of the remarkable Balans Variable sitting tool in 1979. Based on the company's philosophy of "movement and

variation", this innovative rocking seat evolved from the concept that furniture should stimulate a good sitting position and freedom of movement, while providing enough flexibility for a variety of body sizes. The Balans Variable and other seating designs from the Balans range are renowned for promoting excellent posture, and as such are particularly recommended for backache sufferers. During the 1980s, Stokke produced Opsvik's unusual Garden Seat and the playful Ekstrem chair designed by Terje Ekstrøm (b. 1944), which function as much as interactive sitting sculptures as ergonomic seating products. In 1999, Stokke launched a new children's furniture concept designed by Grønlund & Knudsen known as Sleepi, which transmutes from a crib to a bed to a sofa or two chairs. Like the hugely successful Tripp Trapp chair, this innovative design can be adapted to the children's needs as they grow and underscores the Stokke Group's belief that "good design is good business". Stokke's specialization in ergonomic seating and innovative children's furniture typifies the Scandinavian attitude towards not only health issues, but also children's development. It is interesting to note that as early as 1901 the progres-

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4a844d32a894ffcbddec3656b5f3bac7af3e0e5ef39356d231868dbab384a422.jpg)  
sive Swedish social reformer Ellen Key (1849- 1926) was predicting Barnets arhundrade (The Century of the Child), and that in Norway today there is a government ombudsman specifically concerned with the rights and interests of children.  
Per Øie, Move stool for Stokke, 1984 - allows full freedom of movement through 360 degrees.  
Terje Ekstrøm, Ekstrem chair for Stokke, 1984 - designed so that the sitter can adopt a number of sitting positions while still maintaining full back support - first launched in 1987

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9c4ff827924f26f56c6f46dac9d9b58dd802d9c3731cdadf9f2310fc5f783db0.jpg)  
Peter Opsvik, Multi Balans folding sitting tool for Stokke, 1982

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7d9874ade1082fa994525807b450d18f79471b9b84121a5ac1e4289811927940.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8368f6d71a49a6ffe405053847e1268ef730bd510b80ba4acb139f8bcf3ee5fa.jpg)  
Ilkka Suppanen, Nomad chair (limited edition/selfproduction), 1994

# ilkka suppanen

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4d1216b48cbaebdd98fdfa190deb288b3537c64c50d341b423dd41cb2d28b466.jpg)

One of the leading avant-garde Scandinavian designers of his generation, Ilka Suppanen studied architecture and design at the Polytechnic and the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki, graduating in 1989. He later completed his design studies at the Rietveld Academy, Amsterdam, in 1992 and the same year exhibited at the Fifth International Exhibition of Architecture at the Venice Biennale. In 1995 he began teaching at the Faculty of Design in Helsinki, and the following year designed the innovative Airbag chair for Valvomo, an inflatable chair/ mattress for indoor/outdoor use that can be folded away or hung up when not in use. He also became a founding member of the Scandinavian design co-operative Snowcrash in 1996 and devised the group's trademark. He later teamed up with Timo Salli in 1998 on the design of the Nomadhouse, which included the Nomad chair (1994) with an undulating felt sling seat. The Nomad project focused on how "people are driven by two conflicting impulses: the desire to move and the desire to stay. ... Nomadic designs are made from as few materials as possible. They disassemble easily and are readily transported. They must be

extremely functional." The same year he designed a sofa version of the Nomad chair for Cappellini known as the Flying Carpet - so called because of the way the seat section appears to hover in the air. Suppanen also received considerable recognition when he presented his work with Snowcrash at the 1998 International Furniture Fair in Milan. Utilizing materials that "demand and create new forms", such as synthetic fibres and copper cloth, Suppanen designs highly innovative product solutions that are practical yet possess a strong aesthetic identity. As an independent designer, he has worked for, among others, Cappellini, Proventus, Nokia and Saab, and was nominated by Ettore Sottsass for the Dedalus prize for young European Designer as one of the four most important young designers under forty. Believing that design is like poetry: absolute and precise, with the minimal use of means employed to achieve the maximal result", Suppanen's nomadic and frequently multi-functional designs are primarily concerned with exploring the relationship "between the transitory and the timeless". In 2000 he became the creative director of Snowcrash while continuing to operate his own design practice in Helsinki.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8a44a6cb1f6b5b0906bac19863c98f155484c69c9793c2ed4b706e0459de275a.jpg)

His work has also been selected for the permanent collections of numerous institutions, including the Stedelijk Museum in Amsterdam and the Museum für Angewandte Kunst in Cologne.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a8edaaef44a9bd7b6e5f3b598d226ce19413807fb552b65176e35b4e7fb786b1.jpg)  
Ilka Suppanen, Roll-light floor lamp for Snowcrash, 1997  
lIka Suppanen, AV Rack cabinet (self-production), 1998 IIkka Suppanen, Game-shelf table for Snowcrash, 1999

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/0512d03a580e4d33e9d87bb33abe0bb51e5d1de5c2aa9af399d37224e173ae0c.jpg)

limari Tapiovaara demonstrating his Wihelmiina stacking chair for Wilhelm Schauman, c. 1959

> Ilmari Tapiovaara, Wilhelmina stacking chair for Wilhelm Schauman, 1959

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b9572a65cb718c72f42519bc49c15b090ea5b7ac64aa0ca6724a5ead4994774c.jpg)

# ilmari tapiovaara

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b463ae1515850bb6dfcd6565bad92a2cac2f3609d93e4f4f6420d3ef3e3b881a.jpg)

Throughout his career, Ilmari Tapiovaara dedicated himself to the design and development of the "ideal" multipurpose chair. From 1935 to 1936 he worked in the London office of Artek, a designled company founded by Alvar and Aino Aalto. He later studied industrial and interior design at the Taideteollisuuskeskuskoulu (Central School of Industrial Design) in Helsinki, graduating in 1937. Following this, he served an apprenticeship in the Paris office of Le Corbusier (1887-1965) before working as the art director of Asko (a furniture manufacturer based in Lahti) from 1937 to 1940. In 1946, he designed his well-known solid birch and moulded plywood Domus chair (1946) sitespecific for the Domus Academica student hostel in Helsinki. This pioneering knock-down design was manufactured by Keravan Puuteollisuus, a furniture company established and directed by Tapiovaara. Around 1950, he co-founded with his architect wife Annikki Tapiovaara (b. 1910) a design office in Helsinki, and subsequently worked as a freelance designer developing various "universal" seating solutions. As a functionalist and one of the most important pioneers of knock-down furniture, Tapiovaara believed particularly strongly in the principle of revealed construction. With an eye on the ever-increasing furniture export market, he concentrated in the late 1940s and early 1950s on developing low-cost, efficiently constructed chairs that could be easily dismantled and packed for overseas shipment - an example of which he submitted to the 1948 "International Competition for Low-Cost Furniture Design" at the Museum of Modern Art, New York. His later tubular metal and plywood stacking Lukki / chair (1952) for Lukkiseppo, which was first used in the Olivetti Showroom in Helsinki, featured a plastic cord wound around its arms that not only afforded the user much comfort, but also functioned as a protective device when the chairs were stacked. His elegant Wilhelmina chair (1959) for Wilhelm Schauman, with its moulded plywood seat and back elements, was similarly inventive in the way it could be stacked. He was awarded six gold medals in total for his chairs at the 1951 (IX), 1954 (X), 1957 (X1) and 1960 (XIl) Milan Triennale and received a Good Design Award in Chicago in 1950. Apart from designing highly rational furniture, which also included pieces for Knoll International, Thonet, and Asko, Tapiovaara worked extensively as an interior

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/14539270daab1d06af9943548939b4c3cc25d867def3b2f0f0d36ff5d9cb1384.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/61903cc3d8cb053d20901217777d4b0f74470b6c271f59995a92d7087c0897b7.jpg)

Ilmari Tapiovaara, ebonized wood and leather armchair, c. 1947 - This chair was designed for the Museum of Modern Art's "International Competition for Low-Cost Furniture Design" (1948) and was later distributed by Thonet

Δ> Ilmari Tapiovaara, Domus stacking chair for Keravan Puuteollisuus, 1946 - Later produced by Wilhelm Schauman, this chair was retailed under the names Stax and Finnchair in Britain and America respectively

IIlmari Tapiovaara, Domus stacking chair for Keravan Puuteollisuus, 1946

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a86e4b77db126c1664fbff63719b0dd135856ac576f7c1cdfca9ce2bafcebcb5.jpg)

designer and extended his skills into other product disciplines, including lighting, glassware, stainless steel flatware (Hackman), stereo equipment, carpets, textiles, wallpaper and toys. Between 1950 and 1952, he headed a department at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki and from 1952 to 1953 was a visiting professor at the Institute of Design, Illinois Institute of Technology in Chicago. During this American sabbatical, he also worked in the Chicago design practice of Ludwig Mies van der Rohe (1886- 1969). In 1971 he received the Finnish State Design Award, and later a prize from the Finnish Culture Foundation in 1986. Tapiovaara was one of the first Finnish designers to work extensively abroad and once noted, "Being a designer is rather like being a doctor: once you have the professional skill, you can practice wherever you like. If what you do is good, it's good everywhere." Having been directly inspired by some of the greatest names of the Modern Movement, Tapiovaara evolved a distinct language of design, which, while rooted in functionalism, imparted a sense of timelessness and character to his work.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/2c017625ee9acb39b02f9e0e1ad686c520e9970e94c0ecb114bce807db61a700.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/19d904b45de9c99877c245d788990dc2238c70c51de917c555abfdb3b946154c.jpg)

First Tetra Pak packaging, 1951

Ruben Rausing, founder of Tetra Pak

# tetra pak

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/12e8f965f751ed66fb4b0d38055343c4925b4b4c1de891178ad2935bad0a10c0.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/613b5f5b7b6fbe1792adf5bb8f25be3b933047d26a8593abdb324aeeb899ace9.jpg)

A world leader in food packaging for over half a century, Tetra Pak revolutionized food retailing, distribution and hygiene with its simple yet ingenious product designs. The company was founded in 1951 by the visionary industrialist Ruben Rausing (1895-1983), who studied at the Stockholm School of Economics and later at Columbia University, New York. While in America, he was exposed for the first time to "self-service" stores and was convinced that the concept would eventually be adopted in European countries, which would in turn increase the demand for pre-packaged food stuffs. On his return to Sweden, he founded in 1930 with Erik Åkerlund the packaging company Akerlund & Rausing, the first Scandinavian specialist packaging company, based on Rausing's belief that, "A package should save more than it costs". In 1943 the company began researching and developing a form of milk packaging that would require the minimum amount of material while providing the maximum hygiene. These early studies of the laboratory assistant Erik Wallenberg ascertained that the optimum shape for a milk carton was a tetrahedron. The following year saw the invention of new techniques for coating paper with plastics and for sealing packages. In the late 1940s, Rausing and Wallenberg established AB Tetra Pak as a subsidiary of Akerlund & Rausing, and the same year launched their new packaging system based on the tetrahedron-shaped carton. A year later, the Lundaortens Mejeriförening in Lund became the first to use a Tetra Pak machine to package cream into 100 ml cartons. Several other Swedish dairies introduced the revolutionary packaging system in 1953, and the same year the company began using polyethylene as a coating for its paperboard. In 1954, the first Tetra Pak machine was exported to Germany and the halflitre packaging machine was introduced. The commercial packaging of milk in 300 ml cartons subsequently began the following year. The company eventually opened a much larger manufacturing plant in Lund and commenced the development of an aseptic packaging system (launched in 1961). By 1959, the factory was producing one billion cartons per annum and had begun working on a new brick-shaped design, the Tetra Brik (introduced in 1963). Over the next few years, the business expanded its production operations into Mexico, the United States, Lebanon and Italy. In 1965,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f2242ea9852cf3f0f0cb9012881594b015c573f4f0b06ec38152912e9d4f0963.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/000e823e47a9e8007749663fd2627bbdc8eb492fe557bf10e0a66d17b16eeaa6.jpg)

Akerlund & Rausing was sold, but Ruben Rausing retained its subsidiary, AB Tetra Pak, and with the funds from the sale was able to invest more resources into the research and development of even better packaging designs, such as the ubiquitous gable-top package known as Tetra Rex (1965). During the 1970s, the company exported its packaging machinery all over the world, including the USSR, Japan, China, Australia and Iran. By the beginning of the 1980s, over 30 billion packages were being sold per annum for the storage of milk, cream, fruit juice and wine. In 1991, AB Tetra Pak merged with Alfa-Laval and subsequently diversified its product line to include PET bottling equipment. Today, Tetra Pak remains the undisputed world leader in the field of packaging, selling over 250 million products a day in some 165 countries. In 2001 Rausing's son, Hans (b. 1926), sold his share of Tetra Pak in order to purchase a majority shareholding in EcoLean (est. 1996), a small Swedish company making innovative packaging materials based on the egg. Recently, it has launched a revolutionary new "green" milk bottle made out of chalk - an odourless and tasteless material that breaks down into carbon, oxygen and

Tetra Pak packaging machine, c. 2000

Detail of Tetra Brik Aseptic Recap packaging, 1994

Tetra Brik packaging range, introduced in 1963

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dce0bcf792f69d28351b53005b26f3ad9a96593266e81af20f11ba66eb7bc807.jpg)

chalk dust over a period of two to three months when exposed to sunlight. With a vast range of potential applications for EcoLean's products and technology, Hans Rausing looks set to follow in his father's footsteps by not only revolutionizing the packaging industry, but by helping to solve some of the world's litter problems.

Oiva Toikka, Kastehelmi (Dew) glassware for Nuutajärvi, 1964 - Although best known for his "art" glass pieces, Toikka has also designed for mass production, most notably this mouldedglass tableware range

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b3e0657bee6826fe36150f167f7664c812bec9584ba6d342dea06395acc3d42c.jpg)

# oiva toikka(

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/e1467758f12813d867189049ba560c36c8ca1465fe3f198bfb79407e0c771ada.jpg)

One of Finland's most inventive glass designers, Oiva Toikka studied at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design) in Helsinki from 1953 to 1956. Following his training, he worked as a ceramics designer for Arabia from 1956 to 1959, and later taught at the Taideteoli nen Korkeakoulu from 1959 to 1961. He began working at Nuutajärvi in 1963 and the same year designed his gigantic free-blown Kurkupurkki (Pickle Jar) glass sculptures, which were among his first one-of pieces in glass. His subsequent studio art glass, which was distinguished by the use of bright colours and bold forms, had an abstracted pictorial quality that was inspired by both folk-art and contemporary design. In 1969 he produced his wel-known Lollipop Isle glass sculptures for Nuutajärvi which epitomized the joyful and carefree Pop aesthetic of the time and embodied the growing confidence of the Finnish glass industry. The 1960s were the golden age of Finnish glass design, and as Toikka noted, "The sixties was in a way a wonderful time, because one was still carried along by the idea that Finnish was good. It was also a happy time in that people still had faith in glass and design generally .

it was fun ... the sky was the limit as far as the appreciation of all the applied arts was concerned." Like many other Finnish artist-designers, Toika also produced a number of designs for industrial mass production, such as his well-known Kastehelmi (Dew) glassware (1964), which became one of Nuutajärvi's best-selling product lines. Embellished with concentric circles of pearl-like beads, the Kastehelmi range included plates, bowls and candlesticks, and was available in a range of colours - grey, brown, red, blue, green and colourless. Toikka's later Pioni production tableware similarly explored the expressive potential of moulded glass. Apart from his glassware, Toikka also produced fanciful costume and scenery designs for opera and theatre productions in Tampere and Savonlinna. In 1977 he designed the first of his annual Vuosikuutiot (Year Cube) cubic paperweights for Nuutajärvi, which can be stacked to create a miniature decorative glass wall. Often disregarding function in favour of fantastic form, Toikka's playful, individualistic and frequently inventive designs possess a poetic quality that conveys the almost alchemical nature of glass making. He has received numerous awards for his glassware, including the prestigious Lunning Prize in 1970 and the Pro Finlandia Medal in 1980. More recently, he has become best known for his distinctive glass bird figures that have been produced for over two decades, with a new "species" being added to the range each year. Speaking of these widely admired pieces, Toikka has said that, "Nature is an unending source of inspiration for me - and glass, as a natural material, is its best interpreter". Through the extraordinary range of his work, Toikka has blurred the boundaries of art and design while creating some of the most expressive pieces of studio and production glassware to have ever come out of Finland.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/93779fbb2720264b4e0ea739d9923b0051ed2ff29b697a132ed13183cb7aa55a.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/302abdd33d2a1f7e73b9e30329cb3501e2a52a03cf4140a16ba1c71e8ba187ac.jpg)

Oiva Toika, Oz vases from the Pro Arte collection for Nuutajärvi, 1995

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/70fde568042b3b1091e6861a814914afa469267d5b16343fe71c06fd4fa01c47.jpg)  
Paavo Tynell, brass and leather table lamp for Taito, 1941  
Paavo Tynell, brass chandelier for Taito, 1948

# paavo tynell

Paavo Tynell studied metalworking at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design, Helsinki) in Helsinki and then taught there from 1917 to 1923, instructing plate iron, tin and copper-smiths and playing an important role in disseminating handcraft skills. In 1918 he founded his own manufacturing company, Taito Oy, and subsequently produced a wide range of metal objects, including the enormous bronze doors of the Finnish Parliament building in Helsinki. Under Tynell's directorship during the 1920s and 1930s the firm employed a number of young designers such as Alvar Aalto, Gunnel Nyman and Kaj Franck, who went on to become key exponents of Finnish design. By the 1940s, Taito Oy was producing innovative light fittings designed by Tynell, including a brass and leather table lamp (1941) with a scallop-shaped shade and an extraordinary chandelier (1948) with a cascade of brass snowflake-like elements suspended from delicate wire arcs. Inspired by antique crystal chandeliers and traditional Finnish Yuletide himmeli mobiles made of straw, Tynell's highly decorative chandelier was intended to cast interesting shadows and produce a shimmering light effect reminiscent of candle or gas-light. In 48, Taito Oy began exporting lght fittings e Unitd States, whic were ai the Finnish company Finland House in New York. Through economic necessity Taito Oy was merged with Idman Osakeyhtiö in 1950, which resulted in Tynell's designs becoming less fanciful and more suited to industrial production. During this period he designed a number of lamps that were manufactured under the Taito label, many with perforated shades and sweeping sculptural forms. While Tynell's lighting products were notable for their structural simplicity, their constructions often featured unusual combinations of brass and glass, which gave them an opulent yet elegant appearance. In many cases his lights were hand-made, and so production was frequently limited to only a few examples of each design. Tynell received numerous awards for his work, including the Ornamo golden badge of honour in 1961.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/08667900b8a04fa6a84af87df6d378f8d1991ea1a2bd48cb72bbade7a859d2ee.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f0c5f1bb633918c6ba876e91c6e10677680676ef7f2dd791eae90d57cd785dc8.jpg)  
Jorn Utzon, Floating Dock sofa for Fritz Hansen, 1968

# jorn utzon

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/d1d30655592e5b5c55f4de0a0dc677f4c62492e6df733fdb2625ab1b046c698e.jpg)

The Danish architect and industrial designer Jørn Utzon is best known for his dynamic, highly innovative, but problematical design for the Sydney Opera House (1957-1973) - one of the most famous buildings in the world. He studied under Kay Fisker and Steen Eiler Rasmussen (1898- 1990) at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) from 1937 to 1942, and following this worked for three years in the design practice of the great Swedish functionalist Erik Gunnar Asplund. It was, however, the unified architectural schemes of Alvar Aalto and Frank Lloyd Wright (1867-1959) - the two great patriarchs of Organic Modernism - that had the biggest influence on his work. Having established his own design practice in Zurich and Alsgarde in 1947, Utzon went on to win a number of building competitions, most notably first prize for the design of the Sydney Opera House and Concert Hall in 1956. The resulting building with its magnificent sail-like vaults brought him much international fame, but from the start the project was dogged by controversy. Construction of the prefabricated concrete roof shells, which were inspired by the cellular structures of nature that

Utzon described as "additive architecture", posed a variety of problems. He eventually worked out a solution for their construction with an ingenious piece of spherical geometry, but was nevertheless forced to resign from the project in 1966 (the building and interiors were completed by an Australin firmfarchitects) In adition tobuildings, during the 1960s and 1970s Utzon also designed lamps (for Nordisk Solar), glassware, and furniture. His Floating Dock furniture collection (1967) for the Danish furniture manufacturer Fritz Hansen was also born of his concept of additive architecture. Comprising a number of differently sized tables and chairs (including a curious sofa-bed style chair), the construction of the Floating Dock range was based on an innovative system of arclike components of anodized triangular-section exrded aluminium that locked tgether at a orty five degree angle. His undulating, expressionistic 8101 chair (1969), which could be linked with additional pieces to create a wider seating unit or a sofa, was similarly inspired by his architectural concepts, and reflected his fascination with inventive construction operating "at the edge of the possible". Beyond his love of nature, Utzon also

Jorn Utzon, Floating Dock modular seating with matching table for Fritz Hansen, 1968

vv Jorn Utzon, lounge chair and ottoman for Fritz Hansen, 968

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a056b2c81987b715fc021f5dea682bb678e2ed0e7c82077762b9bd20aca5daa2.jpg)

found much inspiration in the buildings of other ages and cultures, such as Chinese, Islamic and Mayan. Throughout his career he received numerous awards, including the Eckersberg Medal (1957), the German Institute of Architects Medal (1965), the Danish Furniture Prize (1970), and a gold medal from the Royal Institute of British Architects (1978).

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/75bb2ce15042b9ef2476016477e7371fdfee126eef673039e8a6c98e0bb439c7.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ffa88b0e4aa1d96157d9ab4839e918bd694c4ce2f654903586cb60be88e26da8.jpg)

Gertrud Vasegaard, glazed porcelain and bamboo tea service for Bing & Grøndahl, 1955-1957

Gertrud Vasegaard, stoneware vase, 1937

# gertrud vasegaard

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/eac067a8582346ec949ca80ac598f4a891683746b9a1dd9b5ddcd28d8ec58b4a.jpg)

The Danish ceramicist Gertrud Vasegaard came from afmily  pottrs who lived o thesland of Bornholm. She studied at the Kunsthándvaerkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts) in Copenhagen from 1930 to 1932, and then trained in the ceramics studio of Axel Salto and Bode Willumsen (1895-1987) in Saxbo from 1932 to 1933. Following this she set up a studio with her sister, Lisbeth Munch-Petersen (1909-1997), in Gudhjem, Bornholm. She later established her own independent workshop/studio in 1936 in Holkadalen, Bornholm, where she produced high-quality handmade stoneware ceramics inspired by Oriental antecedents. The influence of Oriental art on Danish designers such as Vasegaard not only reflected Denmark's centuries-old sea-trading links with the Far East, but also a general respect for economical forms and high levels of craftsmanship. Between 1949 and 1959 Vasegaard worked as a designer (on both a freelance and full-time basis) for the Bing & Grøndahl ceramics factory in Copenhagen, where she executed a number of designs suitable for industrial mass production, including decorated bowls and vases. She also designed a porcelain dinner and tea service (1955-1957) for

Bing & Grøndahl, which with its hexagonal teapot complete with a wicker handle and its curious flask-like tea-box was directly inspired by traditional Chinese forms. Awarded a gold medal at the 1957 (X1) Milan Triennale exhibition, this design predicted the mainstream taste in the 1970s for all things Oriental. Between 1959 and 1975, Vasegaard also designed a number of ceramics on a freelance basis for the Royal Copenhagen factory, including her stoneware Gemina tableware range decorated with an abstract Oriental-style pattern in Chinese blue. Like many other Scandinavian ceramicists working in industry, Vasegaard used traditional craft methods - hand throwing on a potter's wheel  to produce the prototypes for her mass-produced designs. Her choice of stoneware for the Gemina range ensured a slightly uneven texture that gave the service a hand-made effect. In 1959 she established a studio with her daughter, Myre Vasegaard (b. 1936), in Frederiksberg, and also worked with Aksel Rode (1905- 1989) until 1969.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4bd2e7c62e32a80bf8dcb82524a10b8c1a355cef4a0e31267baa756b434082c5.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/5465259ea121cd3106fb58158749badf1c2fd22c596da8c3652323fe2ee07c88.jpg)  
Kristian Vedel, adjustable child's chair for Torben Ørskov & Co., 1957

# kristian vedel

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fcd31b800e6216cf9353c8b29db86fc3df669316be7dee212cc9ddd88c7c8002.jpg)

The Danish furniture designer Kristian Vedel served an apprenticeship as a cabinet-maker and became a journeyman joiner in 1942. He trained under Kaare Klint in the furniture department of the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts) from 1944 to 1945, and graduated from the Kunsthandværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts) in 1946. Following this, he was employed as an assistant between 1946 and 1949 by the architecture and furniture design husband-and-wife team Tove and Edvard Kindt-Larsen (1906-1994 & 1901-1982). He later worked for Palle Suenson (1904-1987) from 1949 to 1955, and was a teacher at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen in Copenhagen from 1953 to 1956. Strongly influenced by Klint's standards of economy, simplicity and function, during this period Vedel designed an innovative child's chair, which was formed as a curved plywood shell and fitted with slots at various heights for seat adjustment. Two of these chairs could also be joined together to make up an integrated chair-cum-feeding table. This highly practical design, which was manufactured by Torben Ørskov & Co., was based on Vedel's belief that, "The starting point for an

industrial artist's work must always be that he, from his own point of view, and as objectively as possible, takes a position with regard to what he feels society and his fellow men need; he must personally take a stand on the existing possibilities and responsibilities." In 1955 he established his own design practice and the following year produced a range of multi-functional melamine kitchenware - storage jars, bowls, beakers, salad servers and an egg-cup - that was remarkable for its sculptural simplicity. Five years later he designed his well-known Modus furniture system with its low and high-backed lounge chairs, armless dining chairs and matching glass-topped tables. Manufactured by Søren Willadsen from 1963 onwards, the Modus range was notable for its use of padded leather slings that formed the arm and back sections when fitted onto the supporting rosewood frame. In 1962, Vedel was awarded the Lunning Prize for his utilitarian consumer products in plastic that "demonstrated his acceptance of materials beyond those traditional to the furniture craftsman". Between 1966 and 1968 he was chairman of the IDD (Industrial Designers of Denmark), and from 1969 to 1972

v Kristian Vedel, children's furniture executed by I. Christiansen, 1949 - This furniture was designed to grow with the child

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a05d6a07a6975e09b11ece269b5ff013eca15fe26fabe74beb78ce86c2a2c1e5.jpg)

organized and led the department of industrial design at the University of Nairobi in Kenya with the aim of meeting "the needs of the population as a whole". On his return to Denmark in 1972, Vedel and his wife, Ane, established a design studio in Thyholm and began exploring different uses for wool. As one of the leading Danish furniture designers in the late 1950s and 1960s, Vedel received numerous accolades including silver and gold medals at the 1957 (X1) and 1960 (XII) Milan Triennale.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b08728bd46bcb88cef3119d8a38fe15325ffba9b466efdd2d9aeec6c252594c0.jpg)

#

Poul Volther, teak dining chair for FDB, 1951

Poul Volther, Model no. EJ 605 Corona chair for Erik Jørgensen, 1961

# poul volther

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dcdb783034af38452b24684542c58dd902a6c9c4c23ef674d7f32756af71d88d.jpg)

The Danish architect and furniture designer Poul Volther worked as a journeyman joiner before studying at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts) in Copenhagen. Upon graduating in 1949 he established his own architecture and design practice, which he operated until 1985. Over his career Volther designed numerous pieces o furniture, including an elegant teak dining chair (1951) for FDB (Association of Danish co-operative stores), which can be seen as a radical reworking of the traditional ladder-back chair. A decade later he used similar floating elements for his wellknown Corona chair (1961) which were reminiscent of time-lapse photographs of solar eclipses. Constructed of moulded plywood upholstered with cold-cured polyurethane foam, these elliptical seat pads could also be used as armrests. The frame of the chair to which they were attached was first produced in solid oak, but in 1962 this was changed to chromed spring steel. Similar to the upholstered furniture designed by Arne Jacobsen and Verner Panton, the Corona chair, which was and still is manufactured by Erik Joørgensen, exemplified the trend among the Danish avant-garde away fom the e-workingexistig urniture types towards a more sculptural and "synthetic" language of design. But while forward-looking designs such as these embodied the optimistic spirit of the period, they did not embrace the Pop culture ethos of ephemerality and disposability - they were highquality products intended to stand the test of time. Beyond his architecture and design work, Volther also taught at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (until 1953) and later at the School of Furnishing and the Polytechnic in Copenhagen between 1955 and 1980. As a leading Danish architect, he was also a member of the Academic Architects' Society from 1955. In keeping with other Scandinavian designers at the time, Volther combined traditional craftsmanship with modern technology so as to produce furniture that was not only durable and comfortable, but also visually exciting

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/14b21fb793d64f1fcd5a623bc86500d0defa153c90c647b00e6aec932085eca6.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1c2aec6cd8c2cb5f6689aed9e56bb5fcb41a02a644d1ea6cf29484af7d7d9974.jpg)  
Volvo PI800, launched 1960 - the car driven by Roger Moore as "The Saint"  
Gustaf Larson & Assar Gabrielsson, founders of Volvo

## volvo

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/551293b714f958eab132ba2666cbb9422b06ce8f263588350c56c8e29ad527ab.jpg)

Volvo is recognized around the world as one of the greatest pioneers of safety systems within the automotive industry. The origins of this remarkable company can be traced to the 1920s, when Sweden was still heavily reliant on car imports - at the time between 12,000 to 14,500 automobiles were being imported annually. To remedy this situation, Gustaf Larson (1887-1968) and Assar Gabrielsson (1891-1962) decided to join forces and produce their own automobiles in the summer of 1924. Gabrielsson had previously studied economics and was a successful businessman, while Larson was an engineer and a designer who had worked on the development of Morris engines at White & Poppe in Coventry, England. In 1926, they came up with the idea of designing high quality car components that would be manufactured to their exacting specifications by selected suppliers, and then assembling them with the assistance of experienced car builders. Having secured the necessary financing from the company for which they both worked, Larson and Gabrielsson acquired factory premises and established their own firm, which they named Volvo (Volvo meaning "I roll" in Latin). Their first series-manufactured car, the Öv4, left their fac-

tory in Gothenburg in April 1927, thus marking an important turning point in Swedish industrial design history. Nicknamed Jakob, this first Volvo production car was based on contemporaneous American models. With its strong chassis and four-cylinder engine, the Jakob had a top speed of 90 kph, but sales were slow during the first year partl because te ar's high build quality meant that it was relatively expensive - the opentopped version cost 4,800 Swedish krona, while the sedan cost 5,800 Swedish krona. In 1929 Volvo introduced its first six-cylinder model, the PV651 - "PV" meaning PersonVagn (People Wagon) - which had a more robust chassis than the Jakob and was especially favoured by taxi fleets. In 1935 the company launched the streamlined PV36 designed by Ivan Örnberg (1881- 1936), who had spent the whole of his career working in the US car industry. With its integrated headlamps and sleek design the PV36, which was also known as the Carioca, was undoubtedly inspired by Carl Breer's famous Airflow of 1934. During the Second World War Volvo produced several military vehicles, including a jeep, and also developed a number of civilian prototypes. 1944 saw the launch of the PV444, which combined American styling with an unconventional, unitized all-steel body. Developed by Helmer Petterson (1901-1980), the PV444 was the first Volvo that "could be afforded by the average-man-in-thestreet". In 1956, the classic 120 (known as the Amazon in Scandinavia) was introduced and featured numerous innovative safety features, including a padded dashboard. This stylish design was exhibited at the New York Motor Show in the spring of 1956, and within three months Volvo had gained more than one hundred dealers in America. With its emphasis on safety, in 1959 Volvo became the first manufacturer in the world to equip all its cars with three-point safety belts which were invented by Nils Bohlin and lessened the risk of internal injuries by having the buckle placed on the side rather than over the abdomen. During the 1960s and 1970s, models such as the 144 sedan (1966), 145 estate (1967), and 240/260 series (1974) became best-sellers while pioneering other innovative safety features such as disc brakes all round, split steering columns and outer rear-seat three-point safety belts. In 1976, Volvo presented the world's first catalytic exhaust

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/ca82e1e20334e0a407a7e5ec72a94e3ce0bdbfa0fc56b7b6c51963160295fd4e.jpg)  
Volvo ÖV4 (Jakob) automobile, introduced in 1927  
Helmer Petterson, Volvo PV444, introduced in 1944 - Volvo's first small car. It stayed in production in modified form for more than 20 years.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b5f46bd5fa0a782a3853949d2d9c56c304cc2830786b75a039afedbb368e04d1.jpg)

Volvo P120 (Amazon), introduced in 1956

Nils Bohlin, three-point safety belt, introduced in 1959 - making Volvo the first car company in the world to offer safety belts as standard equipment

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/b34ab0b28b4168a5b949f8ea4e1d11a148c2e4b135ae4bcf8be7e0ece7f505ad.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7ecc96274cf7ea1362ea24c37a3aaee7b2c9d8345befeee5e4547c6f8fa33348.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/498a76ff591c15b071965884d8dd36d877ee09e14bd6a6957beb9c91d3470f54.jpg)

emission control and by 1988 seventy percent of its new cars were fitted with catalytic converters. The highly successful 700 series was launched in 1982, with the subsequent 740/760 estate models (1985) totally dominating their market segment. During the 1990s, the company attempted to shake off its conservative image and introduced a number of new cars that featured smoother styling, yet retained a strong Volvo identity, such as the S80 (1998). More recently, Volvo's truck division has introduced its VN series of vehicles, which has taken the US market by storm: Way ahead of its competitors in terms of design, Volvo has developed a truck that is renowned as much for its safety as for its comfort. With its roomy and ingeniously planned integrated cab and sleeper compartment, within a year of its launch the VN defied all expectations and became the best-selling truck in the United States. As a leading Scandinavian company, Volvo has always been guided by a humanocentric approach to design that has dismissed superfluous styling in favour of product integrity and high build quality. This approach has also ensured that Volvo cars have become renowned as durable workhorses that just keep on going.

Indeed, Volvo has historically added much value to its brand and enjoyed increasing success and brand loyalty by raising the overall durability its products. Thus annual sales of used Volvo cars actually surpass the number of new vehicles it produces each year (around 400,000). The huge and ever-increasing secondary market for its vehicles and branded parts is massively profitable for the company. But while durability equals profitability at Volvo, the company's success can also be attributed to its outstanding reputation for safety. From the seat belt tensioner and the integrated child booster seat to the self-adjusting belt reel and the integrated side impact protection system, Volvo's innovative design programme has afforded its drivers and passengers superior protection and in so doing has set the standard in the automotive industry. Since 1999, the car manufacturing division of Volvo has been fully owned by Ford Motor Corporation and forms along with Jaguar, Aston Martin, Lincoln, and Land Rover the Ford Premier Automotive Group.

Volvo promotional photograph of two-tone Volvo P120 (Amazon), introduced in 1956 Volvo 245 Estate, part of the Volvo 240 series introduced in 1974

## pia wallén

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/593ff26ce0e4469e2209e3828db135a5b6adfd9513375b7f629e62eda430a374.jpg)

Pia Wallen founded her own design company, Wallen Form, in 79 before studying fashion design from 1981 to 1983 at the Beckman School ii StocolFolloihshe n e exhibitions for the Museum of Architecturein Stockholm and was commissioned to participate in the nationwide project "Knitting with Tradition and Rejuvenation" (1985-1986). Since 1983 she has explored in depth the textural and sculptural properties of various textiles - especially felt, which she describes as "the oldest form of textile material" and as a material o survivalwith theobjiv ofreviving traditional skills through the development of innovative contemporary products. In 1989 Wallén received the Designer of the 90s Award from the Swedish magazine Sköna Hem, and in 1992 she was commissioned to design the Felt Programme for Oggetti Cappellini. Since 1998 Asplund has retailed a number of her product designs, including her ubiquitous Felt slippers with distinctive contrasting stitching and her Krux blankets and rug, which use a cross motif as a "powerful symbol" that transcends global cultures. In 1998 she also designed a range of sleek silver jewellery with felt nings. She has participated in numerous exhibitions, including "New Scandinavia in Cologne (1999), "Swedish Style in Tokyo" (1999) and "Design World 2000" in Helsinki. Her more recent work includes the Dot Collection (2000), which focuses on tennis ball construction and comprises clothes, bags, bowls, and jewellery in different coloured felts. As a prominent member of the Swedish design community, Wallen's work reveals the continuing attraction of natural materials among the Scandinavian avant-garde, and the belief that "continuity between tradition and the present is important".

Pia Wallén, Krux (Cross) blanket for Element Design,
