Pia Wallen, Dot carpet for Asplund, 2000

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f53c8d15f84d687cc9ee2dbaadd5cbfce354a5f7bb8cbc9771dc39b8d3221617.jpg)

Ole Wanscher, Egyptian Stool for A. J. Iversen, 1960

# ole wanscher95

The Danish furniture designer and historian Ole Wanscher studied under Kaare Klint at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts). His father was an art historian who influenced Wanscher's life-long interest in furniture design history. He made several trips to Europe and Egypt to study vernacular and historical furniture types, which provided the background research material for his many books, including Funiturpe9Oute History Fur (1941), English Furniture c. 1680-1800 (1944) and History of the Art of Furniture (1946-1956). Wanscher was one of the founders of the influential annual exhibitions arranged by the Cabinetmakers' Guild in Copenhagen and designed elegant, classical furniture for numerous manufacturers, including P. Jeppesen, Rud. Rasmussen, France & Son and A. J. Iversen. Like his mentor Kaare Klint, Wanscher firmly believed that designers should study historical furniture types that had been functionally and formally honed over time. His own work was especially influenced by 18th century English furniture and Chinese, Egyptian and Greek antecedents - the folding form of his mahogany and leather Egyptian Stool (1960), for example,

was based on an ancient exemplar in the collection of the Staatliche Museen zu Berlin. Wanscher's furniture was also notable for its high quality construction and finish, and as such, it exemplified the sheer breathtaking virtuosity of Danish craftsmanship. In 1955, he succeeded Klint as professor of furniture design at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (1955-1973), where he continued to promote the study of period furniture. In 1960 he was awarded a gold medal at the Milan Triennale and the Copenhagen Cabinetmakers' Annual Prize. He also participated in numerous exhibitions, including "Design in Scandinavia" (USA 1954-1957) and "Neue Form aus Dänemark" (Germany 1956-1959). Although Wanscher's furniture was exquisitely crafted and used luxury materials such as rosewood and leather, not all of it was intended solely for the exclusive end of the market. In actual fact, he was one of the first Danish designers to develop furniture for serial mass production. The exquisite detailing of his designs underlined his masterful understanding of furniture-making techniques, while th eleant,eles orse hy interest in the analysis of period types.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/660e4cc0b02c2d78c9a4442e70b8f3c713b407cae7cfeb42554e2ef930456941.jpg)  
Ole Wanscher, oak and cane chair for Rud. Rasmussen, 1951  
Ole Wanscher, rosewood nest of tables for A. J. Iversen, 1953

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/218d1d6eed105ec6ca46eb3c72009e064667eb55cd0ab52f56f7a72ceb3f0100.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9336035b0834a4366d0ec0c4f9bca28cbaae91ca4f0cff7cbd403f1f119d8a1a.jpg)  
Björn Weckström, Planetaariset Laaksot (Planetoid Valleys)necklace for Lapnia 969

# björn weckström

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/8822d4fdd366180c3e413678abbaee64d4051c3dc0086009ad0d4fc9d001b217.jpg)

As a child Björn Weckström dreamt of becoming a sculptor, but his family insisted he pursue a more practical career and so he studied at the Kultaseppäkoulu (Goldsmith's School) in Helsinki, graduating in 1956. Following this he studied in Europe, America, Mexico and the Far East prior to working as a freelance designer from 1956 to 1963. He established his own workshop and gallery in Helsinki in 1958 and a year later received second and third prize in a cutlery design competition arranged by Hackman and Sorsakoski. In 1961 he received a challenging commission from a client who asked him to design and execute a piece of jewellery using raw Lemmenjoki gold nuggets. The resulting piece emphasized the "true, primordial nature" of the material and came to be seen as the first example of so-called jewellery of Lapland" - highly sculptural jewellery inspired by the raw and rugged landscape of Weckström's native land. The same year his Fairy Castle ring made of gold and smoky quartz was purchased by the Victoria & Albert Museum in London, and in 1962 he was awarded second prize in a jewellery design competition held by the Finnish Goldsmith's Association. The following year he was appointed principal designer for

Lapponia Jewelry, which had been established in 1960 as a manufacturer of art jewellery. Like his fellow countryman Tapio Wirkkala, Weckström was captivated by the romanticism of Lapland and took annual trips there so as to capture the "northern mystique and its beauty" in his work. Using the ancient "cire perdu" method of casting from wax, and inspired by the sculptures of Henry Moore (1898-1986), Weckström has produced new organic forms that celebrate the inherent characteristics of gold ("a hot material" that has a dramatic and charged character) and silver ("a cool material" that is sot and requires tranquil design). Freeing jewellery from its traditional forms, Weckström's pieces, such as the Flowering Wall pendant (1965) and the Planetoid Valleys necklace (1969), which was worn by Princess Leila in Star Wars, not only function as "sculpture in miniature", but also produce a sense of "complete landscape". In 1968, Weckström was awarded the Lunning Prize and shortly thereafter made a trip to Uganda, where he found much new inspiration. During the late 1960s he began incorporating acrylic elements into his jewellery pieces, and from 1974 also began designing glass and large bronze sculptures and domestic glassware. With his Lappgold and Space Silver collections, Weckström brought a new expressive sensibility to jewellery design. While his informal, modern and pioneering designs are primarily aimed at the luxury market, the manufacturer Lapponia has explained the nature of their exclusivity: "Aesthetics which are not based on the principle of fulfilling social needs remain open, forever seeking new possibilities. They are the free aesthetics of an intermediate zone. A process." As a sculptor, Weckström approaches jewellery design as an art form in itself and states, "To me, jewellery is a sculpture with a human background."

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/870b4b3a0e6f98b340b4a4ec74933493ae9c9a8a298e5504098791b4e25e2f26.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/dcbdfff49ff0f1263e86c081d952edbb4ab08be0ec49f9c453ea27574c8d1517.jpg)  
Δ Björn Weckström, Kukkiva Muuri (Flowering Wall) necklace for Lapponia, 1965

Björn Weckström, Big Drop pendant for Lapponia, 1971

Björn Weckström, Kukkiva Muuri (Flowering Wall) pendant for Lapponia, 1965

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/a153a0892801010587a777b4b353e8758c5e5f00683eb0598ad0245e8f000da7.jpg)  
Hans Wegner, 3-benet skalsto/ (Three-Legged Shell Chair) for Johannes Hansen, 1963

# hans wegner

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/42c6ce74259625ae94180d68ede3d29006f4b798bea4b6f0e8bae821f860ad95.jpg)

Onof the greatest Danish designers ofll time, Hans Wegner was the son of a master cobbler who grew up with a deep appreciation of handcraftsmanship and the inherent qualities of materials. At an early age he developed a real love and understanding of wood, which led him to undertake an apprenticeship in H.F. Stahlberg's carpentry workshop. After becoming a qualified cabinetmaker, Wegner remained at the workshop for three years until he began his military conscription. He later studied joinery at the Polytechnic in Copenhagen, where some of his lessons involved measuring period furniture displayed at the Copenhagen Museum of Decorative Arts. This experience, as well as the annual furniture exhibitions held by the Copenhagen Cabinetmakers' Guild, so influenced the young carpenter that he eventually decided to become a designer. With this intent, he began his training under the furniture designer Orla Mølgaard-Nielsen at the Kunsthåndværkerskolen (School of Arts & Crafts) in 1936. Two years later, Wegner worked in the design practice of Erik Møller and Flemming Lassen in Arhus, and in 1940 began working in the office established by Møller and Arne Jacobsen for

the design of rhus Town Hall. For this project, Wegner designed simple yet well-crafted furniture that epitomized his approach to design at this point in his career, which he described as stripping theold chairs f their outer style and letting them appear in their pure construction". From 1943 to 1946, Wegner ran his own Århus-based design studio and with Børge Mogensen designed a range of good quality yet inexpensive furniture (1945-1946) for the director of the Danish Cooperative Union, Frederik Nielsen, which was intended for small post-war apartments. Following this, Wegner worked in partnership with the architect Palle Suenson (1904-1987) in Copenhagen from 1946 to 1948, prior to establishing his own independent design practice in Copenhagen. From 1940 he also worked with the furniture-maker and chairman of the Cabinetmakers' Guild, Johannes Hansen, and designed numerous chairs for his manufacturing company, including the famous Round chair (1949), which became known as The Chair or the Classic Chair. By the 1950s, Wegner had become one of the leading Scandinavian designers and was internationally celebrated for his exquisitely balanced and beautifully crafted furniture that for the most part was constructed of solid wood. Manufactured by Johannes Hansen, Fritz Hansen, Andreas Tuck, Getama, A. P. Stolen, Carl Hansen & Søn and PP Møbler, Wegner's furniture was intended, as he explained, to be "as simple and genuine as possible, to show what we could create with our hands, to try to make wood come alive, to give it spirit and vitality, and to get things to be so natural that they could only have been made by us". His characteristically Scandinavian organic approach to design, as exemplified by his Chinese chair (1943), Peacock chair (1947), Y-chair (1950) and Valet chair (1953), focused on the importance of the physical, psychological and emotional connections between users and their tools for living. By simplifying and purifying form and construction, Wegner created timeless, supremely beautiful modern re-workings of traditional furniture types that expressed both a material and structural honesty. Over his long career he won numerous awards, including the Lunning Prize in 1951 - the first year it was established. A gifted designer and master craftsman, Wegner made Danish design world famous.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fecaff9403f4c0a3c3922c247a5fbe7fc92e5fc6dbd602c2bbb4aeb020707cf2.jpg)

Gunnar Wennerberg, Iris vase for Gustavsberg, 1897

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/1a5c8e23d5d33916fb77896a494257d9c12ed2f3ecbccbcfb117cd74ac936d02.jpg)  
Gunnar Wennerberg, Gullviva and Liljekonvalje Cowslip and Lily-ofthe-Valley) plates for Gustavsberg, 1897

# gunnar wennerberg

(b.1863 Skara, Sweden - d. 1914 Paris, France)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/86d3d17bbb78ae8d23a72d22c188feaddea30aa8b78848488f9cb8f09e3a8e78.jpg)

The Swedish painter, ceramics, glass and textile designer Gunnar Wennerberg studied painting under François Bonnat (1811-1894) and Henri Gervex (1852-1929) in Paris from 1886, and also trained at the Sevres Porcelain Factory. On returning to Sweden in 1895 he was appointed art director of the Gustavsberg ceramics factory. During his thirteen-year tenure there, Wennerberg introduced a new stylistic direction to the company that drew inspiration from the natural world. As a gifted painter of flowers, Wennerberg produced a number of exquisite designs that were decorated with Art Nouveau style floral motifs. His Gullviva (Cowslip) and Liljekonvalje (Lily-of-the-Valley) plates were meticulously hand-painted in soft natural colours with stylized representations of wild flowers native to Sweden. These designs were displayed at the 1897 Stockholm "Konst- och Industriutställningen" (Arts and Industry Exhibition) and revealed not only the growing influence of the "New Art", but also the respect with which nature was commonly held in Sweden: while the flowers Wennerberg depicted were stylized, they did not display the frenzied swirling and almost tortured quality of similar floral motifs found in French Art

Nouveau or German Jugendstil designs. Wennerberg also executed a number of designs that incorporated high-relief modelling, such as his Iris vase of 1897. Between 1898 and 1909 he also designed cameo glassware for the Kosta factory, which bore more than a passing resemblance to designs by Emile Gallé (1846-1904), but the floral motifs Wennerberg embellished these with were treated in a much more naturalistic way. From 1902 to 1908 Wennerberg taught painting at the Konstfackskolan (University College of Arts, Crafts and Design), and from 1908 he designed ceramics for the Sevres Porcelain Factory and woven textiles for Handarbetets Vänner. He returned to Paris in 1909 and lived there until his death in 1914. As one of the leading Swedish designers at the turn of the century, Wennerberg's simplified and poetic interpretation of the Art Nouveau style was of much influence in Scandinavia.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/aa23d3ccb092af6f135847b5ee233ab6737e34fb910bb16011f144d43b8be6a2.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/f6a3e929e06266bbdf8fb6fc98263385afc5cb57601cc1c53854c3edc646c7ea.jpg)

Bjørn Winblad, Lotus cutlery for Rosenthal, 1964

Bjern Winblad, nativity Christmas plates for Rosenthal, first introduced in 1971 - A new plate with a different motif was produced annually until 1983

# bjorn wiinblad

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7d297728705277f4a6aaf8a0b9e5cd3327b3535b5deb35b319f9b929964d9319.jpg)

While the Danish designer Bjørn Winblad has worked in numerous design disciplines including textiles, glassware, silverware and graphics, he is best known for his exceptionally decorative ceramics adorned with highly stylized and poetic motifs. Wiinblad trained at the Polytechnic in Copenhagen (1936-1939) and then later studied illustration at the Kongelige Danske Kunstakademi (Royal Danish Academy of Fine Arts, 1940-1943). Following this, he worked as a ceramicist under Lars Syberg at Taastrup (1943-1946) and was later employed as a designer under Jacob Bang by the Nymølle faience factory (1946-1956). In 1952, he established his own design studio at Kongens Lyngby, where for the first year he worked alongside Gutte Eriksen (b. 1918). From 1957 onwards Wiinblad designed numerous ceramic pieces for the renowned German porcelain factory Rosenthal, which are distinguished by a whimsical exuberance. Some of Winblad's designs possess the playful spirit of Scandinavian folk art, while others (often depicting religious subjects) capture the solemn beauty and mysticism of Byzantine art. His curious hybrid style, which mixes the age-old with the modern, came to epitomize the decorative revival that occurred in Scandinavian design during the 1960s. Wiinblad has also received numerous commissions for the decoration of restaurants and hotels, and has worked as a theatre designer for the Dallas Theater Center and the Pantomime Theatre in Copenhagen's Tivoli Gardens.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/fda11794df846b64eb6cc1e0d5a96acacfbb8b90c8bd429d5d21e257ad1c7d6c.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/9ba38021802217071acaac9a6f1514819d03613cac63c8f14d6ae74a9ad2f456.jpg)  
Tapio Wirkkala, Kantarelli Chanterelle)vase for littala, 1947 - Originally designed for a competition organized by the littala glassworks, this vase was later exhibited at the 1951 X) Milan Triennale

# tapio wirkkala

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/241ebd602682664305e1ffafe1f3a2191b5cd20c75da51b902f052dc985c711d.jpg)

## (b. 1915 Hanko - d. 1985 Esbo, Finland)

Tapio Veli Ilmaari Wirkkala was one of the great figures of Finnish design, whose work evoked a strong sense of national identity by embracing the traditional materials and processes of Finnish handcraft and the emotionally seductive forms found in nature. He was a designer of remarkable versatility, who worked in a diverse range of disciplines, including glass, ceramic, metalware, furniture, lighting, jewellery, exhibition, graphics, packaging and industrial design. His output ranged from the everyday to the highly exclusive and often blurred the boundaries between art, craft and design. A bushybearded pipe-smoker, Wirkkala took a workmanlike approach to design and acknowledged the need to "get away from the drawing board, to see just how a product is made and get to know the people who will help make it ... [in order to] get a real contact with the job". Beyond his technical virtuosity, Wirkkala also possessed a remarkable empathy for the materials he used. As he noted, "All materials have their own unwritten laws. This is forgotten far too often. You should never be violent with the material you're working on, and the designer should aim at being in harmony with his material." By sensitively exploring the formal potential of different mediums such as metal, wood, glass, ceramics and plastics, Wirkkala produced innovative and poetic designs that elegantly expressed the materials' intrinsic qualities. Through his extensive body of work he pioneered an expressive language of organic form that was astonishingly modern, yet also projected a sense of timeless beauty. This aspect of his work resulted from the way in which he tirelessly honed his designs, often drawing a hundred sketches just to perfect one model. Deeply inspired by and spiritually connected to the natural environment that surrounded him, Wirkkala used forms that captured the abstract essence of the natural world - from birds and leaves to the frosted patterns made by ice. He was also interested in the Lapp (Sami) culture and drew much inspiration from it: he employed the age-old form of a traditional Lapp kurska (a wooden drinking vessel), for example, as the starting point for a number of his ceramic cup designs. Guided by a dialogue between "eye, hand and thought", Wirkkala produced objects that can be seen as a unity of opposites - traditional and innovative, rational and romantic, organic and geometric, artistic and functional, visual and tactile,

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/7ead4a6693d30554a2d509f708fb4eeece01cc59e04b3720730fcb2c198b555c.jpg)

Tapio Wirkkala, Century tea service for Rosenthal, 1979

Tapio Wirkala, Model no. 3851 Paperbag vase for Rosenthal, 1977

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/819785f55b834a2e4f47eea5c85a2ac444f90fdc6120fd7b2045f68661c8c8d8.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4d7bb720374b312762cc5af551645f6db2b5474dac2679f0fee83d72fd561e5c.jpg)

national and international. Frequently he brought ancient craft sensibilities to the design of modern objects for mass production, whether it was for a range of inexpensive pressed glassware or a plastic-handled hunting knife. Having studied sculpture at the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu (University of Art and Design), Helsinki, from 1933 to 1936, he later worked as a sculptor and graphic designer. In 1947, he shared first prize with Kaj Franck in a glassware competition organized by littala and subsequently produced designs for the glassworks on a freelance basis until his death in 1985. His blown-glass Kantarelli vases, the forms of which were inspired by chanterelle mushrooms, captured the fluid organic abstraction that came to exemplify post-war Scandinavian design. This series of vases was produced from 1947 to 1960 and helped establish his reputation internationally. Wirkkala was also widely celebrated for his laminated wood leaf-shaped bowls and furniture, which although made from an essentially man-made material, possessed an extraordinary natural beauty. The multi-coloured wood laminates were laid up by him and then cut and scooped to reveal a remarkable striped effect. Using this

technique, he also produced a series of outstanding plywood sculptures and sculptural reliefs that demonstrated his complete mastery of expressive abstracted form. His designs were shown at the 1951 (IX) and 1954 (X) Milan Triennale where they were awarded six Grand Prix. Over his career he won many other awards, including the Lunning Prize in 1951, the Golden Obelisk, Domus Italia in 1963 and the Prince Eugen Medal in 1980. From 1951 to 1954 Wirkkala was the artistic director of the Taideteollinen Korkeakoulu, Helsinki, and later, between 1955 and 1956, worked briefly in the New York office of Raymond Loewy (1893-1986). While Wirkkala worked extensively for the littala glassworks, producing a plethora of memorable and highly influential designs - such as his Ultima Thule range (1968), which spawned the widespread fashion for frosted glassware in the 1970s - he also designed colourful glassware for Venini, ceramics for Rosenthal, knives for Hackman, furniture for Asko and lighting for Airam. He designed a number of exquisite hand-made jewellery pieces that were executed by Mexican silversmiths, packaging (including the well-known Finlandia vodka bottle), domestic appliances, electrical fitings and

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/51db6fe2390f948908e88d2c6454c9bf66eb84b77c2482c1eb1d3442e71599ac.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/25dd29f543d18d30c79bc39145e9cca40d8b996507628a8f2c57648d61d11a7a.jpg)  
Tapio Wirkkala, Kurve flatware for Rosenthal, 1968  
Tapio Wirkala, Pollo vases for Rosenthal, 1970  
Tapio Wirkkala, Tapio silver flatware for Kultakeskus, 1955-1957

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/badc247d2e9265cbc08ff1d958b9e657bf84f947e798c6fd14e19f232002371e.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/44ac5db5ac33e7c9632f3fde30dfd224035a43f15a343768fda647c527dc230f.jpg)

Tapio Wirkkala, Model no. TW412 silver candelabrum (hand-made to order), 1969

Tapio Wirkkala, Model no. TW2 silver-plated alpaca vase executed by Nils Westerback, 1954

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/983ba6173bed993f172724617cf9da9f65816810fa7881d2bca74a6487993428.jpg)

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/c5f3d71fac69b3b13df20551710fe770e19b25a059350cd37e229934a29707b4.jpg)

Tapio Wirkkala, Tapio glassware range for littala, 1954 - The air-bubble in the stem of each glass was made with a wet stick

Tapio Wirkkala, doubleheaded cased glass vases for littala, 1955-1956

even a lavatory seat. A recent travelling retrospective exhibition (2000-2001) has led to a profound reappraisal of Wirkkala's work, which has entranced and mesmerized audiences through the sheer breadth of its expression. Although Wirkkala once observed, "No one has ever invented new forms. It's only a question of the way in which line and shape are used", his unquestionable form-giving skills enabled him to create objects of extraordinary originality that are imbued with intelligence, gestural simplicity, inviting tactility and enchanting beauty. More than any other designer, Wirkkala's extensive body of work powerfully embodied the underlying humanist ethos that has shaped and continues to define Scandinavian design.

![](../../Scandinavian Design (Charlotte Fiell, Peter Fiell)/images/4046fb54c1f3981e699a5d95894faaf6a7cd17fc4f5ca415767e56991a5c4815.jpg)

Tapio Wirkkala, Solaris 2200 centrifugal-cast glass plate for littala, 1974
